<HL> Offer for Steinberg
@  Lifted to $1.07 Billion
@  By Unicorp Group </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> SBG.A UNI.A SVX.A CANDA </CO>
<IN> TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM)
RESTAURANTS, SUPERMARKETS AND DRUGSTORES (RFD)
REAL ESTATE, REITS, LAND DEVELOPMENT (REL) </IN>
<DATELINE> TORONTO  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   A group including Unicorp Canada Corp. said it is raising its offer for all the shares of Steinberg Inc. it doesn't already own to about 1.26 billion Canadian dollars ($1.07 billion U.S.) from C$1.27 billion, setting the stage for a war of nerves with rival bidder Socanav Inc. 

   The group, Oxdon Investments Inc., said it is increasing its offer for Steinberg Class A shares to C$53 from C$50 a share. 
It said it doesn't plan to change its C$75-a-share offer for Steinberg common shares. 
Steinberg family members and associates hold 87% of the common. 

   Socanav, backed by the Quebec government's pension fund agency, Caisse de Depot et Placement, plans to offer C$75 for each Steinberg common share and C$51 for each Steinberg Class A share. 
Steinberg, a Montreal real estate and food retailer, is about 20 times larger than Socanav, a small Montreal shipping concern. 

   Socanav wouldn't comment on Oxdon's plan to raise its bid. 
But a Caisse de Depot spokeswoman said Socanav will make its bid as planned at C$75 a common share and C$51 for each Class A share. 

   Socanav holds an exclusive option to acquire a 52% voting stake in Steinberg from a Steinberg family group. 
Socanav must make a bid of at least C$75 a common share and C$51 a Class A share by midnight July 31 to exercise the option. 
A lawyer for the Steinberg family group wouldn't say if a higher offer by Socanav would void the option agreement. 

   Oxdon could block Socanav from taking Steinberg private if it acquires enough shares to end up holding at least 33.3% of Steinberg's Class A stock. 

   Steinberg's board conditionally recommended the original Oxdon bid in the absence of a firm Socanav bid or a higher competing bid. 
Oxdon said because Steinberg's board supports a cash bid, it will offer only cash to holders. 
Previously, it offered a choice of cash or securities. 

   To finance the increased bid, Oxdon said its shareholders agreed to commit an additional C$250 million to the company. 
Oxdon is owned equally by Unicorp Canada's Kingsbridge Capital Group unit, Oxford Development Group Inc. and Gordon Investment Corp., all of Toronto.

<HL> Business Brief -- Sea Containers Ltd.:
@  Temple Holdings Ltd. Says
@  Court Denied Injunction Bid </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> SCR U.THK </CO>
<IN> TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM)
TRANSPORTATION, TRUCK AND SHIP LINES, RAILROADS (TRA) </IN>
<GV> JUSTICE DEPARTMENT (JUS) </GV>
<TEXT>
   Sea Containers Ltd. had a setback in court in its efforts to thwart a $50-a-share hostile takeover bid. 

   Temple Holdings Ltd. said a federal court in Washington, D.C., denied Sea Containers' motion for a preliminary injunction to block Temple from proceeding with its $777 million tender offer. 
As a result of the court's decision, Temple said it remains free to pursue a merger. 

   Sea Containers, a Bermuda-based equipment-leasing and shipping concern, didn't return phone calls seeking comment on the decision. 

   Temple is an affiliate of Stena Holding AG -- a unit of Stena AB of Sweden -- and Tiphook PLC, based in London.

<HL> Business Brief -- Anchor Savings Bank:
@  Loss Widened Sharply
@  In Fiscal-Fourth Quarter </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> ABKR </CO>
<IN> EARNINGS (ERN) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Anchor Savings Bank said its fiscal fourth-quarter net loss widened sharply, largely because of the adverse impact of interest rates on its net interest yields, market losses on its high-yield security portfolio, and provisions for credit losses. 

   The Hewlett, N.Y., thrift's loss widened to $26.9 million for the quarter ended June 30, from $14.8 million a year earlier. 

   The company also said the quarter included charges associated with restructuring activities. 
Nonperforming assets fell to 0.82% of total assets from 0.96% a year earlier. 
The bank increased its allowance for credit losses to $20.1 million in the quarter from $17.9 million a year ago. 

   "As a result of recent management actions and the recent decline in interest rates, gradual improvement is likely to continue," said James M. Large Jr., chairman and chief executive officer. 
But "further negative one-time adjustments" may be necessary "as the restructuring continues." 

   In national over-the-counter trading yesterday, Anchor closed at $4.25 a share, down 12.5 cents.

<DOCNO> WSJ890728-0055 </DOCNO>

<AN> 890728-0055. </AN>
<HL> Armco Net Soared
@  In Second Quarter;
@  Sales Declined 20% </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> AS </CO>
<IN> EARNINGS (ERN)
MINING, METALS (NON-PRECIOUS) (MIN) </IN>
<DATELINE> PARSIPPANY, N.J.  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Armco Inc., buoyed from extraordinary gains from its joint venture, said second-quarter net income nearly doubled to $75.6 million, or 84 cents a share. 

   In the year-earlier quarter, the steelmaker earned $39.7 million, or 43 cents a share, including a $15 million charge related to the discontinued insurance operations. 
Quarter sales, however, fell 20% to $665.6 million from $826.7 million. 

   The latest-quarter's results include gains of $112.3 million as a result of completing a joint venture with Kawasaki Steel Corp. of Japan and $15 million from a favorable ruling on certain foreign-export commitments. 
Earnings also included restructuring charges of $40.4 million and $45 million in charges related to Armco's discontinued insurance operations. 

   Armco benefited from relatively strong demand for carbon, stainless and electrical steel through much of the second quarter, although the company said it noticed softening late in the period. 
Armco said even though it "anticipates a seasonal slowdown in steel shipments for the third quarter, inventory reductions in the second half will reduce shipments further." 

   Armco's carbon-steel segment recorded operating profit of $143 million on sales of $348.5 million, including the gain from the sale of 40% of the business and net assets of Eastern Steel Division to Kawasaki Steel. 
Last year, that division had an operating profit of $50.1 million on sales of $504.6 million. 

   Operating profit in the specialty-steels business dropped to $18.5 million from $23.7 million a year ago, as a result of production problems and lower prices for steel products. 
Sales also dropped to $246.1 million from $254.8 million. 

   In composite trading on the New York Stock Exchange, Armco closed at $10.75 a share, down 37.5 cents.

<HL> Business Brief -- Fairfield Communities Inc.:
@  Pact Bars Atlantic Group
@  From Increasing Its Stake </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>

   Fairfield Communities Inc., Little Rock, Ark., said it reached an agreement with Atlantic Group Inc. that prohibits Atlantic from buying more Fairfield stock and gives Atlantic access to confidential Fairfield information. 

   In a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission, Atlantic said it had discussed with Fairfield and other parties the possibility of a "business combination transaction" with Fairfield, a builder and developer of retirement and recreational communities. 
Atlantic, a Jacksonville, Fla., retailer and distributor of natural foods and vitamins, said it wasn't certain a proposal would be made. 

   Marcel Dumeny, Fairfield senior vice president, said he didn't know of any specific agreements or ventures under discussion by the companies. 
He said the new agreement barred Atlantic and its affiliated investors, for two years, from increasing their holdings in Fairfield stock. 

   An investor group including Atlantic, has owned about 18% of Fairfield's common since September 1988 when Atlantic said it was interested in acquiring a "substantial equity position" in Fairfield.

<HL> Business Brief -- McCaw Cellular Communications:
@  Drexel to Provide Financing
@  For Proposed LIN Takeover </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> MCAWA LINB </CO>
<IN> TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM)
MEDIA, PUBLISHING, BROADCASTING, ELECTRONIC PUBLISHING (MED)
TELEPHONE SYSTEMS (TLS) </IN>
<TEXT>
   McCaw Cellular Communications Inc., Kirland, Wash., said Drexel Burnham Lambert will provide as much as $2.5 billion for McCaw's proposed acquisition of LIN Broadcasting Corp. 

   McCaw said Drexel indicated in a letter it is "highly confident" that based on current conditions, it can arrange $2.5 billion of financing "either privately or publicly." 
McCaw said the agreement with Drexel makes it confident it can complete financing "on a timely basis." 

   In the testy relations between LIN and McCaw, New York-based LIN has expressed concern over McCaw's financing. 
McCaw, the nation's largest cellular-phone concern, has offered $110 a share in cash for LIN, which holds valuable cellular franchises in several major cities. 
The bid values LIN at about $5.96 billion. 
LIN has rejected the offer as inadequate. 

   Earlier this week, LIN set a July 31 deadline for completing a definitive agreement, and said it considers $127.50 a share a fair price. 
McCaw responded to that yesterday in a letter to LIN Chairman Donald Pels, stating McCaw believes its $110-aashare offer is "full and fair."

@  Meeting, Big Holder Asserts </HL>

<CO> SBS </CO>
<TEXT>
   Salem Corp.'s management has declined to announce a shareholder meeting scheduled for next Monday, according to Edward Marshall, a Beverly Hills, Calif., real-estate developer and owner of 2.2% of Salem's 1.2 million common shares outstanding. 
The Pittsburgh-based company makes heavy equipment for the metal and coal industries; the annual meeting was called by Mr. Marshall. 

   "But a meeting will be held, anyway," Mr. Marshall said, adding: "There will at least be a meeting of concerned shareholders of Salem." 

   As previously reported, Mr. Marshall exercised his right under Pennsylvania law to call an annual meeting of shareholders. 
He said Salem has not had an annual shareholders' meeting since 1985. 

   Mr. Marshall is trying to force Salem to buy out its chairman and chief executive officer, Miami investor Victor Posner, whom he contends "is not running the company in what you would call a normal manner." 

   Salem's management has refused Mr. Marshall's request for a list of Salem's shareholders.

<HL> Who's News:
@  American Electric
@  Picks New Presidents
@  For Five Subsidiaries </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> AEP WNEWS </CO>
<DATELINE> COLUMBUS, Ohio  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   American Electric Power Co. named five new operating-company presidents in what is believed the biggest management reshuffling in the company's 84-year history. 
Triggering the changes are the retirements of several operating chiefs. 

   The new appointees, averaging 47 years in age, include the youngest unit-president ever. 
He is 36-year-old James M. Allison, who will become president and chief operating officer of Wheeling Power Co., effective Oct. 1. 

   Mr. Allison, who has been South Bend, Ind., division manager of Indiana Michigan Power Co., succeeds Thomas R. McCaffrey, 50, who becomes president and chief operating officer of Columbus Southern Power Co. here, effective Oct. 1. 
He succeeds William J. Lhota, 49, who has been named executive vice president, operations, for the AEP Service Corp. unit. 

   Also named: 

   --Joseph H. Vipperman, 48, who has been in the AEP Service Corp. position being assumed by Mr. Lhota, as president and chief operating officer of Appalachian Power Co., effective Jan. 1. 
Mr. Vipperman, who will be executive vice president at Appalachian Power until he assumes the top spot there, succeeds John W. Vaughan, who is retiring Jan. 1 at age 65. 

   --Richard C. Menge, 54, senior vice president of Indiana Michigan Power, who will become president and chief operating officer of the Fort Wayne, Ind., unit Oct. 1. 
He succeeds William A. Black, who is retiring at 65. 

   --Coulter R. Boyle III, 41, vice president at Kentucky Power Co since 1987, elevated to head the Ashland, Ky., unit beginning May 1, 1990. 
He will succeed Robert E. Matthews, who will retire May 1 at 65. 

   "We are fortunate to have been able to develop the management breadth needed in the 1990s," said AEP Chairman W.S. White Jr., 62, in announcing the changes. 
AEP has eight operating companies. 

   In New York Stock Exchange composite trading, AEP closed at $30.125, up 12.5 cents.

<HL> Alaska Drops
@  Criminal Probe
@  Of Oil Disaster
@  ---
@  State May Sue Exxon Corp.,
@  Alyeska, Or Both Firms;
@  FBI Probe Is Continuing
@  ----
@  By Charles McCoy
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> XON </CO>
<IN> ENVIRONMENT (ENV)
PETROLEUM (PET) </IN>
<GV> JUSTICE DEPARTMENT (JUS) </GV>
<TEXT>
   Alaska dropped its investigation into possible criminal wrongdoing by Exxon Corp. and Alyeska Pipeline Service Co. in connection with the Valdez oil spill. 

   Exxon and Alyeska, the consortium that operates the Trans-Alaska pipeline and its tanker-loading terminal, remain subjects of a probe by the Federal Bureau of Investigation. 
The state also is continuing its preparations for civil litigation and is expected to file soon a massive lawsuit against one or both of the companies. 
But Larry Weeks, head of the Alaska state attorney office's criminal division, said the state concluded that it doesn't have the money or the statutes to justify a prolonged criminal probe. 

   Last March 24, some 11 million gallons of oil spilled from the supertanker Exxon Valdez when it ran aground in Prince William Sound. 

   Mr. Weeks said his office's preliminary investigation indicated that the most serious criminal charges the state was likely to bring against Exxon or Alyeska would have been misdemeanors under state law, punishable only by fines of as much as $100,000. 
He said that federal laws that may apply to the same types of activities are felonies that carry fines of as much as triple the dollar amount of environmental damage. 
To date, neither Exxon nor Alyeska has been charged with any criminal wrongdoing. 

   "The costs of the investigation would have been enormous, and we didn't feel it would be worth it. . . .trying to get misdemeanor convictions," he said. 
"The federal government has more resources and better laws that make it more appropriate for them to deal with any possible criminal violations," he added. 

   Exxon declined to comment; Alyeska couldn't be reached for comment. 
But some state officials saw the decision as a break for the companies. 
Because of widespread outrage in Alaska over the spill and the companies' ineffective containment and clean-up efforts, some state officials say, Alaska might have been in a position to pursue the companies more aggressively for both civil and criminal damages. 
They suggested that the federal government might be subject to political pressure to ease up somewhat in the interest of national energy policy. 
"Exxon and the oil industry have many friends in Washington and not very many in Alaska anymore," said one state official. 

   The state is still pursuing its criminal investigation of Joseph Hazlewood, the captain of the Exxon Valdez at the time it ran aground. 
The state has charged Capt. Hazlewood with operating a vessel while intoxicated, reckless endangerment and negligent discharge. 
All the charges are misdemeanors. 
Together, they carry maximum penalties of 27 months in jail and $11,000 in fines. 
His trial is scheduled for October. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Business Brief -- Aluminum Co. of America:
@  Plan Is Set to Idle Potline
@  As Result of Jamaica Strike </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> AA LATAM LABOR </CO>
<IN> MINING, METALS (NON-PRECIOUS) (MIN) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Aluminum Co. of America, citing shortage of alumina because of a strike at its partially owned alumina refinery in Jamaica, said it will idle a 42,000-metric-ton-a-year potline at Wenatchee, Wa. 

   The move accelerates Alcoa's plan to curtail aluminum production and is expected to add more upward pressure on the price of aluminum. 

   The potline, which makes primary aluminum from alumina, is one of five at the plant, the Pittsburgh company said. 
Aluminum products, such as beverage cans and foils, are made from primary aluminum. 

   The action follows a walk-out Saturday by 600 workers at the Clarendon alumina refinery, a 50-50 partnership of Alcoa and the Jamaican government. 
The refinery produces about 750,000 metric tons of alumina a year. 

   Alcoa said primary aluminum production was being curtailed because of weakening demand for it, which has pushed aluminum-ingot prices down in the past several months.

<HL> Washington Wire:
@  A Special Weekly Report From
@  The Wall Street Journal's
@  Capital Bureau
@  ----
@  Complied by Ronald G. Shafer </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> FREST JAPAN TAXES </CO>
<IN> MONETARY NEWS, FOREIGN EXCHANGE, TRADE (MON)
REAL ESTATE, REITS, LAND DEVELOPMENT (REL)
AEROSPACE (ARO) </IN>
<GV> JUSTICE DEPARTMENT (JUS)
CONGRESS (CNG)
EXECUTIVE (EXE)
TREASURY DEPARTMENT (TRE)
DEFENSE DEPARTMENT (DEF) </GV>
<TEXT>
   SPY BUSTERS stumble so far in efforts to crack the latest espionage case. 

   The FBI's failure to arrest suspected Soviet spy Felix Bloch, or get a confession, could soon permit him to seek a court order ending surveillance, defense lawyers say. 
No matter how the case comes out, the FBI faces questions about why the Soviets were able to warn Bloch of the inquiry, forcing it into the open. 
A nagging worry: Was there another breach of U.S. security? 

   The U.S. videotape of Bloch handing a briefcase to a KGB agent in Paris may not be enough evidence for a court case. 
"It's not illegal to transfer a briefcase, even to a KGB agent," says lawyer Frank Warren Bennett, "unless it contains national security documents." 
The U.S. still doesn't know what was inside. 

   But "if Bloch was a spy, the Soviets weren't paying him just for the routine stuff he was authorized to have," says former Pentagon aide Richard Perle. 

   POLITICAL TURMOIL in Japan jeopardizes chances to cut trade barriers. 

   The U.S., which has a huge trade deficit with Japan, wants to focus coming talks on practices it complains keep out Americans. 
These include bid-rigging, Japan's boycott of foreign goods, its distribution system and its government procurement policies. 
But political uncertainty may make it hard for Tokyo to push through any concessions. 

   The Japanese, for their part, want to discuss the U.S.'s low rate of savings and its budget deficit. 
Bush administration officials hope the talks, which start late next month, will produce proposals from both sides by year's end. 
But if the meetings bog down, Congress may press the administration to take more direct action against Japan. 

   BUSH DISAPPEARS on the abortion ruling, despite his anti-abortion stance. 

   The White House strategy is to avoid getting the president personally involved in the battle for state restrictions on abortion. 
Officials don't want to offend younger pro-choice Republicans, especially women. 
"I think his advisers are attempting to keep him away from a very divisive issue," says former Reagan aide Gary Bauer. 

   Bush's silence raises questions about how deep his convictions are against abortion. 
It is an issue that aides say he isn't comfortable with. 
His reticence on abortion contrasts sharply with his calls for a constitutional amendment against flag burning. 

   One aide seeks to put a positive spin on the silence by saying, straight-faced, Bush "feels so strongly" about abortion that he doesn't want to hurt the cause by seeming to interfere with the states. 

   DRUG BATTLE: Drug-czar Bennett clashes with Justice Department officials who "think they're running the show" in setting anti-drug priorities, says an administration official, who adds that Bennett believes he is in charge. 
Attorney General Thornburgh has said he is still the boss on drug-law enforcement. 

   DEBORAH DEAN'S FILES at HUD show favors she received and those her former boss, secretary Samuel Pierce, got. 
While ill, she received a teddy bear from developer Joseph Strauss, who reaped huge consulting fees on HUD projects. 
And contrary to federal regulations, HUD kept a car for Pierce's exclusive use in New York. 

   ADOPTION ADVOCATES plan to use the Supreme Court's decision limiting the right to abortion as leverage to seek new federal support for adoption. 
A tax deduction to help cover adoption expenses is a priority. 
GOP Rep. Smith of New Jersey sees political potential: "The Republicans need to be out in front on this." 

   PARTING SHOT? 
Customs Commissioner William von Raab, a Reagan-era holdover, may announce his departure as soon as Monday, and one colleague predicts, "He won't go quietly." 
The outspoken customs chief has tangled with Treasury bosses from Bush's team. 
Democratic Rep. Rangel urges Bush to keep von Raab because of his strong anti-drug stance. 

   PROSECUTORS CLOSE IN on top targets in the Pentagon fraud investigation. 

   The government is near to bringing cases against former Navy research chief Melvyn Paisley and consultant William Galvin. 
Both men, who have denied wrongdoing, are major figures in the year-long procurement probe. 
At least six major defense contractors also are under active scrutiny. 
Unisys Corp. may agree to pay nearly $100 million to resolve its part of the scandal. 

   The Justice Department shows no sign of reducing manpower for the investigation. 
Lead prosecutor Henry Hudson and even some defense lawyers predict nearly 100 convictions. 
There have been over 20 so far. 
In the wake of the charges, the Pentagon has tightened contracting procedures and sharply restricted the use of consultants. 

   The scandal may have scuttled hopes for any administration post for former Navy Secretary John Lehman, though he isn't under investigation. 

   MINOR MEMOS: The International Platform Association announces a major speaker for its Washington convention: former House Speaker Jim Wright. 
His topic: "The Human Side of Congress." . . . 
For sale in Florida for $17 million is the Monkey Business, made infamous by Gary Hart and Donna Rice. 
The yacht, an ad says, "has a very strong, masculine feeling throughout." . . . 
Also available at an Indiana auction is "an 1860s hearse reportedly used during one of President Abraham Lincoln's funeral ceremonies." (He had services in eight cities.)

<HL> Transco Energy Faces
@  Fine of $37 Million
@  In Archaeology Case </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> E </CO>
<GV> ENERGY DEPARTMENT (ERG) </GV>
<DATELINE> HOUSTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Federal Energy Regulatory Commission proposed fining a Transco Energy Co. unit $37 million for allegedly damaging archaeological sites in Alabama. 

   FERC charged that the Transco unit, Transcontinental Gas Pipeline Corp., failed to get necessary clearances before constructing its Mobile Bay pipeline system in 1987. 
Natural-gas-transportation companies must submit information on an area's potential historic status before beginning construction. 

   A Transco spokeswoman said the company intends to fight the proposed penalty. 
She said Transco believes it had appropriate approvals, and has been assured it hasn't damaged any historic sites. 
Transco has 30 days to respond. 

   The commission said environmental consultants working for Transco surveyed the area for the pipeline and identified 77 archaeological sites. 
But, FERC charged, Transco began construction before determining whether the sites were eligible for special status, as required. 
It also alleged that Transco failed to get proper authorization from Alabama's historic preservation officer to build and operate the facilities. 

   FERC alleges Transco damaged or destroyed at least 22 historic sites that are "at least in part within the right-of-way of the Mobile Bay pipeline." 
It proposed "at this juncture" a $37 million civil penalty, though it said the maximum penalty allowed is $93 million. 
A commission spokeswoman said a final determination will be made after the company answers the charges. 

   Alabama Historic Commission representatives couldn't be reached for comment.

<HL> Business Brief -- Immunex Corp.:
@  Drug Doubles Short-Term
@  Survival in Cancer Patients </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> IMNX </CO>
<IN> DRUG MANUFACTURERS (DRG) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Immunex Corp. said an experimental biologic has proved to double the short-term survival among cancer patients who suffer bone-marrow-transplant failure. 

   Of 37 cancer patients who received the new drug GM-CSF, 59% survived for 100 days compared with 30% survival among a historical control group not receiving the drug, according to John Nemunaitis, a physician and researcher at the Fred Hutchinson Cancer Research Center in Seattle. 
Immunex is a Seattle biotechnology concern. 

   Granulocyte macrophage colony-stimulating factor is a form of the naturally occuring protein that sparks production of infection-fighting white blood cells by the bone marrow.

<DOCNO> WSJ890728-0044 </DOCNO>
<DD> = 890728 </DD>
<AN> 890728-0044. </AN>
<HL> Economy:
@  Conferees Clear Thrift-Rescue Bill,
@  Reject Bush Plan to Fund Package
@  ---
@  How to Raise $166 Billion
@  Was the Last Big Issue;
@  Test of Wills Expected
@  ----
@  By Paulette Thomas
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> GOVMT </CO>
<IN> SAVINGS AND LOANS, THRIFTS, CREDIT UNIONS (SAL)
BANKS (BNK)
TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM)
FINANCIAL, ACCOUNTING, LEASING (FIN)
BOND MARKET NEWS (BON) </IN>
<GV> CONGRESS (CNG)
EXECUTIVE (EXE)
TREASURY DEPARTMENT (TRE) </GV>
<DATELINE> WASHINGTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   House and Senate negotiators on the sweeping savings-and-loan bill set the stage for a test of wills with President Bush by rejecting his plan for financing the package, then approving the 1,000-page bill. 

   How to raise $166 billion for closing sick savings-and-loan companies was the last major issue to be decided by the conferees on the landmark bill, which replenishes funds in the insolvent Federal Savings and Loan Insurance Corp., overhauls banking and thrift industry regulations, and requires S&amp;Ls to risk more of their own capital before FSLIC insurance bails out depositors at an insolvent institution. 

   The compromise bill heads back to both chambers for final consideration next week, and could be on the president's desk by next Friday. 

   After months of the stalemate and almost six hours of debate of the financing plan yesterday, the Senate conferees persuaded Alan Cranston (D., Calif.), the lone Democratic holdout, to adopt the House financing plan, largely in exchange for acceding to him in an earlier agreement to keep the nation's top thrift official in place. 

   "I reluctantly support this motion," Sen. Cranston said. 
"Otherwise I see everlasting impasse in this conference." 

   Bush administration officials have vowed that such a move would prompt a veto recommendation, and yesterday Treasury Secretary Nicholas Brady and Budget Director Richard Darman reiterated the message. 
"We are pleased that the conference committee has finished work on the savings-and-loan legislation, which is generally an excellent product," Mr. Brady said. 
"However, the administration position on financing is perfectly clear and remains unchanged." 

   But President Bush may be hard pressed to summon a veto on a bill he unveiled in February as "emergency" legislation, and one in which the administration's plan prevailed in almost every other important issue. 
"The bill has so much that the president wants, and it's so important for the country, it's hard to believe that he would veto it" because of the funding issue, said Rep. Charles Schumer (D., N.Y.), one of the proponents of the House financing plan. 

   The primary purpose of the bill is to raise $50 billion that would be used to close or merge hundreds of insolvent savings-and-loan companies. 
But how that money will be raised became a game of chicken between the administration and Democrats, who otherwise have largely backed the administration's tough positions. 

   The administration plan would raise $50 billion using industry bonds, which wouldn't be included on the federal budget, and thus not increase the budget deficit. 
The interest of more than $100 billion, to be paid mostly by the taxpayer, would be on the budget. 
But using industry bonds would increase the cost by more than $5 billion over 30 years, because they would carry a higher interest rate than Treasury bonds. 

   The House approved a plan to raise the money using Treasury bonds, and including the spending on the budget. 
The ultimate costs are lower, but it would require a waiver of the Gramm-Rudman deficit-reduction law, a precedent the administration viewed as the first step in eroding discipline in the budget. 

   In a peculiar exchange, the fate of the financing plan hinged upon the close relationship between Sen. Jake Garn (R., Utah) and his former aide, Danny Wall, now chairman of the Federal Home Loan Bank Board. 
House conferees, who had been intent on forcing Mr. Wall to again face confirmation by the Senate to keep his job, dropped their opposition in exchange for the on-budget vote. 

   "I'm not the kind who makes deals," said House Banking Committee Chairman Henry Gonzalez (D., Texas), "but {several senators} said they could get some roadblocks out of the way if there could be some understanding on Garn's insistence on Wall." 
On Wednesday, Mr. Gonzalez paved the way for speedy approval of steps to protect Mr. Wall's job. 

   Sen. Cranston, who voted for the administration plan in the Senate, also had reason to preserve Mr. Wall's job without reconfirmation hearings. 
His confrontations with Mr. Wall over a now-insolvent California thrift would have undoubtedly come under scrutiny again in a hearing. 

   Sen. Cranston proposed several compromises that would have put some of the money off-budget, but still violate the administration's proposal by requiring a Gramm-Rudman exemption. 
In the end, he conceded. 
"Deadlock is intolerable," he said. 
"Delay is intolerable." 

   Late last night, the conferees were finishing up provisions in the bill, including an attempt to strip the bill of special exemptions for certain institutions. 

   Late last night, the conferees completed final provisions in the bill, including one to establish conflict-of-interest rules for employees of the Resolution Trust Corp., the huge new arm of the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp., which will oversee thrift closures and sales of their assets. 

   The bill, which conferees hope the president will sign before the August recess, could still face hurdles, however. 
The Senate could invoke a rule to require 60 votes for its passage, due to the Gramm-Rudman exemption. 
Sen. Phil Gramm (R., Texas) has said earlier that he would call for the rule. 

   Key provisions of the bill include: 

   --Minimum capital, or the cash cushion for thrifts, would be 1.5% of assets. 
The greater the risk to a thrift's assets, the greater must be its capital base. 

   --The Federal Home Loan Bank Board would become an arm of the Treasury. 
Bank regulators and the Treasury would oversee the Resolution Trust Corp., which will sell or merge insolvent thrifts and their assets using $50 billion during the next three years. 

   --Deposit insurance premiums would be raised for banks and thrifts. 

   --Bank-holding companies would be permitted to acquire healthy thrifts immediately, which was prohibited under current law. 

   --Investments in high-yield junk bonds and other risky activities would be prohibited or allowed only in separately capitalized affiliates. 

   --Thrifts would be required to invest more in home mortgages. 

   --Federal Home Loan Banks would be required to contribute a percentage of their earnings toward low-income homebuyers. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Chrysler Unveils
@  Cost-Cutting Plan,
@  Boosts Rebates
@  ----
@  By Melinda Grenier Guiles and Neal Templin
@  Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> C </CO>
<IN> AUTOS, AUTO PARTS (AUT) </IN>
<DATELINE> DETROIT  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Chrysler Corp. has launched an aggressive $1 billion cost-cutting program that includes plans for an 8% reduction in its white-collar ranks by the end of this year. 

   The program takes effect immediately and will run through the end of 1990. 
Chairman Lee A. Iacocca unveiled the measures to the auto maker's top managers at a meeting here yesterday. 

   The moves come as the U.S. auto industry struggles to cope with a slowing economy and growing overcapacity -- the ability to build more vehicles than consumers want to buy. 
To help deal with this in the short-term, Chrysler yesterday doubled rebates on six of its slowest-selling cars. 
The midsized Eagle Premier, for example, now carries a $2,000 cash incentive instead of $1,000, and Omni and Horizon subcompacts now have a $1,500 rebate that amounts to nearly 25% off the base price. 

   At a news conference, Bennett E. Bidwell, chairman of the Chrysler Motors unit, predicted that total U.S. car and truck sales in 1990 will fall to about 14.2 million vehicles from an estimated 14.7 million this year and almost 16 million in 1988. 
Chrysler, with many aging models, is bearing a disproportionate share of the decline. 

   But Mr. Iacocca emphasized in a prepared statement that the company's new program "will not be a one-shot deal" stemming solely from the industry's sales slump. 
"The car and truck business in the U.S. is undergoing a dramatic and permanent transformation that puts enormous pressures on all manufacturers to get their programs and costs in line," he said. 
"We must take the necessary steps to ensure our profitability." 

   These steps include reducing salaried employment by about 2,300, or 8%, from Chrysler's total of roughly 30,000 white-collar workers by year-end. 
Chrysler said it hopes to accomplish most of the cuts through early retirement or buy-outs. 

   This cut, along with cuts in other personnel costs, including fringe benefits and overtime pay, would account for some $240 million of the $1 billion target, Mr. Bidwell said. 
He added that the manpower reductions "will adversely impact" Chrysler's fourth-quarter earnings, but "if we do it right, the actions we're taking should go to the bottom line beginning in the first quarter of 1990." 
He didn't elaborate. 

   Chrysler announced its program after the market closed yesterday. 
Chrysler stock fell $1.25 to $24.875 in composite New York Stock Exchange trading. 

   The cost-cutting program won't affect the No. 3 auto maker's long-range product plans, warranty programs or customer satisfaction efforts. 
But it will include trimming purchased material costs, eliminating "internal inefficiencies" and "redundancies" and improving controls on product design and manufacturing costs in Chrysler's vehicle-making and selling operations, the company said. 
Chrysler technical and financial-services subsidiaries will have their own cost-reduction programs beyond the $1 billion target for Chrysler Motors. 

   To encourage managers to meet the cost-cutting goals, Chrysler is adopting an unusual incentive that allows the managers to "bet" on Chrysler's performance by automatically transferring 5% or 10% of their salary to a special escrow account. 
Participants lose all their wager if Chrysler cuts less than $500 million during the course of the program. 
If the cuts exceed that amount, participants would get payoffs ranging on a graduated scale from half their investment to double their investment if the $1 billion goal is met. 
Participants get an additional payoff if Chrysler's stock price rises above $24.50 a share -- an average of recent prices -- by the end of the 1990, when the program and incentive are scheduled to end. 

   Chrysler will offer the voluntary program to some 2,000 of its managers; all 29 of the top officers, including Mr. Bidwell and Mr. Iacocca, signed on at the 10% level, Mr. Bidwell said. 

   Chrysler's new sales incentives are intended to ease overcrowding on the lots of dealers, who had an average 83-day supply of Omni and Horizon subcompacts and an 85-day supply of Premiers as of July 10. 
A 60-day supply is considered normal. 

   Other models that get doubled rebates include the Sundance and Shadow compacts, with new incentives of $1,500, and the Eagle Summit, with $1,000. 
Chrysler also added a new $300 rebate on certain Colt three-door hatchbacks, and raised to $750 from $500 the rebate on some Colts with automatic transmissions.

<HL> Empire of America
@  Says Holder Equity
@  Is Low After Loss </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> EOA </CO>
<IN> EARNINGS (ERN) </IN>
<DATELINE> BUFFALO  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Empire of America Federal Savings Bank posted a $54.2 million net loss in the second quarter and warned that its shareholder equity for common stockholders would be wiped out by Sept. 30 if such losses persist. 

   The nation's 17th-largest savings institution cited a marked decline in net interest income and a sharp increase in its provision for possible loan losses for the second-quarter loss, which included a $5.2 million reversal of a tax credit recognized in a prior quarter. 
In the year-ago quarter, Empire posted a loss of $19.8 million. 

   In the latest quarter, net interest income fell to $21.6 million, about half the $43.4 million of the year-earlier period. 
The loan-loss provision increased to $40.1 million from $24.4 million a year ago and largely reflected growth in recent years of the bank's consumer-loan portfolio, particularly its VISA credit card and automobile-lending operations. 
Empire recently completed the sale of $650 million, or about 90%, of its VISA portfolio. 

   The second-period loss -- which followed a $3.1 million first-quarter profit and last year's $57.3 million net loss -- reduced Empire's total shareholders' equity to $104.6 million. 
Kent Dixon, president and chief executive officer, said if Empire has another loss the size of the second-quarter deficit, the only shareholders' equity remaining at the end of the third quarter would be attributable to its preferred stock held by the Federal Savings and Loan Insurance Corp. 

   In composite trading on the American Stock Exchange, Empire's stock closed at $1, down 12.5 cents.

<HL> Foreign Exchange:
@  Dollar Is Mixed as 2nd-Period GNP Data
@  Help Assuage Some Recession Concerns
@  ----
@  By Matthew Geiger
@  Special to The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> CANDA JAPAN FREST EUROP </CO>
<IN> MONETARY NEWS, FOREIGN EXCHANGE, TRADE (MON)
FOREIGN-EXCHANGE MARKETS (FRX)
PRECIOUS METALS, STONES, GOLD, SILVER (PCS)
COMMODITY NEWS, FARM PRODUCTS (CMD) </IN>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The dollar was mixed after a report on second-quarter U.S. economic growth assuaged some recession concerns. 

   Traders said the U.S. currency was also aided by technical factors when the dollar encountered buying interest at levels the market considered important. 

   The preliminary estimate of the second-quarter gross national product showed moderate economic growth of 1.7%, in line with market expectations and a substantial slowdown from a revised 3.7% growth rate in the first quarter. 

   While the decelerating economic activity reflected in the report continues to justify interest-rate cuts by the U.S. Federal Reserve and underscores the possibility of a recession, many traders said statistics on U.S. business-inventory levels dispelled worries of a sharp build-up entailing deep production cutbacks. 

   "It looks like we're heading for the soft landing," said Garrett Glass, vice president of foreign exchange at First National Bank of Chicago. 
With manufacturers relieved of additional pressure to reduce output, "that could help GNP {growth} to stay around 2% this quarter," he said. 

   Also stimulating the dollar's rebound is the perception that the Fed, after letting the federal-funds rate drop a quarter point to a middling range of around 9% Wednesday, will stand pat on credit for the next several weeks. 
Federal funds are overnight loans of reserves among Fed member banks. 

   In late afternoon New York trading, the dollar was at 1.8782 marks, up from 1.8675 marks Wednesday, and at 139.26 yen, down from 140.02 yen. 
The pound was at $1.6554, down from $1.6590. 

   In trading in Tokyo Friday, the dollar opened at 139.30 yen, down from 139.35 yen at the close there Thursday. 

   Strong demand returned for the yen Thursday after its sharp fall on recent Japanese political concerns, boosting it against all major currencies. 
The mark, which registered some of the strongest gains against the yen at the height of the concerns, slumped to 74.145 yen late Thursday in New York, well below 74.98 yen Wednesday. 

   Traders said the yen was helped by the modest reverberations from the ruling Liberal Democratic Party's defeat Sunday in a national election for half the seats in parliament's upper house. 
The LDP has moved swiftly to find a safe replacement for embattled Prime Minister Sosuke Uno and to reform its image. 

   Unless the LDP's struggle to jettison its scandal-ridden past appears to be failing, the yen should make further gains during the next few weeks as the market buys back short-yen positions and again focuses on investment opportunities in the strong Japanese economy, said Dan Brooks, chief foreign-exchange dealer at Westpac Banking Corp. in New York. 

   While the yen made steady gains in Asian trading, the dollar held fast against other currencies. 
When Europe took over the arena, activity diminished as participants awaited the U.S. GNP report. 

   The dollar initially sank on the report's release as some participants sought to ignite a move on the low GNP growth, but the drive quickly lost momentum after running into a wave of dollar buyers around 1.8625 marks, which dealers termed an important level on charts. 

   The inability to overcome buying interest and the positive aspects of the GNP report triggered short-covering by players who had sold the dollar during its recent slide and lifted the U.S. currency through the New York afternoon. 

   The GNP report showed inventories grew at a $22 billion annual pace, down from a $24.5 billion rate in the first quarter. 
Two important inflation measures released with the GNP, the implicit price deflator and the fixed-weight deflator, both reflected inflation levels in the quarter close to predictions, at 4.9% and 5.2% rates, respectively. 

   On New York's Commodity Exchange, gold for current delivery settled at $373.70 an ounce, down $1.20. 
Estimated volume was a moderate 3.5 million ounces. 

   In early trading in Hong Kong Friday, gold was quoted at $372.85 an ounce. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Bill to Bar Burning
@  Of Flag as Protest
@  Clears House Unit
@  ----
@  By John E. Yang
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<GV> CONGRESS (CNG) </GV>
<DATELINE> WASHINGTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The House Judiciary Committee approved legislation designed to circumvent a Supreme Court ruling that prohibits states from punishing a person who burns the U.S. flag as a political protest. 

   The 28-6 vote by which the proposed criminal statute was passed belies the partisan division created by the issue in the House. 
It came after a sometimes raucous committee meeting in which Chairman Jack Brooks (D., Texas) blocked Republican efforts to substitute a constitutional amendment and committee Democrats defeated other GOP attempts to derail the measure. 

   The full House is to consider the bill in mid-September, shortly after lawmakers return from a monthlong recess. 
In the Senate, Democratic and GOP leaders have agreed to consider both a statute and a constitutional amendment in October. 

   The Judiciary Committee's measure would make it a crime for anyone to "knowingly mutilate, deface, burn or trample upon" a flag, punishable by as long as one year in prison and an unspecified fine. 
An exception would be made for disposing of a worn or soiled flag. 

   Currently, federal law makes it a crime to "knowingly cast contempt" upon a flag by "publicly mutilating, defacing, defiling, burning or trampling upon it" and provides penalties of as long as a year in prison and a fine of as much as $1,000. 

   Last month, the Supreme Court threw out the conviction of a Texas man who set a flag afire during a 1984 demonstration. 
The court said he was "engaging in political expression" that is protected by the First Amendment. 
By dropping the phrase "cast contempt," proponents of the legislation believe it would avoid the court's objection by targeting an action rather the intent behind it. 

   The committee-passed bill includes a provision calling for expedited judicial review of the statute, and Rep. Brooks vowed to introduce a constitutional amendment himself should it fall. 

   House Republicans, along with President Bush, say only a constitutional amendment would protect the flag. 
White House Chief of Staff John Sununu this week urged House GOP leaders to continue pressing the flag issue because he believes it could divide House Democrats, according to House Minority Leader Robert Michel (R., Ill.). 

   The Democratic leadership is more wary of tampering with the Bill of Rights and wants first to try a statutory remedy. 
But Democrats also are angry and nervous over the way Mr. Bush used the flag to associate himself with patriotic values in last year's presidential campaign and are fearful of being driven in the same corner that Michael Dukakis found himself regarding the Pledge of Allegiance issue. 

   House Democrats, including Speaker Thomas Foley of Washington and Majority Leader Richard Gephardt of Missouri, jammed a small stage to face television cameras and praise the bill. 
They also used the opportunity to fire a salvo in the battle of symbols. 

   While Mr. Bush used the Iwo Jima Memorial as a backdrop when he called for a constitutional amendment, the lawmakers came armed with the flag that flew over the Capitol the day Pearl Harbor was bombed, in Rome after that city was liberated in World War II, and over the USS Missouri when Gen. Douglas MacArthur accepted Japan's surrender. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Raychem's Profit Fell
@  75% in Fourth Period;
@  Raynet Unit Blamed </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> RYC </CO>
<IN> EARNINGS (ERN) </IN>
<DATELINE> MENLO PARK, Calif.  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Raychem Corp., citing an unprofitable subsidiary and slimmer profit margins, said net income fell 75% in the fourth quarter ended June 30 on a 4.5% drop in revenue. 

   The electronics concern said profit fell to $12.97 million, or 37 cents a share, from $52.80 million, or $1.52 a share, a year earlier. 
Revenue fell to $300.81 million from $314.87 million. 
Fiscal 1989 net fell 71% to $36.35 million on a 1.1% drop in revenue to $1.08 billion. 

   The company attributed its lackluster bottom line to its Raynet subsidiary, which had a $15 million loss in the quarter and $55.07 million in the year. 
Raynet, a relatively new unit that designs fiber-optics systems, had a loss because it is still developing products and markets, a spokesman said. 
Raychem is seeking outside investment in the unit. 

   Raychem also blamed slimmer profit margins caused by a shift in its sales away from military buyers to civilian aircraft and automobile customers, where profits are thinner. 
The shift has caused factory overcapacity in some products, it said. 

   Raychem's marginal effective tax rate -- 26% in fiscal 1988 -- climbed to 43% in the third quarter of 1989, which affected net income, the company said. 
And a strengthening dollar cut profit on overseas sales, which make up about 60% of the company's revenue, it said. 

   Raychem, which makes wires, cables and connectors for a broad range of industries, said it expects revenue to grow 5% to 10% in fiscal 1990. 
Development outlays at Raynet will reduce fiscal 1990 pre-tax profit about $60 million, it said. 

   Raychem's shares closed yesterday at $35.25, down 25 cents on New York Stock Exchange trading.

<HL> Rep. Markey Raises
@  Possibility of Limiting
@  Stock-Index Futures </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> STOCK INDEXES (NDX)
COMMODITY NEWS, FARM PRODUCTS (CMD) </IN>
<GV> CONGRESS (CNG)
SECURITIES AND EXCHANGE COMMISSION (SEC) </GV>
<DATELINE> WASHINGTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The chairman of the House subcommittee that oversees financial markets raised the possibility of clamping down on stock-index futures activity if studies show traders are manipulating the indexes for their own benefit. 

   Rep. Edward Markey (D., Mass.) made his comments in response to a Securities and Exchange Commission staff study that found a link between index arbitrage -- taking advantage of small differences between stock prices and futures prices -- and price volatility in the stock markets. 
Mr. Markey has expressed concern that volatility is frightening small investors away from the markets. 

   "Getting to the bottom of what creates the spread between the futures and the stock market should be a top priority for our market regulators," Mr. Markey said. 
"If we find that the spreads are caused by inefficient market mechanics or manipulative trading practices, we will have to reassess the relationship between futures trading and the stock market." 

   Limits on stock-index futures aren't likely soon. 
But the SEC economics division said it was studying whether price differences are caused by "news or noise," that is, whether the markets are responding to legitimate economic data or not. 

   Mr. Markey released the SEC staff report at a hearing at which some former brokers and exchange officials said they believe some manipulative practices -- such as "front-running," in which brokers use knowledge of coming customer orders in trading for their own account -- are common. 
The SEC staff said it doesn't believe front-running is "widespread." 
Subcommittee staff members said they planned to pursue the issue in hearings this fall with regulators and exchange officials. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Neoax Unit to Pay
@  $2.3 Million to Settle
@  Federal Contract Case </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> NOAX </CO>
<IN> AEROSPACE (ARO) </IN>
<GV> JUSTICE DEPARTMENT (JUS) </GV>
<DATELINE> WASHINGTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   A unit of Neoax Inc. agreed to pay the government about $2.3 million to settle claims that the company submitted false information in connection with contracts to build radar equipment for F-15 fighter aircraft, the Justice Department said. 

   The manufacturing company previously paid the government about $1 million in connection with the same alleged contract fraud, the department said. 

   A spokesman for Neoax, based in Stamford, Conn., said, "The settlement will have no impact on Neoax's results of operations or financial condition." 

   Neoax earlier voluntarily disclosed to the government that its Nuclear Automation Inc. unit had committed contract fraud between 1982 and 1986. 

   The Justice Department said that Neoax's total payment of $3.3 million would resolve the company's potential liability for monetary damages and civil penalties. 

   In national over-the-counter trading yesterday, Neoax shares closed at $11.75, unchanged.

The financing plan was the last major issue to be resolved before the House and Senate negotiators finally approved the landmark bill.

   Bristol-Myers and Squibb agreed to merge in a stock swap valued at $11.52 billion, creating what may be the world's second-biggest drug manufacturer, after Merck. 
The industry's recent consolidation reflects competitive pressures and huge costs. 

   ---

   Economic growth slowed to a 1.7% annual rate in the second quarter, easing fears of inflation without indicating a recession. 
The GNP growth was much smaller than the revised 3.7% rate in the first quarter. 

   ---

   Ford Motor agreed to acquire Paramount Communication's consumer and commercial finance unit, Associates Corp., for $3.35 billion. 

   ---

   Workers at a Nissan plant in Tennessee rejected by 2-to-1 being represented by the United Auto Workers. 
The vote, a major defeat for the union, was viewed as a referendum on Japanese management in the U.S. 

   ---

   Chrysler launched a $1 billion cost-cutting program that includes an 8% cut in its white-collar work force. 
The action comes as auto makers struggle to cope with slumping sales. 

   General Motors' profit fell 3.5% in the second quarter, while Ford's dropped 15%. 
Results are expected to worsen in the coming months. 

   ---

   Stocks and bonds rallied, helped by the Fed's latest credit-easing move. 
The Dow Jones industrials closed up 22.38 points, at 2635.43, another postcrash high. 
The dollar was mixed.

Traders also may be charged under the federal anti-racketeering law.

   MAI Basic Four dropped its hostile bid for Prime Computer but still plans to seek control in a proxy fight.

   BANK OF NOVA SCOTIA, Toronto, said it filed a prospectus for a preferred-share issue that will add 199.8 million Canadian dollars ($168.8 million) to its capital. 
The bank said the issue of 10.8 million non-cumulative Series 3 perpetual preferred shares will be priced at C$18.50 a share. 
Dividends will be the greater of 9 1/2% or the dividend on the bank's common shares. 
The Series 3 preferred shares are redeemable by the bank after five years, subject to regulatory consent, either in cash at premiums declining from 7% to par in late 1999, or in common shares at 95% of their then current market price.

<HL> What's News --
@  World-Wide </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<TEXT>
   SOVIET LAWMAKERS ADOPTED economic resolutions for the Baltics. 

   The Supreme Soviet overwhelmingly approved two resolutions paving the way for Lithuania, Latvia and Estonia to have greater control of their economies. 
One measure would allow the three Baltic states to trade independently with the 12 other Soviet republics. 
The other supports a draft law calling for economic independence by Jan. 1 for Estonia and Lithuania, which have declared themselves sovereign states. 

   The parliament's action was considered a major victory for Baltic activists, who have accused Moscow of sapping the republics' natural resources. 

   ---

   House GOP leaders abandoned support of Bush administration efforts to kill production of Grumman Corp.'s F-14 jet fighters. 
The House, approving a $305 billion defense authorization bill, also rejected Bush's proposal for simultaneous development of the Midgetman and MX mobile-missile systems. 

   ---

   A Korean Air DC-10 crashed about a mile short of a runway at Tripoli's airport while trying to land in fog. 
Officials in Libya said at least 82 people were killed, including four on the ground. 
The plane, with 199 people aboard, was en route from Seoul. 
The cause of the crash wasn't immediately known. 

   ---

   A court in Sweden convicted Carl Gustav Christer Pettersson of murdering Prime Minister Olof Palme in Stockholm in February 1986. 
The court of two judges and six lay assessors sentenced Pettersson, a 42-year-old with a long criminal record, to life in prison. 
His lawyer said he would appeal. 

   ---

   Poland's Jaruzelski plans to give up the Communist Party leadership tomorrow at a Central Committee meeting that could also produce a purge of Poland's ruling hierarchy, sources in Warsaw said. 
The government also is expected to unveil a plan to allow steep food price rises next week. 
A party official warned of possible riots. 

   ---

   U.S. researchers reported encouraging results from a trial of the drug DDI in patients with AIDS and AIDS-related complex, without the toxicity of AZT, an antiviral drug approved for AIDS treatment. 
The National Institutes of Health scientists found evidence of improved immune function, as well as weight gain. 

   ---

   Alaska has dropped its inquiry into possible criminal wrongdoing by Exxon and Alyeska Pipeline Service, which likely would have faced misdemeanor charges in connection with the March 24 oil spill in Prince William Sound. 
The companies remain under FBI investigation. 

   ---

   The IRS commissioner said that he was convinced after three days of a House subcommittee's hearings into ethical conduct and standards at the agency that improvements need to be made. 
The official pledged to review how the agency investigates allegations of wrongdoing among employees. 

   ---

   An immigration appeals panel ruled that leftist writer Margaret Randall is a U.S. citizen and can't be deported. 
In a 3-2 decision, the panel said the poet and author, an outspoken critic of U.S. policy, became a Mexican citizen in 1967 under economic duress. 

   ---

   The National Research Council reported that despite economic gains made by blacks in the U.S. over the past 50 years, their progress has stagnated or worsened relative to whites since the early 1970s. 
The report said blacks have been hurt by persistent racial inequalities in housing and education. 

   ---

   The House Judiciary panel cleared legislation making it a crime to desecrate the American flag as a political protest. 
The bill is aimed at circumventing a Supreme Court ruling that such acts are a protected form of free speech. 
GOP members of the committee had sought a constitutional amendment. 

   ---

   Rafsanjani appeared set to win today's presidential election in Iran. 
The parliamentary speaker, who faces a lone challenger, reportedly already was assembling a cabinet to govern in the post-Khomeini era. 

   ---

   Secretary of State Baker is expected to insist on a comprehensive settlement of the conflict in Cambodia during Sunday's international peace conference in Paris. 
The four warring Cambodian factions, meanwhile, settled a dispute over representation and will attend the 20-nation forum. 

   ---

   A military tribunal in Burma sentenced to death three opposition members for a July 7 bombing that killed two people. 
The sentences were the first against scores of Rangoon's opponents detained under martial-law rules imposed last week. 

   ---

   Philippine troops captured a prominent communist guerrilla leader in a Manila ambush. 
Officials said the arrest of Satur Ocampo on subversion charges was a major setback to the 20-year insurgency. --- 

   Khashoggi was released from federal custody in New York after his lawyer posted $10 million bail in an arrangement approved by an appeals judge over prosecutors' objections. 
The Saudi financier is charged with helping ousted Philippine leader Marcos in an alleged $100 million real-estate fraud.

<HL> Bethlehem Steel Sets
@  An Expansion at Cost
@  Totaling $300 Million </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> BS </CO>
<IN> MINING, METALS (NON-PRECIOUS) (MIN) </IN>
<DATELINE> BETHLEHEM, Pa.  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Bethlehem Steel Corp. said it will spend $300 million to build three new sheet-coating lines, increasing its sheet-coating capabilities by about 765,000 tons a year. 

   The nation's second-largest steelmaker, after USX Corp., has a current coating capacity of more than one million tons, and said it will build a 72-inch galvanizing line at its Burns Harbor plant in Indiana designed primarily to serve the automotive market. 
The project, scheduled for completion in 1992, will produce 450,000 tons of steel that can better accommodate paint and adhesive products. 

   Bethlehem said it believes the automotive industry, while currently in a slump, will need "substantial additional tonnage" of so called hot-dip galvanized product by 1995 as car makers increasingly shift from cold-rolled sheet steel, which doesn't have the same corrosion protection as hot-dip products. 

   A 48-inch line to make galvanized sheets for the non-automotive market will be built by 1992 at Bethlehem's Sparrows Point, Md., plant and replace some existing hot-dip capacity at that plant. 
A third line, essentially a duplicate of the 48-inch Sparrows Point facility, will be built on an undisclosed site in the South or Southwest to serve the construction market. 
Bethlehem said it expects the third line to be a joint venture with at least one other company. 

   Bethlehem said the two lines will allow it to take advantage of expected growth in the light construction market. 
The company expects to fund the capital improvements with debt financing. 

</TEXT>

<CO> CTUS GOVMT </CO>
<DATELINE> EMERYVILLE, Calif.  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Cetus Corp. said its fundamental patent application covering the thermostable enzyme tag DNA polymerase has been approved by the U.S. patent and trademark office. 

   The company said its tag DNA polymerase is a key component of its geneamp polymerase chain reaction technology and is one of the largest selling enzyme products in the molecular biology research market. 
Cetus said its Geneamp technology amplifies specific gene sequences and is used in human diagnostics and forensic analysis, among other things. 

   Cetus said the patent is expected to be issued in the next few months.

<HL> One Fallen Guru
@  Is Rising Again
@  ----
@  By John R. Dorfman
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> STOCK MARKET, OFFERINGS (STK) </IN>
<TEXT>
   He used to dress up as Moses, let his "followers" come on stage to touch him, and expect them to do exactly as he said. 

   This was no religious cult leader. 
This was Joe Granville, stock market technician, news letter writer and showman extraordinaire. 

   For a decade in the 1970s and early 1980s, his words moved in vestors to terror or elation. 
In January 1981, for instance, he said to "sell everything," and a lot of investors did just that. 
The Dow Jones Industrial Average plunged 24 points -- a huge loss at the time -- on record volume. 

   Today, Joseph Granville's words mainly evoke snickers, in part because of his stubborn refusal to recognize the great bull market that started in August 1982. 
It took him some four years to reverse his position, and by that time, his huge following was gone. 

   But Mr. Granville's recommendations since the stock market crash have been right on target, and at age 65 he's trying to make a comeback. 
"I want to spend the bulk of my remaining years teaching people how to use {the market indicators Mr. Granville has developed} so when I'm gone they will know how to do it themselves," he says. 

   The man who quietly publishes his Granville Market Letter in Kansas City, Mo., seems far removed from the Joe Granville of a decade ago. 

   At the height of his influence, Mr. Granville was a glitzy showman. 
Chimpanzees romped on stage with him; laser lights blazed to entertain the crowds at his investment seminars. 

   The man was "out of control," says New York money manager Jack Orben. 
He "turned the profession into a burlesque," charges New Jersey money manager Kenneth Hackel. 

   Mr. Granville's response: "I observed that when people are entertained they will retain more knowledge." 

   Whatever the case, his followers loved him. 
Even people who say Mr. Granville's advice cost them money still talk about him affectionately. 

   John B. Olinger, a retired postal worker in Florida, used to subscribe to Mr. Granville's letter (now $250 a year) and paid extra for Mr. Granville's telephone early-warning service. 
He says he felt he'd "finally hit on a secret formula to beat the stock market." 

   Dutifully following Mr. Granville's advice, Mr. Olinger says, he sold all of his stocks in 1981. 
In addition, he bought put options and sold stocks short, two tactics designed to make money when stock prices decline. 

   "I lost over $100,000," Mr. Olinger says. 
"It's taken me 10 years to gain back what I lost in just a few months by going with Joe Granville." 

   So what does Mr. Olinger think about Joe Granville now? 
"You can't help but like the guy," he says. 
"He's a good talker. 
His theory is real sound, and it makes a lot of sense. 
Basically, it's a good theory. 
The only trouble is, it doesn't work." 

   Mr. Granville's method is a technical system that emphasizes trading volume when stock prices are rising compared with volume when stock prices are declining. 
He also uses a wide variety of other gauges, many of which he devised or refined. 

   Mark Hulbert, whose Hulbert Financial Digest estimates the performance investors would get by following newsletters' advice, figures that people who acted on Mr. Granville's recommendations lost 98% of their money from mid-1980 through the end of 1987. 

   A week after the 1987 crash, however, Mr. Granville began what he hopes will be his comeback. 
On Oct. 26, while almost everyone else was still scurrying for cover, Mr. Granville boldly proclaimed that people should buy stocks and hold them. 
Ever since, defying occasional market dips, Mr. Granville has steadily reiterated his advice to buy. 
So far, he has been right. 

   Mr. Hulbert now ranks the Granville Market Letter first among some 110 investment newsletters for the first half of 1989. 
Investors who followed Mr. Granville's advice would have gained an estimated 156%, he figures. 

   As Mr. Granville rightly says, "No one else is even close." 

   "My Oct. 26, 1987, call will probably be the best market call I ever made in my life, but to me it was the easiest call," Mr. Granville says. 
"We had the most classical selling climax. 
The public were scared out of their wits." 

   Before long, Mr. Granville was looking at his charts and discerning an "ascending triangle" formation. 
To him, it signaled that the market's recovery wouldn't stop until the Dow Jones Industrial Average hits 2840. 
That's about 200 points above current levels. 
He expects it to happen by Labor Day. 

   He then expects a drop of 100 to 200 points. 
"But further out I see tremendous things in 1990," he says. 

   Recently, he has been advising subscribers to lighten their holdings by using "stop" orders, in which investors tell their brokers to sell if a stock drops below a specified threshold. 
Mr. Granville says investors with gains should progressively raise their stops, but allow themselves to be "stopped out" when the market dips. 
That way, he says, investors will have cash on hand to do some heavy buying around the end of the year. 
In attempting his comeback, Mr. Granville has some strong boosters. 
One is Michael Bronzino, a Merrill Lynch &amp; Co. commodity representative who tries to apply versions of Mr. Granville's techniques to commodity trading. 
"He knows market history like no one I've spoken to before, and he knows human psychology," Mr. Bronzino says. 

   When he tells other people that he consults with Joe Granville, "They think I'm out of my mind," Mr. Bronzino says. 

   Mr. Granville still calls his subscribers "followers," but he won't say how many there are. 
And he says his theatrics are behind him. 
"That's completely out of my system." 

   As much as he enjoys his new hot streak, Mr. Granville knows he has a long way to go to recapture his former prominence. 
When major brokerage houses such as E.F. Hutton, Dean Witter and Merrill Lynch used to sponsor his seminars, he demanded guaranteed crowds of at least 1,500 people. 
In June 1981, he packed New York's Carnegie Hall for a presentation in which he gave market guidance and played the piano. 

   Last month, he came to New York for another seminar. 
Only 14 people showed up, by Mr. Bronzino's count. 
Mr. Granville doesn't quarrel with the number, but he says the seminar was "the most successful one I've given." 

   "I don't measure success in terms of numbers. 
I look at it as Chopin would, giving a teaching lesson to his pupils. 
In time, if I keep calling the market the way I've been calling it, obviously the world will beat a path back to my door." 

</TEXT>

<HL> PS of New Hampshire Bid
@  Gets State's Endorsement
@  ----
@  A Wall Street Journal News Roundup </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> NU PNH NES </CO>
<IN> TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM)
UTILITIES (UTI)
BANKRUPTCIES (BCY) </IN>
<TEXT>
   The state of New Hampshire endorsed a revised offer by Northeast Utilities to acquire troubled Public Service Co. of New Hampshire for about $1.9 billion, but creditors of PS of New Hampshire said they consider the new proposal too low. 

   Northeast's latest offer is the first of several reorganization plans expected to be proposed shortly in PS of New Hampshire's Chapter 11 bankruptcy proceedings. 

   While Northeast could benefit from the state's support, any plan -- whether a purchase of the company or an internal reorganization -- must be approved by the court and creditors. 
As a result, the state's backing doesn't guarantee success for Northeast's bid. 

   Indeed, some creditors say they will oppose Northeast's proposal. 
"This is not an adequate bid from our perspective," said Daniel Frank, a fund manager at Fidelity Investments, a giant mutual-fund company that is the largest holder of PS of New Hampshire unsecured debt and stock. 
"We think the value of the company is well in excess of $2 billion." 

   An internal reorganization plan supported by PS of New Hampshire officials is expected to be submitted soon. 
In addition, New England Electric Service, which made an earlier proposal, said it will make a revised offer within the next month that will top Northeast's latest proposal.

<HL> Auto Sales in Europe
@  Increased Last Month
@  But Decline Foreseen </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> EUROP </CO>
<IN> AUTOS, AUTO PARTS (AUT) </IN>
<DATELINE> LONDON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The pace of new car sales in Europe quickened in June against year-earlier levels, but even as the industry's boom hums along into its fourth year some observers see dark clouds down the road. 

   While sales growth remained solid in southern Europe's biggest markets, gains were more modest in West Germany and Britain and sales actually declined throughout Scandinavia. 
Analysts note that the gains in some places were probably the product of incentive programs. 

   "The quality of sales is deteriorating," says Stephen Reitman, an industry analyst for Phillips &amp; Drew Ltd. 
In West Germany and Britain in particular, auto makers are "fighting very hard for market share." 

   Industry and government statistics from 12 countries, which normally account for about 94% of non-communist Europe's passenger car sales, indicated volume rose 6.8% in June from a year ago to 1,134,238 units. 
The June figure also rose 0.6% fromMay. 

   The rise in the latest month appears to confirm analysts' and industry projections that the upturn in auto sales is continuing. 
It follows a slight 1.2% year-on-year decline in May but otherwise fits the 6.5% average growth rate for the year so far. 

</TEXT>
