<HL> International:
@  It's Good for Soul,
@  But Iranians Vote
@  With Few Choices
@  ----
@  By Geraldine Brooks
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> MDEST </CO>
<DATELINE> TEHRAN, Iran  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   In this Islamic republic, getting out the vote isn't a problem -- even when the winner of the election isn't in doubt. 

   Voting here is a religious obligation as well as a civil one. 
And with everybody over the age of 15 eligible to cast a ballot and voter turnout traditionally about 70%, election authorities are bracing for an estimated 17 million of Iran's 50 million population to take part in the presidential poll and constitutional referendum today. 

   The late Ayatollah Ruhollah Khomeini used to make a point of voting first thing on election day. 
Pictures of the imam casting his ballot are almost as plentiful in the streets of Tehran as banners of parliamentary leader Hashemi Rafsanjani, the principal presidential candidate. 

   Pictures of his opponent, Abbas Sheibani, aren't nearly so evident. 
The parliamentarian and former agriculture minister is the only one of 78 would-be challengers to Mr. Rafsanjani not to be disqualified. 
The others all were deemed unfit because they lacked sufficient political credentials or personal religious piety. 

   Dr. Sheibani's political platform is almost indistinguishable from Mr. Rafsanjani's. 
Both men, according to official biographies, stress their numerous terms of imprisonment under the shah -- Dr. Sheibani boasting 13 years in jail while Mr. Rafsanjani has to make do with five arrests that led to sentences ranging from 40 days to three years. 
Both say they'll hold to a nonaligned foreign policy, and both stress economic reform and reconstruction as a priority. 

   "There isn't any Western-style competition between us," Dr. Sheibani said in an interview with an Iranian newspaper. 
One of his main reasons for running, he said, is that the Iranian parliament "isn't ready to lose such a good speaker" as Mr. Rafsanjani. 

   Dr. Sheibani says he told would-be backers not to waste their money on campaigning for him but to donate their contributions instead to purchasing stationery for needy schoolchildren. 
Mr. Rafsanjani apparently didn't give his backers similar advice, or if he did, they haven't heeded it. 
Bazaaris, or merchants, have bought expensive front-page advertisements in the press urging a vote for Mr. Rafsanjani. 
The less well-heeled students association issued a statement in his support. 
And a group called "Hezbollah Youth from Qom" has brought out a poster showing the speaker in a diverse range of moods -- looking pensive and intellectual, and as a man of action, without clerical turban, heading off to the battlefield in military fatigues. 

   But perhaps the real clincher for Mr. Rafsanjani is the endorsement he has received from the Khomeini family. 
Both the late ayatollah's son Ahmad and a daughter, Zahra Mostafavi, have urged his election, praising both his management abilities and his loyalty to their father. 

   Voters also are expected to endorse a referendum for constitutional reform that will consolidate the power of the presidency, making it possible for Mr. Rafsanjani to institute reforms virtualy unhindered. 
However, the referendum stops short of abolishing the power of the leader of the Islamic republic to dismiss the president, so Mr. Rafsanjani will remain in office at the will of the Ayatollah Ali Khameini. 

</TEXT>
</DOC>
<DOC>
<DOCNO> WSJ890728-0022 </DOCNO>
<DD> = 890728 </DD>
<AN> 890728-0022. </AN>
<HL> AT&amp;T-Italian Joint Venture Targets
@  U.K., Greece, Portugal, Spain Markets
@  ----
@  By Sara Gay Forden
@  Special to The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> T EUROP </CO>
<IN> COMMUNICATIONS TECHNOLOGY (CMT)
TELEPHONE SYSTEMS (TLS)
TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM) </IN>
<TEXT>
   The new partnership between American Telephone &amp; Telegraph Co. and Italtel S.p.A., although rich with the potential of combined strength, must be ready to fight to win a major slice of the European private telecommunications market, industry analysts say. 

   Under terms of an operating agreement worked out between AT&amp;T and Italtel, the two partners have agreed to set up a joint-venture company for the distribution of private telecommunications equipment in Europe. 

   The new company will focus initially on four markets -- Britain, Greece, Portugal and Spain, according to a confidential document obtained by Dow Jones International News Service. 

   An Italtel spokeswoman in Milan declined to comment, and AT&amp;T officials in Rome couldn't be reached for comment. 

   According to the document, AT&amp;T will own a majority stake, while Italtel Telematica SPA, Italtel's manufacturer of private telecommunications products, will own the rest of the joint venture, which doesn't have a name yet. 

   The accord is the first concrete step resulting from a wide-ranging collaboration accord signed in June between the U.S. telecommunications giant and the Italian state-owned telecommunications equipment maker. 
Italtel is a unit of Stet S.p.A., the state holding company for the telecommunications industry. 
AT&amp;T acquired a 20% stake in Italtel as part of the June accord. 

   "This is a very interesting deal, but you don't just walk into the European telecommunications market and find that everyone bows down to welcome you," says Richard Cunningham, telecommunications analyst with Citicorp Scrimgeour-Vickers in London. 

   "AT&amp;T already tried once before with the Phillips (Gloeilampenfabrieken N.V.) joint venture, which failed. 
It remains to be seen how they fare this time around," Mr. Cunningham said. 
According to industry sources, the two companies are planning to acquire a series of small European companies that could distribute products already manufactured separately by AT&amp;T and Italtel. 

   "There are a lot of companies who are going to be very anxious to send them {AT&amp;T and Italtel} back home packing," says Mr. Cunningham of Citicorp Scrimgeour-Vickers. 

   Analysts agree that the toughest market targeted by AT&amp;T and Italtel is in the U.K., where intense competition between Sweden's Telefon AB L.M. Ericsson and a joint venture formed by Britain's General Electric Co. PLC, which isn't related to the U.S. company, and Plessey Co. PLC, means only the most advanced products will be able to find a niche. 

   In Spain, one of Europe's fastest-growing telecommunications markets, AT&amp;T has already managed to cull some phone-exchange contracts. 
However, the AT&amp;T-Italtel venture will have to move quickly to build up its market share and "may have already missed the boat," one analyst said. 

   The less advanced Greek and Portuguese markets have farther to go and lack a major supplier of telephone exchanges, but they are also largely dependent on European Community funding for such investments. 

   As for public telecommunications equipment, Italtel's Linea UT switching system and AT&amp;T's ESS5 switch will be the core products to be marketed initially by a 50-50 joint venture that will be formed between Netherlands-based AT&amp;T Network Systems International and Italtel. 

   In Italy, Italtel will continue to distribute the Linea UT switch, which will be beefed up with such AT&amp;T technology as toll-free dialing services and advanced transmission systems. 

   In addition, the possibility of introducing the Linea UT to the U.S. market will be studied, according to the agreement. 

   However, analysts described the two latter provisions as more "diplomatic" measures designed to reinforce Italtel's autonomy in the young partnership. 

   Products assembled from parts made by both companies may be available as soon as next year, but industry experts aren't expecting a jointly developed product to hit the market until at least 1991. 

   "What's going to be most interesting isn't the here-and-now, but what happens further down the road," says Dorian Foyil, telecommunications analyst for UBS-Phillips &amp; Drew in London. 

   Adds Mr. Foyil: "They need to get used to each other and hone their products. 
It remains to be seen whether or not the cooperation between AT&amp;T and Italtel really bears fruit." 

   In the long term, analysts agree, the test for the new partnership will be whether it can develop and maintain the kind of state-of-the-art technology the market demands, and, simultaneously, move into the more advanced French and West German markets, where there already are major suppliers. 

</TEXT>
</DOC>

<HL> Abreast of the Market:
@  Industrial Stocks Advance 22.38;
@  Falling Interest Rates Are Cited
@  ----
@  By Craig Torres and David Wilson
@  Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> STOCK MARKET, OFFERINGS (STK)
STOCK INDEXES (NDX) </IN>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Falling interest rates sent investors to the stock market in droves, and share prices finished generally higher in heavy trading. 

   The Dow Jones Industrial Average hit another post-crash high, rising 22.38 to 2635.43. 
That left the average needing to rise just 87 points, or 3.3%, to pass its record close of 2722.42 set in August 1987. 

   Yesterday's rally came after a government report said gross national product growth in the second quarter slowed to a 1.7% annual rate from a revised 3.7% in the first three months of the year. 
Investors figure that slower growth will prompt the Federal Reserve to continue to lower interest rates gradually and keep the economy from falling into a recession. 

   Volume on the New York Stock Exchange swelled to 213,680,000 shares, the highest since June 16, when 244,510,000 shares were traded in the latest quarterly "triple witching hour" session of futures and options expirations. 

   Broader averages that reached all-time highs Wednesday made still more impressive gains yesterday. 
The Standard &amp; Poor's 500-stock index climbed 3.94 to 341.99 -- equivalent to a 30-point rise in the industrial average -- and the New York Stock Exchange composite index climbed 2.08 to 190.22. 
The Dow Jones Equity Market Index rose 3.63 to 320.60. 

   Traders said second-quarter earnings reports, merger news, asset allocation and stock-index arbitrage all played a role in yesterday's gains and volume. 

   In the merger arena, Squibb soared 24 3/4 to 112 1/2 in Big Board trading of 3.4 million shares, and Bristol-Myers, the most active Big Board issue, dropped 2 3/8 to 49 1/2 on 4.5 million shares. 
A merger agreement between the two calls for Bristol-Myers to exchange 2.4 common shares for each of Squibb's 97 million shares outstanding. 
At yesterday's closing prices, Squibb holders would receive more than $118 of stock for each share held. 

   Other drug stocks that rallied on the news and were actively traded included Upjohn, which went up 1 5/8 to 33; Pfizer, up 2 1/2 to 63 3/4; Eli Lilly, up 7/8 to 63 1/2, and Syntex, up 1 3/4 to 50. 

   The product of an earlier industry merger, SmithKline Beecham, advanced 3/4 to 42 3/8 on 3.6 million shares. 
Marion Laboratories, which has agreed to combine its operations with those of Dow Chemical's drug unit, rose 1/8 to 35 7/8. 

   Stock-index arbitrage buy programs, in which traders sell index futures and buy the underlying stocks to profit from small, temporary price differences, kicked off the heavy trading in the morning. 

   This was followed by a large amount of buying by asset allocators who juggle their investments among stocks, bonds and cash. 
Falling interest rates generally prompt a switch out of cash and bonds into stocks. 

   Kenneth Spence, technical analyst at Salomon Brothers, said money flows showed the huge amount of cash pouring into stocks yesterday. 
He said $784 million flowed into the New York Stock Exchange, of which $634 million was in block trades. 
That's bigger than most other recent flows and is regarded as a sign of a heavy cash commitment by large institutional accounts. 

   Mark Donahoe, block trader for Piper Jaffray &amp; Hopwood, said a number of technically oriented traders also jumped into the market. 
He said stocks such as Anheuser Busch, United Telecom and Symbol Technologies appear to be breaking out of recent trading ranges on strong volume. 

   Among stocks in the Dow Jones industrials, Merck rose 2 1/4 to 77 7/8, and Eastman Kodak, parent of Sterling Drug, rose 1 1/2 to 48 3/4. 
Other standout gainers included American Telephone &amp; Telegraph, which set a 52-week high, rallying 1 1/4 to 40 5/8 on 3.8 million shares; USX, which rose 1 1/2 to 36 5/8; and Primerica, which advanced 1 3/8 to 27 1/4. 

   Security Pacific rose 1 1/8 to 51 1/2 on 4.2 million shares. 
Activity related to the company's forthcoming quarterly dividend swelled volume in the stock; also, the company moved to forge closer ties with Japan's Mitsui Bank by signing a letter of intent to acquire a 20% stake in Mitsui's California subsidiary. 

   Rumors that someone would soon disclose ownership of more than a 5% stake in Canadian Pacific helped send the stock up 3/4 to 22, a 52-week high, as 2.5 million shares changed hands. 
Talk of a possible restructuring also contributed to the advance. 

   Lyondell Petrochemical dropped 1/2 to 22 1/4 on 2.1 million shares. 
Goldman Sachs lowered its estimate of 1989 earnings by 9%, based on "industry factors related to volume and margins," according to the company. 

   Sterling Chemicals fell 5/8 to 9 3/8 in active trading after its earnings for the June quarter turned out to be well short of analysts' forecasts. 
Other stocks hurt by disappointing quarterly results included Norsk Hydro, which went down 1 1/2 to 22; Great American Bank, down 2 1/4 to 10 1/2, and Allied Products, down 3 1/4 to 14 3/8. 

   But Digital Equipment rose 7/8 to 92 3/8 even though earnings for the June quarter were on the low end of expectations and the company cautioned analysts that results for the fiscal year ending next June will depend on the U.S. economy's performance. 

   In their first appearance on the S&amp;P 500, United Telecommunications Technology advanced 3 1/2 to 73 1/4 and Tyco Laboratories gained 2 to 44 7/8. 
Typically, stocks new to the index attract buying interest from managers of index funds, portfolios designed to mirror the movements of a given market measure. 

   The American Stock Exchange Index set another all-time high by gaining 2.57 to 375.35. 
Volume totaled 14,790,000 shares. 

   Healthcare International plummeted 2 1/8 to 1 3/4 after a management group's proposal for a leveraged buy-out collapsed. 
Healthvest dropped 3 5/8 to 10 1/4; Healthcare International has a 9.8% stake in the partnership and would have spun it off to shareholders in the buy-out. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Business Brief -- NWA Inc.:
@  Alfred Checchi Completes
@  $3.65 Billion Acquisition </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> NWA </CO>
<IN> TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM)
AIRLINES (AIR) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Investor Alfred Checchi completed his $121-a-share, or $3.65 billion, acquisition of NWA Inc. following the expiration of the waiting period required under the Hart-Scott-Rodino Antitrust Act. 

   Under the offer, which expired Wednesday, more than 99% of NWA's 29.5 million outstanding shares were tendered. 

   A spokeswoman for Mr. Checchi said the Department of Transportation hasn't completed its review of the transaction. 
Transportation Secretary Samuel K. Skinner has expressed concern about the debt the transaction would impose on NWA. However, the department doesn't have the power to block the transaction.

<HL> Technology &amp; Health:
@  AIDS Drug DDI Gets Boost From Study
@  ---
@  Therapy May Help Patients
@  Who Cannot Use AZT
@  Because of Its Toxicity
@  ----
@  By Marilyn Chase
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> BMY GOVMT </CO>
<IN> DRUG MANUFACTURERS (DRG) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Scientists at the National Insitutes of Health reported encouraging results from their trial of the drug DDI in patients with AIDS and severe AIDS-related complex. 

   Publication of the report in the journal Science today comes shortly after the U.S. recorded its 100,000th case of acquired immune deficiency syndrome. 
More than half that number have died. 

   The DDI study has become the focus of enormous hope and political activity. 
The drug is seen as a potential boon to the 40% to 80% of AIDS patients who cannot tolerate the drug AZT, which is made by a unit of Wellcome PLC. AZT is the only antiviral drug approved for treatment of AIDS, but it can cause a severe anemia that can force patients to end the treatment. 

   DDI, which was developed by the National Cancer Institute and licensed to Bristol-Myers Co., is a chemical cousin of AZT. 
Both are classed as "nucleoside analogues," or false building blocks of DNA that insert themselves into the virus and interrupt its replication. 
However, doctors haven't seen anything like AZT's toxicity in studies of DDI so far. 

   The scientists said DDI was given for 42 weeks to 26 patients. 
They noted evidence of improved immune function, including rises in both T4 and T8 lymphocytes, types of white blood cells involved in fighting infection. 
The patients also showed decreases of 80% in their blood levels of virus and experienced mean weight gains of about 3.5 pounds. 
More than half the group said their energy levels improved. 
Patients at the highest dosage levels enjoyed the greatest improvements. 

   Six patients in the study developed opportunistic infections, and one patient who started on the lowest dosage level died. 
Side effects included rises in uric acid, mild headaches and insomnia. 
Two out of a larger group of 45 developed pancreatitis, an inflammation of the pancreas. 
"This is a warning that this drug has to be treated with respect," said the paper's senior author, Samuel Broder, director of the National Cancer Institute. 
"So far, it {hasn't limited use of DDI}. 
But at higher doses or more chronic uses, it'll have to be watched." 
He added that patients should avoid alcohol while taking DDI. 

   The study, co-authored by Robert Yarchoan and colleagues at the cancer institute, was a phase-one study, designed to determine safety and dosage levels. 

   As the phase-one trial has proceeded, DDI has become a rallying point for groups pressing for faster, broader distribution of experimental agents. 
The AIDS Coalition to Unleash Power negotiated with officials of Bristol-Myers to obtain a promise that DDI would be available to people who couldn't participate in clinical trials. 

   Bristol management has said it will give the drug to patients in critical need even if they don't qualify for entry into a studies of more than 1,200 people that are scheduled to start in September, when scientists launch the second phase of clinical trials designed to determine whether DDI is effective. 
Such programs have heightened urgency in view of the fact that bootleg DDI is being advertised for sale by mail-order entrepreneurs in Florida. 

   Finally, the criteria for determining effectiveness are becoming a point of scientific and social debate. 
In the AZT trial in 1986, an untreated control group suffered many more infections and deaths than the AZT group, thus proving the drug worked to preserve health and prolong life for some patients. 
But increasingly some scientists and activists are questioning the harsh necessity of using death as the sole endpoint of a trial. 

   Other factors short of death -- such as changes in T4 cells and in virus levels -- are being discussed as alternative criteria. 
Such factors should be given weight, Dr. Broder asserted, along with reversal of AIDS dementia in adults and neurological problems in children. 
But he added that "the jury is still out" on the question of whether such softer criteria are enough to secure the Food and Drug Administration's approval for DDI or for any other drug. 

</TEXT>

<HL> South African Gold Output </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> AFRIC </CO>
<DATELINE> JOHANNESBURG, South Africa  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   South African gold output dropped 2.6% to 1,606,000 ounces in June from 1,647,000 ounces a year earlier, but rose 1.6% from 1,580,000 ounces in May, the Chamber of Mines, which represents the country's mining houses, said. 

   Output for the first half of this year declined 1.9% to 9,626,000 ounces from 9,810,000 ounces a year earlier.

<HL> Letters to the Editor --
@  For DQ Veteran:
@  To Listen Is to Learn </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> INDQA </CO>
<IN> RESTAURANTS, SUPERMARKETS AND DRUGSTORES (RFD) </IN>
<DATELINE> As an 18-year veteran of the Dairy Queen industry  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   including 15 years as an employee of International Dairy Queen, and for the past three years, a "territory operator" -- I feel I am qualified to comment on the July 6 article "When Franchisees Go Their Own Way." 

   While the tenor of the article seems to be one of portraying Dairy Queen as a group in turmoil made up of fractious people intent on doing things their own way, I must disagree, and from my perspective suggest that one of the great things about DQ is the willingness of both sides to listen and react to each other. 
This ability to react has created a chain that is remarkably free of the "red ink" one sees in many of the chains listed in the article. 
It seems as if one cannot pick up a trade publication these days without getting the latest tale of woe on chains that are retrenching, laying off, closing units, dealing with disgruntled franchisees or stockholders in the courts -- or just generally being involved in all kinds of nonproductive activity. 
Fortunately at DQ, we who sit on the dealer councils are able to spend time on how we can improve the stores and listen and learn from each other. 

   Finally, the comments of John Campbell are amusing. 
I had an office two doors down from him when he was at International Dairy Queen, and am tempted to paraphrase the old saw: "He who can, does. 
He who cannot, consults." 

   Walter S. Tellegen 

   President 

   Interstate Dairy Queen Corp. 

   Washington

<HL> Money Funds' Assets
@  Grew $1.85 Billion
@  During Recent Week </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> MUTUAL AND MONEY-MARKET FUNDS (FND) </IN>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Assets of the 467 money-market mutual funds tracked by the Investment Company Institute grew $1.85 billion, to $332.78 billion, during the week ended Wednesday. 

   "Even though inflows of new money continued, the rate of increase has slowed markedly in recent weeks reflecting the gradual downward drift in short-term interest rates," said Jacob Dreyer, vice president and chief economist of the Washington trade group. 

   Institutional investors pulled $313.2 million out of 141 funds, during the week, dropping assets to $84.79 billion. 
This was attributed to the "sharp mid-week narrowing of yield spreads on institutional funds over Treasury bills," said Mr. Dreyer. 

   Individuals continued to add money to funds, however. 
The 231 general-purpose funds the Institute tracks grew $554.8 million, to $105.96 billion, while 95 brokerdealer funds increased $1.6 billion, to $142.03 billion. 

   Yields on money funds dropped again in the week ended Tuesday, according to Donoghue's Money Fund Report, a newsletter. 
For the first time since February, the average seven-day compounded yield fell below 9%. 
The average fund pays 8.97%, down from 9.03% the week before.

<HL> Letters to the Editor:
@  What Goes Up... </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> CONSTRUCTION, MATERIALS (CON) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Having supplied your readers with a witty and insightful article on some developers' determination to leave their mark on history by erecting the world's tallest building (July 7), I hope you will follow up with an article examining an important corollary issue. 
How will we ever get rid of such monstrosities when they reach the end of their useful lives? 

   Even if the materials used to construct 100-story-plus buildings last forever -- which is doubtful -- it's unlikely that the design concepts on which they're based will prove equally long-lived. 
If, as your article indicates, construction costs per square foot rise dramatically as building height soars, one can only imagine what happens to demolition costs. 

   David A. Salem 

   Charlottesville, Va.

<IN> TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM) </IN>
<DATELINE> WASHINGTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Milan Panic, chairman of ICN Pharmaceuticals Inc., said ICN is considering acquisitions of related businesses and is conducting negotiations which could lead to possible business combinations. 

   In a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission, Mr. Panic said the Costa Mesa, Calif., pharmaceutical and biochemical concern is also considering raising part of its future capital requirements by selling equity to the public or by forming new subsidiaries and selling a portion of their equity to the public. 

   Additionally, ICN and certain of its subsidiaries may sell certain assets in the future, the SEC filing said. 
ICN earlier announced that its board authorized the company to repurchase up to 10 million shares of its common, of which 6,513,389 had been purchased as of July 7.

   Canadian steel ingot production totaled 299,366 metric tons in the week ended July 22, down 1% from 302,523 tons a week earlier, Statistics Canada, a federal agency, said. 

   The week's total was up 7.9% from 277,345 tons a year earlier and brings the total for 1989 to 8,923,486 tons, up 5.3% from 8,475,840 tons a year earlier.

<HL> Real Estate:
@  News Corp.'s U.S. Arm Ceases Talks
@  On Lease With Manhattan Developer
@  ----
@  By Joan Lebow
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> NWS </CO>
<IN> REAL ESTATE, REITS, LAND DEVELOPMENT (REL)
MEDIA, PUBLISHING, BROADCASTING, ELECTRONIC PUBLISHING (MED) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Rupert Murdoch's News America Publishing Inc. said it has ceased negotiations with Solomon Equities, developer of a Manhattan office building where Mr. Murdoch's company was considering leasing about one million square feet of space. 

   The deal, in a 42-story office tower now under construction at 1585 Broadway just north of Times Square, would have been the biggest real estate transaction of the year in New York. 

   A spokesman for News America said, "There was a letter of agreement signed by both principals, and the Murdoch organization was ready, willing and able to proceed. 
Solomon indicated they were not prepared to proceed, and since that time, there have been no further discussions between Solomon Equities and News America." 

   "Mr. 
Solomon walked from the table," said Kenneth D. Laub, real estate broker for News America. 
He added, "My client was amazed, after negotiating for six months in extraordinarily good faith, that the developer would change the terms and conditions of the deal." 

   Mr. Solomon said his policy is not to comment on the progress of lease negotiations. 
However, he did say he is talking to a number of prospective tenants, including News America. 
He said 1585 Broadway is "a good product with good financing and I'm not worried or concerned about filling it." 

   News America is the U.S. arm of Australia-based News Corp. 
Its publications include TV Guide, Seventeen, Premier and New York magazines, the Boston Herald and the Star supermarket tabloid. 

   The company, which operates out of several New York locations is planning to consolidate. 
The firm is considering sites in New York and New Jersey, the spokesman said. 
Another option would be to divide its approximately 1,200 employees between those locations. 

   The aborted News America deal has attracted attention in the local real estate market for a number of reasons. 
The high-profile tenant's space needs are substantially larger than those of most businesses in the city, and the deal would be twice the size of many deals that are considered big. 

   Also, a News America lease on Manhattan's West Side would help fill a big block of the city's newly built office space. 
It would be a boost to owners of half a dozen new, and still vacant, buildings nearby that couldn't accommodate the publishing concern but would like to have it as a neighbor. 

   Individuals familiar with the talks say the deal hit a snag in recent weeks because Solomon was unable to secure permanent financing on the building given the proposed length and value of the Murdoch lease. 

   A real estate developer will typically finance new building construction with a short-term construction loan at a relatively high interest rate. 
Then, with a major tenant on board, the developer secures longer-term financing at a slightly lower interest rate from an insurance company, pension fund or foreign bank. 
Then the initial loan is paid off. 

   In theory, such financing should be obtainable for a fully leased Manhattan office building because there's more money available than there are quality properties in which to invest. 
In reality, however, each side -- the developer, lender and possibly the tenant, as well -- can reach a dead end when they try to agree on how much equity each will retain in the building. 

   Industry sources suggest that the proposed deal may have provided insufficient rental revenue to the developer to safely cover his debt service and operating expenses. 
If so, lenders may have found the project too risky to finance and sought more equity to compensate for its risk. 

   Mr. Solomon, however, denied that he is concerned about getting sufficient financing or attracting a tenant. 
He said he has a seven-year construction loan on the property and points out that many top-notch tenants are currently in the market for newly constructed office space. 

   Negotiations for the News America lease were complicated by the presence of another major tenant with claims to the property. 
Last fall, the law firm of Proskauer Rose Goetz &amp; Mendelsohn signed a lease for 426,000 square feet smack in the middle of the 1.3 million square-foot building. 
Many observers viewed that transaction as a coup for Solomon Equities, a young company headed by Mr. Solomon and his wife, Jean. 
The couple had taken on considerable risk by building three Manhattan office buildings without prior tenant commitments. 

   The arrival of another prestigious tenant, News America, seeking almost the entire building, provided an ironic twist; the law firm lease suddenly became more of a problem than a prize. 

   Aside from negotiating with the developer, Murdoch representatives found themselves trying to cut a deal with the law firm to buy it out of its lease before it even moved into the building. 
The firm is slated to move in sometime next year. 
"They {News America} did approach us several times," said Herbert Weinstein, chairman of Proskauer's real estate department. 
He describes those meetings as "very preliminary, but its clear they were willing to offer significant bucks." 
He declined to put a dollar amount on the offer. 

   The city's real estate brokers began buzzing about what it would mean to have the law firm back in the market and imagining how the pieces of the new puzzle would fit together. 
To help Proskauer avoid realizing a capital gain on the sale of its lease, they reasoned, News America might take additional space elsewhere, then sublease the new space to Proskauer. 
The law firm would in turn sublease its space in Solomon's building to News America. 
But now the law firm is planning to move in next spring, and these other ideas remain scribbles on a napkin at a power lunch. 

</TEXT>

@  Imperial Chemical Industries PLC </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> ICI </CO>
<IN> EARNINGS (ERN)
CHEMICALS, PLASTICS (CHM) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Imperial Chemical Industries PLC said pretax profit rose 14% in the second quarter, to #483 million ($804.2 million) from #425 million a year earlier. 

   The British concern's sales grew 17%, to #3.43 billion ($5.71 billion) from #2.93 billion, boosted by increases in both volume and price and by exchange-rate factors. 

   For the first half, pretax profit rose 18%, to #925 million from #783 million, and sales increased 13%, to #6.64 billion from #5.87 billion. 
Profit attributable to shareholders, or net income, grew 32% for the half, to #562 million from #425 million in the corresponding year-earlier period. 
There were no one-time charges in the latest half. 
In the year-earlier six months, the company's results included a #44 million extraordinary loss.

<HL> Marketing &amp; Media:
@  Knight-Ridder's Hall,
@  Lawrence and Capen
@  Receive Promotions </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> KRI WNEWS </CO>
<DATELINE> MIAMI  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Knight-Ridder Inc. promoted three of its top executives in a move that the newspaper company said "expresses confidence" in the outcome of a pending court decision involving the company's Detroit paper. 

   Richard G. Capen Jr., 55 years old, publisher and chairman of the Miami Herald, was named vice chairman of Knight-Ridder. 
Succeeding him will be David Lawrence Jr., 47, publisher and chairman of the Detroit Free Press. 
Robert J. Hall, 44, president of the Free Press, will succeed Mr. Lawrence. 

   The Supreme Court has agreed to hear an appeal from an ad hoc Michigan citizens group opposed to the plan to operate jointly parts of the Free Press and Gannett Co.'s Detroit News. 
Mr. Lawrence played a central role in guiding the Free Press through its planned operating agreement with the News. 
James K. Batten, Knight-Ridders's president and chief executive officer, said Mr. Hall now "will be the leader the Free Press needs and deserves as we push to the end" of the Supreme Court's review. 

   Mr. Capen will have responsibility for operations of Knight-Ridder's Business Information Services division and the company's cable-television operations, as well as assuming new responsibilities in the area of public affairs.

<HL> Plastics Industry Uses Marketing Tactic
@  To Tell Public Products Are Recyclable
@  ----
@  By Bill Paul
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> ENVIRONMENT (ENV)
CHEMICALS, PLASTICS (CHM)
PULP, PAPER, PACKAGING (ALL TYPES) (PUL) </IN>
<TEXT>
   The plastics industry is betting that a new marketing ploy will convince Americans that plastic food containers are recyclable and thus don't add to the nation's solid-waste crisis. 

   Millions of plastic containers with funny little triangles molded into them are starting to show up on America's grocery shelves. 
Inside the triangle, which generally appears at the bottom of the container, is a number from one to seven. 
The number identifies the container's plastic resin or resins, thereby indicating whether it's a candidate for recycling. 

   The industry claims that roughly 90% of all plastic bottles are either a one (for polyethylene terephlate, or PETE) or a two (for high density polyethylene, or HDPE). 
Both are recyclable. 

   So far, the ploy is working. 
Seventeen states have incorporated part or all of the code into their state regulations. 
As devised by the Society of Plastics Industry, a trade group, the code is intended to cover all bottles and jars of 16 ounces or more, plus other containers having at least an eight-ounce capacity. 
Deadlines for implementation currently vary from Connecticut's Jan. 1, 1990, to Iowa's July 1, 1992. 
The most populous state, California, has set a Jan. 1, 1992, deadline. 

   Many big consumer-product concerns have adopted the code. 
Procter &amp; Gamble Co., for instance, says 90% of its plastic containers now carry the code, although the Cincinnati-based concern adds that it will take another six to nine months to work off its pre-code inventory. 
"There are products (in coded bottles) out there now, and you'll soon see a lot more," says a P&amp;G spokesman, rattling off such household staples as Tide, Cheer, Jif peanut butter and Crisco oil. 

   In New York, Colgate-Palmolive Co. says its newly coded plastic bottles have started reaching stores. 
Such familiar items as Fab and Dynamo 2 laundry detergent, and Palmolive and Ajax dishwashing liquid, are affected. 
In Pittsburgh, meanwhile, H.J. Heinz Co. says it started turning out ketchup bottles bearing the code about six weeks ago; they'll be in supermarkets within eight weeks, a spokesman adds. 

   Fast-food chains are gearing up, too. 
That's not surprising given that fast-food packaging has been the focus of the bans that some 30 states and dozens of communities have either passed or have under consideration. 
PepsiCo Inc.'s Kentucky Fried Chicken division, for example, says it will start coding its food containers and bucket lids sometime next year. 

   Fast-food packaging, however, probably will remain a heated political issue. 
That's because such packaging is made from polystyrene, which will carry a code number of six. 
While a Kentucky Fried Chicken spokesman acknowledges that polystyrene still isn't yet recyclable, he stresses that new technology is being developed and the company expects polystyrene to be recylable before 1995. 

   Separately, motor oil sold by Du Pont Co.'s Conoco unit now carries the triangular marking, a reflection of giant Du Pont's fear of plastic bans. 
"The public's distrust of our business threatens our industry," Nicholas Pappas, a Du Pont executive vice president, recently told an industry conference. 
"We shouldn't let the environmentalists take the high ground," he said. 
"We give a damn about the environment." 

   PETE is what most soda bottles are made of, while HDPE is commonly used to make milk jugs. 
Under the coding system, three is for vinyl, while four is for low-density polyethylene. 
Five is for polypropylene, while six is for the dreaded polystyrene. 
Seven, meanwhile, is simply for "other." 

   One "other" that's been catching a lot of flak is Heinz's ketchup bottle, which contains 6% ethyl vinyl alcohol to prevent oxygen permeation. 
While acknowledging that Heinz has been singled out for having a bottle that isn't readily recyclable, the company spokesman says that consumers are more interested in product freshness, and that Heinz has no current plans to change the bottle's formulation. 

   The coding system is just one of a number of ways the plastics industry is fighting to keep plastic packaging from being banned. 
Another is an industry-funded research program for developing products that use recycled plastic. 
Park benches and flower pots are among the program's first successes. 

</TEXT>
</DOC>

<HL> Sears Tries to Update Image by Using
@  Du Pont High-Tech Furniture Material
@  ----
@  By Teri Agins
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> S DD </CO>
<IN> RETAILING (RET)
HOME ELECTRONICS, FURNISHINGS, APPLIANCES (HMF) </IN>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   As part of its strategy to remake its dowdy image by adding well-known brand names to its merchandise mix, Sears, Roebuck &amp; Co. has turned to the furniture floor. 

   This fall, it will introduce a collection of 11 upholstered sofas and chairs that employ a high-tech seating-support material developed by Du Pont Corp. 

   But Sears, Chicago, has taken on a tough sell: It will have to convince consumers that a woven fabric stretched across the furniture frame is as strong as the traditional springs, webbing and other upholstery innards it replaces. 
Thus far, a few furniture makers have been willing to try the new material called Dymetrol that Du Pont brought out last year. 

   "This is a breakthrough technology . . . but the furniture industry hasn't moved quickly on this," concedes Dan Schmierer, a manager at Du Pont. 
"We think we can make it more visible through Sears." 

   Even furniture makers who applaud the product admit that it isn't winning rave reviews among consumers. 
For instance, John Bost, director of manufacturing at Carson's Inc., which has installed the fabric in about 10% of its sofas, love seats and chairs, says that "not only does it sit well-comfortable and firm, but it really is resilient. . . . 
We're sold on it." 
But Mr. Bost says his retail dealers are having trouble persauding consumers. 

   The problem is that once shoppers remove the seat cushions and discover that there's nothing that looks sturdy supporting the furniture, they believe it is cheaply made. 
"You still have to convince homeowners that this (Dymetrol) performs better than springs," Mr. Bost says. 

   Industry experts also say that Sears, which of late has been stressing its "everyday low prices," may be hindered in its ability to sell its line, which it has positioned as more fashionable and more expensive than the typical furniture it sells. 
For example, a three-piece sectional couch is about $1,700. 
Sears' typical sectional couches sell in the $1,000 to $1,400 range. 

   Despite the challenges, Sears dominance in selling furniture -- its annual furniture sales are estimated at more than $1 billion -- is such that analysts say the nation's biggest retailer is well-poised to win over skeptical consumers. 
"Sears has the clout and the credibility when it comes to furniture," says Jerry Epperson, a securities analyst at Wheat First Securities. 

   Sears and Du Pont introduced the Lasting Images furniture line at the American Craft Museum here in a model living room similar to the ones that will be set up this fall in 450 of Sears' larger stores. 

   To help explain the product, Sears will install television monitors playing videotapes which show a man poking slits through the furniture fabric with a knife. 
The demonstrator then jumps up and down on the furniture to illustrate Du Pont's claims that the woven product is "six times more durable than typical spring construction." 

</TEXT>
</DOC>

<HL> Palmer Financial Gets
@  Conditional Approval
@  To Buy Florida S&amp;L </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> CFSF GOVMT </CO>
<IN> TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM)
SAVINGS AND LOANS, THRIFTS, CREDIT UNIONS (SAL) </IN>
<DATELINE> TAMPA, Fla.  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Palmer Financial Corp., controlled by Tampa investor Hugh F. Culverhouse, said the Federal Home Loan Bank Board approved its application to acquire Coast Federal Savings &amp; Loan Association of Sarasota, Fla. 

   The company said the Bank Board's approval is subject to certain conditions, which Palmer Financial and Mr. Culverhouse are reviewing. 
A decision on Palmer's ability to comply with the conditions will be made on or before next Monday, the company said. 

   Mr. Culverhouse called off a previous $20-a-share, or $71.9 million, merger pact with Coast Federal in November, citing conditions required by the Bank Board to approve the transaction. 

   He returned earlier this year with a lower, hostile tender offer of $60 million, or $15 a share, which Coast Federal has been trying to fend off. 

   In February, Coast sued Mr. Culverhouse to halt the tender offer by alleging that he and Palmer violated securities laws through a series of misrepresentations and omissions in its tender offer disclosure materials. 
But Palmer said it already has more of the company's stock than it needs to complete the tender offer.

<HL> Correction </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>

<HL> Lost Sheep Investors Find New Bo-peeps
@  ----
@  By Kevin G. Salwen
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> STOCK MARKET, OFFERINGS (STK)
SECURITIES INDUSTRY (SCR)
BOND MARKET NEWS (BON)
AUTOS, AUTO PARTS (AUT)
PULP, PAPER, PACKAGING (ALL TYPES) (PUL)
COMMODITY NEWS, FARM PRODUCTS (CMD)
MONETARY NEWS, FOREIGN EXCHANGE, TRADE (MON)
FINANCIAL, ACCOUNTING, LEASING (FIN)
ECONOMIC NEWS (ECO) </IN>
<GV> TREASURY DEPARTMENT (TRE) </GV>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Like so many sheep, investors love to be led. 

   That isn't news to people like Joseph Granville, Robert Prechter and Elaine Garzarelli; they took turns leading investors in and out of the stock market in the past decade or so. 
Stocks are cheap, they would say, and investors would dutifully flock to their brokers. 
Or their negative signals would cause floods of sales. 

   But one by one, those gurus stumbled or faded from view. 
Mr. Prechter, for example, the "Elliott Wave" theorist who became a hero during the bull market of the mid-1980s, was calling for the Dow Jones Industrial Average to hit 3600 just before the stock market crashed. 

   Now investors are scrambling to find their next leader. 
"People have an absolutely insatiable desire for simple answers to the complex questions of life," says Charles Minter of Comstock Partners, a New York investment firm. 
"Everybody wants the secret." 

   So who are the investment professionals following these days? 
How about Boston money manager and trader Gerald R. Jordan Jr. 
While hardly a household name, he has been called the best stock-market timer in Boston. 
Or Steven C. Leuthold, a Minneapolis strategist who gives portfolio advice to many of the country's biggest pension funds. 

   Like many of Wall Street's past gurus, these strategists rely on a heavy dose of technical indicators, numerical measures that help project market trends. 
Unfortunately, the conclusions they reach vary markedly. 

   Mr. Jordan thinks the Dow industrials are headed over 3000 sometime next year. 
But Ned Davis, a highly regarded Florida adviser, is bearish about the next year and suggests that clients increase their cash positions. 

   As yet, none of these three necessarily moves the stock market. 
But they force the pros to rethink portfolios, look up from their computer screens and sometimes change their trading. 

   Here's a look at the three. 

   NED DAVIS: Mr. Davis isn't interested in being a guru, thank you. 
He'd rather sell analytical work. 
That's where the money is. 

   "We really try to downplay our forecasting," the 44-year-old adviser says. 
"You make a bad forecast and your business is down the tubes." 
So Mr. Davis sells reams of charts and graphs on several hundred market indicators to almost 500 institutional clients. 

   But many market pros happily pay Mr. Davis for his predictions. 
"Historically he's been very, very accurate," says George Neisloss, head trader for the Connecticut state pension fund. 
"I use his type of service to give me a framework so that I can be more aggressive or less aggressive in buying." 

   Mr. Davis's service, Ned Davis Research Inc., Nokomis, Fla., combines technical indicators, like the percentage of stocks that advance each day compared with those that decline, and fundamental indicators like Federal Reserve Board monetary policy. 
The goal: avoiding mistakes. 
"A client once said to us that he couldn't remember any big mistakes we had made," Mr. Davis recalls. 
"That was the biggest compliment that he could give us." 

   The technical indicators allow the firm to determine where to place "stop-loss" orders, which tell investors when to sell if the market is falling. 
This keeps the mistakes small and cuts losses. 
That way, clients can change strategy based on a new set of signals. 

   Mr. Davis currently is bearish for the stock market's outlook over the next year or so. 
That conclusion is based on feeble money-supply growth, a wariness about the Federal Reserve and a look at retail sales. 

   Mr. Davis's firm did a study of retail sales over seven-year periods and determined that in no such period has the consumer ever bought more (on a unit basis) than he did in the past seven years. 
"It looks to us like the consumer is pretty much tapped out," he says. 

   But that doesn't mean the firm is steering clear of stocks. 
Armed with a forecast that interest rates have one or two percentage points further to decline, Mr. Davis is buying such interest-rate-sensitive stocks as utilities and high-quality financial stocks. 
In the bond market, he likes quality government issues and the highest-grade corporates. 
Stay away from the stocks of auto makers, paper companies and commodity producers, he argues. 

   And even though the Dow Jones industrials have ploughed through the 2525 target Mr. Davis earlier predicted would be the high for the year, he isn't bearish for the short-term. 
That's where the technical indicators come in. 
"We'll stay in it as long as the trend is up," he says. 
GERALD R. JORDAN JR.: If Boston money manager Jerry Jordan asks for your feelings about the market, don't be flattered, says fellow institutional investor Kenneth Heebner. 
"You're just another vote." 

   And don't be insulted if Mr. Jordan bets the opposite way from your vote and others. 
"He has an incredible ability to take the emotion out of the market," Mr. Heebner says. 

   Mr. Jordan, the president of Hellman, Jordan Management Co., bought his first stock, Bowlmor, for $1.50 a share when he was 13 years old. 
"It went to $26, and I have been absolutely enamored of the stock market ever since," he says. 
While others read novels, Mr. Jordan reads about the market. 
Or market sentiment. 
Or interest rates. 

   And it's his psychological makeup that often offers the best investment advice. 
"When the market has been going down for a while, I usually feel awful emotionally," the 50-year-old Harvard graduate says. 
But that's the best time to buy stocks. 
So Mr. Jordan learned "that when people throw in the psychological towel," he should muster the courage to buy. 

   That type of talk might sound too touchy-feely for the numbers-crunching world of Wall Street, but Mr. Jordan's arrows on market directions point to some strong moves. 

   His expectations: a bull market in bonds, coupled with strong upward moves for the dollar (look for parity with the British pound in the early 1990s) and a continuing rise in stock prices. 
Mr. Jordan expects to see 30-year Treasury bonds yielding about 6 3/4% sometime next year. 
"I think that will be good enough to take the Dow by that time over 3000," he contends. 

   The Federal Reserve will be forced to ease monetary policy as the economy weakens, his thesis goes, which will bring lower interest rates. 
"If my expectations on interest rates come to pass, people will be forced into the stock market," he says. 
With lower rates, "we're running out of places for people to invest." 

   STEVEN C. LEUTHOLD: Ask Steve Leuthold where the stock market will be headed for in the next month or two, and he might refer you to Evel Knutson, a fictitious Norwegian daredevil-guru. 
According to Evel, investors should forget about the Elliott Wave Theory, the popular technical thesis used by such analysts as Mr. Prechter. 
Evel employs the "permanent wave theory" in his market forecasts. 

   Says Mr. Leuthold, "I'm just trying to point out that it's absurd for a serious person in the investment business to divine where the market is going in the next month or so." 

   But his Minneapolis firm, Leuthold Group, gets some seriously high marks from portfolio managers. 
"He's listened to by a lot of us portfolio types," says Richard Fontaine, who runs a money-management firm in Baltimore. 

   Central to Mr. Leuthold's success is his ability to change the indicators he watches. 
"You can find the keys for a while, then conditions change and sort of turn the old rules upside down," he says. 
"If cash flow becomes a more important thing than earnings and is making investors react, then we'll start factoring that in." 
The place to start, of course, is to create a history for the indicator, which allows Mr. Leuthold to gather perspective. 

   But he is uncomfortable if his views agree with the consensus. 
That's why he is now taking a hard look at science and technology stocks. 
"They're very out of focus, and eventually science and technology will be popular again," he says. 

   In keeping with that, the firm's largest position is stock-market laggard International Business Machines Corp., which makes up 7% of the portfolio. 
His worry: "We try to anticipate, so we're occasionally early. 
That's both a virtue and a vice." 

   Indeed, Mr. Leuthold turned bearish too early in 1987 and took heat from some clients as the Dow Jones industrials rocketed past 2700. 
But he was redeemed when his advice proved sage in light of the crash. 

   Making mistakes is part of the advisory business, Mr. Leuthold realizes. 
Each year, he sends clients a review of the errors he and his staffers have made. 
"It keeps us all from getting too taken with our work." 

</TEXT>

<HL> Politics &amp; Policy:
@  Publisher Friend of HUD Ex-Secretary
@  Got an Unusual Ad Deal From Agency
@  ----
@  By Jill Abramson
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> GOVMT </CO>
<IN> REAL ESTATE, REITS, LAND DEVELOPMENT (REL)
MARKETING, ADVERTISING (MKT)
CONSTRUCTION, MATERIALS (CON) </IN>
<GV> CONGRESS (CNG) </GV>
<DATELINE> WASHINGTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Black Enterprise magazine, published by a friend of former housing Secretary Samuel Pierce, received at least $55,000 from the Department of Housing and Urban Development in an unprecedented advertising deal, newly obtained documents show. 

   The 1986 federal expenditure, at a time when HUD was slashing public-affairs staff and spending, occurred over the vociferous objections of several agency staffers. 
"There was a big fight over it," recalls Robert Nipp, a HUD spokesman. 

   Mr. Pierce's direct involvement in helping his friend Earl Graves provides further evidence that Mr. Pierce wasn't always a "hands-off" manager, which is how he described himself at a congressional hearing in May and that he interceded on behalf of friends and minority developers. 

   Separately, one of the congressional panels investigating the HUD scandal is delving into connections between Lance Wilson, Mr. Pierce's first executive assistant, and a developer in Fort Worth, Texas. 
Mr. Wilson helped win HUD approval for a controversial project pushed by the developer, Leonard Briscoe, HUD officials said. 
After Mr. Wilson left the government, he received a 15% financial interest in another project sponsored by Mr. Briscoe, according to project records. 

   Mr. Wilson is scheduled to testify before the employment and housing subcommittee of the House Government Operations Committee today. 

   The $55,000 spent by HUD on an eight-page "advertorial" in the May 1986 issue of Mr. Graves's magazine was augmented by $35,000 from the National Association of Real Estate Brokers Inc., a trade association of black real-estate agents and developers. 
The advertising spread promoted the "partnership" between HUD and the association, with pictures of Mr. Pierce and the group's president at the time, T.L. Holmes. 
The text focused on the association's efforts to promote minority homeownership. 

   "It helped get our story across," Mr. Holmes said. "'Black Enterprise' is the bible for the black entrepreneur," he added. 
The advertising "did a lot for our esteem and membership," Mr. Holmes said. 

   It is unclear what benefit HUD derived from the venture. 
Mr. Nipp said the unusual and expensive advertising project was pushed at HUD by Bernice Williams, who headed the department's office for assisting minority businesses. 
When the public-affairs staff, which supervised the department's advertising, opposed the large expenditure, Ms. Williams went directly to Mr. Pierce and won his approval, Mr. Nipp said. 

   In a "Dear Earl" letter to Mr. Graves, Deborah Gore Dean, Mr. Pierce's powerful executive assistant, authorized the $55,000 expenditure in January 1986. 
In April, Black Enterprise billed HUD for $66,987, which included extra charges for layout and production. 

   Mr. Graves has been a friend of Mr. Pierce for years, according to Carolyn Odom, a Black Enterprise employee. 
Messrs. 
Graves and Pierce both are veterans of the civil-rights movement and New York politics. 
Ms. Odom described Black Enterprise as an "upscale" magazine for black entrepreneurs, with 230,000 subscribers 

   Former HUD officials said the two men were close, and Mr. Graves in 1981 called Mr. Pierce's appointment as HUD secretary a "real victory" for black people. 

   Mr. Graves didn't return messages, and Ms. Williams couldn't be reached for comment. 
Mr. Pierce and Ms. Dean have repeatedly refused requests for comment. 

   Mr. Pierce and Ms. Dean had frequent dealings with Mr. Holmes's real estate organization, according to newly released HUD documents. 
In June 1986, Ms. Dean approved spending $1,000 for an advertisement in a souvenir program for the real-estate brokers association's annual convention. 
Ms. Dean received an award at the convention. 
In December 1986, Mr. Holmes sent her a plant and fruit basket for Christmas, according to the documents. 

   The group also participated in several HUD programs. 
In January 1984, Mr. Pierce backed the group's proposal for a program to boost minority home-ownership. 
He urged the Federal National Mortgage Association to commit $100 million to the program, in which minority developers and real-estate brokers association members received loans to build minority housing in 21 cities. 
The real-estate brokers group also acted as a clearinghouse for black developers interested in purchasing foreclosed HUD properties, according to Mr. Holmes and HUD documents. 

   Mr. Wilson helped win approval for a $7 million HUD Urban Development Action Grant for a housing project in Fort Worth built by Mr. Briscoe. 
Officials in HUD's regional office in Fort Worth "strongly recommended that it not be approved," said Walter Sevier, HUD's deputy regional administrator in Fort Worth. 

   Among the regional office's objections were the project's high rents, the density of federal housing projects in the area and Mr. Briscoe's lack of private equity, Mr. Sevier explained. 
Last week, the project went into foreclosure, according to Mr. Sevier. 

   Mr. Wilson overrode the Fort Worth housing officials and approved the grant, Mr. Sevier said. 
Mr. Wilson couldn't be reached for comment. 

   In May 1986, almost two years after he left HUD, Mr. Wilson received a 15% interest in a $24 million HUD-backed project that Mr. Briscoe's development group built in Riviera Beach, Fla., with another urban development grant. 
Earlier this month, Mr. Wilson told the Fort Worth Star-Telegram, which first reported the relationship between Mr. Wilson and Mr. Briscoe, that he hadn't received any profits from the project. 

   A HUD internal audit of the Riviera Beach project obtained by The Wall Street Journal under the Freedom of Information Act said the development group overcharged HUD $3.5 million on the project and refused admission to families receiving federal housing assistance who wanted to live in the 520-unit apartment complex. 
Mr. Briscoe didn't return calls. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Heard on the Street:
@  Pundits Differ on Whether Holiday Managers
@  Are Planning to Take Share Benefits and Run
@  ----
@  By Peter Waldman
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> HIA </CO>
<IN> STOCK MARKET, OFFERINGS (STK)
HEARD ON THE STREET (HRD)
REAL ESTATE, REITS, LAND DEVELOPMENT (REL)
CASINOS AND GAMBLING (CNO)
TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM) </IN>
<DATELINE> ATLANTA  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   How rich do Holiday's executives want to get, and how fast? 

   That's the question facing investors in Holiday, the giant hotel and casino operator. 
After Nov. 11 Holiday's senior executives, who already own about 5% of the Memphis, Tenn., company, can immediately double their holdings under a 1986 recapitalization plan -- but only if they agree to sell the company. 
That's obviously a powerful incentive, especially in view of the high prices foreign investors have paid for some U.S. lodging concerns. 

   So powerful, in fact, that takeover speculation has more than doubled Holiday's share price this year, with the stock soaring more than 25% just this month. 
Holiday closed yesterday at 67 1/8, up 4, on heavy volume. 

   The stock benefits were promised to Holiday's top executives as "golden handcuffs" to entice them to stay at the company. 
The shares available to the executives were scheduled to build up over eight years through 1995, with a sizable penalty if the company changed ownership before this November. 
But a clause in the agreement lets management sell the company after Nov. 11 and immediately receive all the shares due them -- even if the executives acquire the company themselves in a leveraged buy-out. 

   Although a sale this fall would probably reap Holiday's executives more than $350 million between them, some analysts say management may opt to shoot for an even bigger payoff later. 
The company, since increasing its debt to $2.8 billion in a move meant to fend off raiders, has sold 66 hotels at unexpectedly high prices, while retaining the management contracts on them, analysts say. 
Holiday has used the sale proceeds to reduce debt by some $900 million. 
Now, some analysts predict Holiday, as it lowers debt further and narrows its focus to managing hotels and casinos, can continue rising in value. 

   "I don't see any evidence that says management is necessarily intent on selling Holiday," said analyst Steven Eisenberg of Bear Stearns. 
"I'm a little bit concerned that the Street is putting two and two together and getting six." 

   A Holiday spokesman says the company won't comment on movement in its stock or on speculation about possible transactions. 
Asked about a recent statement by analyst Daniel Lee of Drexel Burnham Lambert that Holiday officials have said they aren't ruling out a sale, the spokesman called this assertion "Dan Lee's statement. 
I don't know who he's talking about." 

   In the past year, several U.S. hotel chains have been sold for huge premiums to Japanese, Hong Kong and British buyers. 
Analysts say the offshore investors enjoy more favorable "goodwill" accounting rules that enable them to pay higher prices for the chains than U.S. companies can bear. 
"I think lodging concerns would be foolish not to consider some sort of transaction to realize these kinds of values," said Napoleon Overton, an analyst with Morgan Keegan &amp; Co. 

   According to Joseph Doyle of Smith Barney, Harris Upham, foreign interests have paid between 14 and 20 times previous-year's cash flow for such chains as Grand Metropolitan PLC's Intercontinental Hotels unit, Ramada Inc. and UAL's Westin Hotels. 
Holiday, itself, sold Holiday International to Bass PLC for about 14 times "trailing" cash flow, he said. 
Hilton Hotels, whose management indicated in May that it might sell the company, now is trading around 16 times its expected cash flow this year, he said. 

   Given these multiples, "I think Holiday management would consider selling for in excess of $100 a share," says Mr. Doyle, "but I don't think an offer of that size will come forth." 
Other analysts say the company could see a bid in the mid-70s or higher. 
Mr. Doyle says the $100-a-share price -- plus Holiday's roughly $80 a share of debt -- is about 11 times Holiday's expected cash flow this year. 
Currently, he says, Holiday is trading at about nine times expected cash flow. 

   Some institutional investors also praised Holiday's upside potential. 
"They're the only gaming company with casinos in all five gaming areas in Nevada and New Jersey, and they have the No. 1 suites concept with Embassy Suites," says an analyst for a large fund that holds about a million Holiday shares. 
"You're talking about a company that's still at a 60% discount to its asset values." 

   A Holiday competitor, naturally, sees things differently. 
Michael Leven, president and chief operating officer of Days Inns of America Corp., says, "All my research numbers say they're losing market share in their core {Holiday Inns} brand"something Holiday denies. 
"If I were sitting in the offices there and I had a chance to make that kind of money, I'd sell the company."

<HL> REVIEW &amp; OUTLOOK (Editorial):
@  Capital-Gains Compromise </HL>
<DD> 07/28/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> TAXES </CO>
<IN> ECONOMIC NEWS (ECO) </IN>
<GV> CONGRESS (CNG) </GV>
<TEXT>
   House Speaker Tom Foley is starting to talk compromise on a cut in the capital-gains tax, floating the idea of indexing capital gains to avoid taxing illusory, inflation-driven gains. 
Seems only fair. 
And as the bargaining gets serious, someone needs to get on the table the next point of equity, equal treatment for capital gains and capital losses. 
This is important because even in Congress the merits of the issue are not totally irrelevant. 

   Indeed, bargaining is now opening because Mr. Foley, Majority Leader Richard Gephardt and Ways and Means Chairman Dan Rostenkowski have failed in their effort to ignore the merits and cast the issue solely in terms of party loyalty. 
Six Ways and Means Democrats are blocking their attempt to bottle the issue up in committee and avoid a floor vote they fear they would lose. 

   The six represent the more conservative and mostly Southern Democrats who have recently been frozen out in the party's leadership changes. 
Beyond that, they understand the issue well enough to know how much hot air there is in the "official" Democratic position that the benefits of a capital-gains cut would go only to "the rich." 
It's worth repeating that the numbers behind this assertion include the capital gain itself, and that a large share of gains are realized by taxpayers with very little ordinary income, mostly retirees cashing in lifetime gains (see chart). 
The six Democrats have all no doubt talked to an allegedly rich constituent who retired and sold his dry-cleaning shop. 

   Once the official-line Democrats are forced to debate the issue, their first recourse is indexing-adjusting the base of the capital gain for inflation. 
This is an obvious admission that in the grand "compromise" in the 1986 tax law, their price was not treating capital gains equally but treating them punitively. 
On the merits, there is no rationale whatever for taxing "income" that represents no addition to its recipient's command over real economic resources. 
If the gain on the asset is less than inflation, you can in fact be taxed on a real loss. 

   While the treatment of capital losses is not as widely recognized, it represents a similiar inequity. 
Wages and salaries do not usually go below zero, but capital assets can not only appreciate in value but also decline. 
If the appreciation of an asset is added to other income for tax purposes, its decline ought to be deducted from other income for tax purposes. 
But in fact, deductions of capital losses from ordinary income are limited to only $3,000 a year. 

   In other words, the government plays heads-I-win, tails-you-lose. 
If you have a loss, only the first $3,000 is "ordinary income." 
If you have a gain, "ordinary income" includes not only the first $3,000 but the whole thing. 
The administration and other supporters of a tax cut for capital gains need to start demanding some redress for this obvious inequity. 

   It's true, of course, that taxpayers have discretion about when to realize capital gains. 
Despite occasional assertions to the contrary, the ability to delay your gains has no great weight on the issue of equity; it's hard to buy anything with an unrealized capital gain. 
But if losses were fully deductible, taxpayers would have an incentive to realize them quickly. 

   Now, quick recognition of losses is not only good investment strategy but good economic strategy. 
Full deductibility would promote mobility of capital and efficient pricing of assets. 
It would obviously promote more venture capital, with high gains and high risks. 
Mere indexing, by contrast, would help the big and dull investments with low risks and humdrum rewards. 
But if full deductibility of losses would be good for economic growth, it would be "bad" in two other ways. 

   One is appearances. 
The truly rich sometimes have large capital losses, and these could be used to offset large income streams and pay "no tax." 
This happened during the Great Depression, which is the historical reason capital gains and losses are not treated symmetrically. 

   Full deductibility, secondly, would be bad for Treasury revenues. 
Not surprisingly, the heads-I-win, tails-you-lose approach is profitable for the IRS. Capital losses are far from rare, as stock market investors discovered in October 1987. 
Indeed, if gains do not greatly exceed losses, why tax capital gains at all? 
This is one large reason many nations do not. 

   Once the discussion gets down to the merits and all these issues are on the table, you start to understand why the traditional U.S. treatment has been excluding some more or less arbitary portion of capital gains from taxable income. 
Short of full indexing and full deductibility, this is a pretty good compromise itself. 

   ---

<HL> Letters to the Editor:
@  Bastille Day Oversight
@  Duly Noted </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> EUROP </CO>
<TEXT>
   How dare The Wall Street Journal overlook Tom Paine on its Bastille Day editorial page (July 14). 
Editorial top billing went, instead, to Paine's nemesis Edmund Burke, opponent of American independence, albeit sympathetic with many colonial causes. 

   While Burke deplored from afar the sanguinary wrongs in revolutionary France, Paine was on the scene, as one of only two foreign deputies to the French National Convention, doing his moral best to replicate the American revolutionary experience. 

   Publication of Paine's "Rights of Man" peaked the Paine-Burke feud by rattling the feudal shackles still extant in Britain. 
Paine was tried and convicted, in absentia, for his seditious frankness; possession of his influential pamphlet was made a crime. 
But the man on the street, in England as in France, understood and believed Tom Paine when he declared in his "Rights of Man," "Whatever is my right as man is also the right of another; and it becomes my duty to guarantee as well as to possess." 

   No wonder the key to the Bastille was entrusted to Paine by the Marquis de Lafayette, to be forwarded to George Washington, as a symbol of liberty. 

   Well said, Tom; the Journal's oversight stands corrected. 

   Bonner Bell 

   Banning, Calif. 

</TEXT>
</DOC>
<DOC>
<DOCNO> WSJ890727-0182 </DOCNO>
<DD> = 890727 </DD>
<AN> 890727-0182. </AN>
<HL> Letters to the Editor:
@  Paying for Respite
@  From 'Second Shift' </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<TEXT>
   Regarding your June 21 article "The Working Woman's Nightmare": 

   I don't expect men to join the guilt-driven overwork syndrome we career women somehow accept from our own and society's demands. 
Laundry-pile neuroses and dust-ball anxiety are ailments women need to cure themselves of -- not spread to others. 
We have learned three-level market forecasting and sophisticated business strategies, but we have not yet replaced ourselves at the helm of home and family life. 
Performing that "second shift" poses a serious threat to women's physical and emotional health. 
The question of who will care for the family is such a serious one that many bright young career women and their husbands are deciding to forgo having children -- a decision that represents a loss to all of us. 

   Creating a warm and wonderful home environment is an important and time-consuming job. 
But who will give the necessary time and energy to the home is a key issue for this and future generations. 

   Any working mother certainly could have told the authors of "The Second Shift" that women give up leisure time to do housework, and men don't. 
A more important question than how to get men to do more is how to help women to do less, how to prevent them from curtailing almost all recreational time in favor of the necessary "second shift" for housework. 

   I suggest that a good part of the answer lies in hiring paid home workers. 
Almost all budgets can accommodate some type of household help, even if it is part time. 
It would be nice if each of us had a wife at home to anticipate and meet our needs, self-directed, self-motivated and unsalaried. 
But that is not possible for working mothers. 

   As I close this letter, I am snatching still-damp laundry from the dryer. 
Our home helper is on vacation, my son ran out of clean T-shirts for day camp, and I'm going to be late for work. 
I wish I had a wife] 

   Betty Cittadine 

   Chicago 

</TEXT>

<HL> Letters to the Editor:
@  The Right Pitch
@  For Answer System </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<TEXT>
   In your July 12 Managing column, it was pointed out that almost all "automated attendants" and "voice-processing" systems use a female voice. 
While you discuss that this is viewed as perpetuating stereotypes and slows women's progress, you also might have noted that for millions of hearing-impaired individuals who must struggle daily with the telephone, a female voice is usually 70% harder to understand than that of a male. 
This is because a large percentage of hearing loss is in the high-pitch range. 
Most of the companies utilizing these systems must want to communicate effectively with their callers. 
If so, they should do their homework, research the problem and learn that a male voice is often more readily understood by a larger part of the population. 
They should worry less about subtle backlash and aim for clearer speech. 

   Television producers, who insist upon turning up the volume of the background music so that the spoken word is lost in sound overload, and commercial advertisers might contact organizations or hire hearing-impaired consultants to review their products to see if it is possible to make these more readily understood. 

   Robin Ferst Howser 

   Shalimar, Fla. 

</TEXT>
