<HL> Politics &amp; Policy:
@  Cleaning Up Problems From Reagan Era Drains
@  Energy and Raises Bills of Bush Administration
@  ----
@  By James M. Perry and David Wessel
@  Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> GOVMT </CO>
<IN> ECONOMIC NEWS (ECO) </IN>
<GV> EXECUTIVE (EXE)
CONGRESS (CNG) </GV>
<DATELINE> WASHINGTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   "Some of us are like a shovel brigade that follows a parade down Main Street cleaning up," Donald Regan, White House chief of staff under President Reagan, once noted. 

   The Reagan brigades are gone now but the cleanup has barely begun, and President Bush will need regiments, entire divisions, to finish the job he has inherited from his predecessor. 

   "The chickens are coming home to roost," says Fred Greenstein, history professor at Princeton University. 

   "What we are left with," says Rudolph Penner, former director of the Congressional Budget Office, "is a government that is very constrained when it comes to taking care of serious problems." 

   And "the attention of the key people in this administration isn't so much forward looking but, instead, is dealing with the problems inherited from the past," says Murray Weidenbaum, an economist at Washington University in St. Louis and first chairman of President Reagan's Council of Economic Advisers. 

   The familiar problem is paying the interest on the long string of budget deficits run up during the Reagan years -- $1.3 trillion in all, with no end in sight. 
The year Mr. Reagan came to Washington, the federal debt was less than $800 billion. 
The year he left, it was about $2.2 trillion. 
In fiscal 1981, the government paid $69 billion in interest on the debt; this fiscal year, it will pay $169 billion. 

   "We've run up that debt and now we have to worry about servicing it," says John Palmer, dean of the Maxwell Graduate School of Citizenship and Public Affairs at Syracuse University. 
"We've mortgaged the future." 

   But Reagan defenders say the problem is being greatly overstated. 
"Sure it's a problem finding money to do all these things," says Jeffrey Bell, a private economic consultant and a long-time advocate of supply-side economics. 
"But setting limits on growth was a part of the Reagan years. 
What we need to remember is that the Reagan expansion was one of the greatest success stories in American history." 
Mr. Bell says money can be squeezed out of the economy, without "significant" tax increases, to pay the nation's bills. 

   But now, added to the earlier budget woes is a major scandal at the Department of Housing and Urban Development that is consuming almost all of the time and the energies of Jack Kemp, the new HUD secretary, and the emergence of a whole set of problems that had been largely swept under the rug. 
These problems will cost hundreds of billions more to solve over the next decade or so. 
By some experts' estimates, they include: 

   --Bailing out the nation's failing savings-and-loan associations, which will cost at least $164 billion over the next 10 years. 

   --Modernization of the nation's rusting and dangerous nuclear-warhead plants and the cleanup of the mess they have created over the past 40 years, which will cost more than $200 billion. 

   --Repairs to bridges and maintenance of interstate highways, $300 billion. 

   --Renovation of crumbling public housing, $20 billion. 

   --Purchase of modern computers and a new long-distance phone system for federal bureaucracies, $15 billion. 

   --Modernization of the nation's overburdened air-traffic-control system, $25 billion. 

   That hardly begins to tell the story. 
From every side, legitimate interests demand more federal funding. 
The Defense Department seeks to push ahead with the Stealth bomber, at $530 million a copy, and the Strategic Defense Initiative, which the General Accounting Office says could eventually cost $1 trillion for the full system. 

   The GAO estimates that the Pentagon is planning on spending $200 billion more over the next five years to man its ships, build its planes and pay its personnel than the administration is prepared to seek. 

   The new defense secretary, Dick Cheney, is spending most of his time looking for ways to cut spending, including the elimination of whole classes of new weapons. 
But, says Mr. Penner, "the Congress is balking" at making the cuts. 

   Billions more are being sought to pay for long-term health care for the elderly, for treatment of AIDS patients, for housing for the homeless, for new federal prisons for a burgeoning jail population, for better schools for poor children, for deferred maintenance of the national parks, for cleaning up toxic-waste dumps, for sending a manned mission to Mars. 

   Abroad, nations struggling to achieve democratic reforms and others pleading for help to solve their huge debt problems look to the U.S. for help. 
But the cupboard is bare. 

   And "we have a president in George Bush who has vowed never to raise taxes," says Democratic Sen. Daniel Moynihan of New York. 
"He made a calculated decision to do nothing about these problems, and the pity is he didn't need to do it. 
He would have been elected anyway. 

   "It is an iron law of history," says Sen. Moynihan, "that power passes from debtor to creditor, and it is Ronald Reagan's legacy that we are a debtor nation." 
Mr. Moynihan notes that President Bush, visiting Poland and Hungary, could offer little more than "walking around money" to bolster the economies of those two nations. 

   Mr. Bell, the supply-sider, says the nation's drift into debtor status began under President Nixon and is largely "structural" in nature. 
But, he concedes, "Mr. 
Reagan didn't address it or try to correct it." 

   "Does this mean that the baton of leadership is going to be passed to other nations?" 
Democratic Rep. Lee Hamilton of Indiana asked Treasury Secretary Nicholas Brady last Wednesday. 

   "I must admit," replied Mr. Brady, "that from the point of view of the Treasury it is a source of some embarrassment in world-wide forums not to be able to come up with our agreed-to subscriptions to the World Bank and other multilateral institutions. 
I don't think we have a passing of the baton yet, but it is a serious worry." 

   No one lays the blame for all of these problems at the feet of Mr. Reagan. 
Congress shares a heavy burden, too. 
The criticism of the Reagan administration is that it did so little to solve them. 
"They inherited these problems," says Charles Bowsher, the comptroller general. 
"And then they didn't do anything about them." 

   In the savings-and-loan arena, the Reagan administration pressed deregulation -- giving thrifts more freedom than they had ever previously enjoyed -- and cut back on government supervision at the same time. 
Coupled with unsually treacherous swings in interest rates at the beginning of the decade, the result was case after case of bad loans, wild investments, corruption and billions of dollars in losses. 
More banks and thrifts failed during the Reagan years than in any eight-year period since the Depression. 

   Despite loud warnings that the federal insurance fund that guarantees depositors' money wasn't big enough to cover the losses, the administration and Congress procrastinated. 
Legislation passed in 1987 to pump money into the insurance fund proved far too stingy. 
Regulators were overwhelmed. 
Finally, Reagan administration officials simply handed the problem over to their successors. 
Each month's delay added $1 billion to the cost of the final solution. 

   The problem with the nuclear-warhead plants began under President Carter, who in his final days ordered up a faster pace in production. 
"But it was Reagan who really accelerated it," says David Albright, senior staff scientist for the Federation of American Scientists. 
Old weapons plants that had been working eight hours a day, five days a week, went on a seven-day, around-the-clock schedule. 
The Reagan goal was to replace all of the nation's 25,000 warheads and build 17,000 new ones. 

   The rusty old plants couldn't handle the load, and several of them have been closed because of safety concerns. 
Once, says Democratic Sen. John Glenn of Ohio, "many of us believed in the old adage that nuclear weapons provide 'the biggest bang for the buck.' But now we are experiencing 'sticker shock.'" 

   Until last fall, no one seemed to worry very much about the consequences. 
Now it is estimated it will cost $200 billion to modernize the plants and clean up the mess around them. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Letters to the Editor:
@  Education's Problems Reflect Our Own </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<TEXT>
   The June 27 editorial "What Doesn't Work" and related page-one story "Lacking Good Results, Corporations Rethink Aid to Public Schools" illustrate the frustration with educational reform. 
There is little correlation among expenditure per pupil, teachers' salaries and the quality of education. 
New York has one of the highest expenditure per pupil and the average teacher salaries are among the highest, yet the high-school graduation rate is below the national average. 
But we should not conclude that we as a nation can afford to spend less on education. 

   The difficulty and complexity of raising educational performance are related to teen-age pathological behavior. 
Many young people are in trouble physically, emotionally and mentally. 
Recent government statistics reveal that close to 25% of all children born in the U.S. are born out of wedlock. 
The traditional family unit is no longer the norm, as teen-age mothers increase on the one hand and divorce rates among couples escalate on the other. 
School performance is clearly undermined by rising juvenile delinquency, increasing drug and alcohol abuse, increasing violent crime, and neglected and abused children. 
We see around us an increase in self-destructive behavior among young men. 
The homicide rate for black teen-agers is a national tragedy. 
Finally, the increase in teen-age suicide has been attributed to the profound changes in the American family. 

   There are no easy panaceas to the educational crisis today. 
Raising teacher salaries to recruit competent and dedicated teachers is necessary but not sufficient to achieve the best educational system in the world. 
We also should examine the content and style of textbooks, the courses that students elect to take and the learning environment in the classroom. 
But even those steps are partial and incomplete. 

   The Coleman report concluded that the primary factor that affected student learning was family background. 
The educational system always has been a reflection of our socioeconomic fabric. 
The evidence suggests that our consensus about the "welfare of society" has fragmented and our social fabric is unraveling. 

   Ben Kasper 

   Broome Community College 

   Binghamton, N.Y. 

</TEXT>

   Your June 28 editorial "The Battle Over Shoreham" covered the relevant issues regarding this specific situation. 
There are pending electric-supply problems elsewhere in the U.S. due in part to regulatory disarray of a type being experienced in New York. 

   It is difficult for consumers to accept that there is such a supply problem in the U.S. Electricity consumers historically have accepted, as a right, a high level of service from the utility industry. 
We face a different problem today and, it is hoped, this message can be delivered without an electric-supply crisis in some area of the country. 
Possibly Shoreham in Long Island will help deliver the point. 

   Leonard J. Kujawa

<HL> Exchange Bancorp
@  Set to Be Purchased
@  By Dutch Concern
@  ----
@  By Scott Kilman
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> EXCG N.ABN </CO>
<IN> TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM)
BANKS (BNK) </IN>
<DATELINE> CHICAGO  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Dutch banking concern Algemene Bank Nederland N.V. agreed to buy its third major bank here with a $24 a share, or $420 million, accord for Exchange Bancorp. 

   The agreement would establish the Dutch concern as one of the largest lenders to companies in the Chicago area with annual sales between $1 million and $100 million, the so-called middle market. 

   Banking analysts here said Algemene would also acquire a management group to shore up the lacklaster financial performance of its Chicago unit, LaSalle National Corp., which it bought in 1979. 
Under terms of the accord, John Rau, Exchange president, would become the new president and chief executive officer of the combined Chicago operations, which would have assets of $6 billion. 

   Under Mr. Rau, Exchange became one of the best performing banks in Chicago. 
Its stock price has risen an average 60% annually since 1983, when it traded around $2 a share, said Joan T. Goodman, an analyst at Pershing &amp; Co., Chicago. 
In national over-the-counter trading, Exchange closed at $21.875 a share, up $4.125. 

   The acquisition is subject to a definitive agreement, approval by Exchange shareholders and regulatory approvals. 
A group of Exchange officers and directors, which owns more than 25% of Exchange securities, has made option agreements with the LaSalle unit. 

   A pact might cap a trend among foreign banks to acquire a large foothold in Chicago. 
In September 1984, Bank of Montreal acquired Harris Bankcorp. for $546 million. 
Algemene's LaSalle unit bought a string of suburban Chicago banks last year. 

   Some banking analysts doubt a London or Tokyo bank would make a similar acquisition anytime here soon. 
Among other things, some foreign banks have been shying away from acquiring assets in the U.S. because of unexpectedly high expenses managing them from afar. 
Instead, the trend among foreign banks is to increasingly build alliances with U.S. banks. 

   But the Amsterdam bank's agreement for Exchange, which has $3 billion in assets, seems timed to beat possible suitors that might emerge from big banks in cities such as New York in December 1990, which is when a recently passed state law lowers the barrier to mergers between Illinois banks and banks through the nation. 

   Bank analysts said the offer for Exchange is fair. 
If completed, the deal would reap a windfall for Exchange Chairman Ira J. Kaufman and Mr. Rau, who led a group that took control of Exchange in July 1979 by buying 1.2 million shares of new common stock at about $1.45 each. 

   Exchange officers and directors control more than 45% of its 17.2 million fully diluted shares outstanding. 

   Mr. Kaufman said he won't continue with Exchange after the proposed acquisition, and would instead concentrate on his brokerage business. 
Mr. Kaufman is chairman and chief executive of Rodman &amp; Renshaw Capital Group Inc., Chicago. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Letters to the Editor:
@  Career Balancing Act </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<TEXT>
   Your July 12 Managing column mentions that a majority of men and women surveyed by Robert Half would accept slower career progress for more time with family. 
In my opinion, all the talk about a "mommy track" or a "parent track" is really a red flag waving at corporate America, warning it that a growing number of its valued managers refuse to put work above all else. 
This issue is not just for people with families, but for all professional types who realize that maybe they need more balance in their lives. 
As with most things corporate, the change in attitude needs to take hold at the top. 
A 70-hour workweek should not be necessary to succeed. 
It already has been shown that the attitude we bring to work is influenced by the quality of our off-time. 
Why should people have to accept a reduced chance at career opportunities just to spend time with their families? 

   John F. Langan Jr. 

   Sarasota, Fla. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Business Bulletin:
@  A Special Background Report
@  On Trends in Industry
@  And Finance
@  ----
@  By Christopher Winans </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> WCI </CO>
<IN> FINANCIAL, ACCOUNTING, LEASING (FIN)
CASINOS AND GAMBLING (CNO)
MARKETING, ADVERTISING (MKT)
RECREATION, ENTERTAINMENT, TOYS, MOVIES, PHOTOGRAPHY, SPORTS (REC) </IN>
<TEXT>
   CASH-FLOW DISCLOSURE for annual reports stirs debate. 

   A year ago, the Financial Standards Accounting Board started requiring that annual reports break down assets and investments by streams of cash. 
A recent survey says most securities analysts feel the rule helps them but won't help individual investors. 
Patricia McConnell of Bear Stearns says the rule results in "a more level playing field." 
Stuart Sheppard of Ketchum Public Relations says, "Companies are being bought, sold and ripped apart on the basis of cash flow and breakup values." 

   Quaker Oats Co., Chicago, an ardent believer in increasing cash flow, analyzed it in its annual reports before the FASB requirement. 
Rorer Group Inc., Fort Washington, Pa., says the rule doesn't go far enough, noting it doesn't permit stating cash flow on a per-share basis. 
Train Trailer Co., Chicago, says operating cash flow is important but too complex to explain. 
"Very few people would end up understanding it," says Robert Zimmerman, chief financial officer. 

   Financial institutions find gathering cash-flow data costly, so the FASB proposes to ease the disclosures for them. 

   WOODSTOCK MEMORIES are profitable -- for big business anyway. 

   It began as an "Aquarian Exposition." 
It ended as a symbol of the '60s. 
The Woodstock Music and Art Fair in August 1969 left a Bethel, N.Y., farm in a shambles, but Warner Communications may yet clean up. 
The firm, which still co-owns rights to the original event, will re-release two albums and home-video version of the film, but the three-day festival won't be repeated this year. 
"Maybe a 21st anniversary concert," says John Roberts of Woodstock Ventures. 
Seven original acts band together for a "69/89 20th Anniversary Celebration Tour." 

   The Woodstock logo -- a dove perched on a guitar neck -- is selling on T-shirts and mugs, among other things. 
Meanwhile, merchants in Woodstock, N.Y., say business is slow. 
"Either they're all waiting for August or they envision mud and long lines of traffic," says Jean Gaede of the local Chamber of Commerce, adding that no special event is planned. 
"The media is making a lot of hype and scaring our customers away," complains Albert Hoffman, an innkeeper. 

   OFFERING THE BEST PRICE won't by itself win and keep customers. 

   The quality of salesmanship is crucial in buyer-seller relationships, but it often falls short of the buyer's expectations, according to a study by Learning International, Stanford, Conn., a sales and management training firm. 
When asked what caused a buyer to drop a supplier, buyers in the survey indicated the fault often lay with the sales person. 
"The driving issue was breach of partnership," says Edward R. Del Gaizo, who directed the study. 

   The hottest trend in buyer-seller strategies is "partnerships," says Robert J. Bretz, materials-management director at Pitney Bowes Inc. 
The goal: reduce cutthroat bidding based solely on price "and get serious about negotiations that go way beyond price considerations." 
In such partnerships, the buyer gives the supplier more internal data than is typical in the course of a bidding war. 
William A. Bales, a Union Pacific Corp. unit purchasing executive, says, "How suppliers serve you depends on how closely you track their performance." 

   When Union Pacific Railroad started tracking suppliers' on-time performance in 1987, it found 40% of orders arrived late. 
Now it's only 14%, Bales says. 

   BETTOR HALF: Just 5% of all adults account for half the spending on state lotteries, says a study by the National Bureau of Economic Research, Cambridge, Mass. 
That group wagers about $1,100 each annually. 
Most likely lottery players: males, Hispanics, blacks, the middle-aged, Catholics, laborers and people lacking college degrees. 

   THE DISABLED ACHIEVER is the theme of new ads for the National Multiple Sclerosis Society, New York. 
Instead of portraying the disabled as helpless, the ads feature, among others, a quadriplegic marine biologist and a paraplegic model. 

   BAD DEBT in 1989 will cost each U.S. resident $250 in the form of higher consumer-goods prices needed to recover related losses, says the American Collectors Association, Minneapolis. 

   VENTURE-LEASING DEALS proliferate as a way to nurture start-ups. 

   Since 1986, equipment-leasing companies have provided $329 million in financing for new companies that couldn't get bank loans, says Lloyd Mintz of Venture Economics, a Wellesley Hills, Mass., consulting firm. 
A total of $154.9 million was invested in 1988, up 69% from 1987. 
To compensate for the risk of lending to a start-up, leasing companies also take an equity position. 
The total returns: 22% to 24%, some leasing firms say. 

   A lessor typically will take equity in return for leasing such gear as computers or machinery and providing a line of credit. 
"Venture leasing is attractive to young, cash-poor companies because it removes equipment debt from their balance sheets," but the strategy is still "a relatively new, unproven investment vehicle," Mintz says. 

   Geoff Woolley of Dominion Ventures, a San Francisco venture-leasing firm, says the field is destined to be a profitable but small niche business. 

   BRIEFS: The average supermarket loses a steak a day from the meat case, says Knogo Corp., a Hauppauge, N.Y., maker of anti-theft systems. . . . 
Fertil-A-Chron Inc., Deer Park, N.Y., guarantees conception in six months using its computerized fertility indicator or your $145 will be refunded. 

</TEXT>

   Fluid for Thought 

   The human body, head to toe, 

   Is mostly made of H20. 

   It fills the interstitial space 

   That holds each molecule in place; 

   And if we grow a trifle stout, 

   We ripple as we move about. 

   The highest wisdom that we know 

   Is simply going with the flow, 

   For time and tide refuse to wait -- 

   Succumbing, we evaporate, 

   And rising to a higher plane 

   Condense, and fall as acid rain. 

   --Joyce LeMers.

   There is eternal life, indeed. 

   Ladies and gentlemen, 

   I give you the weed. 

   --Frank Tyger.

<HL> International -- Corporate Report:
@  Eurotunnel </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> EUROP S.ABB U.HSI BBD.A FIA I.BEF </CO>
<IN> TRANSPORTATION, TRUCK AND SHIP LINES, RAILROADS (TRA) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Eurotunnel, the Anglo-French group behind the construction of the English Channel tunnel, ordered #600 million ($990 million) of train engines and cars from several, largely European groups. 
The purchases include 40 locomotives, 252 passenger cars and 252 heavy freight cars. 

   A group including Asea Brown Boveri AG, a Swiss-Swedish venture, and Britain's Hawker Siddeley Group PLC were awarded a contract for the locomotives. 

   A second group, including Bombardier Inc. of Canada, France's ANF Industrie and BN Constructions Ferroviaires et Metalliques of Belgium won the passenger-car contract. 

   The freight cars will be built by an Italian consortium of Breda Construzion Ferroviarie, a unit of Finanziaria Ernesto Breda S.p.A., and Fiat Ferroviaria, a unit of Fiat S.p.A. 

</TEXT>

<HL> South Korean Surplus </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> FREST </CO>
<IN> MONETARY NEWS, FOREIGN EXCHANGE, TRADE (MON) </IN>
<DATELINE> SEOUL  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   South Korea's current account surplus for the first six months of 1989 fell 58% to $2.4 billion from $5.8 billion in the year-earlier period, Bank of Korea reported. 

   The drop was attributed by the bank to the sharply reduced trade surplus. 
During the first half, exports grew by a mere 6.8% from a year earlier to $29 billion while imports surged 19% to $27 billion. 
As a result, the trade surplus shrank to $2 billion from $4.5 billion. 

</TEXT>

<CO> JAPAN </CO>
<IN> MONETARY NEWS, FOREIGN EXCHANGE, TRADE (MON) </IN>
<DATELINE> TOKYO  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Major Japanese trading companies signed contracts to export 1.083 trillion yen ($7.64 billion) of goods in June, up 11.6% from a year earlier, owing in part to strong demand overseas for plants and ships, the Japan Foreign Trade Council said. 

   Meanwhile, import contracts amounted to 1.581 trillion yen, a 20.9% surge from a year earlier. 
The council compiles its monthly report on the basis of contract statistics supplied by 18 major trading houses, which handle about half of the country's overall exports and two-thirds of imports, and their data provide an early indication of short-term trends in trade. 

</TEXT>

<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> CFBI </CO>
<DATELINE> SAN ANTONIO, Texas  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Cullen/Frost Bankers Inc. said that its board adopted a stock-purchase rights plan intended to deter any hostile takeover attempts but that the move wasn't a response to any effort to acquire the banking company. 

   Cullen/Frost is the last major, independent Texas banking company that hasn't been sold or taken over by regulators. 

   Its rights plan allows holders to buy Cullen/Frost stock at half price if a hostile suitor acquires or makes a tender offer for at least 15% of the shares outstanding, or to buy stock in the surviving company at half price if a person or group acquires more than 50% of the shares or the company is involved in a merger.

<TEXT>
   Australia's consumer-price index rose 2.4% during the second quarter and was up 7.6% from a year earlier, the Bureau of Statistics said. 

   The 2.4% rise, which was close to market expectations, followed a 1% gain in the first quarter. 
But that number was made artificially smaller by a change in the method of calculating the index. 

   The 7.6% rise from the 1988 second quarter compares with a rise of 6.8% in the 12 months ended March 31.

<CO> VFC </CO>
<IN> BUYBACKS, REDEMPTIONS, SWAP OFFERS (BBK) </IN>
<DATELINE> WYOMISSING, Pa.  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   V.F. Corp. said it obtained $500 million financing to proceed with a Dutch auction tender offer for 10 million shares of its common at a price to be determined between $35 and $39 a share. 

   The offer commenced last Friday. 

   The apparel company said Morgan Guaranty Trust Co. of New York is providing $500 million in financing. 

   As of July 18, VF had 68,174,113 common shares outstanding. 

   In a Dutch auction, a company sets a price range within which stockholders can tender their shares. 
The highest price within that range necessary to acquire all the shares the company wants becomes the purchase price for all the shares.

<HL> West Germany's Reserves </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> EUROP </CO>
<IN> MONETARY NEWS, FOREIGN EXCHANGE, TRADE (MON) </IN>
<DATELINE> FRANKFURT, West Germany  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   West Germany's net monetary reserves rose 600 million marks ($318.5 million) to 48.6 billion marks in the week ended Sunday, the West German Bundesbank, the central bank, said. 

   Overall reserves climbed 700 million marks to 93.5 billion marks because of interest income from the Bundesbank's securities held abroad, especially U.S. Treasurys. 
As foreign liabilities gained 100 million marks to 45 billion marks, net reserves were up 600 million marks.

</DOC>
<DOC>
<DOCNO> WSJ890727-0149 </DOCNO>
<DD> = 890727 </DD>
<AN> 890727-0149. </AN>
<HL> PepsiCo's Net Income in Second Quarter
@  Rose 21% Due in Part to Special Gains
@  ----
@  By Michael J. McCarthy
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> PEP </CO>
<IN> EARNINGS (ERN)
BEVERAGES (BVG) </IN>
<TEXT>
   PepsiCo Inc. reported a 21% increase in second-quarter net income, aided partly by special gains. 

   Net for the soft-drink, snack-food and restaurant company rose to $264.9 million, or $1 a share, from $219.6 million, or 84 cents a share, a year earlier. 
The latest earnings included a grab bag of extraordinary items, including a credit related to a Japanese bottling operation and charges at the snack food and Kentucky Fried Chicken divisions. 
The net result was a gain of $15 million, or six cents a share. 
Without these items, net income would have risen 14% to 94 cents a share -- about in line with analysts' estimates. 

   Sales of the Purchase, N.Y.-based concern rose 24% to $3.59 billion from $2.9 billion. 

   Pepsi's increase came despite its aggressive acquisition of bottling operations, which was part of an overall industry consolidation and which diluted second-quarter earnings 12 cents a share. 
But "the underlying strength of our ongoing operations and the impressive cash flow they generate enable us to invest for long-term growth and still achieve our annual performance goals," said D. Wayne Calloway, Pepsi chairman and chief executive officer, in a statement. 

   The rate of Pepsi's quarterly profit growth eased considerably from the first quarter's 46% net increase. 
But "given the fact that they're absorbing the dilution {from the bottling acquisitions}, they've done very well," said Martin Romm, an analyst with First Boston Corp. 
"The acquisitions are long-term and strategic, and I think the market understands there is a short-term price to be paid." 

   Pepsi's stock rose $1.50 a share yesterday, to close at $59 in New York Stock Exchange composite trading. 

   Led by Ruffles, Fritos and Doritos, Frito-Lay's domestic volume rose more than 8% in the quarter, while overall snack-food operating profit rose 24%. 
The strong volume growth helped absorb the costs of promotional spending for new potato chip products and high potato costs, which were driven up partly by the residual effects of last summer's drought. 

   Excluding a one-time credit and the net impact of the purchase and sale of bottling operations, soft-drink operating profits rose 32%. 
Retail pricing for Pepsi's company-owned bottlers rose 2% in the quarter. 
Higher pricing and this year's rainier summer have slowed the soft-drink industry's volume growth to about 3%, from a more typical 4% to 5%. 
Led by Diet Pepsi and Mountain Dew, Pepsi's second-quarter domestic case sales rose 3% for the quarter and 4% for the first half of the year. 

   Restaurant earnings rose 15% to $102.1 million in the quarter but were hampered by an $8 million pretax charge to consolidate some domestic Kentucky Fried Chicken operations. 
Taco Bell and Pizza Hut showed strong gains in sales and earnings. 

   But Kentucky Fried Chicken earnings, excluding the charge, fell 19%. 
Pepsi blamed higher costs for chicken and labor and a decrease in restaurant traffic. 
"KFC just isn't on a roll," said Emanuel Goldman, a PaineWebber Inc. analyst. 
Among other things, he said, the world's largest fast-food chicken chain needs new products to generate greater sales and more promotional marketing programs similar to those that helped rejuvenate Taco Bell. 

   In the first six months, Pepsi net rose 29% to $430 million, or $1.62 a share, from $332.9 million, or $1.27 a share, a year earlier. 
The latest period includes the net gain of $15 million, while the year-earlier net includes a $10.4 million charge for reorganizing domestic soft-drink operations. 

   Sales rose 22% to $6.55 billion from $5.38 billion. 

</TEXT>
</DOC>

<HL> Law:
@  Holland &amp; Knight Merges </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> WNEWS </CO>
<IN> LAW AND LEGAL AFFAIRS (LAW)
TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM) </IN>
<DATELINE> ATLANTA  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Florida-based law firm of Holland &amp; Knight established offices in Jacksonville through a merger with Gallagher, Mikals &amp; Cannon. 

   Six shareholders of the Gallagher firm will become Holland &amp; Knight partners. 
They are Daniel J. Gallagher, John J. Mikals, L. Kinder Cannon III, Linda Connor Kane, Lawrence J. Hamilton II and Martha McMahon Wirtz. 
Seven other attorneys from the firm will become Holland &amp; Knight associates. 

   In addition, George E. Schulz, formerly a partner with Beckham, McAliley &amp; Schulz in Jacksonville, was named a partner at Holland &amp; Knight in Jacksonville. 

   Reeder Glass, a managing partner at Holland &amp; Knight, said the merger advances the firm's goal of being a statewide Florida law firm. 
Following the merger and the addition of Mr. Schulz, Holland &amp; Knight has 265 attorneys in eight Florida cities and Washington, D.C.

<HL> Southern Co.'s Profit
@  Rose 20% in First Half </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> SO </CO>
<IN> EARNINGS (ERN)
UTILITIES (UTI) </IN>
<DATELINE> ATLANTA  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Southern Co. said first-half profit jumped 20%, but added that the full year's performance will depend on the outcome of a Georgia rate case. 

   The utility concern said net income rose to $384.4 million, or $1.22 a share, from $318.3 million, or $1.03 a share, a year earlier. 
Revenue rose to $3.56 billion from $3.46 billion, while kilowatt-hour sales grew 3.7%. 

   Continued growth in energy sales and utilization during the period led to "stronger financial results," said Edward L. Addison, president. 
He added that "the outcome of a rate case now in progress in Georgia will have a significant impact on our earnings for the full year." 

   Public hearings on a $374 million rate-increase request from Southern's Georgia Power unit began last week. 
The increase, which would be phased in over two years, is designed to help it recover costs at the Unit 2 Vogtle Nuclear Power Plant. 

   The Georgia Public Service Commission has already disallowed certain costs related to Unit 1 Vogtle, a decision that Southern has appealed in court. 
Unless the commission's decision is overturned, the company will have to take a $205 million charge, Mr. Addison said. 

   For the most recent 12-month period, Southern said net increased 27% to $912.4 million, or $2.90 a share, from $721.1 million, or $2.36 a share. 
Revenue rose to $7.32 billion from $7.26 billion. 

   In New York Stock Exchange composite trading yesterday, Southern's shares closed at $27.50, up 75 cents.

<AN> 890727-0136. </AN>
<HL> LEISURE &amp; ARTS:
@  Film: Man and Mutt Go Through Their Paces
@  ----
@  By Julie Salamon </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<TEXT>
   Five screenwriters are credited with the concoction of "Turner &amp; Hooch." 
And this new product from Walt Disney's Touchstone Pictures does indeed look like something to which many hands have given a twist of the wrench on some assembly line. 
Yet, coolly efficient and uninspired though it may be, the picture's pairing of Tom Hanks and a very ugly dog makes for some good laughs and some generally passable entertainment. 

   Mr. Hanks, adept at transferring his amusing screen personality from film to film without making enormous readjustments, is his expectable appealing self. 
But what keeps the contrived enterprise trotting along is a Disney specialty: a dog. 
The pooch in question is particularly winning because he is such a complete loser by any normal doggy criterion. 

   Hooch is a junkyard mutt, whose physique and manner are a canine version of the late John Belushi's Slobovian frat boy in "National Lampoon's Animal House." 
Hooch is nonchalantly flatulent, thrives on chocolate chip cookies and beer, and has a pronounced drool, one of the movie's running gags. 
He looks smelly. 

   The film makers chose Beasley, a De Bordeaux mastiff, to make his debut as Hooch. 
Despite its elegant French pedigree, the De Bordeaux breed, at least this example of it, is not "tres joli." 
Beasley has enough skin to cover two big dogs; the excess flaps in the breeze when he moves. 

   The movie juggles many themes and situations favored by Disney's Touchstone people in many of their successful, disposable comedies. 
Chief among these are hostile partners who become friendly ("Shoot to Kill"); the pairing of a neat partner and a messy one ("Stakeout"); the humanizing of rigid or otherwise undeveloped men by means of more innocent creatures, usually babies or small children ("Three Men and a Baby" and "Three Fugitives"). 

   In this case, the tables are turned. 
The small creature, Hooch, gets civilized by a neat and clean grownup man -- and the man loosens up. 
Turner (Mr. 
Hanks) is a small-city detective in a picture postcard Northern California beach town and a model practitioner of a strict yuppie regimen: He exercises, takes mega-vitamins, dresses with a casual formality, always buckles his seat belt, grinds his own coffee beans and keeps his person and his house in fastidious order. 
Hooch, who becomes his sidekick, fulfills many functions: He is the initially hostile Other, undeniably messy and childlike, in his fashion (that is, unrestrained in his display of emotion, totally infuriating or endearing, depending on his behavior at the moment). 

   Director Roger Spottiswoode, who directed "Shoot to Kill" as well as the far more compelling "Under Fire," is an able journeyman who introduces no surprises but makes no gaffes either as he allows Mr. Hanks and Beasley to go through their paces. 
Chubby Reginald VelJohnson is on hand to do a milder variant on the supporting cop role he played in "Die Hard." 
The earnest, talented actress Mare Winningham brings romance to Mr. Hanks, so no one can question the purity of his relationship with Hooch. (Hooch has his own love object, a toothsome collie bitch.) 

   The only unpredictable and rather inexplicable bit of business in this movie was the creative decision to have Mr. Hanks repeatedly appear wearing only black briefs. 
This is not particularly flattering to him. 
Yet in a movie like this, where nothing is uncalculated, the costume must be making a point, perhaps to illustrate that the pale Turner had too much sense to venture out to the beach without protecting himself with sunblocking lotion. 

   ---

   "UHF," a parody of trash television, is almost defiantly silly, but when it's funny it is very funny. 
The picture has the pace and feel of a segment of "Saturday Night Live" -- lots of filler in between the occasional hilarious spoof. 
Co-author and star "Weird Al" Yankovic has become famous with his parodies of rock stars and their videos, and his original compositions, such as "Stuck in a Closet With Vanna White." 
His videos include his "Like a Surgeon" takeoff on Madonna's "Like a Virgin"; his "Fat" version of Michael Jackson's "Bad." 
Now Mr. Yankovic has decided to expand his particular talents to the wider horizons of film. 
Eccentric as Mr. Yankovic's humor is, he appears onscreen to be cherubic and slightly remote. 
He's assigned himself the part of George Newman, a young man who has failed in many ways, including working at a fast-food restaurant. 
George's Uncle Harvey has won a ramshackle UHF television station in a poker game and hires George to run it. 

   Mr. Yankovic and his co-author Jay Levey, the movie's director, have concocted some truly goofy variations on bottom-of-the-barrel television ads and shows. 
Some are gross and in terrible taste -- and may make you feel ashamed for laughing so hard at them (for example, "Raul's Wild Kingdom," an animal show with a sadistic host who amuses himself by tossing poodles out of upper-story windows). 

   This sloppy, good-natured satire certainly doesn't threaten "Network's" status as the classic decimation of the television business. 
In fact, you may want to wait and catch it on the tube. 
Nothing will be lost in the shrinking. 

   ---

   Video Tip: "Turner &amp; Hooch" reminded me that the dog days of summer are upon us, a good time, perhaps, to remember "Old Yeller." 
When a friend of mine was quite small, her mother took her and some of her little pals to see "Old Yeller" for a birthday celebration. 
When the mother realized what was coming at the end, she told the kids the picture was over and hustled them out of the theater before Old Yeller met his fate. 
Maybe the kiddies would be better off with the cheery "Lady and the Tramp." 

</TEXT>
</DOC>

<DOCNO> WSJ890727-0135 </DOCNO>
<DD> = 890727 </DD>

<HL> REVIEW &amp; OUTLOOK (Editorial) -- Asides:
@  Political Acid Test </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<GV> CONGRESS (CNG) </GV>
<TEXT>
   Even though 315 out of 435 House Members are co-sponsoring repeal of Section 89, a series of onerous restrictions on employee fringe benefits, the House leadership still wants to block a floor vote. 
But now the Appropriations Committee has voted to bar Treasury from enforcing Section 89; the proposal now must win a Rules Committee vote today to reach the floor. 
Ten of the 13 committee Members are co-sponsors of the repeal bill, but in May a full third of Members opposed to Section 89 bowed to leadership pressure and voted against a procedural effort to repeal it. 
We'll be watching to see how many Members of the Rules Committee end up putting their votes where their rhetoric is.

@  New Zealand Steel Finds
@  Buyer to Replace Chinese
@  ----
@  Special to The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> BHP A.FPI </CO>
<IN> TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM)
MINING, METALS (NON-PRECIOUS) (MIN) </IN>
<DATELINE> WELLINGTON, New Zealand  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   A company controlled by Broken Hill Proprietary Co. of Australia has agreed to pay 323 million New Zealand dollars (US$187.7 million) for New Zealand Steel Ltd., which had been slated for sale to a Chinese concern in an agreement that fell apart last month. 

   Helenus Corp. will pay NZ$225 million to Equiticorp International PLC, a failed investment-banking and finance company now in receivership, for its 80% stake in New Zealand Steel. 
The remaining 20% of the company will be bought for NZ$98 million from Fisher &amp; Paykel Industries, an electrical appliance maker. 

   An agreement to sell New Zealand Steel to China National Metals &amp; Minerals Import &amp; Export Corp. fell through in June, soon after the Tiananmen Square massacre of protesting students in Beijing. 
Chinese officials have refused to say why they cancelled the sale, but denied it was due to political pressure or an inability to raise funds.

<HL> REVIEW &amp; OUTLOOK (Editorial):
@  Wright's Drapes </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<GV> CONGRESS (CNG) </GV>
<TEXT>
   When former Speaker of the House Jim Wright is sent packing, he really packs. 

   It is traditional for the Speaker to take a few political mementos from his office home with him when he leaves office. 
According to the Baltimore Sun, a moving van pulled up to the Capitol steps last month. 
Mr. Wright's desk was loaded onto the back. 
So was his chair. 
So were other chairs from the office. 
So were the couch, and the breakfront, and a few tables. 
The drapery must have held a few memories for Mr. Wright, for it was hauled off as well. 
These items are not officially Mr. Wright's, mind you. 
They are considered on "long-term loan." 
Nonetheless, the office Thomas Foley inherited was virtually an empty box. 

   It's not yet clear what is happening to Mr. Wright's booty. 
Most of it was sent to Texas Christian University. 
The library there is going to set up a James Wright Jr. Room, where visitors will be able to gaze at the former Speaker's furniture (officials at the library have not yet unpacked all of the crates, and so do not know yet exactly what they have or how they are going to arrange it). 

   This story illustrates the way many Congressmen regard their positions. 
Indeed, Mr. Wright is not the first Congressman to prove that you can take it with you. 

   Former Speakers Tip O'Neill and Carl Albert brought the trappings of their power home as well. 
Twenty of last year's 27 retiring Representatives now have some furniture as souvenirs. 
The most complete was Fernand St Germain, the rogue Banking Committee Chairman who helped create the savings-and-loan mess, and who was found in violation of ethics rules even by the normally lap-dog Ethics Committee. 
He completely cleaned out his quarters when he retired. 
The Rhode Islander who replaced him in Congress, Ron Machtley, was forced to hold initial meetings with everyone standing up, because there were no chairs. 
Regular Members, unlike House Speakers, do have to pay for the furniture they keep, but the price is determined by a depreciated scale set by the General Services Administration. 
One Congressman paid $4.80 each for leather chairs, $23 each for couches and $60 for a mahogany desk. 

   Ideally, a Congressman's power adheres to the office, not to the person who temporarily holds it. 
But a seat in Congress is now a fiefdom. 
Virtually guaranteed that seat for life, allowed to keep political contributions if they were in office before 1980, accustomed to the bowing and scraping of huge staffs, intimidated bureaucrats and favor-pleading lobbyists, many congressmen assume an almost royal arrogance. 
There has been a change of manners on the Hill. 
The egotism verges on: "l'etat, c'est moi." 

   No wonder they confuse public property with their own. 
No wonder Representatives are so comfortable with transparent legal dodges, such as "on long-term loan" or the ridiculously depreciated prices (departing Senators, by the way, have to pay the full replacement cost for their furniture). 
The furniture scam is only a few drops; Congressmen swim in an ocean of perks. 

   Speaker Wright in the end came to epitomize this new congressional arrogance, contemptuous as he was toward common-sense ethical standards, executive power and even the minority members of his own body. 
The furniture at Texas Christian will not be as grand, but as visitors wander through the James Wright Jr. Room, they may recall the folly of Versailles. 

</TEXT>
</DOC>

<HL> International:
@  EC and Switzerland Agree
@  On Insurance Firms' Access </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> EUROP </CO>
<IN> INSURANCE (INS) </IN>
<DATELINE> ZURICH, Switzerland (AP)  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The European Community and Switzerland reached an accord on reciprocal access to insurance markets, with the exception of life insurance, the Swiss government said. 

   The agreement specifies regulatory provisions and establishes a framework for arbitration between Swiss insurers and the EC. 

   The Association of Swiss Insurers said it was "deeply satisfied" with the agreement. 
About 40% of the 63 billion francs ($38.95 billion) in premium income earned by Swiss insurance companies in 1988 came from EC countries. 

   The accord still must be approved by the Swiss legislature and the European Parliament.

<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Steve Rubell, the Studio 54 co-owner who presided over New York night life when disco was at its peak, died at age 45. 

   Mr. Rubell died in a Manhattan hospital of complications from hepatitis and septic shock, his publicist was quoted by the Associated Press as saying. 
A private funeral service was scheduled for today. 

   Mr. Rubell, the subject of a page one article in The Wall Street Journal in January, and Ian Schrager operated Studio 54 in Manhattan in the late 1970s. 
The two were convicted in 1979 for tax evasion. 
In January 1980 were sentenced to 3 1/2 years in prison but were released a year later. 

   Within four years of their release, they were operating Morgans hotel in Manhattan and running another nightclub, the Palladium.

<HL> Guilty Plea Follows Probe
@  Of Dallas 'Skinhead' Gang </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<GV> JUSTICE DEPARTMENT (JUS) </GV>
<DATELINE> WASHINGTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   One man pleaded guilty and six others were charged with criminal acts in connection with alleged racially and religiously motivated violence and vandalism by a Dallas "skinhead" group, the Justice Department said. 

   The guilty plea and criminal charges are the result of a continuing federal grand jury investigation of the Confederate Hammerskins, a Dallas organization that prosecutors say committed acts of violence and vandalism against blacks, Hispanics and Jews during the summer and fall of 1988. 
The grand jury investigation is the first such federal probe of a skinhead group. 

   The growth of racially and religiously motivated crime was the subject of a recent Wall Street Journal article. 

   In the Dallas case, Andrew Francis Flowers pleaded guilty to "threatening and intimidating an unidentified black person." 
Six other people were accused of intimidating blacks and vandalizing Jewish-owned properties in the Dallas area. 
Mr. Flowers faces a maximum sentence of one year's imprisonment and a $100,000 fine.

<DOCNO> WSJ890727-0127 </DOCNO>
<DD> = 890727 </DD>
<AN> 890727-0127. </AN>
<HL> LEISURE &amp; ARTS -- Bookshelf:
@  A Wall Street Requiem
@  ----
@  By Steve Swartz </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<TEXT>
   There may yet be a good book in the destruction of one of Wall Street's great firms, E.F. Hutton &amp; Co. 

   Think of the drama in the firm's last year or so, when a cast of characters ranging from the inept to the greedy and callous took a faltering firm, and with the help of the crash, finished it off. 

   There was the former chairman, Robert Fomon, determined to undermine the remaining Hutton executives who had engineered his ouster in 1987 after years of mismanagement. 
There was his successor, Robert Rittereiser, a hard-working nice guy who reached the CEO's office from humble beginnings as a clerk but had trouble making decisions once he got there. 

   There was the board of directors, headed by Peter Ueberroth, then baseball commissioner, which accepted lucrative severance deals after the fall but that had done little to help save the firm. 

   And there were the victims: Hutton's shareholders, who were denied $50 a share for their Hutton stock in 1986 because the board rejected an initial merger proposal from Shearson Lehman Bros. 
Inc., only to be forced to accept one from Shearson a year later at $29.25 a share. 
Also hurt were some 5,000 Hutton employees, cashiered after the Shearson/Hutton merger. 

   All of this was well documented in newspaper and magazine accounts at the time. 
But they could not provide the kind of behind-the-scenes anecdotes and vivid revelations that can come only from months if not years of painstaking research. 
Unfortunately, both Donna Sammons Carpenter and John Feloni in "The Fall of the House of Hutton" (Holt, 322 pages, $19.95) and Mark Stevens in "Sudden Death: The Rise and Fall of E.F. Hutton" (NAL Books, 298 pages, $19.95) rarely get much beyond the public record on Hutton, and they even manage to waste what others gave them, by letting the intrinsic intensity of the events evaporate in shapeless narration. 

   Subjected to closer scrutiny, Mr. Fomon, who ran Hutton for 17 years, could himself be the subject of an interesting book. 
He built Hutton into a brokerage industry powerhouse, became wealthy in the process and married a former Miss America. 
By the early 1980s, however, Mr. Fomon's personal and professional lives were in decline. 
Divorced, lonely and drinking more heavily, Mr. Fomon became increasingly removed from Hutton's day-to-day operations and began making embarrassing public statements. 
He and Hutton suffered a grave humiliation when the firm pleaded guilty in 1985 to 2,000 counts of federal mail and wire fraud in the now infamous check-kiting scandal. 
Thereafter, Mr. Fomon's power slowly slipped away to his newly hired No. 2, Mr. Rittereiser, whose new management team exacerbated the firm's collapse. 

   Ms. Carpenter and Mr. Feloni, unwilling or unable to move smoothly through the history they have undertaken to relate, have broken their book up into a hiccuping series of assertedly significant days culled from Hutton's last seven years. 
Unfortunately, many of these days were not, as Edward R. Murrow used to say, "filled with the events that alter and illumine our times." 
Under the heading Monday, July 24, 1984, we are told the Dow Jones Industrial Average hit its low for the year but aren't told why that mattered. 
In fact, it is a factoid, irrelevant to the Hutton story. 

   Mr. Stevens has a surer hand with a sentence, but he has a weakness for lazy quips: Mr. Rittereiser, for example, is described at one point as a "Santa Claus in a business suit." 
He also at times turns his narrative over to single sources who are quoted at length. 
A longtime Hutton investment banker, Paul Bagley, is permitted to provide without rebuttal a largely negative critique of Mr. Rittereiser's management team. 

   By mid-1986, all the players in this disturbing tale are on stage. 
Hutton's overpaid and by now underperforming sales staff is in open revolt because of the lack of leadership at headquarters. 
Mr. Fomon and Mr. Rittereiser are at war with each other. 
New legal and business problems crop up monthly. 
Perhaps Mr. Rittereiser's most dubious hire in the eyes of many Hutton colleagues, marketing chief Jerry Welsh, tries at one point to illustrate Hutton's problems with a coloring book distributed to all Hutton managers that contained such silly phrases as: "If we don't fix our problems soon, someone could even take our home away." 

   It's unclear just how long this show would have run on its own, but the October 1987 crash pulled the curtain down quickly. 
As the firm was being sold, many of those whose performance through the firm's time of crisis was altogether undistinguished were taken care of nicely. 
Mr. Fomon had already secured a $3 million severance deal, and Mr. Rittereiser was awarded $1.2 million. 
In an even more surprising action, the members of the Hutton board accepted $2.2 million in severance benefits. 
Mr. Ueberroth, already a multimillionare, got $420,000 plus an additional $500,000 for the work he did negotiating the Shearson merger; Dina Merrill, a successful actress and heir, as daughter, to E.F. Hutton himself, got $307,500. 

   Many Hutton employees got something from the merger too: a pink slip. 
Some of them were herded into a conference room, told to clear out their belongings quickly and were then searched on their way out the door. 
Wouldn't it have been terrible if the hired help had walked away from Hutton with anything they didn't deserve? 

   ---

   Mr. Swartz is a page one editor with the Journal. 

</TEXT>
</DOC>

<DD> = 890727 </DD>
<AN> 890727-0125. </AN>
<HL> LEISURE &amp; ARTS -- Opera:
@  Sellars Does Mozart, Dark and Depraved
@  ----
@  By Heidi Waleson </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<DATELINE> Purchase, N.Y.  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   After 10 years of impressive avantgarde events in theater and music, the PepsiCo Summerfare festival is going out with a bang. 
Audiences in this very pleasant venue just north of Manhattan are getting a complete look at the three Mozart-Da Ponte operas that Peter Sellars created one by one in past seasons. 
And they will hear Roger Norrington's two-day "Beethoven Experience" exploring the Ninth Symphony. 
You couldn't have more disparate bookends than these two visions of some of the most revered totems of the literature. 

   Mr. Norrington's work, with original instruments, is all about the rediscovery of period context for Beethoven. 
Mr. Sellars, on the other hand, has run headlong in the other direction, presenting what amounts to a complete and ahistorical deconstruction of the Mozart operas. 
Mr. Sellars's productions cannot be considered merely interpretations or updatings of "Le Nozze di Figaro," "Don Giovanni" and "Cosi Fan Tutte." 
They are radical rethinkings of what these operas are about. 
The texts, sung here in the original Italian, are treated as poetic obbligatos rather than the dramatic frameworks that Lorenzo Da Ponte gave Mozart. 
Instead we have Mr. Sellars's own elaborate plot synopses in the program book, which delve into psychological motivations that sometimes diverge markedly from any traditional understanding of the librettos. 
Clearly, Mr. Sellars hears something quite different in the music, which is his privilege. 
Whether he can persuade us that what he hears is there is another matter altogether. 

   The most sensational example is "Don Giovanni," an opera hard to make sense of at the best of times. 
Mr. Sellars claims that it is about addiction, and creates an entirely new environment for it: a Spanish Harlem underworld of gangs, drugs and violence. 
Don Giovanni is a corrupt kingpin/rapist on his way out and balked at every turn by Anna, Ottavio, Elvira, Zerlina, Masetto, Leporello and finally, the Commendatore. 
None of his tormenters is innocent, however: Even Donna Anna is a drug addict, who shoots up midway through "Non mi dir." 

   In the original PepsiCo staging two years ago, the Don's declining state was graphic; he was made to fade into the background, leaving, essentially, an opera without its central character. 
Mr. Sellars's redirection of the production this year features a somewhat more assertive Giovanni in Eugene Perry. 
He has also introduced a confusion: Mr. Perry's twin brother, Herbert, sings Leporello, and the two are so physically and vocally alike that its hard to know who's the boss. 
Maybe that's the point. 
But so what? 
The twin casting is only one example of the murkiness of this show. 

   The darkness of Mr. Sellars's world is unrelieved. 
Long stretches of the opera are played in almost total blackness, a directorial conceit that has the audience working hard to figure out what's going on. 
Even more troubling is the premise it expresses: there is no possibility of redemption in this "Giovanni." 

   The Don's descent into hell is one of the production's better moments. 
The set cracks apart, and Giovanni is sucked into a manhole. 
But the final sextet has the characters stuck in their own hell as they peer out, naked, from fiery trap doors in the floor. 
Indeed, the unrelenting pessimism of Mr. Sellars's point of view effectively prevents emotional involvement on the part of the audience. 
How can you care about these people? 
They're all awful, and not even pitiable. 

   Mr. Sellars's "Cosi" is just as cruel. 
This sharp but ultimately tender comedy of human foibles has been transformed into a vicious, bludgeoning account of interpersonal sadism. 
The cartoony Act I, set in Despina's diner by the sea, is staged with mirror moves for the two couples and a lot of silly television-reference stage business (Despina waves a roll of paper towels, Bounty-style, at one point) to show the superficiality of the four lovers, and the malice and frustration of Alfonso and Despina. 

   In Act II, we are plunged into literal and figurative darkness again, and Mr. Sellars makes an especially radical break from Da Ponte: He postulates that the girls know about the trick that's being played on them and are getting even. 
No self-knowledge or forgiveness is possible under such circumstances; the working-out of the joke is just ugly and unresolvable. 

   Mr. Sellars takes less license with "Figaro," probably because it has fewer inherent ambiguities to exploit. 
He sets the action in a luxurious Trump Tower duplex, with Figaro and Susanna, chauffeur and maid, installed in the laundry room with a fold-out couch that occupies most of the space. 
Mr. Sellars reduces the opera to a parable about sexual jealousy and desire, but some of his scenes are right on the mark. 
The convergence of Cherubino, the Count and Basilio in Susanna's room in Act I, for example, is high comedy. 
Even more titillating is the spectacle of the Countess and Susanna undressing Cherubino and dressing him in the Countess's clothes in Act II: two adult women teasing an overheated adolescent. 

   But in Act III, Mr. Sellars takes the conceit too far. 
He has the Countess practically making out with Cherubino during the flower presentation scene. 
The Countess loses her dignity, and we lose the Countess. 
Cherubino, too, has lost his charm. 
Why would any woman want this gawky teen-ager toting a hockey stick and skates and accompanying "Non so piu" with pelvic thrusts? 

   This is typically excessive Sellars Mozart. 
In exploring the subconscious underpinnings of these scores, he reduces their message to evil, violence and depravity. 
In all three productions, characters are constantly throwing things (often food), slamming doors, beating each other, threatening each other with weapons (the Count pulls a pistol on the Countess in Act II of "Figaro"). 
The result is a depressing tearing apart of an 18th-century world in which manners counted and the unexpressed undercurrents were more powerful for being left that way. 
The sophomoric reliance on food fights leads one to suspect a directorial failure of invention, of which there are plenty of other examples, in addition to the frequent, alienating darkness. 
Why do the quick finales have to be staged as conga lines and cha-chas? 
Why do characters frequently lie down on the floor in the same elegant Mark Morris-style movement? 
And why do the arias usually go flat as theater, while the ensemble scenes are imbued with energy? 

   How do the singers justify all this? 
Clearly they are at one with the concepts. 
They navigate the music and complex staging with varying degrees of vocal beauty but they perform the Sellars business, complex as it is, with absolute precision. 

   Many of the singers work regularly with Mr. Sellars, and the best ones are impressive. 
The "Figaro" cast was the strongest, with Sanford Sylvan (Figaro), Jeanne Ommerle (Susanna), Jayne West (the Countess) and James Maddalena (the Count). 

   In the pit, Craig Smith took the same sorts of liberties with accents and tempi that Mr. Sellars took with the staging, though oddly enough he often smoothed out passages where an edge probably would have underscored the director's message better. 
The fact that audiences responded so positively to these productions is troubling. 
Perhaps people are so bored by standard productions of these familiar operas that any deviation, however perverse, is welcome, especially when it is so skillfully done. 
Perhaps the updating makes them seem more immediate, and the slickness of execution turns them into a kind of high class MTV, where the language barrier doesn't matter. 
But Mr. Sellars did not persuade me that Mozart is all about darkness and depravity, and the emotional barrenness of his productions is chilling. 
Opera has to be something more than an intellectual exercise. 
These particular pieces are humanist works about redemption, forgiveness and light as well as anger and despair. 
That is what makes them great. 

   ---

   Ms. Waleson writes frequently about music. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Canadian Firms
@  Plan 27.9% Rise
@  In Capital Outlays
@  ----
@  By John Urquhart
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> CANDA </CO>
<IN> ECONOMIC NEWS (ECO)
MONETARY NEWS, FOREIGN EXCHANGE, TRADE (MON) </IN>
<DATELINE> OTTAWA  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Canadian manufacturers, encouraged by the new U.S.-Canada free-trade pact, intend to boost their spending on plant and equipment by 27.9% this year, Statistics Canada, a federal agency, said. 

   The new projection is up from a March forecast of a 20.7% rise in 1989 capital outlays. 
Last year, capital spending by manufacturers increased 14.9%. 

   The latest spending estimates show that Canadian business is "responding to the realities of the free-trade agreement," said Mike McCracken, president of Informetrica Ltd., an economic-consulting firm. 

   The trade pact, which took effect Jan. 1, provides for gradual elimination of most trade barriers between the U.S. and Canada, including a phase-out of all tariffs by January 1998. 

   Roger Hamel, president of the Canadian Chamber of Commerce, said the prospect of the free-trade agreement was a "significant" factor in business planning for this year's capital spending. 

   Among manufacturers, producers of paper products, primary metals and chemicals are expected to show the biggest increase in 1989 outlays. 

   Other business sectors, except for the mining industry, also intend to increase capital outlays this year. 
Capital spending by the mining sector is expected to drop 11.6%, mainly because of an anticipated 15.1% drop in outlays by the oil industry. 

   Economists said Canadian capital outlays would have risen this year even without the free-trade agreement, but probably not as strongly. 
Some industries have been operating at close to capacity; these include primary metals, electrical and electronic products, primary textiles, and chemicals. 
Manufacturers generally have been operating at about 84% of capacity. 

   Another factor cited in the big planned increase in capital spending was the desire of business to take advantage of the tax system's accelerated write-off rates for manufacturing investments. 
These rates are to be reduced further in 1990 and 1991. 

   Capital-spending intentions have strengthened, despite Canada's high interest rates. 
Prime lending rates of Canadian banks have been at 13.5% since March, the highest level in five years. 

   Manufacturing, however, has held up fairly well. 
New orders rose 2% in May, partly offsetting a 4.6% decline in April, Statistics Canada said. 
Shipments rose 0.5% in May. 
The ratio of inventories to shipments was 1.55 to 1 in May, unchanged from April and up slightly from 1.54 to 1 a year earlier. 

   John Clinkard, senior economist at Canadian Imperial Bank of Commerce, said he doesn't expect any further capital-spending growth next year, because of what he predicts will be an erosion of corporate profits and a decline in capacity-utilization rates as the economy softens. 

   Capital spending by manufacturers is expected to rise to 22.53 billion Canadian dollars (US$18.97 billion) this year from C$17.62 billion last year, Statistics Canada said. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Money-Fund Yields
@  Fell Below 9% for First
@  Time Since February </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> MUTUAL AND MONEY-MARKET FUNDS (FND) </IN>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Money-fund yields have dropped below 9% for the first time since February. 

   The average seven-day compounded yield on the 381 taxable money-market mutual funds tracked by Donoghue's Money Fund Report slipped to 8.97% in the week ended Tuesday. 

   While that's slightly under the previous week's 9.03%, the move below 9% is apt to be at least psychologically important to many investors who had grown accustomed to double-digit yields just a few weeks ago. 

   Investors looking for higher rates elsewhere are almost certain to be disappointed. 
For example, the average six-month certificate of deposit is paying just 8.12%, according to Banxquote Money Markets. 
The average bank money-market account currently pays only 7.11%. 

   Susan Cook, editor of Donoghue's, a newsletter, says the latest drop in money-fund yields "was basically expected with the direction of all short-term interest rates." 

   Yields on money funds have been falling at a slower pace than those on some competing instruments partly because fund managers have been stuffing their portfolios with issues that have increasingly longer maturities. 
By extending the life of their portfolios in this way, they have managed to lock in the higher rates of previous weeks for some time into the future. 

   The average money fund maturity dipped a bit in the week ended Tuesday to 41 days, from 42, according to Donoghue's. 
But, "that should not be misconstrued as a change in money fund manager's sentiment," said Ms. Cook. 
She said fund managers determined that their lengthening the previous week was overzealous in the wake of announcements by the Federal Reserve. 
The Fed indicated interest rates would fall only gradually. 
Last week the maturity jumped to 42 from 40 days. 

   Reflecting the higher yields available to fund managers on investments like Treasury bills and commercial paper just weeks ago, the average 30-day compounded yield remained above 9% in the latest week. 
It slipped to 9.11%, from 9.18%, according to Donoghue's. 

   The money-fund asset level, which grew again this week, reflects investor inability to find comparable returns. 
Assets of the funds measured by Donoghue's grew $2.3 billion to $324 billion. 
Compound yields assume the reinvestment of dividends and that rates remain unchanged for a year. 
Without taking into account the effects of compounding, the average seven-day yield fell to 8.60%, from 8.66%; the 30-day yield dropped to 8.73% from 8.79%.

<HL> P&amp;G to Acquire
@  Fisher Nut Unit
@  Of Kohlberg Kravis
@  ----
@  By Alecia Swasy
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> PG </CO>
<IN> TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM)
FOOD PRODUCTS (FOD) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Procter &amp; Gamble Co., building on its fledgling snack food business, said it agreed to buy the Fisher Nut unit of Beatrice/Hunt-Wesson Inc. 

   Terms weren't disclosed, but it is understood the purchase price would approach $150 million. 

   The sale is subject to approval by the Federal Trade Commission. 
The agency required Beatrice's owner, Kohlberg Kravis Roberts &amp; Co., to sell certain businesses as part of an agreement allowing it to buy RJR Nabisco Inc. last fall. 
RJR Nabisco owns Planters, the leading brand with 50% of the salted-nut business. 

   The Cincinnati consumer products concern quietly had expressed interest in buying Planters, but settled for No. 2 Fisher, which has an estimated 11% of the salted-nut market. 

   The proposed purchase met with mixed reaction from analysts, largely because P&amp;G has a spotty record in the food business. 
"Once again, they're the David going after Goliath," said Lynne Hyman, analyst with First Boston Corp. 

   In potato chips, for instance, P&amp;G's Pringle's brand trails Frito-Lay's chips, while its Citrus Hill orange juice lags in third place behind Tropicana and Minute Maid. 

   But the purchase would offer some synergies for P&amp;G, which already buys peanuts for its Jif peanut butter. 
The Fisher unit, which has a variety of peanuts, cashews and other nuts, has annual sales of about $100 million, analysts said. 

   P&amp;G is expected to bolster the Fisher brand by increasing distribution of the products. 
Only half of the U.S. retail outlets now carry the Fisher line, analysts said. 

   P&amp;G's snack food business could also benefit should its fat substitute, olestra, get Food and Drug Administration approval. 
In a recent interview, John G. Smale, chairman and chief executive officer of P&amp;G, said he considered the fat substitute category to be a "multibillion-dollar market." 

   Mr. Smale wouldn't predict when the FDA will rule on P&amp;G's petition for approval of olestra, but he said P&amp;G scientists have "almost total confidence that approval will come." 
He added: "They are less willing to predict when it's going to come."
