<HL> In the War on Drugs, Keep the Troops in the Barracks
@  ----
@  By Jonathan Marshall </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> LATAM </CO>
<GV> EXECUTIVE (EXE)
CONGRESS (CNG) </GV>
<TEXT>
   America is in real danger of turning its metaphorical "war on drugs" into a bloody reality. 
With the failure of every other enforcement effort, from crop eradication through border interdiction to street-level policing, a growing chorus of influential Washington policy makers is urging the ultimate escalation -- sending U.S. troops to Latin America to battle traffickers. 

   The calls for military intervention are coming fast and furiously from Congress, where frustrated members from all points on the ideological spectrum share a conviction that only armed force will rid America of drugs. 
"We should engage in joint military and paramilitary operations, with congressional approval, including helicopter and air strikes on cocaine fields," Sen. John Kerry (D., Mass.) told a Tufts University audience this year. 
Defending a similar proposal, Rep. Charles Rangel (D., N.Y.), chairman of the House narcotics committee, said, "I have a deeper commitment to fighting the drug traffickers than I did the North Koreans." 
Sen. William Cohen (R., Me.) has argued that the only solution is to "go to the source," even if that means "taking out the machine-gun nest" with troops. 

   Key officials in the Bush administration favor a similar approach. 
Drug czar William Bennett privately advocates the use of special operations forces to hit foreign drug labs and shipment centers. 
A National Security Council task force reportedly will recommend to President Bush that he greatly step up the military's role in fighting drugs overseas, including a possible combat role. 
Mr. Bush will be receptive: He told the Veterans of Foreign Wars in March that drugs represent "a threat no less real than the adversaries you have battled" and vowed to "combat drug abuse with . . . our nation's armed forces." 

   The major source of bureaucratic resistance to this groundswell is the Pentagon itself, which doubts whether the drug war is winnable and fears becoming embroiled in an endless, ill-defined commitment that detracts from its traditional mission. 

   The military has good reason to resist this call to arms. 
Given how many countries produce drugs, including the U.S. itself, the chances of winning are small compared to the likelihood of tearing apart the social and political fabric of countries in which the military intervenes. 

   The first problem for U.S. anti-drug expeditionary forces will be defining the enemy. 
Simply shooting a few known kingpins in Medellin won't dry up the supply of either drugs or criminal entrepreneurs willing to run them across borders. 
Peasant growers and traffickers don't wear uniforms. 
Nor do they belong to a single, defined organization that can be forced to surrender. 
"There is a perfect textbook example of guerrilla warfare," Michael Perez, a Drug Enforcement Agency agent stationed in Bolivia, was quoted as saying earlier this year. 
"You never know who's who -- who's a good guy and who's a bad guy." 

   U.S. troops would have to treat entire sectors of Latin American society as potential foes. 
More than half a million Bolivians live off the profits of coca and cocaine production. 
Tens of thousands of peasant cultivators are tightly organized into militant unions with powerful links to national worker organizations. 
Their roadblocks, mass demonstrations, strikes and other nonviolent tactics have paralyzed government efforts to eradicate coca planting. 
Trying to conquer them with force would be no easy feat. 

   It would be all the harder since U.S. troops wouldn't know who their friends are, either. 
Corruption is so endemic in the drug-supplier nations that anyone up to presidents and army chiefs of staff can be on the traffickers' payroll. 
In Colombia, according to a recent Bogota television news report, traffickers appear to "have access to practically all confidential government information, such as minutes of cabinet meetings." 
The corruption ensures that the kingpins enjoy protection even as they use the local police and military to knock off upstart competitors. 

   Colombia, at least, has never been taken over entirely by the traffickers. 
Bolivia was in 1980, during the famous "cocaine coup." 
The situation is not much better now that the corrupt generals and colonels no longer rule. 
Said a former American adviser to the country's narcotics police to an Atlantic Monthly writer, "I have to tell you I think that 100% of the Bolivian enforcement structure was corrupted." 
After the U.S. sent troops to Bolivia in 1986 -- the model for current interventionist proposals -- a Bolivian congressional investigation accused the interior minister who oversaw the whole operation of taking payoffs to protect the major drug lords. 

   Peru is no better. 
A recent staff report by the House Committee on Foreign Affairs observes that "corruption in all segments of the Peruvian government continues to impede meaningful anti-narcotics efforts." 

   Even honest segments of these societies loudly protest the introduction of U.S. forces in the drug war. 
The Latin allergy to anything smacking of "Yankee imperialism" ties the hands of even the most sympathetic leaders. 
The one exception to that rule -- the 1986 U.S. anti-drug raid in Bolivia -- damaged the otherwise-high popularity of President Victor Paz Estensoro's government. 
Today, Bolivian leaders state emphatically they will never permit such an operation again. 

   Latin leaders know that escalating the drug war risks not only their political future, but their nation's survival. 
In Peru, the army frankly confesses the reasons for its reluctance to take on the traffickers at the same time as it fights the fanatical Shining Path guerrillas. 
It needs popular support to fight terrorism -- and it can't retain popular support if it tries to eradicate coca. 

   Unlike traditional counter-insurgency, which relies on winning the loyalty of peasants and isolating radical guerrilla forces, a military campaign against the peasant economy in the Andean states could unite the entire rural population against the intruders. 
"It would create another Vietnam or another Central America," says Edmundo Morales, author of "Cocaine: The White Gold Rush in Peru." 
Already, adds Peruvian journalist Gustavo Gorriti, "the Shining Path guerrillas have been able to maneuver both the Peruvian government and the Americans into doing exactly what they wanted. 
They have made the fight against eradication and punitive raids from police and helicopter-borne units piloted by Americans the cause to unite most of {the peasants}. 
Any kind of escalation of the current commitment will play nicely into their hands." 

   The Shining Path, although not yet successful at penetrating Bolivia, maintains strongholds on the border between the two nations. 
Its proximity may fuel concerns of the Bolivian government, expressed in a 1986 study, that the response of traffickers to military attack might result in "the emergence of a new and deadly guerrilla movement joining extremists, destitute peasants and drug traffickers." 

   Some proponents of military intervention ignore this danger by assuming traffickers, guerrilla insurgents and terrorists are one and the same -- "narco-guerrillas" or "narco-terrorists" -- despite their vastly different social and political agendas. 
Groups like the Shining Path derive some income from drug taxes but ultimately aim to impose a puritanical communist regime; most traffickers have no problem with democracy or capitalism as long as they are left alone. 
In Colombia as well as Peru, traffickers and guerrillas have waged bloody vendettas for control of territory. 
Yet, a foreign invasion that targets them both is likely to bring them together. 

   As long as U.S. demand for drugs swamps Latin American societies with narco-dollars, military intervention will succeed no better than any of the other failed strategies that have attempted to eliminate the supply at its source. 
Unlike them, however, it will risk substantial numbers of American lives abroad on an open-ended, exceedingly dangerous mission. 
Worse, it promises to inflame urban nationalism and peasant radicalism, social forces that together could destroy the fragile governments of any cooperating countries. 
So far no South American country has volunteered to commit suicide for the sake of the U.S.'s frustrated drug warriors. 
North Americans should be glad they haven't. 

   ---

   Mr. Marshall is editorial page editor of the Tribune in Oakland, Calif. 
He wrote a chapter on foreign policy in "Dealing With Drugs" (Pacific Research Institute, '87). 

</TEXT>

<HL> Breakfast Is Fine, but We Prefer
@  To Skip the Music at That Hour
@  ----
@  By Scott McMurray
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<TEXT>
   Greg LeMond doesn't know what he's missing. 

   Sure, America's ace bicyclist won the Tour de France on Sunday and got to pedal along the Champs Elysees as thousands cheered. 
But he's never had an Iowan in a tuxedo serve him champagne sherbet and Belgian waffles for breakfast, has he? 

   That treat, as it were, is reserved for 300 ticket-buying bike riders converging on the small but cultured town of Randall, Iowa, beginning at 5 this morning. 

   For $6 a head, partakers will dine alfresco in the town park to the sounds of a 1916 Steinway &amp; Sons grand piano. 
Free valet bike parking will be provided. 

   The affair celebrates the passing by of the unofficial Tour de Iowa, a cross-state bike ride sponsored by the Des Moines Register. 
The Register's Annual Great Bicycle Ride Across Iowa, or Ragbrai as it's called, attracts thousands and is in its 17th year -- but has never before come through Randall. 

   Behind the breakfast spectacle is a group that bills itself as the smallest arts council in America. 
"When we found out they were coming through, we knew we had to do something special to make them stop," says Ellis Anderson, a Randall resident, clothing-store operator, singing teacher and arts-council member. 
The council opted for breakfast since one campsite for the week-long ride is about four miles away. 

   But nothing is simple, not even in a town of 170. 
"We wanted to serve champagne, but the town found out we couldn't get a liquor license for insurance reasons. 
So we made {champagne} sherbet. 
That's food, not liquor," says Robert McNutt, the local piano tuner and arts-council impresario. 
Mr. McNutt, who is providing the piano for the breakfast, established the arts group after migrating to Randall two years ago from Minneapolis with his piano-tuning partner Joel Yushta. 

   Not surprisingly, piano recitals are a mainstay at Randall Arts Council fetes. 
A 12-handed rendition (using three pianos and six pianists) of "Stars and Stripes Forever" two years ago has already passed into local legend, Mr. McNutt boasts. 

   He isn't worried that bicyclists who get a kick from champagne, sherbet or no, may prove a traffic hazard. 
With each breakfast, he says, "we're offering a choice of Gatorade, water or coffee." 

</TEXT>
</DOC>
<DOC>
<DOCNO> WSJ890727-0119 </DOCNO>
<DD> = 890727 </DD>
<AN> 890727-0119. </AN>
<HL> Viewpoint:
@  Shamir, Sharon, What's the Difference?
@  ----
@  By Alexander Cockburn </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> MDEST </CO>
<GV> EXECUTIVE (EXE)
CONGRESS (CNG) </GV>
<TEXT>
   "All politicians have to have an active Devil on the string," my father once wrote. 
Israel's Shamir keeps two. 
On one side the PLO, on the other Ariel Sharon; he calls them up as required to bolster his own support, extract some concession from opponents or merely pursue his usual business without serious contradiction. 

   Now and for the past month the devil being jerked hardest at the end of its string is Ariel Sharon, bloody-handed general and aspirant to Israel's Defense Ministry. 
On July 5, Mr. Sharon and two cronies in Likud pressured Mr. Shamir to announce pre-conditions for his elections plan: no participation by the Arabs of East Jerusalem, no negotiations until the intifada was suppressed, no halt to Jewish settlement on the West Bank, no concession of territory and no possibility of agreeing to a Palestinian state -- i.e., no change in the present situation at all and no reason for any Palestinian to go along with it. 
Barely had Mr. Shamir endorsed these conditions at the Likud Party conference than a chorus arose bewailing the bind Mr. Sharon had put him in. 

   This is an essential stage in the effective deployment of the devil. 
People who might otherwise be critical or at least cautious rally round, realizing that any wavering in a life and death struggle with sin will give aid and comfort to the Evil One. 
Israel's Labor Party embraced the original elections proposal, saying that unless it was reaffirmed "without conditions" the coalition government in which it is partner with Likud would fall. 
Secretary of State James Baker reiterated the Bush administration's absolute commitment to the plan. 

   The U.S. corporate press, always bowed in reverence to the official Israeli line, voiced its deepest concern over the "crisis" in Israel with impressive unanimity. 
On July 6 the headline in The Wall Street Journal read "Shamir Puts Israel Coalition at Risk to Appease Hard-Liners." 
The New York Times wrote "Shamir Accepts Hard-Line Moves That Imperil Arab Elections Plan." 

   For weeks the press carried on like this, until the other day when the Israeli cabinet voted almost as one to endorse its original election plan and everyone breathed a sigh of relief that indeed Good had triumphed over Evil. 
"It seems likely," wrote the New York Times's Joel Brinkley, "that Mr. Shamir, as an individual, will negotiate with the Likud conditions in mind while at the same time arguing that the Government as a whole is not bound by them. 
It is just the sort of careful balancing act Mr. Shamir has mastered in the years he has been caught between right-wing political rivals in his own party and left-wing coalition partners -- all of whom have the power to bring down the Government." 

   Mr. Shamir's journey from being a leader of the Stern terrorist gang that in 1941 proposed a treaty with the Nazis and later murdered Lord Moyne and Count Bernadotte to racist provocateur and hardline politician and now to "centrist" is a case study in the manufacture of political acceptability. 
The remarkable thing is that the press continues to adjust the picture while Mr. Shamir himself straight-forwardly displays the despicable views he has always maintained. 
At the time that Mr. Shamir embraced Mr. Sharon's conditions Mr. Brinkley and his fellow U.S. correspondents in Jerusalem reported that he had said, "What is new in this? 
We've said all these things before." 
And indeed when Mr. Shamir first suggested elections back in April, he also pledged to cede "not an inch of territory." 

   But what does the original election plan actually say? 
While proposing negotiations for Palestinian autonomy followed by further talks to "determine the final status of the West Bank and Gaza Strip," it is also loaded with conditions. 
The third of its 20 points lays down "basic guidelines": "Israel opposes the creation of an additional Palestinian state in the Gaza district and in the area between Israel and Jordan. 
Israel will not negotiate with the PLO. 
There will be no change in the status of Judea, Samaria and the Gaza district, unless in accord with the basic program of the government." 

   The question of Jewish settlement, the vote for East Jerusalem and the explicit suppression of the intifada are left in the hazy realm of "details" to be worked out at some undetermined later time, but the premises are clear. 
As Yehoshafat Harkabi, former chief of Israeli military intelligence, wrote in the Los Angeles Times for July 13, "The difference between Shamir and Sharon is only one of style and tactics, not of substance." 

   So what can be expected now that Labor has legitimized as reasonable the utterly unreasonable, and the Bush administration and virtually the whole of mainstream journalistic opinion are proffering variations on the Anti-Defamation League's line that "Israel's willingness to allow all options to be put on the table . . . demonstrates a real readiness to take risks for peace"? 

   Devil Sharon has served his purpose, but a presentiment of what lies ahead came just as the string on which he played had begun to go slack. 
On July 17, Mr. Sharon declared that Israel must "eliminate the heads of the terrorist organizations, first of all Arafat." 
The next day's New York Post had a vast headline "Arafat Must Die]" Other, "serious" papers ran tactful boxes or short items reporting his recommendation. 

   The New York Times, however, inserted Mr. Sharon's death threat in the third paragraph of a story by Mr. Brinkley titled "Israel Asserts Threats by P.L.O. Imperil Bid to Revive Peace Plan." 
The story began: "Even as Israel tries to revive its initiative for Palestinian elections, the Foreign Ministry asserted today that the plan is likely to fail in any case because Palestinians are threatening to kill any Arab who dares to discuss the idea with Israeli officials." 
The string is released; the hand readies to jerk forth its second, more familiar, demon. 
How long will it be before the chorus that rose to defend the "moderate" Shamir, the "courageous" Shamir, against the bully Sharon joins to condemn the PLO for "intrasigence" and finally "rejectionism"? 
Already Foreign Minister Moshe Arens and other spokesmen for Mr. Shamir are increasing pressure on the U.S. to break off its talks with the PLO. 

   In this country, Sens. 
George Mitchell (D., Maine) and Bob Dole (R., Kan.) have engineered the passage of legislation prohibiting the U.S. from talking further with Abu Iyad or any other Palestinian whom the president may document as having participated in attacks upon American citizens. 
Last week The Wall Street Journal carried an essay by Irving Kristol saying "It is perhaps just as well . . . that there is no possibility of a negotiated Israeli-Palestinian peace settlement," and broaching the idea that Israel should decide which slice of the West Bank it would like, annex that territory but leave the rest, and then "cope with West Bank turmoil as it copes with the turmoil in Lebanon." 
Mr. Sharon might then be given another chance to deflect attention from the casual killing committed every day by Mr. Shamir and his fellows. 

   ---

   Mr. Cockburn is a columnist for The Nation and the LA Weekly. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Speaking of Business:
@  Japan's Sinking Unions
@  ----
@  By Lindley H. Clark Jr. </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> JAPAN LABOR </CO>
<TEXT>
   As the Japanese economy has boomed since World War II, one of its assets has been super-smooth labor relations. 
Workers toiled long hours, expecting -- and getting -- only modest pay increases. 
Labor unions existed and weren't totally ineffectual, but by and large they cooperated with management. 

   The times are changing. 

   Japanese labor leaders say workers simply aren't interested in working as hard as their fathers and mothers did. 
There is a growing feeling among workers that they aren't getting a fair share in the nation's prosperity. 

   You might think this would make the workers fair game for union organizers, but it isn't so. 
Since 1975, the unions' share of the work force has fallen from 35% to 28%. 
Among the major industrial economies, only the U.S. is de-unionizing faster: the portion of the U.S. work force in unions has fallen from 25% to 15% over the same period. 

   A pair of National Bureau of Economic Research studies indicates that reasons for the union decline in the two countries are similar. 
Richard B. Freeman and Marcus E. Rebick consider Japan (Working Paper No. 2963), while Henry S. Farber of the Massachusetts Institute of Technology looks at the U.S. (Working Paper No. 2857). 

   In both countries a prime factor seems to be that workers no longer believe that unions will solve any problems the workers may have. 
In both nations, too, one reason for the unions' perceived ineffectiveness is stronger resistance from management. 
Both studies say, moreover, that management resistance has grown in an administrative and legal atmosphere considered to be supportive of management. 

   It's not altogether surprising that the union experience in the two countries has been similar. 
Unions, after all, were more or less an American gift to Japan. 
After World War II, the American occupation forces seemed determined to Americanize Japan, and unions may have been a predictable part of the process. 

   With U.S. encouragement, the Japanese constitution was amended to guarantee workers "the right to organize and to bargain and act collectively." 
The idea caught on, and the early growth of unions was smooth. 
There were special Japanese touches. 
Nearly all of the unionized workers were in "enterprise" unions, which represented the work force of a single company. 
One of the main features of such unions was "lifetime" employment. 
It didn't really mean lifetime; workers were expected to leave at age 55. 
But employers didn't lay off workers, either permanently or temporarily, and workers were expected to stay with the company, even if another firm offered fatter paychecks. 

   All went well for years. 
The booming Japanese economy provided jobs for just about everyone who wanted one. 
Unions didn't have much to do with it, although they may have taken credit for it. 

   The turning point was the big oil price increase initiated by the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries in the early 1970s. 
Japan, totally dependent on imported oil, faced huge increases in energy costs. 
At the same time, Japan was running into growing competition from low-cost producers such as Taiwan, Hong Kong, Korea and Singapore. 
There was a growing world surplus of output capacity in industries that had been important to Japanese growth, such as steel. 
Even U.S. manufacturers were modernizing and becoming more competitive. 
In mid-1985 the yen began a steep rise that made Japanese exports less competitive. 

   Labor relations became less pleasant. 
The oil price increase triggered a burst of inflation, and companies granted big pay boosts. 
But workers felt the boosts were insufficient. 
The "spring offensives," during which unions and management settled pay increases, became more combative. 

   With profits under pressure, it was hardly surprising that management became more aggressive in resisting unions. 
The legal environment changed in ways that made it more difficult for unions to organize new companies. 
Court decisions held that some union actions on the employer's premises or during working hours violated employer property rights. 

   In part because of management opposition, workers began to show less interest in unions. 
The Japanese unemployment rate, which had been almost invisible, began creeping up, and "lifetime" employment began to mean even less. 
Manufacturers began shifting production to Taiwan, Korea, even the U.S. Apparently some workers felt this was no time to make waves by plunging into union organizing. 

   A major handicap for the Japanese unions has been the enterprise setup: one company, one union. 
This made it difficult to launch organizing drives at new, nonunion companies. 
To deal with this problem, Japanese unions have stepped up merger efforts but the outcome is uncertain. 

   Another handicap for Japanese unions has been their long (and mutual) dislike for the ruling Liberal Democratic Party. 
Last Sunday's smashing LDP defeat may eventually lead to political benefits for the unions, but it's too early to tell. 
Before the election, one government survey indicated that the union share of the work force would fall to 14% by the year 2000. 

   In the U.S., Mr. Farber says, the unions need nothing less than a sweeping change of public attitudes. 
The unions, he says, have called for "reform of the National Labor Relations Act to provide an environment where current employer practices to discourage union organizing will be less effective. 
However, until our society as a whole is more favorably disposed toward unions, such reform will be difficult to achieve." 

   Both NBER studies stress that the key problem for the unions is winning over the workers themselves. 
"One recurring theme in the debate over the future of unions in the private sector is that the competitiveness of the economy has increased dramatically and that labor unions may be less viable in such an economic environment," Mr. Farber concludes. 
"Unions need to convince workers that they offer real value in the modern competitive economy." 

</TEXT>

<HL> OTC Focus:
@  Nasdaq Composite Increases 2.65 Points
@  On Late Buying Spree on the Big Board
@  ----
@  By Sonja Steptoe
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> STOCK MARKET, OFFERINGS (STK)
STOCK INDEXES (NDX) </IN>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The late-afternoon buying spree that boosted the Dow Jones Industrial Average also lifted Nasdaq over-the-counter stocks, as the Nasdaq Composite Index rose 2.65, or 0.6%, to 449.12. 

   Patrick Ryan, manager of OTC trading at Johnston, Lemon &amp; Co. in Washington, said the buying came from professional traders and market makers trading for their own accounts and was a response to the 1.2% jump in the industrial average on the New York Stock Exchange, most of which came in the final half-hour of trading. 

   If institutional investors decide to get in on the action and buy OTC stocks today, Mr. Ryan said, the Nasdaq market could advance without further prompting from the Big Board. 

   "There seems to be optimism, but it all happened in the last hour," Mr. Ryan said. 
"We'll have to see what happens" today, he said. 

   Lance Zipper, head of OTC trading at Kidder, Peabody &amp; Co., said investor reaction to this morning's government report on second-quarter gross national product growth also will be important. 
Yesterday's gain was "the first bright spot we've had in a while," he said. 
"But it doesn't mean anything until the GNP comes out." 

   Volume on Nasdaq remained lackluster as only 124.5 million shares changed hands. 
Of the 4,401 issues that traded, 1,124 advanced and 829 declined. 

   The barometers of the biggest OTC issues both were up, as the Nasdaq 100 Index of nonfinancial stocks gained 3.76 to 419.79 while the Nasdaq Financial Index rose 1.46 to 440.90. 

   Exchange Bancorp jumped 4 1/8 to 21 7/8 after Algemene Bank Nederland said its LaSalle National unit agreed in principle to acquire the Chicago commercial bank holding company for $24 a share, or $420 million. 

   American depositary receipts of Volvo rose 2 5/8 to 75 3/8. 
Volvo Concessionaires, a United Kingdom importer and distributor of the Swedish-made automobiles, plans to offer catalytic converters as a no-cost option on all Volvo models beginning next year, the London-based Financial Times reported. 

   Cerner rose 1 to 13 3/4. 
Several analysts made positive comments about the developer of clinical information systems, Dow Jones Professional Investor Report said. 

   While many OTC technology stocks rebounded from sell-offs earlier in the week, Apple Computer lost 1/2 to 38 1/4. 

   Microsoft, which won a favorable ruling in a copyright suit brought by Apple, gained 3/4 to 52 3/4. 
Ashton-Tate rose 1/2 to 15. 
Software Publishing gained 5/8 to 21 1/8. 

   MCI Communications rose 1 1/8 to 38 1/8. 
Standard &amp; Poor's upgraded MCI's subordinated debt rating late Tuesday. 

   Imperial Holly dropped 1 3/4 to 28 1/2, apparently on profit-taking. 
The company reported first-quarter net income of $4.1 million, compared with a year-earlier loss of $882,000. 

   TCI International tumbled 1 5/8 to 8 5/8 after reporting that its third-quarter earnings slipped to 10 cents a share from 44 cents a share a year earlier. 

   Separately, the Securities and Exchange Commission said it suspended trading for 10 days in securities of Lifeline Healthcare Group, beginning yesterday. 

   The commission said it acted because of questions about recent market activity in the securities and about the adequacy and accuracy of several kinds of information about the company. 

   In April, the Broken Arrow, Okla., company reported net income for 1988 of $717,564, or 63 cents a share, on revenue of $2.1 million. 
In May, it paid a 1-for-1 stock dividend to shareholders other than its officers and directors. 
The move was aimed at thwarting what Lifeline Healthcare said was abusive short selling of its stock. 
Short sellers bet that a stock's price will fall by borrowing shares and immediately selling them. 
Their aim is to profit by buying replacement shares later at a lower price. 

   A spokesman for the company said officials welcome the investigation and hope regulators can "get to the bottom" of the irregularities in the trading of the company's stock.

<HL> Bethlehem Steel
@  Restores Payout
@  As Profit Drops
@  ---
@  Second-Period Net Fell 44%;
@  Results Mixed for Nucor
@  And Allegheny Ludlum
@  ----
@  By Clare Ansberry
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> BS NUE ALS </CO>
<IN> EARNINGS (ERN)
MINING, METALS (NON-PRECIOUS) (MIN)
DIVIDENDS (DIV) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Bethlehem Steel Corp., despite a 44% drop in second-quarter earnings, reinstated its quarterly dividend after a four-year lapse, citing a bolstered balance sheet. 

   Bethlehem, the nation's second-largest steel producer, said quarter earnings fell to $84 million from $151 million. 
The decline reflected hefty charges to restructure its BethForge operations, high steel-production costs, and the industry's stellar performance in the year-ago period. 

   Separately, two smaller steelmakers reported mixed second-quarter results. 
While specialty steelmaker Allegheny Ludlum Corp. reported a 40% rise in profit, mini-mill Nucor Corp. saw its profit drop 11% because of start-up costs at a new high-tech mill. 

   Bethlehem directors reinstated the 10-cents-a-share common stock dividend the company had been paying until September 1985. 
Walter F. Williams, chairman and chief executive officer, cited "the progress we have made in rebuilding our financial strength." 
The dividend is payable Sept. 10 to holders of record Aug. 10. 

   Wall Street has been expecting the dividend restoration since watching the company improve its balance sheet during the past few years and begin making sizable payments to its underfunded pension-plan fund. 
Some analysts also raised the dividend issue following a contract settlement with the United Steelworkers union, which restored wage sacrifices made in 1986 and boosted wages by $1.50 an hour during the life of the 50-month pact. 

   "To make restorations to labor without giving a penny in dividends to shareholders would have been inappropriate," said John Tumazos, an analyst with Donaldson, Lufkin &amp; Jenrette Securities Corp. 

   Bethlehem's basic steel operations continue to be plagued by higher production costs. 
Moreover, costs for raw materials such as alloys continued to rise in the quarter -- although at a slower rate than that of the first quarter -- at the same time the company saw its product mix shift to more semifinished steel, which has lower profit margins. 

   Operating profit in that segment dropped to $147 million from $166 million and affected all operations, including the large, modern Burns Harbor and Sparrows Point plants. 

   Mr. Williams said orders declined in the second quarter from strong first-quarter levels as a result of customer inventory adjustments, seasonal factors and an easing of demand from the automotive industry. 
He anticipates second-half shipments to fall from first-half levels. 
Total domestic industry shipments, he predicted, would fall 3.6% in 1989 to 81 million tons from 84 million in 1988. 

   Still, notes Michelle Galanter Applebaum, an analyst with Salomon Brothers Inc., Bethlehem's profits per ton of steel shipped rose to $58 a ton in the latest quarter from $52 a ton in the first period. 
That was lower, though, than last year's second-quarter level of $63 a ton, when the industry didn't have the high raw material costs. 

   At specialty steelmaker Allegheny Ludlum, net surged to $36.7 million, or $1.63 a share, from $27.3 million, or $1.16 a share. 
Sales rose to $333.8 million from $318.2 million a year ago. 
Although demand for stainless steel remains strong, Allegheny Ludlum noted that an uncertain economy, as well as the decline in raw-material prices for some products, has prompted steel service centers and other users to postpone purchases in anticipation of lower prices. 
The company forecast lower third-quarter earnings. 

   At Nucor Corp., earnings dropped to $32.2 million, or $1.51 a share, from $36 million, or $1.70 a share, a year ago in spite of higher sales. 
Sales rose 24.7% to $646.1 million from $518.1 million. 
The mini-mill said earnings were reduced by more than 25 cents a share in the quarter because of higher pre-operating and startup costs of new facilities.

<HL> Northeast Utilities Is Planning to Make
@  Improved Offer for PSNH, Sources Say
@  ----
@  By Lawrence Ingrassia
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> PNH NU </CO>
<IN> UTILITIES (UTI)
NUCLEAR POWER, FUEL (NUK)
BANKRUPTCIES (BCY)
TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Northeast Utilities plans to make an improved offer to acquire Public Service Co. of New Hampshire, a move that could end the stalemate over the New Hampshire company's bankruptcy-law proceedings, according to sources familiar with the case. 

   Northeast, which is expected to unveil the new proposal soon, has disclosed details of the plan to creditors and shareholders in hopes of winning support. 

   It isn't clear, however, if enough parties will go along with the Northeast plan for it to succeed. 
Moreover, negotiations over alternative plans -- including an internal reorganization -- are continuing. 
"Negotiations are at a critical juncture," said a person close to the Chapter 11 bankruptcy reorganization proceedings. 

   Parties that have been briefed by Northeast said that the Hartford, Conn.-based utility will offer $1.4 billion to $1.6 billion in cash plus around $400 million in preferred stock for all of PS of New Hampshire, including its 35.6% stake in the Seabrook, N.H., nuclear power plant. 

   Northeast earlier proposed paying $1.2 billion for the operating assets of PS of New Hampshire and spinning off Seabrook to unsecured creditors and shareholders, and then acquiring Seabrook power -- if and when it begins operating -- in a transaction it valued at about $800 million. 

   Although the initial proposal had roughly the same dollar value as the new offer, investment bankers valued the first proposal at only about $1.3 billion because it involved far less cash and the value of the power-purchase contract was questionable. 
Thus, the latest offer is considered better. 
"This {latest offer} is cash rich," said one PS of New Hampshire creditor, who asked not to be identified. 
"When you dangle cash dollars in front of investors, they're more interested." 

   A spokesman for Northeast declined to comment on whether the company plans to make a new offering, saying only that "it's no secret that we've been involved in negotiations." 

   New England Electric System, based in Westboro, Mass., also has made an offer to acquire certain PS of New Hampshire assets in a package that it values at $2.35 billion, but that some investment bankers value at around $1.5 billion. 

   Northeast's latest proposal would require rate increases of 5.5% a year for five years, said one person familiar with the talks. 
That is in the range of rate increases that state of New Hampshire officials have indicated they would consider reasonable. 

   Sources said that Paul Gioia, an examiner in the case appointed by the court to try to speed negotiations, also is conducting talks with various parties over an internal reorganization plan that would involve annual rate increases of 5.9% for three years and up to 5.5% annually for an additional five years. 

   While an internal reorganization with those rate increases would place a higher value on the company, the state has balked at those increases as being too high. 
Because the state's objections could delay court approval of a reorganization plan, creditors may go along with the Northeast plan in order to resolve the case and get their money as soon as possible. 

   Equity holders, however, are expected to negotiate for a better offer. 
With creditors owed about $1.72 billion by PS of New Hampshire, a purchase of the company for $2 billion would leave less than $300 million for shareholders. 
"I think it needs to be a substantially higher deal . . . well north of $2 billion," said Wilbur Ross Jr., of Rothschild Inc., financial adviser to the equity committee. 

   One reason for the flurry of negotiations is that Mr. Gioia must report on the progress in the case next Monday. 
PS of New Hampshire filed for Chapter 11 in January 1988. 
The company's problem stem from the heavy debt it took on to help build Seabrook, which is completed but hasn't started operations because of controversy over its safety evacuation plan. 
Under Chapter 11, a company is protected from creditors' lawsuits while it works out a plan to repay its debt. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Hertz Is Raising Rates for Both Business, Leisure Rentals
@  ----
@  By Melinda Grenier Guiles
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> F VOLVY </CO>
<IN> FINANCIAL, ACCOUNTING, LEASING (FIN) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Higher prices for new cars this fall are translating into higher rental rates for Hertz Corp.'s leisure and commercial customers, and probably for patrons of other rental firms as well. 

   The No. 1 rental company said it expects to raise rates across the board between 6% and 7% on Sept. 1. 
The increases would mean an average $3 to $4 more a day for commercial customers and $10 to $15 more a week for leisure renters. 
A Ford Tempo that now rents for $170 a week, for example, would cost $180 to $190 after the changes. 

   "We haven't had this kind of an increase in at least five years," said Craig R. Koch, president of Hertz's North America Rent A Car Division. 
"Our industry is just badly behind in passing through inflation" represented by higher costs for cars, licenses and titles, insurance, wages, facilities and advertising. 

   Other rental-car companies said they are studying the Hertz move closely. 
"We applaud their bravado," said Terry Redmond, executive vice president of National Car Rental System Inc. of Minneapolis. 
"We hope they stick with it. 
If anything can help and we can get our prices to better reflect our costs, we're happy." 

   That sentiment was echoed at Budget Rent a Car Corp. 
"We believe this may signal an industry trend," said Robert A. Wilson, vice president of marketing planning. 
"There is obviously a need for more revenue for the industry and unfortunately we're going to have to get it from our customers." 

   Closely held Hertz said the expectation of sharply higher new-car prices this fall was a major factor in its decision. 
Ford Motor Co. and Chrysler Corp. have told their dealers that prices on most 1990 models could go up as much as 7% to 9% from 1989 levels, largely because of new safety equipment required by federal law. 
"Car-cost increases of that magnitude simply cannot go unaccounted for," Mr. Koch said, adding that Hertz didn't raise its overall rates last fall when car prices increased. 

   But competitors pointed to other reasons why Hertz might need to jack up rates. 
Industry sources said the firm has lost some 10 points in market share over the past five years. 
In addition, fewer people flew domestic airlines during the first half of 1989 than a year earlier, and Hertz depends heavily on airport customers. 

   Still, Hertz isn't the only company feeling the pinch. 
In recent weeks, a number of rental concerns have raised prices to vacationers by about 5% to 10%. 
In some cities, increases were higher. 

   Business travelers were unaffected by these changes, but they aren't being left out in what is developing into a new round of increases. 
National's Mr. Redmond said it raised the guidelines by which its sales staff sells commercial rates by $4 a day effective Aug. 1. 

   Commercial rates go up as corporate contracts are renewed. 

   Moves such as this could be "extremely significant" for everyone concerned, said Charles H. Finnie, an analyst with Alex. 
Brown &amp; Sons Inc., an investment banking firm in Baltimore. 
There is tremendous pressure to keep down commercial rates, he says, adding that "companies have been willing to squeeze margins to almost nothing to get that business." 

</TEXT>

<HL> Economy:
@  Fed Again Eases Its Credit Policy,
@  Confirming Lower Trend for Rates
@  ---
@  Latest Move Is Second Step
@  of Strategy Authorized
@  By Panel Earlier in July
@  ----
@  By Alan Murray
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> FINANCIAL, ACCOUNTING, LEASING (FIN)
BANKS (BNK)
ECONOMIC NEWS (ECO) </IN>
<GV> FEDERAL RESERVE BOARD (FED) </GV>
<DATELINE> WASHINGTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Federal Reserve has eased its grip on credit another notch, providing further confirmation that interest rates are headed down. 

   The latest move is the second part of a two-step action authorized by the central bank's policy committee at its meeting earlier this month. 
The Fed's move is likely to result in lower rates on bank loans and other consumer interest rates. 

   The Fed's action became apparent when, for the second day in a row, the central bank took no action to prevent a decline in the key federal funds interest rate. 
The move is expected to reduce the funds rate to about 9%, from its recent level of 9 1/4%. 
That, in turn, could encourage banks to reduce their prime lending rate from the current 10 1/2% to 11% range. 

   Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan made it clear in recent congressional testimony that the Fed is worried about the risk that the economy may be falling into recession. 
Fed Gov. Martha Seger underscored that risk in an interview yesterday. 

   "I see weakness in two industries -- housing and autos -- that's even greater than I expected," Ms. Seger said. 
"And I think that's going to slosh over into other areas of the economy." 

   Most Fed officials are less pessimistic than Ms. Seger, and say they think a downturn is unlikely. 
But they say they believe the risks of recession have increased, and favor a cautious easing of the Fed's credit stance. 
"I think the balance has shifted over the last two to three months to a greater risk of recession," said Roger Guffey, president of the Kansas City Federal Reserve Bank in an interview earlier this week. 
"But that has to be taken in context. 
Before . . . the last couple of months, inflation was really the risk." 

   The fed funds rate, which in recent years has been the Fed's principal policy target, is the rate banks charge on loans to each other. 
The Fed can largely determine that rate by buying or selling government securities to financial institutions. 

   The funds rate has dropped three-quarters of a percentage point since the Fed began easing credit in early June. 
The central bank has been reluctant to move more quickly, fearing that the economy's slowdown will prove to be temporary, and that inflation will begin to accelerate. 

   Bush administration officials have been urging a more rapid reduction in interest rates. 
But the Fed is likely to continue to move slowly and cautiously unless it sees new signs of a sharp slowdown. 

   Nevertheless, many analysts believe the Fed will eventually ease further. 
"In all likelihood, there are further easing moves ahead" because of "the slowdown in economic growth and the moderation in inflation," said Rudolf Thunberg, economist at Ried, Thunberg &amp; Co., a Westport, Conn., investment research firm. 

   The Fed's policy committee, known as the Federal Open Market Committee, has been divided in recent months between several of the Fed governors in Washington, who favor an easier policy, and many of the district Fed presidents, who are reluctant to ease credit. 
The committee's next meeting is scheduled for Aug. 22. 

   Bankers have been waiting for the Fed to act before matching Chase Manhattan Bank's recent move to cut its prime lending rate to 10 1/2% from 11%. 
Some bankers said that if the federal funds rate remains around 9% or 9 1/8%, that would persuade them to lower their prime, or base, rates soon, perhaps as early as today. 

   The fed funds rate actually fell well below 9% at one point yesterday, the final day of a two-week period in which banks settle their reserve positions with the central bank. 
Settlement days often cause sharp technical movements. 

   "It certainly looks as though the funds rate now is at 9% and, if that's the case, we'll see declines rather quickly" in the prime rate to 10 1/2%, says Kathleen Cooper, chief economist at Security Pacific National Bank, Los Angeles. 
"Banks have been waiting to see the funds rate move down" before cutting the prime rate. 

   She also expects further rate declines. 
"I think before the year is out, we'll see somewhat lower rates" as the Fed moves "to make sure that this soft landing stays a soft landing, rather than a hard landing," she said. 

   The prime rate commands widespread attention because it is used by banks as a benchmark to set rates on many types of consumer loans, such as home-equity loans. 
It is also used to set rates on loans to numerous small and medium-sized businesses. 

   ---

   Tom Herman in New York contributed to this article. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Posner Plans to Sell
@  Fischbach to Group
@  For $29.835 a Share </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> FIS APL </CO>
<IN> TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM) </IN>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Miami Beach financier Victor Posner revived efforts to sell the electrical-contracting company, Fischbach Corp., accepting a $29.835-a-share cash offer for the concern. 

   Mr. Posner, chairman of Fischbach, controls the company through APL Corp., which owns 52.9% of Fischbach's common shares. 
APL has agreed to vote its shares for the merger. 

   The offer is being made by a group led by Asher DeVere, a British investor with real-estate and other interests. 
Howard Squadron, a lawyer with the firm of Squadron, Ellenoff, Plesent &amp; Lehrer, who represents Mr. DeVere, said the group has a commitment from a banking house to finance the acquisition. 

   Fischbach has 3,912,099 shares outstanding, which would cost about $117 million at the proposed price; the total would be somewhat higher because of conversion of options at various prices on another 421,000 shares. 

   Mr. Posner's APL in mid-May said it reached a "general understanding" with Mr. DeVere's group to sell Fischbach at $35 a share, or $136.9 million. 
At the time, APL said $5 of the offer was conditional upon resolution of certain lawsuits. 

   Mr. Squadron said the buyers have "taken on the responsibility" of the suits "and reduced the price accordingly." 
The litigation involves construction contracts. 

   In composite trading on the New York Stock Exchange, Fischbach closed at $25.125 a share, up 62.5 cents. 

   Fischbach was also the object of a $25-a-share tender offer last year from a company controlled by New York investor Andrew N. Heine. 

   Mr. Posner's involvement with Fischbach, including his move earlier this year to take operating control of Fischbach following the death of its chief executive officer, has led to suits from some Fischbach directors. 

   Fischbach said the merger offer is subject to approval by its board, certain regulatory approvals, and approval of a majority of shareholders.

<HL> Paramount Is
@  Near to Selling
@  Associates Corp.
@  ---
@  Financial-Services Business
@  Could Fetch $4 Billion;
@  New Bids Made Monday
@  ----
@  By Laura Landro
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> PCI </CO>
<IN> TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM)
FINANCIAL, ACCOUNTING, LEASING (FIN)
RECREATION, ENTERTAINMENT, TOYS, MOVIES, PHOTOGRAPHY, SPORTS (REC) </IN>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Paramount Communications Inc. is close to a sale of its Associates Corp. financial services unit in a transaction worth between $3 billion and $4 billion, according to Wall Street investment banking sources involved in the bidding. 

   Paramount declined to comment. 
The company put the unit on the block in April as part of a restructuring in which the company's name was changed to Paramount Communications from Gulf &amp; Western Inc. Paramount, which made the announcement two months prior to making its ill-fated hostile bid for Time Inc., said at the time that it wanted to focus on expanding its entertainment and publishing operations. 

   According to the Wall Street sources, a second round of bids for Associates was submitted on Monday, including bids from several domestic and foreign concerns. 
The identity of the bidders couldn't be learned, but they are said to include insurance companies and other credit concerns. 

   Associates accounted for about 46% of Paramount's operating profit last year, and has long served as a cushion against its cyclical entertainment and publishing businesses such as Paramount Pictures and Simon &amp; Schuster. 
But Paramount Chairman Martin S. Davis has said that Associates doesn't fit into his long-range strategic plans for the company. 

   Paramount had planned to use the proceeds from the sale to help pay the debt it would have incurred in its proposed $12 billion acquisition of Time. 
The company has said it will now explore other opportunities for expansion in the entertainment business, and it is expected to use the proceeds from the Associates to help finance another acquisition. 

   Paramount acquired Associates in 1968, and over the past two decades has agressively expanded in the consumer and commercial finance business. 
In 1988, the unit had revenue of $2.05 billion and $372.4 million in operating income. 

   In New York Stock Exchange composite trading yesterday, Paramount shares closed at $58.125, down 25 cents. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Lenders' Fees for Small FHA Mortgages
@  Deter Some Home Buyers, Critics Assert
@  ----
@  By Joe Davidson
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> GOVMT </CO>
<IN> REAL ESTATE, REITS, LAND DEVELOPMENT (REL)
FINANCIAL, ACCOUNTING, LEASING (FIN)
BANKS (BNK)
CONSTRUCTION, MATERIALS (CON)
SAVINGS AND LOANS, THRIFTS, CREDIT UNIONS (SAL) </IN>
<DATELINE> WASHINGTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Mortgage lenders across the country discourage borrowers of small amounts by charging extra fees for loans backed by the Federal Housing Administration, critics say. 

   Mortgage-rate sheets from various lenders indicate many tack on fees of as much as 5%, or five so-called mortgage discount points, for small loans. 
A federal law passed in 1987 prohibits lenders from setting a minimum amount for FHA loans, but opponents of the practice say the extra fees deter low-income home buyers from applying for smaller loans. 

   A Housing and Urban Development spokesman said the department currently has no position on the extra fees. 
All HUD programs are being reviewed at the instruction of HUD Secretary Jack Kemp. 
The department is in the midst of congressional and internal investigations following accusations of influence-peddling, embezzlement, poor management and a lack of agency control over outside contractors. 

   Surveys by the Chicago-based National Training and Information Center, a resource center for community organizations, found mortgage lenders routinely added extra fees to their small loans. 
Gale Cincotta, executive director of the center, said the high fees for smaller loans amount to "redlining and denying people access to credit." 
Low-income and moderate-income home buyers are "discriminated against and penalized," because the lenders "just don't want to make smaller loans," she said. 
"They want to do the big, big stuff." 

   The additional points can equal significantly higher charges for those seeking small loans. 
A point is equal to one percentage point of the face amount of the mortgage. 

   Home Loan Corp. in Houston, for example, charges a 5% fee for loans less than $40,000 and no additional points for loans above $60,000. 
This could result in a home buyer having to pay almost $2,000 in additional costs. 
Ben Streusand, president of Home Loan, declined to comment about the rate structure. 

   Walter Buczynski, a senior vice president with Margaretten &amp; Co. in Perth Amboy, N.J., said his company and many others charge additional points for the smaller loans nationwide because the 1% fee allowed by the FHA isn't enough to meet their expenses. 
Margaretten charges five additional points for loans under$15,000. 

   Brian Chappell, a vice president of Mortgage Bankers Association in Washington, a mortgage banking trade group, disagreed with the practice. 
"That's wrong," said Mr. Chappell. 
"That violates the spirit of the (minimum loan) law." 

   Tom Gobby, a vice president for the Talman Home Federal Savings &amp; Loan Association of Illinois, Chicago, agreed. 
"It's discriminating. 
It goes beyond discouraging. 
There is a question of basic morality, for crying out loud." 
Mr. Gobby said that although lenders don't make as much on small loans, Talman didn't charge additional points. 
"You work that into the cost of business," he said. 

   American Securities Mortgage Co. in Houston charges 3% more for loans less than $30,000. 
"I don't want to turn them (low-income buyers) away and say you're not buying enough house," Jeanne Sanders, a vice president of the firm, said. 
"To me that would be more discriminatory." 

   A survey by the National Training and Information Center indicates that seven of the nation's largest mortgage companies charge additional points for small FHA loans. 

   "It all has to do with the cost of money," said Charles Sandine, senior vice president of Guaranty Bank for Savings, Milwaukee. 
"Risk has nothing to do with it." 
Guaranty charges two additional points for loans under $20,000. 

   "The Congress is going to have to find a way to ensure that low-income potential home buyers are served by this program and are not discriminated against," said Scott Baker, a spokesman for Rep. Thomas Ridge. 
Mr. Ridge, a Pennsylvania Republican, sponsored the law prohibiting lenders from setting a loan minimum. 

   "If we don't solve this, what we will have is mortgage bankers . . . just serving the upper market," Mr. Baker said. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Regulators Order
@  Pinnacle West S&amp;L
@  To Add Capital
@  ----
@  By Frederick Rose
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> GOVMT PNW </CO>
<IN> SAVINGS AND LOANS, THRIFTS, CREDIT UNIONS (SAL) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Federal regulators demanded about $192 million in added capital at MeraBank, Arizona's largest savings and loan, renewing pressure on the thrift's parent. 
Pinnacle West Capital Corp. said that its MeraBank unit has been ordered to add the capital by Monday. 

   Analysts saw the move as an effort by the Federal Home Loan Bank of San Francisco to force Pinnacle West to bolster the troubled thrift. 
"Regulators seem to be putting pressure on Pinnacle West to see if it will come up with the money," said Edward J. Tirello, an analyst at Shearson Lehman Hutton Inc. Mr. Tirello added that he expects Pinnacle West to borrow the funds needed to meet MeraBank's new capital requirement. 

   A spokesman for Pinnacle West declined to comment on either the company's borrowing capacities or its plans. 
Pinnacle West said it disputes the FHLB order "as a legal matter," although the company spokesman said no legal action opposing the regulatory order has been filed. 

   Pinnacle West, which is based in Phoenix, Ariz., is a holding company whose other interests include Arizona Public Service Co., the state's largest electric utility; a venture capital company; uranium mining operations; and real estate. 
Almost all of its operations, other than the electrical utility, are troubled. 

   The FHLB's order to MeraBank, which has been anticipated since late last month, formally disallows good will as part of the thrift's regulatory capital. 
Good will is a noncash accounting for intangible items on a balance sheet. 

   MeraBank's traditional regulatory capital, including good will, totaled $265 million -- some $65 million above the minimum -- at June 30. 
However, without good will, its tangible capital is in deficit by $192 million. 

   In a letter dated July 24, the FHLB directed that MeraBank boost its tangible capital from its negative balance to zero by Monday. 
Regulators also ordered MeraBank to boost its tangible capital to 1.5% of total assets by June 30, 1990, and to 3% of total assets by June 30, 1991. 

   In an apparent effort to meet the future capital requirements, MeraBank said earlier this month that it will stop making new loans and will jettison assets -- steps that would reduce the amount of needed new capital. 
Gene E. Rice, chairman and chief executive officer of MeraBank, said he expects assets to shrink to about $4.5 billion in three years from $8.28 billion at March 31, 1989. 

   These steps may help MeraBank in the long run, but Pinnacle West is in something of an immediate regulatory squeeze. 
On the one hand, federal thrift regulators are calling for it to inject more money. 
On the other, the Arizona Corporation Commission, which regulates Pinnacle West's cash-rich utility operation, has limited the dividends that Arizona Public Service can pay to its parent, thus limiting the most obvious source of funds. 

   The FHLB order relies on a rarely used power to require an "individual minimum capital requirement" that is much more stringent than that currently imposed on most thrifts. 
The new requirement at MeraBank is separate from legislative steps that will greatly tighten reserve requirements for thrifts across the country, but at a much slower pace. 

   FHLB officials declined to discuss regulatory actions with MeraBank or any other specific institution. 
However, a spokeswoman said that the bank's power to order changes in capital requirements has the legal standing and enforceability of a cease and desist order.

<HL> The Two Faces of a Cooling PC Market
@  ---
@  As Some Firms
@  Stagnate, a Few
@  Grow Briskly
@  ----
@  By G. Pascal Zachary
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> COMPUTERS AND INFORMATION TECHNOLOGY (CPR)
SOFTWARE (SOF) </IN>
<TEXT>
   When it announces its first laptop computer next month, Apple Computer Inc. expects its loyalists to flock to the machine in droves. 
The Cupertino, Calif., firm is so concerned about sky-high demand for the machine that it fears its sole outside supplier of the laptop's screens may wilt under the onslaught of orders. 

   At Businessland Inc., the big personal-computer retailer, store managers can't get their hands on enough of Apple's Macintosh and International Business Machines Corp.'s PS/2 computers equipped with new communications software. 
Novell Inc. and 3Com Corp., the biggest makers of networking software that links PCs, both expect big sales gains in the next 12 months. 
And Compaq Computer Corp. is selling so many advanced machines that one of its main suppliers, disk-drive maker Conner Peripherals Inc., said yesterday its second-quarter sales more than tripled to $164 million. 

   This is a slump? 

   The personal computer industry is, indeed, slowing down, to an estimated overall growth rate of 10% or so from nearly twice that a year ago. 
But the trend disguises a central fact about the PC business -- rather than slumping broadly, it is increasingly divided between winners and losers. 
The makers of the most advanced machines, and the software companies that specialize in linking them, are still going strong, while hardware and software concerns with older products are experiencing such sharp drops in sales that many are reporting losses. 
One problem for the winners is that investors aren't distinguishing them from the losers, as most PC-related stocks have taken a beating in the past several months. 

   In part, the widening gulf between winners and losers reflects the growing strength of three industry powerhouses. 
This year, Compaq and Apple have cemented their status -- along with IBM -- as the dominant hardware suppliers in the industry. 
IBM, which doesn't break out its PC results, said in June that its sales of those machines were growing at an annual rate of 40%, faster than expected. 

   "Before, the three were leaders," says Charles R. Wolf, an analyst at First Boston Corp. in New York. 
"Today, they are dominant and are building a widening lead over the second tier." 

   Mr. Wolf believes that this year and next, these three firms will account for virtually all of the growth in PC sales world-wide, leaving a bevy of smaller competitors to fight over a stagnant market. 
He estimates that personal-computer sales by Apple, Compaq and IBM will grow a combined 25% this year and 20% in 1990. 
"I have the rest of the market being flat," Mr. Wolf says. 

   The gap between winners and losers also is seen in software, where Microsoft Corp. is increasingly dominant. 
On Tuesday, the company said it expects sales in its current fiscal year, which began July 1, to rise 25% to $1 billion. 

   The software industry remains populated by scores of companies, but few have forged a broad, effective strategy. 
Companies which rely heavily on single products, such as Lotus Development Corp. with its 1-2-3 spreadsheet, Ashton-Tate Corp. with its data-base management program and Aldus Corp. with its Pagemaker publishing program, have seen their stars dim. 
Companies with broader offerings, meanwhile, are gaining appeal, partly because "customers think it's more important to integrate applications," says John Zeisler, vice president of marketing at Claris Corp., a software company owned by Apple. 

   Software companies also are suffering from trying to adapt existing programs to new operating systems. 
"The biggest growth area is moving existing products to these new platforms," says Gordon Eubanks, chief executive officer of Symantec Corp., a Cupertino, Calif., software maker. 

   Chasing these opportunities, however, has exhausted scarce programming resources, and -- what's worse -- hasn't paid off yet. 
Many software companies have adapted popular programs to Microsoft's OS/2 operating system, but customers have been slow to buy OS/2. 
Microsoft still estimates that it will sell one million copies of OS/2 in 1990, but others aren't so sure. 
"I haven't seen a big push from anybody for OS/2," says Phillip E. White, chief executive officer at Informix Software Inc. 

   The time spent on OS/2 has had another adverse effect, diverting attention from customer problems. 
"The basic problem is that for 18 months the industry has been talking to itself," says Stewart Alsop, editor of the PC Letter, a newsletter. 
"That's confused the customer, who's decided to hold off on purchases." 

   The problem in hardware is different: customers know what they want, but seemingly can't get enough. 
For large corporate buyers, speed and power are the most wanted features. 

   That has resulted in a split in the hardware market between makers of low-powered and high-powered computers. 
Sales of machines using Intel Corp.'s fast 80386 microprocessor are growing more than 30% annually, while sales of machines using less powerful microprocessors are eroding, says Michael Murphy, editor of the California Technology Stock Letter. 

   "The trend toward higher-performance systems is clear," says Safi Qureshey, chief executive officer of AST Research Inc., Irvine, Calif., which makes personal computers and related products. 
"Companies that are focusing on this are doing reasonably well." 

   Mr. Qureshey says AST has withdrawn from several peripheral businesses to concentrate on supplying more powerful personal computers, including a new machine using Intel's 80486 microprocessor, that company's newest and most powerful. 

   Both IBM and Compaq, which have strong 80386 offerings, are benefiting from the enthusiasm for higher-powered machines. 
Apple, which offers similarly powered machines using processors from Motorola Inc., also is benefiting. 

   Although the emphasis on powerful machines is driving PC sales, the trend worries some industry officials. 
These computers cost much more than lower-powered machines, so they mask slowing growth in unit sales. 
"That scares me," says Claris's Mr. Zeisler. 
"Are we trading higher dollars for fewer customers?"

<HL> Marketing:
@  Mass-Marketing May Be
@  Elusive for DAT Players
@  ----
@  By Jacob M. Schlesinger and Andrew B. Cohen
@  Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> MARKETING, ADVERTISING (MKT)
HOME ELECTRONICS, FURNISHINGS, APPLIANCES (HMF)
RECREATION, ENTERTAINMENT, TOYS, MOVIES, PHOTOGRAPHY, SPORTS (REC) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Most people thought the hard part in getting digital audio tape on the market would be answering the question of copyright protection. 
That appears done, but don't count on buying a moderately priced player anytime soon -- some technical and economic hurdles remain. 

   After three years of pitched battle, an agreement between record companies and audio equipment makers on a copyright protection plan will be announced tomorrow. 
While a number of industry executives and analysts are excited about the technology, they warn that it may take time before it becomes the latest rage among audiophiles, if it happens at all. 

   So-called DAT machines record and play sounds digitally, similar to compact disk players, which can't record. 
And DAT machines reproduce sound more clearly and accurately than conventional analog tapes, which some analysts say eventually could be rendered obsolete by the new product. 

   Every major Japanese and European consumer electronics company has had the ability to make DAT machines for at least two years. 
But the machines haven't been mass-marketed here because of a bitter dispute between makers and record companies, who fear the high-quality recordings could spur extensive copyright violations. 
That issue could be resolved by the settlement, which in part limits the ability of DAT machines to copy from compact disks. 

   Record-industry reaction to the settlement is mixed so far. 
CBS Records, purchased two years ago by Sony Corp., could play a big role in the success of DAT, since it is the world's largest record company and its parent company is one of the most powerful consumer electronics giants -- and a big proponent of DAT. Tommy Mottola, president of CBS Records, believes digital audio tape will begin making inroads in the next year and a half, and "will give another surge of sales to our industry." 
He says DAT sound quality is "comparable to, if not better than, compact disk," and he predicts that ultimately the DAT technology "will be the one to walk away with the whole ball game." 
At Warner Communications Inc.'s Warner Records unit -- which isn't tied to a hardware maker like Sony -- executives are cautiously optimistic about DAT. 

   "Once you solve the taping problem, we've always been a believer in the technology," says Geoffrey Holmes, an executive vice president of Warner. 
"Any system that enhances the quality of the material of performing artists is something we're in favor of," he adds, as long as it can be done in a cost-effective way. 

   But at Bertelsmann AG's Bertelsmann Music Group, which includes RCA Records, co-chairman Michael Dornemann says the copyright protections offered under the agreement aren't sufficient. 
Under the settlement, machine producers must install a device that allows users to make one copy from a compact disk onto a digital tape, but prevents a second generation of copying -- from one digital tape to another. 

   Mr. Dornemann notes that an unlimited number of copies can still be made from the original. 
He says record producers and artists should be compensated for the increased possibility of copying by a royalty on blank DAT tapes and DAT equipment. 
"With respect to this goal, the agreement offers little," he says. 
"The hardware industry is not prepared to support the request for royalty." 

   And some industry executives say they can't understand why record companies support DAT at all. 
"I cannot, in the furthest reaches of my imagination, think why any of the major record companies would embrace it," says Russ Solomon, president of the Tower Records chain, based in Sacramento, Calif. 
"I'd be surprised if they really came to the party. 
They know that if they do support the software end in a big way, it will fuel the hardware market," bringing more high-quality recording equipment into the home. 

   Mr. Solomon says the copyright-protection agreement "finally gets rid of the strife between hardware and software manufacturers," who now "don't have to be in opposing armed camps." 
But he is pessimistic about the potential for a mass market. 
"DAT doesn't make that much difference, and there's not that much demand on the part of the public," he says. 
"DAT focuses on on a fairly limited audiophile market." 

   One of the biggest impediments to widespread sales so far is price. 
Some professional units are available here from $995 to $2,000, and consumer machines would likely be priced in the same range. 
Virtually no consumer models are sold here now, though JVC Co. of America, a unit of the Japan-based concern, sells some DAT systems for luxury cars. 
Tower Records is selling some pre-recorded tapes for $25 to $30, but fewer than 100 titles are available. 
Blank tapes are $7 to $20. 

   In Japan -- by far the biggest market right now -- machines generally sell in the range of about 200,000 yen (about $1,400). 
While most new high-priced consumer electronics items are later drastically marked down as demand grows, many in the industry question whether prices for DAT machines, because of their technical complexity, can ever be slashed low enough to become mass consumer items. 
Warner officials, for example, doubt that DAT machines can ever be priced below the $300 level that has spurred sales of compact disk players and videocassette recorders. 

   In Japan, for example, when most Japanese companies started selling the machines in 1987, they expected to sell large amounts quickly. 
But sales have been much slower than expected. 
Hitachi Ltd., which planned in 1987 to make up to 1,000 units a month, has now halted production. 

   Even with the new agreement, many companies may be slow to produce in the volume necessary to push down prices. 
Officials were reluctant to discuss their plans openly yesterday in advance of the settlement's formal announcement. 
But some privately said they will proceed cautiously, rather than once again investing heavily only to see demand evaporate. 

   "We hope good cooperation now exists, but we are not confident," a spokesman for one leading Japanese company said. 
"We are anxious about the next new problem. 
We now have a wait-and-see attitude." 

   In April, Nakamichi America Corp., a Torrance, Calif., unit of Nakamichi Corp. of Tokyo, shipped its Model 1000, a ultra-high-end model that carries a ultra-high-end list price of $10,000. 
Jett Logan, Nakamichi's vice president for sales and marketing, said at the time that his company expected to sell 500 units in the first year at that price. 
"DAT hasn't met our expectation," he said this week. 
"We are selling product, but it certainly isn't running out the door. 
The confusion that has existed up till now has hurt sales of the product." 
Mr. Logan said the news of the agreement is "welcome," and hopes it will "remove the cloud that has hung over DAT." 

   Some analysts don't believe that consumers will take to a machine whose copying abilities are limited, and believe the idea of a royalty payment from the proceeds of each blank tape sold will have to be considered eventually. 
Barry Dargan, an analyst in the Tokyo office of James Capel Pacific Ltd., predicts, "I suspect they'll have to come back in a year or two and amend the agreement." 

   ---

   Laura Landro contributed to this article. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Cummins Attracts
@  Second Foreigner;
@  Stock Price Jumps
@  ----
@  By Robert Johnson
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> CUM A.IEL </CO>
<IN> TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM)
AUTOS, AUTO PARTS (AUT) </IN>
<DATELINE> COLUMBUS, Ind.  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Cummins Engine Co. stock jumped as the second foreign company in the past eight months acquired a major stake in the big diesel machine and automotive parts maker. 

   Cummins, whose founding family's descendants bought Hanson USA's 8.8% stake earlier this month for $72 million, ridding the company of that distraction, is now confronted by an even larger holding that could lead to a takeover attempt. 
Hanson USA is the U.S. arm of Hanson PLC of London. 

   Industrial Equity (Pacific) Ltd., a Hong Kong-based concern 70%-owned by Brierley Investments Ltd. in New Zealand, disclosed in a Securities and Exchange Commission filing that it holds a 9.9% stake in Cummins. 
Industrial Equity said it bought the shares for investment purposes but reserves the right to change its plans. 

   In New York Stock Exchange trading yesterday after the stake was disclosed, Cummins stock price closed at $60.25, up $2.50 a share. 

   A tender offer by Industrial Equity "wouldn't be difficult for them with the strong financing we understand they have," said Eli Lustgarten, an analyst at PaineWebber Inc. 
But he said Brierley's usual style is to take long-term investment positions in foreign companies. 
"To my knowledge, they have never acquired one in the U.S.," said Mr. Lustgarten. 

   Other analysts speculated that the stake may be an attempt at some form of "greenmail" -- forcing Cummins to buy back the Industrial Equity stake at a steep profit. 
When Hanson's stake was bought earlier this month, it was descendants of the Cummins family, rather than the company itself, that paid. 

   Although Cummins and the family acclaimed the purchase of the Hanson stake, saying it wasn't greenmail, analysts said that transaction may make such stakes more inviting to investors. 
The Cummins family members involved took a $5 million loss, based on Cummins's stock price before the purchase. 

   Mr. Lustgarten said Industrial Equity "could be after greenmail, or they could be in it for the long term. 
Right now, we don't know." 

   A Cummins spokeswoman said an Industrial Equity official telephoned earlier this week. 
Based on that discussion and the SEC filing, she said: "We tend to take them at their word" that the new stake is merely for investment purposes. 

   Cummins is rebounding from an earnings slump last year. 
In the second quarter ended July 2, net income was $23.9 million, or 83 cents a share, up from $1.7 million a year earlier, when preferred dividend requirements exceeded any per-share profit. 
Sales rose to $982 million from $834.4 million. 

   Cummins has been investing heavily in new products to compete more effectively with overseas engine makers and to meet pollution guidelines, depressing profit in recent quarters. 

   When the Cummins family descendants bought Hanson's stake, they received a so-called standstill agreement from the London-based concern, requiring for 10 years the family's permission to buy Cummins shares.

<HL> The Death Toll of Animals
@  And Birds Keeps Mounting </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> XON </CO>
<IN> PETROLEUM (PET)
ENVIRONMENT (ENV) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Aesthetic issues of the Alaskan oil spill aside, state and federal officials worry mostly about the impact of soiled beaches on wildife. 
As the bird-carcass count approaches 28,000, scientists estimate that the total bird kill, including those whose bodies have sunk or were eaten by scavengers, is at least 50,000, about 10% of the area's population. 
Among the victims: 109 bald eagles. 
Nesting populations have been decimated on some of Prince William Sound's hardest-hit islands. 
On some islands where eagles have survived, nesting birds have experienced "almost complete reproductive failure," says Phil Schempf, a specialist with the U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service in Valdez. 
Prespill estimates of the sound's eagle population were 2,000 birds. 
The official death toll of sea otters is 872, and a few more are still dying in the Gulf of Alaska. 
The otter carcasses recovered probably represent only half or fewer of those killed, officials say. 
Though there are not yet accurate counts, several hundred seals died following the spill, and oily beaches are also thought to be responsible for the deaths of numerous seal pups born on oil-soaked beaches this summer. 
And the spill closed the sound's $12 million herring fishery earlier this year. 
About 30% of the salmon grounds have recently been put off limits to fishing because of moving oil and oily beaches near spawning grounds. 
Rich salmon fisheries off Kodiak Island in the Gulf of Alaska and in Cook Inlet near Homer have been closed, costing fishermen there tens of millions of dollars. 

   (See related story: "Stuck in Alaska: Exxon's Army Scrubs Beaches, but Many Don't Stay Cleaned --- As Oil Persists and Costs Top $1 Billion, Firm Agrees To Resume Job in Spring --- Will the Goo Turn to Asphalt?" -- WSJ July 27, 1989)
