<HL> Prime Computer to Post 2nd-Period Loss;
@  Acquisition by Whitney Thrown in Doubt
@  ----
@  By William M. Bulkeley
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> PRM MBF </CO>
<IN> EARNINGS (ERN)
COMPUTERS AND INFORMATION TECHNOLOGY (CPR)
TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM) </IN>
<DATELINE> NATICK, Mass.  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Prime Computer Inc. reported a $19 million loss on a 6.5% decline in sales for the second quarter, throwing its plan to be acquired by J.H. Whitney &amp; Co. into doubt. 

   Whitney, which has agreed to acquire Prime in a deal valued at $1.2 billion, said that its banks Tuesday evening demanded that Whitney arrange "substantial additional" financing because of Prime's weak operating results. 

   The loss apparently led the banks to question whether Prime has a strong enough cash flow to cover heavy interest costs related to the acquisition. 
Whitney said it is negotiating with the banks and with "a major financial institution" to arrange the needed financing. 

   Prime's stock fell $1.375 to $15.75 in composite trading on the New York Stock Exchange as arbitragers worried about the fate of the Whitney takeover. 
"I still think Whitney wants to do it," said one arbitrager holding Prime stock. 
"If they don't, we're" in trouble. 

   Jay P. Stevens, an analyst with Dean Witter, said "my own view is that the lenders are beginning to realize there are problems in the industry and the price of Prime is too high." 
He asked: "With the computer industry hemorrhaging, what is Prime worth?" 
Chemical Banking Corp.'s Chemical Bank unit and Bank of Boston Corp.'s First National Bank of Boston had agreed to provide $600 million of senior debt financing for the acquisition. 
Whitney said the agreements were conditioned on the banks' being satisfied that there is sufficient additional debt and equity financing to fund the offer and meet Prime's ongoing needs. 

   Late last month, Whitney emerged as an eleventh-hour acquirer of Prime, apparently rescuing it from a seven-month-old hostile tender offer by MAI Basic Four Inc., Tustin, Calif. 
Whitney offered $21.50 a share in cash for 49.5 million Prime shares, and $22 face amount of debentures for 13 million additional shares. 
It extended its offer one day to Friday. 

   Whitney and Prime officials couldn't be reached for further comment. 
One individual familiar with the transaction said "J.H. 
Whitney remains committed to seeing this transaction through to its end. 
The timing and circumstances are being worked out now." 

   Prime continues to resist the hostile tender offer by MAI Basic Four of $19.50 a share in cash for 49.5 million shares and $21 face amount of debentures for the remaining shares. 
MAI extended that offer to today but analysts give it little chance so long as the Whitney offer remains in place. 

   MAI last week offered a plan under which Prime would sell its minicomputer business to MAI for $450 million in cash and $150 million in debentures. 
It suggested Prime could use the proceeds for a stock buy-back. 
Yesterday, Prime said its directors believe the Whitney offer is preferable. 

   In another development, Prime adjourned its annual meeting from yesterday to Aug. 9 to give its holders more of an opportunity to study the latest MAI proposal. 
MAI has nominated a slate of directors to unseat the incumbents, and said they would sell Prime's minicomputer business to MAI if they were elected. 

   Prime said its financial results would have been stronger if it weren't for the high costs and customer uncertainty related to its ongoing anti-takeover battle. 

   Anthony Craig, president and chief executive officer said after the adjourned meeting that second-quarter results were "disappointing." 
The loss contrasted with year earlier net income of $7.2 million, or 15 cents a share. 
Revenue fell to $382 million from $408.4 million. 

   Mr. Craig said that pretax, non-recurring charges, including a $20 million payment to Whitney as reimbursement for its expenses, amounted to $27.3 million in the quarter. 
Before such costs the company had a pretax operating profit of about $10 million, he said. 

   Most midrange computer makers have been reporting weak results in recent quarters, and Prime's problems are exacerbated by the uncertainty over its possible acquisition, said Barry Willman, an analyst with Sanford C. Bernstein. 

   For the first six months, Prime reported a loss of $24 million, compared with net income of $21.7 million, or 45 cents a share, a year earlier. 
Revenue fell to $768.9 million from $772.1 million.

<HL> Stocks Soar on Program Trades
@  ---
@  Tech Companies
@  Take a Beating
@  ----
@  By Craig Torres and Sonja Steptoe
@  Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> STOCK MARKET, OFFERINGS (STK)
SECURITIES INDUSTRY (SCR)
COMPUTERS AND INFORMATION TECHNOLOGY (CPR)
SOFTWARE (SOF) </IN>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Technology stocks were trounced yesterday. 

   Even as broad market indexes rallied to all-time highs yesterday, panic selling prevailed in technology shares as investors dumped everything from International Business Machines Corp. to Apple Computer Inc. 

   Cray Research Inc. was the biggest loser on the New York Stock Exchange yesterday, falling $2.125 to $42 a share. 
Among other technology stocks, IBM dropped 25 cents to $112.50, Compaq Computer Corp. tumbled $1.25 to $86.875, Prime Computer Inc. slipped $1.375 to $15.75. 
In American Stock Exchange composite trading, Amdahl Corp. shares fell 62.5 cents to $15. 

   Reflecting investor pessimism about the computer industry, Compaq shares fell even though the company reported that second-quarter earnings rose to $1.96 a share from $1.46 a year earlier. 
No matter what the second-quarter results so far are for a variety of computer stocks, analysts and investors are forecasting a miserable performance for the technology sector in the second half of 1989. 

   Privately, the chief executive officers of many computer firms are advising analysts to lower their sights for 1989. 
Yesterday, Cray Research had a private conference call with analysts and "didn't lead investors to look for a rebound," said Patricia Laupheimer, a computer-industry analyst at Shearson Lehman Hutton Inc. 

   While the stock market ascends to new highs, the sell-off in technology stocks underscores just how skittish investors are about the impact of an economic downturn. 

   "Investors don't do too much on good earnings, but when they see bad earnings, they rip stocks apart," said Jack Baker, head of equity block trading at Shearson. 

   Indeed, forecasts of slower economic growth ahead in the U.S. and Europe are a major factor squashing technology stocks. 
"A lot of these companies didn't see the slowdown coming and didn't make necessary adjustments," said William Easterbrook, a computer analyst and senior vice president at Kidder, Peabody &amp; Co. 

   Aside from slow growth, analysts said a decline in U.S. defense spending will hurt earnings of computer firms this year. 
Also, computer users aren't upgrading systems fast enough to absorb all the new products firms are producing, analysts say. 

   The high-tech sell-off has punished Big Board and over-the-counter shares. 

   Unisys Corp., a Big Board stock, is down to lows not seen in about three years, finishing at $20.75 yesterday, for a gain of 50 cents. 
The Nasdaq 100 Index, heavily weighted with OTC technology shares, has fallen 3% since July 17. 
Meanwhile, the Nasdaq Composite Index, a broad market measure, is off only 1%. 

   Among the recent casualties of the high-tech sell-off: 

   Apple Computer, after reporting on July 18 third-quarter earnings slightly above last year's, told analysts to take a cautious view of its earnings prospects over the near term. 
The advice prompted a number of analysts to slash future earnings estimates for Apple, and the stock lost 3.7% in active trading that day. 

   On the same day, Intel Corp. announced earnings at the low end of analyst expectations, and its stock tumbled 8% on heavy volume. 

   Microsoft Corp. had its turn Tuesday. 
Although the software maker's fiscal fourth-quarter earnings surged 48%, it forecast that a computer-industry bellwether, microprocessor unit growth, would expand only 10% over the next 12 months. 
The company also indicated that its new OS/2 operating system for PCs hasn't been selling well and that some analysts' estimates of fiscal 1990 earnings are high. 
Some analysts had been forecasting 1990 profit growth of about 25%. 
Microsoft's stock fell 6% on the news. 

   Meanwhile, Ashton-Tate Co. plummeted 11% Tuesday after announcing that it suffered a second-quarter loss of $19.8 million. 
The software company earned $11.5 million a year earlier. 

   (See related story: "Wednesday's Markets: DJIA Up 29.97 In Late Surge" -- WSJ July 27, 1989)

<HL> IRS Employees
@  Reporting Offenses
@  Allege Harassment
@  ----
@  By Hilary Stout
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> LABOR TAXES </CO>
<GV> CONGRESS (CNG)
INTERNAL REVENUE SERVICE (IRS)
JUSTICE DEPARTMENT (JUS)
TREASURY DEPARTMENT (TRE) </GV>
<DATELINE> WASHINGTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Current and former Internal Revenue Service employees told a House subcommittee that they were harassed by superiors in the agency after reporting violations by fellow employees. 

   Although many of those testifying said they felt they were victims of isolated retaliation, most said changes are necessary in the IRS's internal-security system to ensure that employee misconduct is uncovered and corrected. 

   "I have seen instances of preferred treatment for senior officials of the IRS and a coerced resignation of an employee who had uncovered malfeasance by a senior management official," said Stanley Shirai, a special agent in the IRS's Los Angeles office who investigates income-tax evasion. 

   The employees testified on the second day of hearings by the Commerce, Consumer and Monetary Affairs Subcommittee into ethical conduct and standards at the IRS. 
The hearings, which follow a year-long investigation by the panel, focused on eight cases of alleged wrongdoing by 25 senior IRS officials, including taking gratuities from a businessman while providing him with confidential tax information and improperly intervening in a Justice Department tax investigation. 
Committee investigators say they have turned up at least 25 more incidents of what they regard as serious misconduct. 

   The subcommittee has been paying particular attention to how the IRS investigated the employees once the allegations came to light and whether it disciplined them appropriately. 

   In one case, three former employees and one current employee in the IRS's Los Angeles office said there was a perception in the office that an inspection supervisor -- responsible for examining charges of employee misconduct -- was too close to the people she was supposed to be investigating. 
One employee who complained that the perception was discouraging witnesses from talking about one case was told by Washington that headquarters officials had decided against taking her off the case. 

   The subcommittee also heard from three employees who in 1984 told a regional inspector that their supervisor had been providing confidential tax information to a Chicago businessman, accepting theater tickets and other gifts from the businessman, and otherwise violating agency rules. 

   Two of the employees, Stanley Welli and Ronald Koperniak, said they were snubbed after the incident and eventually demoted. 
They have since been restored to their positions, after appealing to higher IRS officials. 
The third, George Ecola, said he was ordered to transfer to another city. 
After he refused to move "because it was retaliation," his superiors withdrew their order. 
But he said he stopped receiving merit pay increases, and that in 1985, for the first time in 22 years of service, his performance rating was less than "above average." 

   Mr. Koperniak said a regional inspector said to the three employees: "The organization will get you." 

   An IRS spokeswoman said, "Our basic longstanding policy is all employees have an obligation to report wrongdoing they see. 
And the corollary to that is you have to feel confident you can report it, and it will be investigated properly, and not have any fear you will be retaliated against." 

   The subcommittee may seek to have the IRS's internal inspection operations put under a special inspector general's office answering only to the IRS commissioner. 
It might also seek to transfer some inspection functions to the Treasury Department's inspector general. 
Though the IRS is part of Treasury, the Treasury inspector general's office normally only reviews IRS cases; it isn't actively involved in them. 

</TEXT>

<DOCNO> WSJ890727-0098 </DOCNO>

<HL> Who's News:
@  Zenith Electronics
@  Names Treasurer,
@  Financial Officer
@  ----
@  By Robert L. Rose
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> ZE WNEWS </CO>
<TEXT>
   Zenith Electronics Corp. named a chief financial officer and a new treasurer in the wake of the company's recently disclosed foul-up in not hedging against a rise in the dollar. 

   The Glenview, Ill., computer and consumer-electronics firm said it named Kell B. Benson, 41 years old, as chief financial officer and vice president for finance, and Willard C. McNitt, 40, as vice president and treasurer. 

   In taking the title of chief financial officer, Mr. Benson assumes a post that had been vacant since May 1988. 
Zenith Chairman Jerry K. Pearlman said in a memo to employees that the move "will strengthen further the coordination between the company's controller and treasury functions." 
Mr. Benson continues in his position as controller. 

   Mr. McNitt, the new treasurer, succeeds David S. Levin in that post. 
A spokesman said Mr. Levin will report to Mr. McNitt in a new position as executive director of financial services. 
Mr. Levin couldn't be reached for comment. 
Mr. McNitt had been vice president and treasurer at Gould Inc., a computer and electronics concern acquired by Nippon Mining Co. last year. 

   Asked if the changes were related to the mistake in hedging against the dollar, a spokesman said "no comment." 

   Last Friday, Zenith reported a widened second-quarter loss and said it may not meet its previous projection that it would be more profitable this year than last year. 
The company in large part blamed its failure to protect itself against a wrong guess on the direction of the U.S. dollar. 
The company had thought the dollar would weaken this year, but it rose. 

   Mr. Pearlman said in an interview Friday that the company could have limited its risk by hedging with foreign exchange options instead of forward exchange contracts. 
Not doing so resulted in what he termed a "$25 million dollar problem -- "the total pretax effect this year if exchange rates stay where they are. 

   Last year, Zenith earned $11.7 million, or 45 cents a share, on sales of $2.69 billion. 

   Mr. Pearlman wouldn't comment Friday when asked if the mistake would lead to any executive changes at Zenith. 

   One securities analyst yesterday questioned whether the moves would make much difference in the company's performance. 
"The company has for years generated billions of dollars of revenue without profit," said Mark Hassenberg, who follows Zenith for Donaldson, Lufkin &amp; Jenrette Securities Corp. "Changing one or two people in the organization is not going to change the problem."

<AN> 890727-0097. </AN>
<HL> IBM Agrees to Purchase a 19.8% Stake
@  In Policy Management for $116.5 Million
@  ----
@  By Paul B. Carroll
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> IBM PMSC </CO>
<IN> TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM)
SOFTWARE (SOF)
COMPUTERS AND INFORMATION TECHNOLOGY (CPR) </IN>
<TEXT>
   International Business Machines Corp. agreed to buy 19.8% of Policy Management Systems Corp. for $116.5 million, marking the biggest purchase yet in its accelerating investment drive. 

   In the past year and a half, IBM has bought pieces of nearly a dozen companies, stepping up the pace in recent months. 
The computer giant is mainly trying to buy technology or expertise in certain areas such as software for insurance companies in the case of its Policy Management stake. 
IBM also seems to be trying to buy software developers' loyalty in the increasingly competitive industry. 

   Together with recent moves to increase its ability to install and even operate computer systems for customers, the investment strategy means IBM is forging ahead with its plans to turn itself into a software and services company that happens to make its own computer hardware. 

   An IBM spokesman says IBM doesn't have any targets for the number of investments it will make. 
"We're trying to pick and choose," he says. 
"It's not like we want to do one a month." 
But he said there will probably be more. 

   Consultants and industry executives say they expect many more. 
They say, though, that the investments will follow the scattershot approach of recent months, because, under IBM's new decentralized management structure, the investment decisions are being made primarily by the numerous independent business units. 

   Like many big companies, IBM doesn't have the greatest record with investments, acquisitions and joint ventures. 
In the early 1980s, IBM gave up on a joint venture with MCA Inc. that reportedly spent $100 million on the videodisk business. 
A major venture with Merrill Lynch &amp; Co. was disbanded three years ago. 
Another venture, Satellite Business Systems, failed to go anywhere, and when IBM traded its holding for a stake in MCI Communications Corp., that didn't produce any synergies, either. 
More recently, IBM has been unwinding its purchase of Rolm Corp. 

   In addition, IBM's size can smother small companies. 
Some have said that IBM employees visiting them can at times outnumber their own employees. 
Interleaf Inc., which has a marketing arrangement with IBM, said earlier this year that its IBM product had been a money loser and that IBM needed to market it harder. 

   "I see a lot of activity but I don't see a lot of leadership in this area," says Fred Gibbons, chairman of Software Publishing Corp., which has held talks with IBM on various possible relationships. 

   Still, IBM seems to have learned from some of its earlier mistakes. 
It seems to have patched up its differences with Interleaf, for instance, and companies that IBM has bought into say the giant has gone to great lengths to shield them from its formidable bureaucracy. 

   "What impressed me is that we dealt with one person through the whole process," says David Sarna, chief executive of Image Business Systems Corp., an image-processing software company that recently sold a stake to IBM. 
"And within general guidelines, he seemed to have complete authority to act," Mr. Sarna adds. 

   He says that IBM executives "are feeling their way, just like we are." 
But he says they moved surprisingly fast, completing the agreement in 10 weeks. 
He also says that while IBM, under a previous marketing arrangement, paid its salesmen only for the IBM equipment they sold, it agreed to pay commissions on sales of Image Business Systems software, too. 

   "If you say IBM is buying loyalty, that's a true statement," Mr. Sarna says. 
"But it works both ways." 

   IBM is so big and has so many people arranging so many deals that there is still plenty of room for confusion. 
Doug Kahn, chief executive of Interactive Images Inc., a maker of software-development tools, says a glitch developed the day before he was to sign his agreement with IBM. 
He says IBM had an overlapping arrangement with another software company, and that the two companies got conflicting information from inside IBM. Mr. Kahn says several executives from his company got on a conference call with executives from IBM and the other company at 4 p.m. and didn't finish until 3:30 a.m. 
Some of Interactive Images' vice presidents then stuck around to type up changes to the contract, and Mr. Kahn didn't see the final contract until he arrived at an IBM office later that morning to sign it. 

   Mr. Kahn says he has a full-time person keeping track of what goes on inside IBM. 
"There are many, many different organizations inside IBM that have different agendas, and we have to make sure that we stay visible inside IBM," he says. 
Still, he says he's been impressed with IBM's ability to move fast and shield him from its complexity. 

   While IBM's investments have been all over the lot-in fiber-optic technology, data-retrieval systems, computer networks and so on -- the Policy Management investment illustrates IBM's considerable interest in developing expertise in various industries. 
Sam Albert, a consultant in Scarsdale, N.Y., says IBM once sold its own software for the insurance market, but that it was rendered obsolete, so this is a way for IBM to renew its commitment to the area. 

   The Policy Management investment also shows IBM's willingness to make bigger investments than it has made in this recent wave. 
The dollar value is greater than that of any other investment that IBM has disclosed. 
The size of the holding -- preferred stock convertible into a 19.8% stake, plus the right to buy a further 10.2% in the open market -- is also one of the largest that IBM has bought. 

   Besides all the software investments, IBM has been active on other fronts as it pursues its aim of generating more than 50% of its revenue from software and services by the early 1990s. 
IBM has been emphasizing systems integration, a fast-growing field in which consultants set up whole computer systems for customers and often write the software used on them. 
As part of that, IBM has bought stakes in two companies -- Computer Task Group Inc. and American Management Systems Inc. 

   In New York Stock Exchange composite trading yesterday, IBM shares closed at $112.50, down 25 cents. 
In national over-the-counter trading, Policy Management shares rose 50 cents to close at $31.50.

<HL> American Express
@  Profit Rose 16%
@  In Second Quarter
@  ----
@  By James C. Hyatt
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> AXP </CO>
<IN> FINANCIAL, ACCOUNTING, LEASING (FIN)
EARNINGS (ERN) </IN>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   American Express Co. said second-quarter net income rose 16%, with strong earnings growth in most operations. 

   The travel and financial services company said net rose to $316.1 million, or 73 cents a share, from $272 million, or 64 cents a share, a year earlier. 
The 1988 period included $12.4 million, or three cents a share, from the company's share of earnings of Fireman's Fund Corp., now being treated as a discontinued operation. 
Operating earnings rose 20%. 
Revenue rose 29% to $6.36 billion from $5.03 billion. 

   American Express's biggest unit, the Travel Related Services Co., had record profit of $214.2 million, up 17%, on a 24% revenue gain to $2.04 billion. 
American Express credit card usage continued to surge, with cards in force rising 13% to 33 million, including an 18% increase outside the U.S. Card charges rose 13% to a second-quarter record of $25 billion. 
Travelers check sales rose 4.8% to $6.3 billion, while travel product sales rose more than 10%, led by gains in the U.S., Asia-Pacific-Australia and in Latin America. 

   IDS Financial Services earnings rose 18% to a record $42.8 million, while assets owned or managed by the mutual fund and financial planning unit rose 16% to $43 billion. 
Revenue rose 22% to $463.1 million, and mutual fund sales rose 59% to $1 billion. 

   American Express Bank's profit rose 9.9% to $35.4 million although operating results, excluding less developed country debt activities, were essentially unchanged. 
The bank charged off $52 million of loans in the quarter, up from $42 million a year earlier. 
And it added $900,000 to its provision for credit losses, down from $3.5 million a year earlier. 
LDC loans totaled $739 million at June 30, down from $1.23 billion a year ago; the reserve for credit losses totaled $448 million, down from $678 million. 

   Information Services Co. profit rose 30% to $17.4 million in the quarter on $173.4 million in revenue, which rose 71%, reflecting internal growth and acquisitions. 
The unit had a pretax gain of $38 million from sale of its transportation and leisure cash advance business. 
Most of the gain was offset, however, by write-downs and by a $24 million reduction in pretax earnings and revenue in a change in accounting for profit of a health care-related business. 
The unit provides various services, including credit card processing, hospital information systems, mutual fund transfer and record keeping, and cable television subscriber billings. 

   Shearson Lehman Hutton Holdings Inc., the brokerage concern, has reported a 43% gain in earnings. 
American Express' share of the net income was $34 million after preferred dividends, up from $23 million a year earlier. 
It holds a 60% interest in Shearson on a fully diluted basis. 

   John Keefe, an analyst at Drexel Burnham Lambert Inc., called the results a "good quarter that fulfilled people's expectations of earnings growth. 
The numbers in the important divisions were very, very strong." 

   He said he expects the company to earn $2.70 a share for the year, "although with Shearson that needs to be bumped up a little bit." 
In 1988 the company earned $2.43 a share. 

   In composite trading on the New York Stock Exchange, American Express closed yesterday at $35.50 a share, up 25 cents.

<HL> House Votes to Suspend B-2 Production
@  Pending Deep Cuts, Revision of Program
@  ----
@  By Andy Pasztor
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> NOC </CO>
<IN> AEROSPACE (ARO)
NUCLEAR POWER, FUEL (NUK) </IN>
<GV> CONGRESS (CNG)
DEFENSE DEPARTMENT (DEF) </GV>
<DATELINE> WASHINGTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The House, increasingly doubtful about the cost and strategic value of the radar-eluding Stealth bomber, voted to suspend production of the new airplane until the Bush administration agrees to deep cuts and a major revision of the $70 billion program. 

   The 257-160 bipartisan vote would force the administration, which lobbied hard against any cuts, to slash its request for 132 of the bombers -- also known as the B-2 -- and to demonstrate the aircraft's technical capabilities before production can resume. 

   In the face of sharp criticism of both the Air Force and Northrop Corp., the Los Angeles-based primary contractor for the B-2, the House also approved legislative language that would require extensive independent testing and unclassified cost studies prior to the start of full-bore production. 

   The hurdles that would be imposed by the House are significantly tougher than B-2 spending restrictions expected to be adopted by the Senate -- a disparity that sets the stage for a prolonged fight over the most expensive, controversial and closely guarded weapon currently in the pipeline. 

   "This issue crosses party lines and crosses philosophical lines," said Rep. John Rowland (R., Conn.). 
Before Congress agrees to spend $8 billion a year on the bat-shaped plane, he argued, "let's kick the wheels, let's fly it" and assess its ability to penetrate Soviet air defenses. 

   Members of both parties complained that the military kept basic information about the plane's cost and other matters classified for too long. 
"The Russians probably know more about this technology than this Congress," joked Rep. Arthur Ravenel (R., S.C.). 
A total of 49 Republicans voted in the majority to slow the B-2. 

   "There's no chance, no way we can go ahead with a program that is as costly" as the Pentagon's current B-2 projections, said Armed Services Committee Chairman Rep. Les Aspin (D., Wis.), a co-sponsor of the successful amendment. 
The Pentagon currently estimates that the bombers will cost $530 million each, including research and development funds already expended. 

   The administration's military priorities received another setback when the House voted to temporarily block Pentagon moves to put MX nuclear missiles on railroad cars. 
President Bush asked Congress for money to deploy both the mobile MX and the smaller Midgetman missile, which is designed to be carried on special trailers. 

   As part of the $305-billion defense authorization bill for the fiscal year starting Oct. 1, lawmakers approved an amendment offered by Rep. John Spratt (D., S.C.) removing $502 million earmarked to purchase railroad equipment and extra MX missiles. 
But the measure permits continued research and development for the so-called rail garrison program. 
The vote was 224-197. 

   The Defense Department's plan, the latest in a variety of schemes to field MX missiles, is to protect the 10-warhead missiles by moving them on the nation's rail system in time of crisis, making it impossible for an enemy to target. 
House Democratic aides said they expect Congress will approve funds for both the mobile MX and the smaller Midgetman once differences between House and Senate bills are worked out. 

   The House bill would cut about $1 billion from the Pentagon's request of $4.7 billion for the B-2 for the fiscal year starting Oct. 1. 
It would provide enough funds to produce just four of the bombers over the next two years, instead of eight as the administration requested. 
More important, none of the production money could be spent unless Congress and the White House agree on a scaled-down program with leaner production rates and revised plans for the bomber's strategic use. 

   Rep. Ike Skelton (D., Mo.) asserted that gutting the B-2 program effectively would take the Air Force "out of the bomber business" after the year 2000 and undercut U.S. arms-control negotiators. 

   But critics blame the Pentagon for wanting to build the most-expensive airplane in history without defining a clear-cut mission. 
"The plane can't perform the mission for which it was built," said Rep. John Kasich (R., Ohio), urging lawmakers to kill the B-2 outright and mothball the production line. 

   Before the current debate, proponents primarily touted the B-2's ability to go after mobile missiles and other movable targets. 
But Pentagon officials now concede it won't be able to perform that mission for the foreseeable future. 
In the past few weeks, Air Force and administration officials have emphasized instead the plane's versatility, saying it could destroy everything from underground Soviet military command posts to tactical targets and even foreign terrorist groups. 

   In Los Angeles, Northrop officials played up the House's decision to reject an amendment offered by Rep. Kasich and others to kill the B-2 altogether. 
A company spokesman said that both the House and Senate "seem to be focusing on the value of the weapons system" and are strugggling "to come up with the best plan to keep {production} at an efficient rate." 

   Still, the House action was a major setback for the defense contractor, which has sunk more than $1 billion of its own money into developing the Stealth. 
One Northrop executive noted that the company has prepared to turn out 24 to 36 bombers a year. 
Assembling less than three a year, he suggested, would begin "to erode the team of people working on the plane" and reduce production efficiencies. 

   Northrop's management and its public relation efforts on behalf of the plane also rankled lawmakers. 
The company has "miserably failed to manage this program," Rep. Mike Synar (D., Okla.) said. 
Another critic, Rep. Patricia Schroeder (D., Colo.), said she was offended and "astounded by the amount of money Northrop is spending on advertising." 

   ---

   Rick Wartzman in Los Angeles contributed to this article. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Hooker Corp. Avoids Fight With Creditors
@  As Provisional Liquidator Takes Reins
@  ----
@  By S. Karene Witcher and Jeffrey A. Trachtenberg
@  Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> A.HKR </CO>
<IN> TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM)
REAL ESTATE, REITS, LAND DEVELOPMENT (REL)
RETAILING (RET)
CONSTRUCTION, MATERIALS (CON) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Ailing Australian property developer Hooker Corp. turned itself over to a court-appointed provisional liquidator, avoiding a protracted confrontation with its bank creditors. 

   Hooker, whose major retailing and real-estate operations in the U.S. include Bonwit Teller and B. Altman &amp; Co., asked the Supreme Court of New South Wales to appoint John Harkness "to take charge of the company." 
Mr. Harkness heads the receivership, reconstructions and insolvency division of accounting firm Peat Marwick Hungerfords in Sydney. 

   In a statement, Hooker directors said they believe their "prompt action" of relinquishing control to a court-appointed agent "will resolve the impasse with the banks and is therefore in the best interests of employees, creditors and shareholders." 

   The Sydney company's rift with its major bank creditors surfaced Tuesday when a group of banks terminated an agreement signed earlier this month. 
The accord called for 42 of Hooker's bank creditors to maintain their current level of credit lines to Hooker for four months ending in October. 
During that time, Hooker was to make interest payments on about $900 million Australian (US$676 million) in loans that aren't secured by specific assets and wouldn't have to repay principal payments falling due. 

   U.S. retail sources close to Hooker said the appointment of a provisional liquidator may speed the sale of the company's U.S. assets. 
"It's possible now that Bonwit Teller and B. Altman &amp; Co. will be sold separately rather than as a package together with Hooker's retail malls," said one source. 

   But Gilbert Harrison, chairman of Financo Inc., an investment banking firm that is providing financial advisory service to Hooker, said the appointment of Mr. Harkness as provisional liquidator is good news for Hooker shareholders and lenders because Mr. Harkness may be able to raise additional capital and retain assets. 
"A fire sale wouldn't be appropriate now," said Mr. Harrison. 

   Meanwhile, Hooker's sagging fortunes have prompted Robert Sakowitz, president and chief executive of Sakowitz Inc., a Houston retailer, to actively search for investment capital to acquire Hooker's 80% stake in his company. 
"We don't want to be tarred by the problems affecting Bonwit Teller and B. Altman," said Mr. Sakowitz. 
"Our company is completely separate." 

   As provisional liquidator, Mr. Harkness said his first task will be to "assess the financial position of the company and to recommend the best possible action to be followed in maintaining the worth of the company's assets." 
A provisional liquidator, with creditor approval, can either restructure or liquidate assets and distribute proceeds to creditors. 

   Some bankers in Australia are optimistic that Mr. Harkness can salvage Hooker. 
Among other tactics, a court appointee can freeze debt repayments, including interest, thus improving a company's cash-flow position enabling it to operate. 

   Hooker shares closed yesterday on the Australian Stock Exchange at 25 Australian cents, down 11 cents. 
Early this year, the stock traded above A$2. 

   As of March 31, Hooker had total liabilities of A$1.92 billion. 
Of that, Australian and other international banks are owed A$1.6 billion. 
In addition to A$900 million in unsecured loans that were covered by the now-defunct debt accord, Hooker owes A$700 million that is secured against specific Hooker assets, mainly in the U.S. 

   Tensions increased between Hooker and its bank lenders over the past month and negotiations broke down Tuesday mainly over Hooker's need to quickly raise A$55 million in additional credit lines from a group of its biggest bank lenders. 

</TEXT>

<HL> FAA Moves to Fine
@  Boeing Co. $200,000
@  On Wiring Problems </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> BA </CO>
<IN> AEROSPACE (ARO) </IN>
<GV> TRANSPORTATION DEPARTMENT (TRN) </GV>
<DATELINE> SEATTLE  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Federal Aviation Administration proposed a $200,000 civil fine against Boeing Co.'s Boeing Commercial Airplanes unit for allegedly failing to report wiring problems in some of its jetliners "in an appropriate and timely manner." 

   In one instance, Royal Brunei Airlines reported to Boeing on Aug. 3, 1988, that a cargo compartment fire-extingishing system on a Boeing 757 jetliner had been miswired. 
On Oct. 18, 1988, Delta Air Lines reported finding a similar problem on a Boeing 757, according to the FAA. 

   The FAA contends that in both cases, "Boeing failed to transmit to FAA a report of these miswirings in an acceptable form until Jan. 4, 1989. 
It appears Boeing violated federal aviation regulations by not reporting to the agency within 24 hours after determining defects existed." 

   Boeing said the FAA's allegation is "unmerited and the threatened enforcement action is unfortunate and unnecessary." 
It added that it will "decline to offer the suggested $200,000 levy." 

   Boeing has 15 days to respond to the FAA's settlement offer or provide additional information concerning the alleged violations. 

   The aerospace concern asserts that it properly reported the problems to the FAA in a timely manner, and that Boeing was "responsive to the situation and acted aggressively in cooperation with the FAA to assure that any miswiring was found and corrected." 

   Boeing said that although there haven't been any failures of the fire-extinguishing systems in service, it has redesigned them at the suggestion of the FAA. 

   Boeing has been doing extra tests on the systems since first learning about numerous cases of faulty wiring and plumbing on its entire family of aircraft built after 1980. 

   As of this spring, 94 separate instances of flawed fire-control systems had come to light. 
Of these, 15 were identified in the engines or cargo compartments of the more than 200 757s in service. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Technology:
@  Lotus to Begin Shipments
@  Of Its Release 2.2 Program </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> LOTS </CO>
<IN> SOFTWARE (SOF) </IN>
<DATELINE> CAMBRIDGE, Mass.  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Lotus Development Corp. said it will start shipping 1-2-3 Release 2.2 today, weeks earlier than generally anticipated. 

   The software company, which ran into major delays upgrading its bestselling spreadsheet program, finally started shipping Release 3.0 last month. 
Release 2.2 is a less ambitious upgrade, designed for people with older IBM compatible personal computers with smaller memories than that needed for Release 3.0. 

   While Release 2.2 includes improved graphics and better database features, as does Release 3.0, it lacks the bigger program's three-dimensional abilities that let a user display a series of spreadsheets that can be consolidated. 

   Lotus officials generally expect Release 3.0 to outsell Release 2.0, but many analysts and software distributors think Release 2.2 will be more popular. 
Release 3.0 has earned favorable reviews in several computer industry publications, and consultants say corporate customers like the improvements. 

   Getting Release 2.2 out the door will help Lotus's credibility. 
It will also help its earnings, because 30 days after the shipment, it will stop offering free upgrades to buyers of Release 2.0.

<HL> Du Pont Reports
@  Profit Rose 12%
@  In Second Quarter
@  ----
@  By Richard Koenig
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> DD </CO>
<IN> EARNINGS (ERN)
CHEMICALS, PLASTICS (CHM) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Du Pont Co. reported that second-quarter profit rose 12% with operating gains in all business segments except its strike-hurt coal operations. 

   The chemical company earned $714 million, or $3.02 a share, compared with $639 million, or $2.67 a share, a year earlier on more shares outstanding. 
Sales were $9.28 billion, up 11% from $8.34 billion. 

   Those results were in line with Wall Street estimates, after accounting for nonrecurring items in the latest and the year-earlier quarters. 

   "Everything was a little bit better than we expected, except coal" said Leslie Ravitz, a chemical-industry analyst at Salomon Brothers Inc. 

   If not for the nonrecurring items, per-share earnings would have grown 35% in the second quarter, despite more signs that the economy is weakening. 
That would support Du Pont's contention that its change in business mix and its belt-tightening has left it less vulnerable to cyclical swings. 
Still, analysts expect the company's pace to slow in the second half. 

   Edgar Woolard Jr., Du Pont's chairman, acknowledged in a statement that the year's last half may be hurt by "a somewhat weaker U.S. economy and the stronger dollar." 
A stronger dollar reduces profit that the Wilmington, Del.-based company reports when translating overseas results into dollars. 

   In New York Stock Exchange composite trading yesterday, Du Pont shares rose 37.5 cents to close at $115 a share. 

   In the latest quarter, the sharpest improvement came in industrial products, where profit soared 93% to $158 million. 
The segment was helped by strong results for white pigments, which are used as whiteners in paper, plastics and paints. 

   Du Pont has benefited from a pigments manufacturing process that satisfies tightening environmental regulation as well as from continuing strong demand. 
Some competitors have had to close plants or take on the expense of altering them. 

   In the fibers segment, profit rose 19% to $182 million, as higher volume and selling prices for most textile products more than offset higher raw material costs. 
And in Du Pont's diversified businesses segment, profit was $166 million, up 18%, mostly because of strong demand for agricultural products. 

   Du Pont's Consolidation Coal Co. confronted striking members of the United Mine Workers only toward the end of the second quarter. 
Although volumes and prices rose from year-earlier levels, so did costs for each unit of coal shipped because there remained fixed costs at the struck mines. 
Profit edged down 2% to $48 million. 
The miners struck in sympathy with workers at Pittston Co., which has refused to sign an industry labor contract. 

   Profit also fell at Du Pont's Conoco Inc. oil company, by 16% to $115 million, but only because of nonrecurring items. 
Operating profit climbed 66% on higher crude oil prices, refined product margins and natural gas volumes. 

   Likewise, the polymers business showed a 4.7% drop in profit to $142 million because the year-earlier quarter included a $28 million gain on technology sales. 
On an operating basis, however, the segment posted a 17% profit increase in strong markets for ethylene-based polymers, used in packaging, among other things, and for fluoropolymers, such as Teflon. 

   In the first half, net increased 18% to $1.45 billion, or $6.10 a share, from $1.23 billion, or $5.12 a share, a year earlier. 
After the exclusion of nonrecurring items, per-share earnings climbed 30%. 
Sales were $17.95 billion, up 11% from $16.2 billion. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Marketing &amp; Media -- Advertising:
@  Airlines Win Federal Support
@  For Way Air Fares Are Advertised
@  ----
@  By Thomas R. King </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> HAS ROST GREY </CO>
<IN> AIRLINES (AIR)
MARKETING, ADVERTISING (MKT)
RECREATION, ENTERTAINMENT, TOYS, MOVIES, PHOTOGRAPHY, SPORTS (REC)
FINANCIAL, ACCOUNTING, LEASING (FIN) </IN>
<GV> TRANSPORTATION DEPARTMENT (TRN) </GV>
<TEXT>
   In the ongoing turf battle over who has the right to regulate advertising -- the federal government or the states? -- score one for the federal government. 

   Over the past few months, state attorneys general have been cracking down on advertising they consider misleading. 
California and Texas have been especially aggressive, with both focusing on airline advertising and demanding that advertised air fares include all charges, not just a base price. 
The states contend that low advertised prices are too often misleading because they don't add in all charges. 

   But yesterday, the Department of Transportation aimed a slap straight in the face of the state attorneys general. 
The DOT proposed amending its rules to specifically allow certain charges to be listed separately from the base ticket price. 
In a statement, DOT said the "changes would reflect the Department's longstanding enforcement policy." 
And it said ads with separate charges wouldn't be "unfair or deceptive. . . . 
The Department's experience indicates that these types of ads do not cause passengers harm." 

   Advertisers, naturally enough, welcomed the move. 
The attorneys general "went way too far to protect the marketplace for consumers, and we're pleased that the DOT acknowledges that fact," said Daniel Jaffe, an executive vice president with the Association of National Advertisers, a trade group. 

   But the state attorneys general were quick to lash out at the proposal and they vowed to fight back. 
"In this act, the DOT has in essence thrown down the gauntlet," fumed Steve Gardner, an assistant state attorney general for Texas. 

   Indeed, the DOT's proposal is just the latest salvo in an increasingly pitched battle between the federal government and the state attorneys general. 
The National Association of Attorneys General has proposed strict rules regulating rental-car advertising as well as airline ads. 
Advertisers, and increasingly the federal government, contend that the attorneys general have no right to regulate national advertising. 
They contend the attorneys general are usurping federal authority. 

   Advertisers also insist that if states are allowed to regulate advertising, a resulting patchwork of different rules in different states would make national ad campaigns impossible. 
"If you want to have a national economy, then you have to have national regulation or you get economic chaos," contends Mr. Jaffe. 

   And in fact, the attorneys general have been reined in lately. 
A Texas court earlier this year temporarily banned the state attorney general from regulating airline advertising. 
What's more, Texas's Mr. Gardner says the attorneys general are feeling the heat from the food and automotive industries, both of which are also chafing at state attempts at regulation. 

   "I was very hopeful that President Bush would herald a change in the attitude we saw in the Reagan administration," which favored a laissez-faire stance when it came to regulating advertising, Mr. Gardner says. 
But instead, the Bush administration is "carrying on with business as usual . . . not giving a whit to the rights of consumers," he contends. 
He says he would file suit against the DOT if its proposal becomes a rule. 

   The attorneys general are especially infuriated because DOT made its proposal in response to a request for a ruling by the Air Transport Association -- an airline trade association whose members would benefit from the proposal. 

   The states' attempts to crack down on airline advertising included a complaint filed in March by California's attorney general. 
The complaint cited a Trans World Airlines advertisement promoting "The Drop-everything fare: $219 London." 
Small print at the bottom of the ad made clear the round-trip purchase requirements and additional fees; given the restrictions, the attorney general said, the cheapest price for the package would actually be $461. 
A TWA spokesman says the ad "is not deceptive," and adds that the ad clearly states the price is based on a round-trip purchase. 

   Such advertising, if the department's proposal is adopted, would apparently be fair play. 
"The advertising of a fare that is not being sold is a deceptive practice," protests M. Howard Wayne, deputy attorney general in California. 
"If the DOT has acquiesced in that, I don't believe they're acting in the interests of the consuming public." 

   ---

   Hasbro Toy Accounts 

   Hasbro Inc. awarded the ad accounts for toy assignments -- worth $15 million to $20 million -- to ad agency Jordan, McGrath, Case &amp; Taylor, New York. 

   Jordan McGrath emerged the victor in two separate reviews, one for a girls' toy assignment and one for a number of board games in the firm's Milton Bradley Co. division. 
Executives indicated Jordan McGrath's track record with Tonka Corp., an account it serviced from 1982 until May of this year, was a credential that played in its favor. 

   Other agencies competing for the girls' toy assignment included Hill, Holliday, Connors, Cosmopulos, Boston, and D'Arcy Masius Benton &amp; Bowles, St. Louis. 
Scali, McCabe, Sloves, New York, a unit of WPP Group, was the only other contender for the Milton Bradley assignments. 

   The accounts were previously handled at Griffin Bacal, the New York shop that is Hasbro's only other agency, servicing more than $120 million of its ad business. 
A spokesman for Pawtucket, R.I.-based Hasbro said the decision to hire Jordan McGrath wasn't a reflection on its principal agency, but simply that it wanted to add an agency to service its burgeoning games businesses. 

   ---

   Ad Notes. . . . 


   NEW ACCOUNTS: Ross Stores Inc., Newark, Calif., awarded its $8 million to $10 million account to Ketchum Advertising, San Francisco. 
Mojo MDA, also of San Francisco, handled the account before its merger with Chiat/Day. . . . 
Cannon Mills, New York, named Lintas:MCM, New York, agency of record for its sheets and towels account. 
It had been handled inhouse. 
Billings weren't disclosed. . . . 


   Christian Dior Fashion &amp; Accessories named Grey Advertising, Paris, to handle its international ad account. . . . 
Sheaffer Eaton Inc., Lincoln, R.I., named Cipriani Kremer Advertising, Boston, agency of record for its pens, key holders and emblematic jewelry. 
Billings weren't disclosed. . . .

It had been handled by Glazen Advertising, Cleveland. . . . 


   American Floral Marketing Council, Alexandria, Va., elected to keep Earle Palmer Brown Cos., Bethesda, Md., for its $3.5 million account after a review. . . .

<DOCNO> WSJ890727-0088 </DOCNO>
<DD> = 890727 </DD>
<AN> 890727-0088. </AN>
<HL> McDonald &amp; Co. Investments </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> MDD </CO>
<IN> BUYBACKS, REDEMPTIONS, SWAP OFFERS (BBK) </IN>
<DATELINE> CLEVELAND  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   McDonald &amp; Co. Investments said its board approved the continuation of an open-market securities-repurchase program. 

   Under the program, initiated in July 1987 and set to expire Monday, the company purchased 427,600 shares of common stock and $2,025,000 principal amount of its 8% convertible subordinated debentures due March 1, 2011. 
The company has also purchased 359,790 shares in a tender offer which expired March 3. 

   The extension authorizes the investment company to buy as many as 100,000 shares of common stock and up to $1,000,000 principal amount of the outstanding debentures, depending on market conditions and subject to regulatory restrictions. 
The authorization expires July 31, 1990.

<HL> International
@  ---
@  The Road to European Unity -- 1992:
@  Unity Drive Feeds EC Bureaucrats' Power
@  ---
@  European Commission Is
@  Branching Into New Areas
@  ----
@  By Philip Revzin
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> EUROP </CO>
<DATELINE> BRUSSELS  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Next Tuesday when all self-respecting Europeans should be starting their monthlong vacations, top officials of the European Community's Commission will be convening a 23-nation meeting to work out a two-year program of food aid to Poland and Hungary. 

   This meeting is a remarkable example of Europe's new activism. 
For one thing, it is a political exercise being carried out by the normally apolitical European Commission. 
For another, it is taking place just two weeks after the seven-nation economic summit in Paris asked the EC to organize the food program on behalf of the industrialized world. 
"Europe doesn't have a government, but the commission is being asked to do more and more things governments do," said an elated commission official after the summit. 
The commission has recently proposed setting up a European Environment Agency, is writing a European Charter of Fundamental Social Rights, and has the task of representing EC member states at GATT trade negotiations. 
When it discusses possible quotas for U.S. television shows being broadcast on European airwaves, Hollywood and Washington shudder. 

   "It's like a tree," says another commission official. 
"At first you can see the trunk shoot up, and now the roots and branches are extending in all directions very fast, anchoring it solidly." 

   That the EC's bureaucrats are branching so fast into previously forbidden areas is the latest startling indication of how quickly Europe is getting its economic and political acts together. 
As the aid to Eastern Europe is being organized other bureaucrats will meet to work on plans leading to a single European currency managed by a system of central banks similar to the U.S. Federal Reserve System. 

   Although this is much more controversial than food aid to Poland, even the French are giving up some of their summer holiday to push things along. 
As chairmen of the EC's decision making Council of Ministers for the second half of this year, the French are stepping on the gas on monetary union, which they favor. 
They hope to have a workable plan by September and gain provisional endorsement at a summit meeting in Strasbourg in December. 
Next July, when a previously approved law goes into effect freeing the movement of capital throughout the EC, the French will call a treaty-amending conference to implement monetary union. 
This would mean real movement toward common European economic policy-making by the end of 1992. 

   That of course, is the date by which the EC's other bold plan, to abolish most inter-European barriers to the movement of people, money, goods and services, is supposed to be completed. 
Progress on this, too, has been faster than most Europeans had forecast. 
The commission has written more than 80% of the directives needed to bring down the barriers, and the Council of Ministers, the representatives of all 12 member countries who make the real decisions, have approved more than half. 

   Some controversial matters remain unresolved, such as how to harmonize the rates of value-added tax, a sort of national sales tax, that currently range from zero to 38% according to the country and the goods sold. 
Governments of high-tax countries fear losing business to low-tax countries, but also fear cutting tax rates will cut government revenue. 
Governments of low-tax countries fear taking the unpopular step of raising rates. 
But even here a compromise looks likely soon, allowing for a fairly wide range of tax rates. 

   Businessmen have moved faster than anybody predicted. 
Mergers and strategic alliances abound. 
In certain key industries, such as food, the 1992 preparation game is nearly over. 
BSN S.A. of France, for instance, made a preemptive bid to snap up RJR-Nabisco Inc.'s European cookie businesses before rivals Nestle S.A. and Unilever Group could even sniff at them. 

   "There's no question now that 1992 is a major dynamic force, helped by the fact the world economy is healthy," says commission official Hubert Onidi. 
"We were a little lucky to have launched the drive for the single market in the middle of a world-wide boom." 

   The enthusiasm and imagination generated by the 1992 plan are carrying over into areas like common political initiatives and a common currency that aren't part of the 1992 plan. 
The recent EC summit in Madrid reached unanimous political agreement on such diverse political matters as condemning Chinese repression and supporting closer East-West relations. 

   "The perspective of 1992 has created a tremendous psychological change in every actor in the EC," says Raymond Barre, a former prime minister of France and former vice president of the European Commission. 
"Habits and structures are changing in ways unthinkable five years ago because everybody is talking about the single market." 
Mr. Barre, for instance, now thinks economic and monetary union, EC jargon for common decision making leading to a common currency, is possible within 10 years. 
That's still not a view held by everybody in Europe, especially not British Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher. 
She supports the barrier-reducing single market plan, but thinks anything else -- like a single currency -- is unnecessary, would mean too much transfer of power from national governments to Brussels bureaucrats, and would distract attention from finishing the single market program. 
Nonetheless, the psychological pressure of 1992 has swept Mrs. Thatcher along into the process; she'll show up at the meetings on monetary union with alternate plans and hope to convince everybody else. 

   What almost nobody is talking about yet is what specific political structures might be needed to run a United States of Europe. 
Commission President Jacques Delors frequently ducks the question as premature. 
Mrs. Thatcher is holding out for a "Europe of nations," or a loose confederation within which national governments would maintain most of their powers. 
Former French President Valery Giscard d'Estaing envisions a more-federal Europe, with a president of the Council of Ministers apppointed for a five-year term whose "cabinet" would be composed of ministers from national governments. 
Others see national ministers sitting in a sort of Senate that would share power with the elected European Parliament. 

   Says Pascal Lamy, Mr. Delors's chief of staff: "We are building a federal Europe though we're not sure exactly what it is. 
There is no precedent for this sort of non-hegemonic unification." 

   He adds: "We're putting up the walls and other bits and pieces of a house even though we don't have a blueprint for what it's supposed to look like when its done." 
And the walls are going up ahead of schedule. 

</TEXT>

<DOCNO> WSJ890727-0086 </DOCNO>

<HL> Tea in Tehran:
@  Mrs. Khomeini Is
@  At Home Mourning
@  ---
@  Invited Guests Get to See
@  Ayatollah in a New Light
@  That May Presage Change
@  ----
@  By Geraldine Brooks
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> MDEST </CO>
<DATELINE> TEHRAN, Iran  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   At the Khomeinis', afternoon tea comes in a little glass, and Revolutionary Guards serve fruit on a plastic plate with a picture of two ducks on it. 

   Khadijeh, the 76-year-old widow of the Ayatollah Ruhollah Khomeini, sits in a corner of a courtyard, its high walls draped in black crepe. 
Guests -- women only -- leave their shoes at the gate and approach the widow with their condolences. 
Smiling, she offers a gnarled hand or a cheek to be kissed, then gestures to each of perhaps 30 visitors to join her, squatting on rugs on the ground. 

   Female Guards, their khaki tunics emblazoned with the fist-and-rifle emblem of their corps, pass the sugar bowl and platters of melon and dates. 

   "I'm sorry we have received you so simply," Mrs. Khomeini has her interpreter say. 
"But all through my husband's life, he insisted on simplicity." 

   The Khomeini home is indeed modest. 
Set in a northern Tehran suburb called Jamaran (which opponents like to point out means "place of the snakes"), the family quarters are spartan, almost shabby. 
A boxy, two-story building houses, in addition to Khadijeh, her 53-year-old son Ahmad and his wife and children. (Khadijeh's three daughters have their own homes but visit often. 
An older son died.) In one room, the thin mats used to sleep on are rolled up in a corner. 
A torn fly-screen dangles from a window; green paint flakes from the walls. 
In the kitchen, an old-fashioned stove and an electric samovar are the only appliances in evidence. 

   Off the courtyard, a striped curtain marks the passageway to the ayatollah's three-room suite -- bare but for a few books, a chair and a couch -- where he worked and slept. 
He joined the family to take his meals, stroll in the yard and watch television occasionally in the evening. 

   Even without the ayatollah, this little house remains a shrine. 
Weeks after his death, mourners still gather outside the house, weeping and praying. 
The balcony from which he greeted the faithful has had to be glassed in, so as to stop mourners who were clawing their way up to embrace his chair. 

   Mrs. Khomeini sometimes receives small groups of important women mourners. 
At such gatherings she, well-dressed, wears basic black. 
Only the grandchildren add a splash of color to the dark cluster of cone-shaped figures in their enveloping chadors. 
The children run about among the guests, oblivious to the eulogizing and occasional tearful outbursts. 

   "We have come to this place where the great imam used to breathe," sobs one of the guests. 
"We all have gathered here in this holy place to show our allegiance to his way." 

   Mrs. Khomeini nods her acknowledgment, wiping away an occasional tear. 
When her black veil slips a little, it reveals silver hair, which had apparently been colored with henna while her husband was alive. 
That isn't unusual among religious women in Iran. 
While they conceal their beauty in public, staying attractive for a husband is encouraged. 
In fact, plucked eyebrows are evidence that a woman is married. 

   A recent afternoon's guest list was a who's who of Iran's exported revolution: wives of the leaders of the Hezbollah or "Party of God," the most radical of Lebanon's Shiite factions; Turkish university students, visiting Tehran, who had been expelled from school for wearing Islamic veils; a South African Muslim who belongs to a radical, fundamentalist congregation whose key members are on trial for treason in South Africa. 
All had been invited to tea in Tehran because of their involvement in radical religion; they had been asked to come by the ayatollah's 48-year-old daughter, Zahra Mostafavi, for a two-day conference "to examine aspects of His Highness Imam Khomeini's personality." 

   The conference, featuring several meet-the-family get-togethers, appears to be part of a quiet post-mortem overhaul of the ayatollah's image, which is also getting some humanizing. 
Many pictures of him in public buildings nowadays show him hand-in-hand with a grandchild. 
One pose has him laughing as a toddler tries to push a forkful of food into his mouth. 

   Even more surprising to many Iranians has been the posthumous publication of his poetry. 
Among hard-line mullahs, verse of any kind is suspect. 
But the ayatollah's melodious, intricately rhymed poems are full of unexpected imagery, using, for instance, the intoxication of wine and the celebration of female beauty as metaphors for religious ecstasy. 

   Some Iranian academics and business people see a move to lessen the severity of the ayatollah's image as heralding some future relaxation of social strictures. 
The conference was apparently the brainchild of Mrs. Mostafavi. 
She teaches philosophy and heads the Iranian Women's Society. 
She has strongly endorsed Hashemi Rafsanjani's bid for the presidency. 
He, in turn, delivered the opening speech at the conference. 

   In that context, the family seemed eager to share all sorts of personal details about the ayatollah, ranging from how he wooed his bride to how he took his medicine toward the end of his life. 
Through the eyes of the women who lived with him, the West's bloodthirsty bete noire seems almost to be someone else. 
The man they see is a considerate husband, a caring father and a rather quirky old man, who was worried about germs and obsessive about punctuality. 

   "If I wanted to know the time, I would look at the imam rather than at the clock," says Fatima Tabatabai, the wife of his son Ahmad. 
"If he was reading Koran, I'd know it was 11:30. 
If he was taking a walk, I'd know it must be half past four." 

   The man who was to lead Iran didn't look like much of a catch to the wealthy parents of Khadijeh, when, as a 27-year-old theological student, he applied for her hand. 
His proposal might well have been rejected had the 16-year-old girl not had a dream in which the prophets appeared, praising her suitor. 

   Even so, moving from her affluent home into student penury was a wrench. 
"But he compensated her with his kindness," says Mrs. Mostafavi, who claims to have never heard her father ask his wife to bring him so much as a glass of water. 
And when their babies were born, he took his turn getting up in the night to feed them. 
Mrs. Mostafavi remembers just one argument between her parents -- over a rubber ball her mother bought for Ahmad. 
Her father didn't want the boy to have anything frivolous or distracting. 
Generally, though, the children were allowed to play as they liked, their father intruding only when he perceived physical or moral danger. 

   "If I wanted to play at a house, and he knew there was a boy there, he would say, 'Don't go there, play at home,'" Mrs. Mostafavi recalls. 
"You couldn't say, 'Come on, Dad, let me go,' because what he said was based on Islam, not on his own opinion." 

   The consequences of flouting Islam were made brutally clear. 
He once told Ahmad (according to Ahmad's wife): "I love you because you are obedient toward God. 
If you stand against God, I will stand against you, and if it is necessary according to the law, I will even send for your executioner." 

   He required frugality of his household. 
A Revolutionary Guard who has spent six years serving the family remembers him once noticing two pomegranate seeds that had fallen into the kitchen sink. 
"He wanted to know why we were wasting food," she says. 

   He was equally careful about cleanliness, wearing a specially made plastic and toweling bib at meals and using separate spoons to select pills from each of his medicine bottles, rather than taking out the tablets with his fingers. 

   Even with the ayatollah gone, the family still keeps to a strict schedule. 
The first note of the meuzzin's call to sunset prayer gives the signal that the tea party is over. 
Mrs. Khomeini, like her husband, prefers to pray as soon as the call comes, rather than waiting for a time as the law permits. 

   Reminiscing about the ayatollah, the family weeps a good deal -- disobeying him in that, if in little else. 

   His daughter-in-law Fatima recalls his words as he left the house for the hospital and the surgery from which he did not recover. 
"I'm going, and I'm not coming back," he told the family. 
"Don't cry for me. 
It is God's will." 

</TEXT>

<HL> 1st American, Comfed
@  Post Big Losses; Blame
@  Real-Estate Problems </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> FAMB CFK </CO>
<IN> EARNINGS (ERN)
DIVIDENDS (DIV) </IN>
<DATELINE> BOSTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Two mid-sized Massachusetts bank-holding companies announced big losses, and blamed them on continued deterioration in the New England real-estate market. 

   1st American Bancorp, Boston, reported a second-quarter loss of $64.1 million, suspended its dividend, and said it is negotiating a "bulk sale" of its nonperforming real-estate assets. 

   The loss reflects its decision to add $72 million to its loss reserves, partly in anticipation of the asset sale. 
The bank-holding company had net income of $2.3 million, or 22 cents a share, a year ago. 

   1st American said its capital after the special provision is $61.7 million, or 10.02% of assets, including a $10 million tax benefit. 
Total assets on June 30 were $615.5 million. 1st American paid a dividend of 15 cents a share, most recently in June. 

   For the six months, 1st American posted a loss of $60.6 million, compared with profit of $5.6 million, or 53 cents a share in the year-ago period. 

   In national over-the-counter trading, 1st American closed at $3 a share, down 12.5 cents. 

   Separately, Comfed Bancorp, Lowell, Mass., posted a $3.7 million loss for its fiscal-first quarter, ended June 30, after a loan-loss provision of $3.5 million. 
In the year-ago quarter, Comfed had net income of $509,000, or six cents a share. 
The company has $1.7 billion in assets. 

   In American Stock Exchange composite trading, Comfed closed at $3.125 a share, up 12.5 cents.

<HL> FCC Fines USAir
@  Unit for Interrupting
@  Airport Conversations </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> U </CO>
<IN> AIRLINES (AIR) </IN>
<GV> FEDERAL COMMUNICATIONS COMMISSION (FCC)
JUSTICE DEPARTMENT (JUS) </GV>
<DATELINE> WASHINGTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Federal Communications Commission fined USAir and a subsidiary for deliberate interruptions of conversations between Philadelphia airport's control tower and pilots for Eastern Airlines. 

   The fines against USAir and Jetstream International Airlines were part of a joint enforcement effort by the FCC, the Federal Aviation Administration and the Federal Bureau of Investigation to eliminate intentional interference at several airports nationwide. 
Each airline was fined $2,000, according to the Associated Press. 
USAir is a unit of USAir Group Inc. based in Arlington, Va. 

   The illegal conversations pose a severe safety hazard, and the agencies have been alarmed by a flurry of jamming over the past two weeks, the FCC said. 

   A spokesman for USAir would say only that the company is conducting an internal investigation into the matter. 

   Most of the activity has occurred at the Philadelphia airport. 
In recent days, at least 20 conversations between the control tower and Eastern flights have been jammed or interrupted with harassing comments aimed at Eastern's pilots, said Jeffrey Young, an engineer in the enforcement division of the FCC's field operations bureau. 

   Pilots of Eastern, a unit of Texas Air Corp. have been on strike since March 4. 

   The agencies are analyzing tape recordings of the conversations, and the next step will be trying to match individual pilots with the illegal transmissions, Mr. Young said. 
Action against individuals will be up to the FAA and FBI. Interfering with airport communications carries hefty penalties. 
The maximum criminal penalty is a $250,000 fine and a jail term of 10 years, while the maximum civil fine is $2,000 per violation and revocation of FCC licenses.

<DOCNO> WSJ890727-0083 </DOCNO>
<DD> = 890727 </DD>
<AN> 890727-0083. </AN>
<HL> Late Arrivals
@  Score Guiding
@  Client Assets
@  ----
@  By Matthew Winkler and William Power
@  Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> SB Z.CSU </CO>
<IN> SECURITIES INDUSTRY (SCR) </IN>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Goldman, Sachs &amp; Co. and Salomon Brothers Inc. are finding a bonanza in the money management game, despite their belated arrival. 

   Two months after hiring a top money manager and some of his colleagues from rival First Boston Corp., fledgling Salomon Asset Management has already lured $400 million from institutional investors; it says it has agreements to manage nearly $1 billion more within the next 12 months. 

   And two months after Goldman decided to re-enter the business of managing stock portfolios, which it abandoned in the 1970s, Goldman Sachs Asset Management has already been offered a tidy $200 million from rich individuals, industry officials say. 
That's even though Goldman isn't going to actually accept any money for its new venture until Labor Day. 

   Goldman already manages $16 billion in money market and bond funds, a traditionally sleepy business for the firm; but it was the last big securities firm to stubbornly resist stock management. 

   Both Goldman and Salomon are "making the statement that {securities} sales and trading are going to be less profitable than they were in the past," says Michael F. Holland, chairman of Salomon Asset Management, who was hired away from First Boston in May. 
Salomon and Goldman are betting that money management is the wave of the future even though, as Goldman's top money manager Leon Cooperman concedes, it's "an overcrowded market with plenty of competition." 

   In some ways, the two big Wall Street firms are getting into the money-management game very late. 
The highly competitive money-management industry is showing signs of stagnating after 15 years of tremendous growth. 

   Already, Shearson Lehman Hutton Inc., after years of swallowing the money-management divisions of other firms, manages $105 billion. 
Donaldson, Lufkin &amp; Jenrette Inc., one of the first Wall Street securities firms to plow into money management, has $30 billion in its Alliance Capital Management affiliate. 
And Morgan Stanley &amp; Co.'s 14-year-old money management operation has more than $25 billion; that doesn't include another $1.8 billion in leveraged buy-out funds. 

   For years, Salomon and Goldman resisted such forays out of fear of competing with big institutional clients who were money managers themselves. 
Some critics see a built-in conflict when Wall Street securities firms manage money; they claim that the firms' own traders could unfairly benefit by getting a first look at important stock research by the firms' analysts. 

   But the big securities firms are hard pressed for new ways to make money as they watch their traditional trading and brokerage revenues dry up. 
Potential money-management fees dwarf the diminishing commissions that Wall Street firms are getting from handling trades for others. 

   "These firms need asset-management products to sell to very wealthy people" and institutions, says Michael L. Goldstein, a securities-industry analyst at Sanford C. Bernstein &amp; Co. Mr. Goldstein envisions Salomon sticking to a bond-oriented money management business, while Goldman establishes a presence on the stock side. 
Salomon is among the biggest bond trading firms. 
Goldman is known for its stock research and trading. 

   Mr. Goldstein sees the Wall Street firms using their new money-management operations as a springboard to other, highly sophisticated money-management products, like specialized bond portfolios, especially for foreign investors. 

   Scott Pierce, chairman of Prudential Asset Management Co. and former president of E.F. Hutton &amp; Co., is convinced Salomon and Goldman "will be successful" in money management. 
"I don't think we'll have too much come out of our hide, but there's no question the market is becoming more competitive." 
Prudential Asset Management, a unit of giant Prudential Insurance Co. of America, manages $90 billion for investors. 

   The demand for money management talent has recharged competitive juices among Wall Street firms. 
Despite the loss in May of its money-management team led by Mr. Holland, First Boston insists it didn't lose any accounts and "increased its assets under management to $7 billion from $5.2 billion since Michael left," says James L. Freeman, the new First Boston asset-management chief. 
Nevertheless, "Michael knew how to make the First Boston" sales, trading and research network work successfully in luring new money and managing it, Mr. Freeman says. 

   Mr. Cooperman -- who took over Goldman's money-management unit after 13 years as its highly regarded research director -- declines to comment on the industry estimate that $200 million has already been offered him by rich individuals. 
But he says Goldman has had "many inquiries" from potential investors, mostly individuals rather than institutions. 
"The feedback has been encouraging," Mr. Cooperman says. 

   Salomon hopes "to identify areas where there isn't huge competition and that play directly to our global resources," Mr. Holland says. 
"There is demand" for Salomon's traditional trading and research prowess in guiding clients attracted to such sophisticated investments as high-yield, high-risk junk bonds, mortgage-backed securities and highly specialized stock trading strategies, he says. 

   Nevertheless, the long-term picture is unclear. 
Salomon and Goldman may have done well initially in their new money-management ventures because they can tap into their big customer bases. 
But, some analysts say, the competition only seems to be increasing. 

</TEXT>
