<DOCNO> WSJ890727-0082 </DOCNO>
<DD> = 890727 </DD>
<AN> 890727-0082. </AN>
<HL> Economy:
@  Talks on Capital-Gains Tax Compromise
@  Held by Rostenkowski, Top Bush Aides
@  ----
@  By Jeffrey H. Birnbaum
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> TAXES </CO>
<IN> ECONOMIC NEWS (ECO) </IN>
<GV> EXECUTIVE (EXE)
CONGRESS (CNG) </GV>
<DATELINE> WASHINGTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Rep. Dan Rostenkowski and top Bush administration officials are negotiating a compromise tax package that would include some sort of reduction in levies on capital gains. 

   The talks, if successful, would get President Bush and the House Democratic leaders out of a political jam. 
The two sides have been at loggerheads for a week over whether to give a preferential rate to capital gains. 

   That stalemate continues. 
But the fact that the negotiations are being held at all makes it increasingly likely that the House Ways and Means Committee will approve some sort of special tax treatment on the sale of capital assets. 

   President Bush has lobbied for, and so far won, a majority of votes on the committee for a reduction in the tax rate on capital gains. 
But the Democrats, led by House Speaker Thomas Foley of Washington state, and Rep. Rostenkowski of Illinois, have said such a cut is unacceptable because it would heavily favor the rich. 

   Now that they appear to be behind in the vote count, however, the speaker and Rep. Rostenkowski are offering up a more subtle change as a compromise. 
They are seeking to reduce gains on capital assets by indexing them to inflation before those gains are taxed. 
The indexing proposal is the centerpiece of a proposal that Rep. Rostenkowski has been discussing with Treasury Secretary Nicholas Brady and White House Budget Director Richard Darman. 

   The political stakes in the disagreement are high. 
This is the biggest initial test of the Foley speakership. 
But so far, the two sides haven't come to terms. 
According to Rep. Rostenkowski, the administration officials are still seeking a cut in the tax rate, probably through the use of a tax exclusion, while he is still pushing indexing. 
"We're all working toward the same goal. 
We all want harmony," Rep. Rostenkowski said yesterday. 
"What's keeping us apart is rates as opposed to indexing." 

   Rep. Rostenkowski, a Democrat, explained that administration officials have so far sought to give capital gains a preferential tax rate, much like a proposal advanced by Rep. Edgar Jenkins (D., Ga.). 
Mr. Rostenkowski added ominously, "I don't know that rates are even negotiable" for the House Democratic leadership. 

   Mr. Jenkins's plan, which is supported by the president, would allow individuals to exclude from taxation 30% of their gain from the sale or exchange of long-held assets from July 20, 1989, through Dec. 31, 1991. 
The exclusion would make the effective top rate on capital gains 19.6%, compared with the current top rate of as much as 33%. 
After 1991, the top effective tax rate wouldn't exceed 28%, and the assets would be indexed for inflation. 

   President Bush's proposal has a 45% exclusion, creating a top rate of 15%. 
The president's plan wouldn't cover real estate or timber; the Jenkins plan would. 

   Speaker Foley and Rep. Rostenkowski have sought essentially to replace the rate reduction with indexing. 
While it does lower taxes on capital gains, they see it as more equitable to lower-income taxpayers and more politically palatable to Democrats than introducing a special rate. 
Rep. Rostenkowski argued that the indexing idea is one "all can all claim victory on." 

   But he warned that so far the warring sides haven't accepted his reasoning. 
Some Democrats in the House, for example, oppose any type of capital-gains tax cut. 
"Everybody seems to be worried about saving face," he said. 

   For his part, Mr. Jenkins said he is open to "possible solutions to work out differences." 
But he expressed confidence that his proposal commands an unshakable majority in both the committee and on the House floor. 
"I'd like to vote. 
That's all I want," he said. 
"I'm interested in a completed project all the way through the process." 

   Rep. Rostenkowski said he doesn't intend to call for a vote on any capital-gains proposal until an agreement is reached or it is clear that one can't be. 
"It doesn't have to come up next week or even the week after," he said. 

   But some sort of special treatment for capital gains probably is in the wind. 
"Any compromise," Rep. Jenkins said, "would have to have something on capital gains." 

</TEXT>

<HL> Turn the Valdez Cleanup Over to Mother Nature
@  ----
@  By Wayne Owens </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> XON </CO>
<IN> PETROLEUM (PET)
ENVIRONMENT (ENV) </IN>
<GV> CONGRESS (CNG)
TRANSPORTATION DEPARTMENT (TRN) </GV>
<TEXT>
   After private, unannounced visits last week to a dozen Prince William Sound locations with a fisherman friend on his boat, I concluded that the value of the massive spill-cleanup effort lies primarily in public relations, not just for Exxon but also for Alaskan and federal officials and for Congress. 

   I have a special interest in these events, because as a member of the House Committee that wrote the Alaskan Pipeline Act in 1973, I heard Exxon and Alyeska officials maintain that there would never be a serious oil spill. 
But, they assured us, if a spill ever did occur, state-of-the-art technology would be ready to clean it up without serious damage to animals, fish or landscape. 
Their talk turned out to be cheap, but their overconfidence has cost a terrible sum. 

   An astonishing fact emerged from the House Interior Committee's hearings during our visit to Alaska two months ago: When the accident occurred, Alyeska and Exxon officials were not prepared to implement their contingency plans to clean up oil spills because they had believed the computers that told them such an accident could happen only once every 241 years. 

   Alyeska made no serious effort at containment even though for three days after the accident the weather kept most of the crude in the vicinity of the Valdez. 
Had Alyeska been able to implement its contingency plan, almost all of the spill could have been recovered within 72 hours, according to a study by the Alaskan Department of Environmental Conservation. 
Instead, Alyeska fiddled away the first three days, and then tossed responsibility off to Exxon. 

   The public was understandably infuriated by the spill, and officials -- company and government -- panicked. 
Whatever could be done, at whatever cost, was begun. 
This week Exxon estimated its costs at $1.28 billion, making the spill the world's most costly disaster. 
Three thousand cleanup workers, 7,000 support workers, 50 landing craft, 27 barges and many hundreds of other boats are involved. 

   What is all that money accomplishing? 
Not much. 
Worse, workers may be doing more harm than good to the fragile bays and beaches of Prince William Sound. 
The floating pools and puddles of oil I saw two months ago have been cleaned up. 
But during my quiet visits last week, the massive cleaning operations on rocks and beaches appeared to be reaping little return while causing significant environmental degradation. 

   On Eleanor Island I found nearly 500 men and women working at seemingly fruitless tasks: spraying rocks with hot water from high-pressure hoses without visible success and deploying high-tech skimmers that don't skim and supersuckers that don't suck. 
They were using some 35 boats and barges in previously serene Northwest Bay, which used to be disturbed only by an occasional fishing boat. 
Now the bay's abundant birds and other wildlife are buffeted by a massive, noisy 24-hour assault. 
Rocks and sand are gouged, dredged and rearranged, and each day's efforts result in recovery of only seven barrels of oil, probably less than that spilled by the flotilla of "rescue" boats -- and that doesn't even take into consideration the waste discharged by the 500 workers. 
The efforts are as ineffective as they are intrusive. 

   Similarly, a multi-million dollar operation on Perry Island, set up aboard several barges to respond to fishermen's complaints of oil-soaked kelp, has had little success. 
It had extracted only two barrels of oil from 25 pans of kelp in three weeks, and now sits idle because search crews can't find any more oily weed to process. 

   And Smith Island, which I first visited in early May, has been twice "treated" (Exxon no longer uses the term "cleaned") but looks just the same: It's still covered by oil-soaked rocks. 

   Exxon, which spent almost nothing to prepare for a spill, is spending enormous sums trying to clean up. 
All willing local fishermen have been hired to keep them busy (and quiet) and every known or inventable device has been brought or constructed to try to remove the oil. 
The crude, hand-made implements seem to work the best, according to one Exxon foreman at Gold Bug Beach, as he demonstrated how a piece of screen attached to a rake handle could lift oil out where an expensive skimmer failed. 

   The U.S. Coast Guard, which oversees Exxon's cleanup, continues to hold Exxon to a completion date of Sept. 15 and says it will call Exxon back early next spring if necessary. 

   I wish it were possible to remove the oil and return Prince William Sound and the beaches of three national parks to their former pristine condition, but the truth is that the technology to accomplish that task simply doesn't exist. 
We should therefore stop inflicting more pain on the environment. 
Nature should now be given the chance to finish the cleanup job, with only occasional help from small work crews where oil surfaces or puddles. 

   At the same time, we should demand some benefit from this ecological tragedy. 
Exxon and Alyeska, which are jointly responsible, should pay for every feasible study of the spill's impact on animal, fish and landscape, and for repairs and improvements, whenever they are possible. 
Under tort law principles, which set monetary values for pain and suffering inflicted on individuals, let there be a generous award for the pain and suffering inflicted on Alaska's beaches and animal life. 
Large "punitive damages" should be assessed as well, to make an example of the carelessness of Exxon and Alyeska, with funds earmarked to improve fish and wildlife opportunities and for future protection. 
Exxon -- and its equally culpable partners at Alyeska -- must remain liable for any short-term and long-term damages that yet become apparent. 

   And in the meantime, let's try to avoid future spills by studying other methods of containerization. 
It'll cost more, but this problem has become intolerable -- as recent spills in Lousiana, Rhode Island and Delaware prove -- and will only get worse. 

   ----

   Rep. Owens (D., Utah) is a member of the Interior Committee.

<HL> Shell Canada, Husky
@  File Rival Proposals
@  To Develop Gas Field </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> T.SHC RD SC NVA </CO>
<IN> NATURAL GAS, PIPELINES (LNG) </IN>
<DATELINE> CALGARY, Alberta  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Shell Canada Ltd. and Husky Oil Ltd., a Nova Corp. of Alberta affiliate, filed rival plans with Alberta regulators to develop the estimated two trillion cubic feet of sour gas in the Caroline field for as much as 700 million Canadian dollars (US$580.2 million). 

   Shell, the Canadian affiliate of Royal Dutch/Shell Group, and Husky disagree about whether to build a new gas processing plant immediately adjacent to the Caroline field, 74 miles northwest of Calgary, or to expand Husky's Ram River gas plant, 33 miles away. 

   "It's going to come down to a question of producer rights vs. the environmental benefits of not building another plant," said a Husky spokesman. 
The industry tends to support a majority owner's right to develop a gas field the way it wants. 

   Shell, which owns 62% of the Caroline field, plans to spend C$700 million to develop the field and build a new plant immediately adjacent to it, because significant amounts of liquids need to be removed daily from the gas, which has an unusually high sulfur content. 
Husky contends its C$640 million development plan, calling for expansion of its facilities and construction of a 33-mile-long pipeline to reach them, would be more economical and environmentally sound. 

   Alberta regulators are concerned about the rapid growth of new gas processing plants, and might not want another one, a Husky spokesman said. 

   All but one of the 12 other Caroline field gas producers support Shell's new-plant proposal and oppose the plan by Husky, which has a 12% operating interest. 

   Both parties expect provincial regulators to hold a competitive hearing this fall to determine which project will go ahead. 

   The Caroline field, one of Canada's richest finds in the past 20 years, was discovered in 1987.

<HL> Stein Roe to Seek
@  Heavenly Results
@  With 'Star System'
@  ---
@  Fund Adviser Halts Decisions
@  By Committee, Now Uses
@  Solo Portfolio Managers
@  ----
@  By Michael Siconolfi
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> MUTUAL AND MONEY-MARKET FUNDS (FND) </IN>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   When it comes to managing mutual funds, one head is better than two. 

   That's the conclusion of Stein Roe &amp; Farnham, a Chicago investment adviser. 
Irked by the performance of some of its funds, Stein Roe has scuttled its habit of running funds by committee and now is relying on solo portfolio managers. 

   "Too many cooks spoil the stew -- especially in the investment game," says Michael Hirsch, chief investment officer of Republic National Bank of New York. 
"Very rarely does the committee system work." 

   The overwhelming majority of the nation's approximately 3,000 mutual funds are run by a single portfolio manager, a strategy sometimes known as the "star system." 
A handful of funds, such as Twentieth Century Investors, are successfully run by a team approach, with investment decisions made by several managers. 

   The major benefit of the committee approach is that funds rarely suffer when a manager leaves or changes strategy, as can occur with funds under the helm of a single captain. 
But critics contend that it's more effective to have one person calling the shots. 

   Performance was the driving factor in Stein Roe's decision, fund officials say. 
Stein Roe executives were unhappy with the returns of several funds, particularly the $200 million Stein-Roe Stock Fund. 
Although the fund has surged nearly 25% so far this year, its five-year performance barely matches the average of all general stock funds. 
The firm's equity funds typically have been solid bull-market funds but poor bear-market portfolios, industry executives say. 

   "The bottom line (of the switch) is to get better results," says Marshall Front, a Stein Roe partner. 

   Pay levels at Stein Roe also played a part. 
The firm's officials wanted to make it easier to link managers' compensation to performance. 
"To the extent you can do that, it motivates people better," Mr. Front says. 

   Stein Roe contends that the switch to single managers has gradually taken place over the past couple of years, even though on paper the funds have always been run by committee, which involved two or three managers. 
Stein Roe manages 16 funds with assets of $3.4 billion. 

   Separately, the firm recently shuffled several funds. 
It merged its Universe and Discovery portfolios, for instance, into its Capital Opportunities Fund to reduce expenses for some holders. 
And the company renamed its high-yield fund because it held relatively few junk bonds. 
The new name: SteinRoe Corporate Bond Fund-Investment Grade. 

   "Stein Roe has never been active in the junk market, and we didn't want the fund to represent itself that way," Mr. Front says.

<HL> Junk Bond Market Bracing
@  For Loss of Its S&amp;L Clients
@  ----
@  By Constance Mitchell
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> BOND MARKET NEWS (BON)
SAVINGS AND LOANS, THRIFTS, CREDIT UNIONS (SAL)
TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM) </IN>
<GV> CONGRESS (CNG) </GV>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The junk bond market, after years of heady expansion, is bracing to lose what were considered its most promising customers: the nation's savings institutions. 

   The result could be slower growth for the $190 billion junk-bond market, where thrifts now own $15 billion of the high-yield, high-risk bonds, or about 8% of the total outstanding. 

   Junk bond traders found one bright note in an accord reached yesterday in Washington by the House and Senate negotiators responsible for drafting the savings-and-loan bailout plan: The conferees agreed to stretch to Aug. 1, 1994, the deadline for thrifts to shed their junk-bond holdings. 

   "The good news is that it's five years, instead of two years" as proposed earlier, before the thrifts will have to be rid of their junk bonds, says Amy K. Minella, a high-yield bond manager at Deltec Securities Corp. in New York. 

   Junk bond customers "aren't panicking," she says. 
Still, "the market has been dead in the water lately, and this {proposal} means it will continue to stay that way." 
Some of her thrift customers who wanted to get out of the junk market have been selling large portions of their portfolios for months; others are biding their time. 

   Only 5% of the nation's S&amp;Ls currently own the high-yield, high-risk junk bonds. 
But thrifts had been targeted by junk bond salesmen as a promising growth area. 

   "Their portion of the junk bond market was growing, and that made them important buyers," says David Aylward, executive director of Alliance for Capital Access, a trade group of junk bond issuers. 
Yesterday's accord in Washington "means that the market will not be able to expand as it otherwise would have been able to." 

   Regulators concede that junk bonds haven't been the cause of any failure of a savings-and-loan asssociation. 
But there's considerable debate in Washington over whether the government should allow large amounts of federally insured deposits to be invested in high-risk bonds. 

   One unusual aspect of yesterday's agreement allows thrifts to sell their junk bonds to separately capitalized affiliates, which wouldn't put government-insured deposits at risk. 
It's unclear how many thrifts would have the financial muscle or desire to launch such affiliates. 
But "Wall Street will be out trying to do it," one junk-bond analyst predicts. 

   The junk bond market this year has been queasy, reflecting the potential impact of a slowing economy on companies burdened with heavy debts. 
Prices of junk bonds, which are owned by mutual funds and many other instituions, have stagnated in recent months while prices of other bonds, especially Treasurys, soared. 

   In the long run, some thrifts' profit margins could come under pressure as they are cut off from a market that has outperformed most of their other investments. 
One report, published by the government's own General Accounting Office, found that between 1985 and 1987, returns on high-yield bonds in S&amp;L portfolios were second only to those on credit cards, and they ranked ahead of residential mortgages, commercial and consumer lending and Treasury and investment grade bonds. 

   Bond market analysts are divided over whether the Washington plan will prompt many thrifts to bail out of junk bonds right away or, instead, lead to divestitures over a long period. 

   Garrett Moran, manager of the high yield department at Donaldson, Lufkin &amp; Jenrette, says a large percentage of the S&amp;Ls' junk holdings are in debt securities that will mature prior to the deadline or are likely to be redeemed by the issuer as interest rates continue to fall. 

   As a result, he estimates that the liquidations may amount to about $1 billion a year. 
"That's not too traumatic for a market the size of ours," he says, adding: "They won't have to sell in a distressed mode. 
It's a manageable problem." 

   But others aren't so sure. 
They note that legislators didn't address the question of whether thrifts would be forced to "mark to market" the value of their junk bonds to reflect current market prices -- a procedure that many financial institutions dread because it could reduce their earnings and their capital. 

   Under current accounting rules, thrifts that own bonds aren't required to mark them to market value as long as they have the ability and intention to hold those securities to maturity. 
However, if they are planning to sell the securities, their books are supposed to reflect current value. 

   If thrifts were forced to adopt mark-to-market accounting for junk bond investments, the thrifts could be hurt in two ways. 
First, a decline in the market value of junk securities could mean a charge against profit. 
Second, many thrifts probably would prefer to sell rather than hold securities that could have a widely fluctating effect on earnings -- which could lead many of them to dump junk bonds all at once, depressing prices and forcing S&amp;Ls to shed their bonds at a loss. 

   "It shouldn't be the cause of the legislation to impose hardships on institutions," says Alliance for Capital's Mr. Aylward, "but that concept seems to have gotten lost in the legislative process." 

</TEXT>

<HL> Technology &amp; Health:
@  Babies to Get
@  Wellcome Drug
@  At Hospitals </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> U.WCM </CO>
<IN> HEALTH CARE PROVIDERS, MEDICINE, DENTISTRY (HEA)
DRUG MANUFACTURERS (DRG) </IN>
<GV> FOOD AND DRUG ADMINISTRATION (FDA) </GV>
<DATELINE> WASHINGTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Food and Drug Administration authorized hospital-supervised use of an experimental drug designed to prevent respiratory distress syndrome, the leading cause of death and disability among premature babies. 

   The drug, made by Burroughs Wellcome Co. and called exosurf pediatric, has yet to receive marketing approval, but will be made available at about 600 hospitals through the FDA's investigational new drug application. 
Only 13 drugs, mostly related to acquired immune deficiency syndrome, have been authorized in this way. 

   "When there is adequate evidence that a drug is effective and safe for a serious or life-threatening disease, and when no satisfactory alternative exists, we can make the drug available" in this way, said FDA Commissioner Frank Young. 

   Respiratory distress syndrome is a condition caused when the immature lungs of an infant aren't yet producing the coating that prevents lungs from collapsing. 
Each year in the U.S., 250,000 babies are born prematurely, and 50,000 of them have the lung condition. 
About 5,000 die from the syndrome annually. 

   Hospitals that have state-of-the-art prenatal facilities for infants and for mothers will be able to administer the drug. 
Until marketing approval is granted, the drug will be provided free of charge. 

   Burroughs Wellcome, the U.S. unit of Wellcome PLC, London, hasn't yet set a price for the drug, and said it is too early to determine what kind of impact it will have on the drug market. 

   The drug has been in the clinical testing stage since 1986, but use in hospitals could speed up marketing approval, an FDA spokesperson said. 

   Wellcome said the drug has proved effective in reducing death rates from the syndrome in three clinical trials involving 2,000 infants. 
But in a study of 350 infants followed from at least nine months of age, the therapy hasn't yet proved effective at reducing handicaps or other complications associated with premature birth, the company said. 
Further study is necessary to make conclusions about the long-term outcome after the use of exosurf. 

   According to the FDA, the drug will be made available for preventive treatment immediately after birth of infants weighing 1.5 to 2.5 pounds and for infants weighing more than 1.5 pounds who have already developed the syndrome and weren't treated right after birth.

@  Power Corp. of Canada
@  Financial Unit Picks
@  Gratton for President </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> T.POW T.PWF WNEWS BCE </CO>
<DATELINE> MONTREAL  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Paul Desmarais has sold Montreal Trustco Inc., but he decided to hang on to its chief executive officer. 

   Power Financial Corp., the financial services subsidiary of Desmarais-controlled Power Corp. of Canada, named Robert Gratton president. 
Mr. Gratton, 45 years old, was chairman, president and chief executive of Montreal Trustco Inc. Power Financial sold its 64% stake in Montreal Trustco to BCE Inc. in April. 

   Mr. Gratton succeeds Mr. Desmarais's son, Paul Desmarais Jr., 35, who was named vice chairman. 
The younger Mr. Desmarais also gives up the post of chief operating officer, which has been eliminated. 
He said he intends "to be as active as I have been in the past" in the company's affairs but said Mr. Gratton will be the operating officer. 

   Montreal Trustco, now a subsidiary of BCE, named Raymond Cyr chairman and John Thompson president and chief executive, effective next Monday. 
Mr. Cyr, 55, is president and chief executive of BCE, a telecommunications, manufacturing, energy and financial services concern. 
Mr. Thompson, 54, was president and chief executive of RoyNat Inc., a Montreal Trustco unit. 

   Mr. Cyr was also elected chairman of BCE, succeeding Jean de Grandpre, who is retiring next Tuesday. 
Mr. de Grandpre, 67, continues as a director and was named founding director and chairman emeritus, a new post. 

   Wesley Scott, 42, was named BCE executive vice president, corporate, effective Tuesday. 
The post had been vacant. 
Mr. Scott was executive vice president, finance and administration at Bell Canada, a BCE unit.

<HL> Foreign Exchange:
@  Dollar Weakens After Evidence Mounts
@  Fed Has Eased Its Credit Stance Again
@  ----
@  By Matthew Geiger
@  Special to The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> CANDA FREST JAPAN EUROP </CO>
<IN> COMMODITY NEWS, FARM PRODUCTS (CMD)
FOREIGN-EXCHANGE MARKETS (FRX)
MONETARY NEWS, FOREIGN EXCHANGE, TRADE (MON)
PRECIOUS METALS, STONES, GOLD, SILVER (PCS) </IN>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The dollar weakened after evidence accumulated that the Federal Reserve has eased its credit stance again. 

   Defying market expectations, the Fed failed to drain reserves from the banking system, allowing the federal-funds rate to slide to 8 7/8% by late in the New York afternoon. 
Fed funds have traded around a 9 1/4% middling rate since the Fed nudged the rate a quarter-point lower in early July. 

   "What we think we know now is that the Fed has eased its foot off the brake some more," said Frank Watson, vice president and head of corporate foreign-exchange trading at Swiss Bank Corp. in New York. 

   Traders said it is still hazy whether the Fed has trimmed the fed-funds rate's middling range to 9 1/8% or 9%. 
For the past two days, trading in the funds has been volatile because of the end of a two-week bank-settlement period. 
The fed-funds rate is applied to money that banks lend each other overnight. 

   But by early today, the extent of the apparent Fed easing should become clear, traders said, and the removal of uncertainty may help support the dollar modestly, as participants collect profits on short-dollar positions taken when the first signs of easing surfaced. 

   In late-afternoon New York trading, the dollar was at 1.8675 marks, down from 1.8830 marks Tuesday, and at 140.02 yen, down from 141.45 yen. 
The pound was at $1.6590, compared with $1.6380. 

   In Tokyo Thursday, the dollar opened for trading at 139.35 yen, down from Wednesday's Tokyo close of 141.43 yen. 

   The early July fed-fund easing represented the first time the Fed had moved toward a more accommodative credit policy since early 1988. 
To control building inflationary pressures, the Fed initiated a program of interest-rate increases in the summer of 1987, but it relaxed credit for several months following the market crash in October 1987. 

   Higher interest rates usually boost returns on investments denominated in the domestic currency, helping to keep demand for the currency high. 
Lower interest rates can depress demand for the unit. 

   Although the market anticipated further easing by the Fed following a series of major indicators that place the U.S. economy firmly in a slowing pattern and comments by Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan suggesting that the central bank's attention has shifted to avoiding a recession, yesterday's move still surprised many traders because it was earlier than expected. 

   On New York's Commodity Exchange, gold for current delivery rose $1.20 to settle at $374.90 an ounce. 
Estimated volume was a moderate 3.4 million ounces. 

   In early trading in Hong Kong Thursday, gold was quoted at $374.10 an ounce. 

</TEXT>

<DOCNO> WSJ890727-0074 </DOCNO>

<HL> Who's News:
@  Geoserve Head Matteis Turns Salesman
@  ----
@  By Robert Guenther
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> MHC </CO>
<IN> BIOGRAPHY (BIO) </IN>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Richard J. Matteis figures the best way to lead is by example. 

   So, in his first three days as head of Manufacturers Hanover Corp.'s new information and transaction services group, dubbed Geoserve, Mr. Matteis made five sales calls to prospective customers. 

   Mr. Matteis, a kinetic, intense 52-year-old who plays five hours of doubles tennis on weekends, is sending a clear message to his staff: Sell. 

   In banking, where careers typically aren't penalized for saying no to customers, such thinking runs contrary to the usual wait-for-the-customers-to-come-to-you attitude. 

   That's especially true for the unglamourous businesses that Mr. Matteis oversees: cash management, corporate and institutional trust, funds transfer, global custody and trade services. 

   Historically, banks made their money on the interest margins of their commercial loans and provided Geoserve-type services primarily to cement the relationship with the buyer. 
Little thought was given to them as significant sources of earnings. 

   But with commercial loan margins under pressure, banks are re-examining the earnings potential of these services. 
One sign of this is Geoserve's identity as a distinct business line -- just like retail banking and investment and commercial banking -- in the continuing restructuring of Manufacturers, the nation's seventh-largest banking company. 

   "We're taking people in what had been regarded as cost centers and are now saying you're a profit center," says Mr. Matteis. 
"That's the challenge: to get a sales culture going. 
I'm going to be spending a lot of time on sales." 

   Mr. Matteis, who joined Manufacturers six years ago after 11 years at Citicorp, will need to do some hustling himself if he is to meet the ambitious goals that he has set for Geoserve. 
This year, Geoserve is expected to generate a pretax return of about 15%, or $60 million on $420 million in revenue. 
His goal is to boost Geoserve's return to 20% next year and to 25% in 1991 -- all the while keeping costs about flat. 

   If he can do so, it will be impressive. 
The field of competitors in several of Geoserve's lines of business, such as electronic funds transfer, is fierce and margins are under pressure. 
Moreover, Manufacturers is behind some of its competitors, including Chase Manhattan Corp., American Express Co., Bankers Trust New York Corp. and Security Pacific Corp., in reorganizing these ancillary services into a unified line of business. 
But Mr. Matteis says Manufacturers has an edge over competitors in several areas, and he foresees growth opportunities, such as global custody for investors' foreign securities. 

   James J. McDermott Jr., research director of Keefe, Bruyette &amp; Woods Inc., said, "The ball is clearly in Matteis's court to show that Manufacturers can take this business and make it visible. 
If Manufacturers is to attract capital on a cost-efficient basis, they have to have a game plan. 
The creation and performance of this unit is an essential part of that game plan." 

   Given his energy, technical expertise and outgoing style, Mr. Matteis may pull it off. 
A physics major at Holy Cross College, he studied industrial administration at Yale University and needs only to complete a dissertation to get a Ph.D. in information services. 

   He spent seven years advising the Army on operations before becoming a consultant with Booz, Allen &amp; Hamilton and Auerbach Associates. 
His consulting put him into contact with Citicorp, which he joined in 1972. 
There he was part of the team put together by John S. Reed, now chairman, who shared a car pool with him for several years. 
In the 1970s, the team made Citicorp's back office the envy of the banking industry. 

   Mr. Matteis's departure from Citicorp was similar to others' in recent years. 
With a comparatively young senior management at Citicorp (Mr. 
Reed recently turned 50), many ambitious executives faced a long wait before they could hope for a shot at one of the top jobs at the nation's biggest bank. 

   Instead, Mr. Matteis, who is married and has three daughters, joined Manufacturers as senior vice president in charge of the financial services group in 1983, where he spearheaded the development of its treasury management system for customers. 
Since then, he has been promoted to executive vice president. 
As general manager of Geoserve, Mr. Matteis will be one of a handful of Manufacturers' top executives when the reorganization is complete. 

   "The value that I add to the operation," Mr. Matteis said, "is the ability to relate comfortably with customers and the ability to deal with the technical issues."

@  As Much as 15% Stake
@  In Georgia Gulf Corp. </HL>

<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> GGC NL VHI </CO>
<IN> TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM) </IN>

<TEXT>
   Investor Harold Simmons and companies he controls said they asked the Federal Trade Commission for clearance to buy as much as a 15% stake in Georgia Gulf Corp. 

   A spokesman for Mr. Simmons said the investor and his companies would like to increase their stake in the Atlanta chemicals concern to 10% to 15% of its shares for investment purposes. 
Federal antitrust laws require the company to seek FTC clearance to acquire 10% or more of a company's stock for investment. 

   If Mr. Simmons wanted to acquire the company, he would have had to seek FTC permission to acquire as much as $15 million in Georgia Gulf stock. 
However, he apparently didn't seek that approval. 

   Mr. Simmons, his 88%-owned Valhi Inc., and a company Valhi controls, NL Industries Inc., said they own 9.42% of Georgia Gulf's shares. 
According to a Securities and Exchange Commission filing, the group bought 272,900 shares between June 30 and yesterday at $37.625 to $38 each. 

   In New York Stock Exchange composite trading yesterday, Georgia Gulf closed at $39, up 37.5 cents.

<HL> International:
@  Purchase Price of Lot
@  In Hong Kong Shows
@  Real-Estate Tumble </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> H.GRE FREST </CO>
<IN> REAL ESTATE, REITS, LAND DEVELOPMENT (REL) </IN>
<DATELINE> HONG KONG  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The last vacant lot available for development in Hong Kong's central business district sold yesterday for 2.7 billion Hong Kong dollars (US$345.9 million), far below its estimated HK$5 billion value before the June 4 Tiananmen Square massacre in Beijing. 

   The sale was viewed as an indicator of how the recent upheaval in China has influenced investor confidence in Hong Kong, which reverts to Chinese control in 1997. 
The site's price works out to about HK$29,000 per square foot of land, or about 33% less than the value of the last major site sold by the government for commercial use, in January. 

   Nonetheless, analysts expect the news to have little impact on the Hong Kong stock exchange, whose capitalization is based largely on the value of real estate, because the price matched expectations. 
The local stock market already has declined about 30% since May 12, the day before student protesters started their hunger strike in Tiananmen Square. 

   The Hong Kong government announced after the close of stock trading yesterday that it awarded the 95,000-square-foot site to a subsidiary of Great Eagle Co. Officials at Great Eagle weren't immediately available for comment, but analysts said the company is planning to spend about HK$2.3 billion to build two office towers, the tallest to be approximately 50 stories, a public parking structure and a private parking area. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Stuck in Alaska:
@  Exxon's Army Scrubs
@  Beaches, but Many
@  Don't Stay Cleaned
@  ---
@  As Oil Persists and Costs Top
@  $1 Billion, Firm Agrees
@  To Resume Job in Spring
@  ---
@  Will the Goo Turn to Asphalt?
@  ----
@  By Ken Wells and Allanna Sullivan
@  Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> XON </CO>
<IN> PETROLEUM (PET)
ENVIRONMENT (ENV) </IN>
<DATELINE> EVANS ISLAND, Prince William Sound  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Once a sanctuary for loons and kayakers, the rocky coves here are swarming with a noisy, mechanized army in yellow rain suits. 
One squad, 100 strong and armed with fire hoses, fans out across a 100-yard stretch of cobbled shore, attacking with plumes of water and jets of steam. 
Others do battle from barges, using huge nozzles mounted on booms to blast cascades of water at the cliffs. 

   They are fighting oil -- the stubborn residue of North Slope crude still clinging to hundreds of miles of beaches four months after the wreck of the Exxon Valdez precipitated the nation's worst spill. 
And Exxon Corp. as well as the workers is finding the oil an implacable enemy. 

   "Some beaches look pretty good after just a half a day" of cleaning, says Randy Vantine, the foreman of a crew of 20 working at another site on Eleanor Island, 20 miles to the north. 
"But in one place, we spent a month," he says, trying to rinse crude from a beach. 
All too often, he adds, the next tide flushes deeply imbedded crude from subsurface crannies and recoats cleaned beaches. 

   Exxon is clearly finding the mess its tanker left nightmarishly difficult to fix, a fact that led to its stunning disclosure Monday that it expects the cleanup costs to total $1.25 billion. 
And that's not counting potentially huge compensatory and punitive damage claims. 

   Lee Raymond, Exxon's president, declined in an interview yesterday to specify its initial cost estimate last May other than to say that "we didn't know very much then, but as time has gone by and things have evolved, the number wasn't out of bounds." 

   But Exxon must be dismayed. 
The new estimate far exceeds the Interior Department's $100 million projection soon after the spill and experts' later estimate that Exxon's bill, including litigation, would be at about $1 billion. 
It will be the costliest industrial accident on record, easily surpassing the $1.3 billion Three-Mile Island mishap and dwarfing the proposed $470 million Bhopal settlement. 

   Exxon's basic problem is the oil itself, which has proved extraordinarily persistent in the area's cold water and cool temperatures. 
Early on, experts hoped that the spill would be largely confined to Prince William Sound and would begin to dissipate naturally in 45 to 90 days. 
Instead, crude loosed on Bligh Reef, 25 miles south of Valdez, has migrated 470 miles to the southwest, wreaking havoc on seabird rookeries in the Gulf of Alaska, fishing grounds off Kodiak Island and remote beaches along the Alaska Peninsula. 

   More than half the 27,548 birds known to have been killed died in the Gulf of Alaska, where roaming patches of oil continue to plague beaches and kill sea otters and seals as well as birds. 
Winter storms may scour some exposed beaches of oil, but scientists fear that by next spring the spill's residue on huge swaths of sheltered beaches will be even more intractable, and expensive to treat, as it hardens to the consistency of asphalt. 

   "If things don't speed up, there'll be parking lots out there in some areas," says John Robinson, a science coordinator for the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Association. 
"We'll need picks and shovels instead of water." 
Government officials agree that beaches reaching such a condition may linger for a decade or longer as aesthetic nightmares, unusable by humans and potentially dangerous to wildlife. 

   Mr. Raymond says the reoiling of beaches has been frustrating and concedes, "The oil has stayed with some beaches somewhat longer than I thought it would." 

   Nonetheless, Exxon remains sanguine about its ability to get most of the oil off beaches by Sept. 15, when the Gulf of Alaska's notoriously stormy winter weather will set in and make cleanup efforts even harder. 
"Assuming that we can have people working 'til mid-September {the pullout will be in stages}, we have a good shot at having all the beaches treated," Mr. Raymond says. 
"But not clean like Mr. Clean who shows up in your kitchen. 
Our objective is to make sure the ecosystems are back in shape." 

   Despite a pledge in May by Exxon Chairman Lawrence Rawl that the company would return next spring to clean up any leftover oil, O.R. Harrison, Exxon's spill manager, stirred up a tempest on Tuesday by saying Exxon will return to Alaska next spring with only a crew of 20 to survey the beaches, without committing itself to further cleanup work. 

   But in the interview yesterday, Mr. Raymond promised that Exxon would abide by the Coast Guard's determination next spring as to whether further work is needed. 
"It's our best guess that there will be a lot less oil than people think," Mr. Raymond says. 
"But if the conclusion is reached by the Coast Guard that something needs to be made right and it can be made right, we'll be there. 
We're not trying to run off." 

   However, Mr. Raymond declines to commit Exxon to restoring the beaches to their prior pristine state, although state and federal officials can, if they choose, enforce statutes requiring Exxon to try to do so. 
"I don't think Exxon is breaking its promise," he says. 
"Maybe people weren't listening. 
Back in May, Larry {Rawl} emphasized getting the toxicity out of the water and off the beaches so the ecosystems can again function. 

   "There are environmentalists who say that we want our beaches back to looking exactly like they did, but that could actually hurt the ecosystem. 
You'd have to dig it up or hit it with so much steam it would kill everything." 

   However, Alaska Gov. Steve Cowper says, "Virtually from the beginning, Exxon promised Alaskans that the company would stay until the job was done. 
I expect them to fulfill the promise." 

   No one faults Exxon for lack of effort so far. 
It says it has already spent $600 million mounting what is, in effect, currently the nation's biggest public-works project. 
It has hired an army of 10,000 people, of which about a third are mopping up the beach. 
"It looks like D-Day," Mr. Raymond says. 
Most work 12-hour days in 14-day stints, collecting almost $17 an hour plus overtime. 
The company has marshaled an armada of 1,000 boats, including barges capable of housing 500 people, and a squadron of 70 aircraft. 

   Ballpark estimates indicate a price tag of $90 million a month just for the workers and boats, excluding a slew of other expenses, such as materials, transportation of workers and equipment, waste disposal and reimbursement of public agencies for their work. 
Although Exxon can recycle some of the oil hosed off beaches, the state is requiring it to ship tons of oil-soaked cleanup materials to a toxic waste dump in Oregon, on the ground that burning would unleash too many toxic pollutants. 
Exxon contends that this has slowed the cleanup and ballooned the cost. 

   In some areas of Prince William Sound, cleanup activities are so frenetic that bays sheltering workers have come to resemble floating military encampments. 
Skiffs turn inshore waters into busy highways. 
Some coves drone like busy municipal airports as helicopters and small planes ferry in supplies or wing off on reconnaissance missions. 

   But despite Exxon's epic effort, observers fret that the company is getting little bang for its buck and, even worse, may inadvertently be doing more harm than good in some areas. 

   Mr. Raymond says the vast majority of beaches haven't been reoiled, and the company claims to have so far "treated" about 140 miles of them -- a figure the state terms about 30% too high. 
However, John Lough, an Alaska Department of Environmental Conservation employee who tracks progress in daily flights over the sound, says, "I'd be hard pressed to say that there's a treated beach out there that hasn't been subject to some reoiling." 

   In addition, many fear increasingly that the most effective cleaning method -- high-pressure, hot-water washing -- may be doing more damage to the sound's near-shore environment than the oil on the beaches. 
That treatment wasn't chosen by Exxon: It was agreed on by state and federal experts after cold-water rinsing proved virtually useless against the weathered oil. 

   However, National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration studies indicate that beach-blasting with water as hot as 140 degrees Fahrenheit is "cooking the intertidal zone," destroying organisms within 100 feet of the shoreline, eroding some beaches and building "sand dunes of oily material" that may pose long-term hazards to fish and wildlife, Mr. Robinson says. 

   Should continuing NOAA studies validate such fears, "we're going to quit washing early, and nobody's going to like that," says Vice Adm. Clyde E. Robbins, the Coast Guard's Valdez-based spill coordinator. 
Exxon says it believes that many of the problems of hot-water rinsing can be allayed by reducing the water temperature and exposure time of the beaches. 

   Nonetheless, Vice Adm. Robbins, NOAA and Exxon are trying desperately to persuade the state to allow the immediate use of an untried Exxon chemical dispersant on the weathered oil. 
Alaska balked, concerned about possible toxic effects on the summer salmon run and mindful of a political constituency skeptical of using any chemicals in the sound. 
But the state has agreed to expedite, though not waive, tests to determine the near-term toxicity of the chemical on salmon and other species. 
Those tests, which typically take a year or longer, are being telescoped into "a matter of days," though unfavorable results could prompt the state to reject use of the chemicals, says Steve Provant, Alaska's onscene spill coordinator. 

   Exxon is conducting the tests, and a final decision appears to be days away. 
Vice Adm. Robbins, who thinks the chemicals could dramatically hasten the cleanup, favors their use even though he sympathizes with the state's point of view. 

   The Exxon chemical is essentially a solvent -- kerosene stripped of its most toxic compounds and blended with detergent -- and undoubtedly is being prescribed for beaches only because of the oil's stubborn resistance to more conventional treatment, he adds. 
Unadulterated kerosene was used as a dispersant on a 1967 spill from the Torrey Canyon off the British coast, and scientists concluded it harmed the environment more than the spilled oil did. 

   The cleanup problems are exacerbated by the porous nature of the sound's rock and pebble beaches. 
State survey crews conducting test excavations of oiled beaches have found crude four feet deep in some places. 
Such beaches not only reoil themselves but sometimes unleash what state observers call slicks of "fugitive hydrocarbons" to soil other beaches. 

   Following a July 14 windstorm, so much oil had been loosed that DEC observer Erlene Blake says some areas "resembled the days right after the tanker went aground." 
Indeed, the intractable problem posed by roaming oil was obvious on a recent DEC overflight. 
Large stretches of beaches on Smith and Little Smith islands, which had been cleaned shortly after the March 24 spill, had been reoiled. 
And just off those islands, wide ribbons of mousselike oil, a quarter-mile long, lay poised to strike beaches with the next tide. 

   On the shores of Perry Island, a large rocky outcrop in the western sound, cleanup crews have successfully scoured oil off many moderately fouled beaches. 
Yet the scene is marred by sheens of oil floating nearby and by the glistening black surface of a large cape at Perry's southern edge that seems to have just been sloshed with fresh crude. 

   That scene is repeated over and over again. 
The beaches at Sleepy Bay, a salmon spawning area on the tip of Latouche Island in the southern sound, demonstrate how weathered oil resists cleanup. 
Exxon crews rinsed the beach several times but recently agreed to stop, and return later, because of concern that cleanup work would impede the salmon run, which has just started. 

   Yet a walk on the "treated" beaches of Sleepy Bay reveals that some portions of upper beach have already turned slab-like, with rocks welded together by encrusted crude. 
Near the tide line, the rocks are slippery from wet crude apparently welling up from below the beach. 

   Hoping to mitigate the damage to salmon that spawn in a stream coursing through a Sleepy Bay beach, Exxon got state permission to dredge up a 20-foot-wide swath of rocky stream bed and replace it with clean fill. 
Though it was supposed to gouge out only 18 inches of soiled rock, crews found that oil had saturated the stream bed as deep as three feet. 
They dug out the entire mess -- an act that the state says may have been harmful -- and rebuilt the bed with tons of new material. 
This ribbon of truly "clean" stream bed winding through blackened "treated" beaches indicates the draconian measures that some feel may be required if beaches are to be truly cleaned. 

   "It's not that Exxon is the Evil Empire in all this," DEC's Mr. Lough says. 
"It's just that so much of what's being done seems to do so little good. 
It's like Humpty Dumpty -- all the king's horses and all the king's men can't put these beaches back the way they were." 

</TEXT>

<HL> Technology &amp; Health:
@  Affluent Women Are More Likely to Have
@  Caesareans Than Poor Are, Study Finds
@  ----
@  By Ron Winslow
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<TEXT>
   Affluent women are about 75% more likely to give birth by Caesarean section than are poor women, a study found, providing evidence that financial factors contribute to what some believe is excessive use of the procedure. 

   "We tried to very carefully control for a variety of {clinical} factors that could account for this {finding}," said Jeffrey B. Gould, chairman of maternal and child health at the University of California at Berkeley and the principal author of the study in today's New England Journal of Medicine. 
"There is no ready medical explanation for why this should be so." 

   Nearly one in four infants in the U.S. are delivered by Caesarean section, and some health experts believe that as many as half of those procedures are unnecessary. 

   The study found that affluent women are 60% more likely to have complications at birth reported than are poor women. 
That may contribute to the higher rate of Caesareans among wealthier patients. 
But even when complications and other considerations are taken into account, the study found that women in families with incomes over $30,000 have a much higher rate of Caesarean births than those in households with incomes under $11,000. 

   The finding of more reports of complications among the affluent was surprising, the authors say, because wealthier women generally have better access to health care and would be expected to have fewer birth complications. 
One complication, a birth canal too narrow for the baby's head, was 2.5 times more prevalent among affluent women, yet health experts say no evidence exists to associate income with this anatomical condition. 

   Dr. Gould and his co-authors speculate that "differences in both health-care setting and clinical decision-making" contribute to the correlation between income and Caesarean rates, and that complications may have been overreported for affluent women or underreported for poor women. 

   "We don't know whether poor people are underserved or rich people are overserved," Dr. Gould said in an interview. 
He suggested a combination of financial and non-financial factors related to Caesarean births generally might help explain his group's findings. 
Reimbursement rates for the procedure may be higher than for vaginal birth, he said, and malpractice concerns may prompt doctors to resort to Caesarean section at the first hint of trouble. 
Otherwise, "your Monday morning quarterback may well be a lawyer," he said. 
Cultural influences from a doctor's training or practice may also figure in how a doctor decides what procedures to use. 
These are questions for further study, he said. 
Sidney Wolfe, director of Public Citizen Health Research Group, a Washington, D.C., consumer organization, said the Caesarean rate could be reduced if more women of any means gave birth at not-for-profit facilities or if hospitals developed conservative guidelines to discourage overuse of the procedure. 
Dr. Wolfe also said some doctors often list non-existent complications to justify doing the procedure, which is generally more lucrative for doctors and hospitals. 

   The consumer group's own broad study of Caesarean sections found, for instance, that some for-profit hospitals in California exceeded a 30% rate for Caesareans in 1986, while the rate at one not-for-profit health-maintenance organization was 19.4%. 

   "Women shouldn't have to bring themselves into poverty to lower their C-section rate," Dr. Wolfe said. 
"They should bring themselves into a better health-care system. 

   The Berkeley study analyzed birth-certificate data on 245,854 women in Los Angeles County in 1982 and 1983. 
The overall rate of Caesarean section was 17.8%. 
For women in families with incomes under $11,000, the rate was 13.2%, while it was 22.9% for women with family income over $30,000. 

   The study also found that 20.6% of nonHispanic white women of all incomes had Caesarean deliveries, while the rate was 18.9% for black women and 13.9% for Mexican-Americans. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Law:
@  Doctor Is Accused
@  Of Inside Trading
@  On Patient's Data
@  ----
@  By Laurie P. Cohen
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> STOCK MARKET, OFFERINGS (STK)
SECURITIES INDUSTRY (SCR) </IN>
<GV> JUSTICE DEPARTMENT (JUS) </GV>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   A psychiatrist was charged with unlawful trading in the stock of BankAmerica Corp. based on inside information he allegedly received from a patient. 

   The psychiatrist, Robert Howard Willis of New York, was indicted by a federal grand jury on 46 counts of securities and mail fraud. 
His lawyer denied the charges. 

   According to the indictment, Dr. Willis learned from a patient in early 1986 that Sanford I. Weill, the former chief executive officer of Shearson Loeb Rhodes, planned to attempt to become chief executive officer of BankAmerica and to invest additional capital in it. 
Shearson Loeb Rhodes was the predecessor of Shearson Lehman Hutton Inc. 

   The patient, referred to in the indictment as "Jane Doe," knew of Mr. Weill's secret plan because her husband had "a business relationship" with Mr. Weill. 
"Had Weill been successful in his effort to become chief executive officer of BankAmerica, there would have been substantial upheavals in the personal lives of Jane Doe and her spouse," the indictment said. 

   The indictment said Jane Doe discussed Mr. Weill's plans with Dr. Willis "in a relationship of trust and confidence and in the context of their professional relationship" with Dr. Willis. 
Her discussions with Dr. Willis, 50 years old, occurred in January and February 1986, before Mr. Weill unveiled his plan to attempt to become BankAmerica's chief executive. 
The government contends the patient also disclosed to Dr. Willis that the plan called for Shearson to invest $1 billion in BankAmerica if Mr. Weill succeeded. 

   The indictment alleges that Dr. Willis purchased $171,130 of BankAmerica stock on the basis of the information he received from Jane Doe. 
He allegedly made a profit of $27,475 in less than 40 days by selling the securities after Mr. Weill disclosed his plan to the public. 

   Mr. Weill failed in his attempt to take control of BankAmerica. 
He is now chairman of Primerica Corp., the parent of Smith Barney, Harris Upham &amp; Co. 

   Edward Brodsky, a New York attorney representing Dr. Willis, said Dr. Willis didn't violate the securities laws or any other laws or trade on inside information. 
"He's going to go to trial and I'm confident at the end he'll be found not guilty," Mr. Brodsky said. 
Dr. Willis is scheduled to be arraigned on the charges today. 

</TEXT>

<HL> National Heritage Inc.
@  Sets Aug. 31 Meeting
@  Sought by Southmark </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> NHR SM </CO>
<IN> BANKRUPTCIES (BCY) </IN>
<DATELINE> DALLAS  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   National Heritage Inc. said it will hold an annual meeting on Aug. 31, setting the stage for a bitter battle for control of this Southmark Corp. affiliate. 

   Southmark, which owns about 62.5% of National Heritage, recently told the company's management that it wants to name a majority of the directors to National Heritage's board. 
Southmark, which recently sought protection from creditors under Chapter 11 of the federal Bankruptcy Code, says it wants to exercise its shareholder's right to have a board of its choosing. 

   However, National Heritage, a manager of nursing homes and retirement centers, has resisted, saying it wants to remain separate from Southmark's problems. 
Last week, it won a temporary restraining order preventing another Southmark unit from soliciting consents to name new directors. 
A hearing on that order is scheduled for today. 

   In response, Southmark yesterday asked an Atlanta judge to require National Heritage to hold a shareholders' meeting on Aug. 31. 
Although the affiliate said it decided Tuesday to have a meeting on that date, the judge ordered the meeting held and set a record date of yesterday. 

   Southmark said it will ask National Heritage's shareholders to change the company's bylaws to eliminate a staggered board and to name five of Southmark's current directors and an Atlanta businessman to the National Heritage board. 

   But Southmark will need some help from other holders: Its 66% stake was reduced to about 62.5% a few weeks ago when National Heritage issued about 800,000 stock options to 37 employees and provided loans to allow the employees to exercise the options. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Technology &amp; Health:
@  Marrow Surgery
@  Saved Few Victims
@  From Chernobyl
@  ----
@  By Bill Paul
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> FREST </CO>
<IN> NUCLEAR POWER, FUEL (NUK)
ENVIRONMENT (ENV)
MEDICAL AND BIOTECHNOLOGY (MTC) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Only two of 13 people exposed to large doses of radiation from the Chernobyl nuclear accident survived after receiving bone-marrow transplants, indicating that such a procedure has limited benefits for victims of massive radiation exposure, according to an article in today's New England Journal of Medicine. 

   "Because large doses of radiation affect multiple systems," the authors said, even a successful bone-marrow transplant did "not necessarily ensure survival." 
The authors included Alexandr Baranov, of Moscow's Institute of Biophysics, and Robert Gale, a hematologist at the University of California at Los Angeles Medical School and a member of the team that performed the transplants. 

   Dr. Gale and his collaborators were careful not to infer that victims of a U.S. radiation accident would fare as poorly as the Russians did. 
Five of the 13 patients eventually succumbed to burns suffered in a major fire that resulted from the accident in the Soviet-designed graphite reactor in 1986. 
U.S.-designed reactors make such a fire highly unlikely. 

   At the same time, the article noted that, contrary to conventional thinking, a bone-marrow transplant apparently isn't risk-free because it can cause both a rejection reaction and interstitial pneumonia. 
At least two of the patients who died suffered from severe rejections, while three who died contracted the pneumonia. 

   Transplantation successfully stimulated blood-cell production in nine of the 13 patients, all of whom were men, ranging in age from 23 to 46. 
Ten of the donors were siblings, while three were parents. 

   Of the 11 who died, none lived longer than 2 1/2 months after his bone-marrow transplant. 
Both of the patients still alive more than three years after the accident are well, the article reported, although one still has burn-related problems and radiation-induced cataracts. 
The two survivors were 25 and 35 at the time of the accident and didn't have the serious burns that afflicted the others. 

   In an accompanying editorial, Christine K. Cassell and Jennifer Leaning, of the University of Chicago and Harvard University, respectively, said Dr. Gale's work "adds greatly to our knowledge of the management of acute radiation injury." 
The editorialists used Dr. Gale's article to criticize the U.S. Energy Department for having, until recently, allegedly covered up health and safety problems at U.S. nuclear weapons-manufacturing facilities. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Business Brief -- SmithKline Beckman Corp.:
@  Merger Completed to Form
@  Prescription Drug Company </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> SKB BECHY AGN BEC SBH </CO>
<IN> TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM)
DRUG MANUFACTURERS (DRG) </IN>
<TEXT>
   SmithKline Beckman Corp., Philadelphia, and Beecham Group PLC, London, completed their merger, creating the world's second-largest prescription drug concern, after Merck &amp; Co. 

   SmithKline Beecham PLC, based in London, also has large interests in over-the-counter drugs, animal health-care products, clinical laboratories and health-related consumer products. 

   SmithKline holders approved the merger at a special meeting with 99% of voted shares in favor of the combination. 

   SmithKline holders will receive for each of their SmithKline shares an equity unit consisting of five B ordinary shares of SmithKline Beecham and one preferred share plus a one-time special dividend of $5.50 and a pro rata quarterly dividend of 22.5 cents. 
The equity distribution makes SmithKline holders equal owners with Beecham holders in SmithKline Beecham. 

   SmithKline holders will also become owners of two SmithKline units that are being spun off, Allergan Inc., an eye and skin care company, and Beckman Instruments Inc., a maker of scientific instruments that was 84%-owned by SmithKline. 

   In composite trading on the New York Stock Exchange, SmithKline closed yesterday at $62.25 a share, up 87.5 cents.

<HL> First City Bancorp
@  Fires High Official,
@  Claims Embezzlement </HL>

<CO> FBT WNEWS </CO>
<DATELINE> HOUSTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   First City Bancorp of Texas said it fired its executive vice president and general counsel, Glen G. Magnuson Jr., after he admitted embezzling $18,484. 

   The bank holding company added, without elaborating, that "investigation has revealed other questionable practices" involving Mr. Magnuson and that "full restitution will be sought." 
A company official said the matter has been reported to bank regulatory officials. 

   Mr. Magnuson, who declined comment, was succeeded as general counsel by William G. Schopf, who will also remain as senior partner in the Chicago law firm Schopf &amp; Weiss, the company said. 

   First City said the embezzlement took place in March 1988, prior to a federally assisted bailout led by Chicago banker A. Robert Abboud. 
Mr. Magnuson joined First City in 1986, a spokesman said. 

   The company added that Mr. Magnuson "made misrepresentations in connection with obtaining employment at First City." 
It also declined to elaborate on this allegation. 
Mr. Magnuson also declined comment on the allegation.

<HL> Technology &amp; Health:
@  Cray Stock Tumbles $2.125 After Firm
@  Slashes Forecasts for 1989 Revenue, Net
@  ----
@  By Richard Gibson
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> CYR </CO>
<IN> STOCK MARKET, OFFERINGS (STK)
COMPUTERS AND INFORMATION TECHNOLOGY (CPR) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Cray Research Inc. shares fell $2.125 in heavy trading after the supercomputer maker slashed its revenue and earnings projections for the year. 

   The company, elaborating on its downward revision late Tuesday, cited a slowing economy that has delayed both government and commercial orders while fueling competition, particularly from International Business Machines Corp. and Convex Computer Corp. 

   "We're going through a difficult period of time," Cray's president, Marcello Gumucio, said in an interview. 
But he expressed optimism about Cray's long-term fortunes, adding, "This is one of the most solid companies in the world." 

   He also said the company is under increased internal pressure to produce a small machine, to compete in the $2 million-$2.5 million range. 
Currently, Cray systems are priced between about $5 million and $25 million. 

   As reported, Minneapolis-based Cray abandoned its forecast of a 10% revenue gain for the year and said achieving per-share earnings of $3.75 would be a challenge. 
For last year, Cray posted earnings of $4.99 a share. 

   Investor reaction to that disappointing news was strong. 
An order imbalance delayed Cray's opening on the New York Stock Exchange yesterday, with early indications of a bid of $38 for the stock, which had closed Tuesday at $44.125. 
The initial trade was at $41.125. 
All told, 1.14 million shares changed hands. 
Cray closed at $42. 

   Mr. Gumucio seemed puzzled by the market's surprise. 
He said he had previously indicated "in the strongest possible words" to analysts and shareholders that "we had a challenging year." 
In the interview, he said revenue is under pressure because of lower-than-anticipated demand, and because of timing issues related to the transition to the new Y-MP supercomputer from its predecessor, the X-MP. 

   "We're also installing more used equipment than anticipated," he said. 
Not only does that mean lower revenue for Cray, but the company indicated that many first-time customers are preferring to lease machines rather than buy them. 
Also, Mr. Gumucio said the percentage of revenue from service and maintenance contracts is running higher than forecast, "and margins on service are lower than we had anticipated." 

   Cray's president wouldn't forecast earnings, either for the rest of this year or next. 
But analysts who talked with him earlier in the day said they felt the company had a "comfort level" this year of between $3.50 and $3.75 a share. 

   Several analysts said they were reluctant to make any forecast for 1990. 
"I haven't a clue. 
They gave no guidance," said Gary Smaby of Needham &amp; Co. 
But Barry Willman of Sanford C. Bernstein &amp; Co. said Cray indicated it expects some improvement in 1990 because of lower research and development costs, gross margin gains from volume shipments of Y-MP systems, and fewer trade-ins of old Cray hardware. 

   R&amp;D expenses this year are dependent on when Cray can spin off founder Seymour Cray's design unit, and its Cray-3 machine. 
The supercomputer is costing Cray Research about $10 million a quarter to complete and is over budget, Mr. Gumucio said. 
The spinoff is awaiting government approval as a tax-free transaction. 

   "We think the economy in the U.S. is going to be tough," Mr. Gumucio said in the interview. 
Noting that government purchases are under budgetary pressures, he said the company's commercial customers "in many ways depend on government spending -- universities, defense subcontractors and so forth." 
Big-ticket outlays by oil and automobile companies also are off, he said, although last month Cray announced lease orders from General Motors Corp. and Ford Motor Co. for the company's big new machines. 

   Mr. Gumucio said Cray is encountering discount pricing by IBM, particularly in bidding for university contracts. 
Convex is being aggressive in battling for departmental-sized machines, at the low end of Cray's line. 

   While overseas sales remain strong, Mr. Gumucio said there is some preference for used and smaller new machines. 

</TEXT>

@  Is Reached With Showtime </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> TBSA </CO>
<IN> MEDIA, PUBLISHING, BROADCASTING, ELECTRONIC PUBLISHING (MED)
RECREATION, ENTERTAINMENT, TOYS, MOVIES, PHOTOGRAPHY, SPORTS (REC) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Turner Broadcasting System Inc. and Showtime Networks Inc. said they reached an "amicable resolution" on litigation involving the use of movies from Turner's MGM/UA Library. 
Terms weren't given. 

   In February, Turner asked for a ruling that its broadcast of certain of the films over its Turner Network Television, or TNT cable station, didn't violate Showtime's pact to show the same films. 

   The original suit, filed in federal court here, said Showtime had a "non-exclusive right" to exhibit as many as 800 films from the MGM/UA Library under an agreement reached with MGM/UA in 1985, before Turner bought MGM/UA in 1986. 

   Both parties expressed "satisfaction" with the resolution of the matter.

<HL> Politics &amp; Policy:
@  Senate Requires Disclosure of Lobbyists
@  In Vote to Clear Natural Resources Bill
@  ----
@  By David Rogers
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> GOVMT </CO>
<IN> PULP, PAPER, PACKAGING (ALL TYPES) (PUL)
PETROLEUM (PET)
ECONOMIC NEWS (ECO)
MARKETING, ADVERTISING (MKT)
ENVIRONMENT (ENV)
TRANSPORTATION, TRUCK AND SHIP LINES, RAILROADS (TRA)
MINING, METALS (NON-PRECIOUS) (MIN)
AIRLINES (AIR) </IN>
<GV> CONGRESS (CNG)
DEFENSE DEPARTMENT (DEF)
TRANSPORTATION DEPARTMENT (TRN) </GV>
<DATELINE> WASHINGTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Senate approved a nearly $11 billion natural resources bill incorporating major timber and oil provisions as well as sweeping requirements to force the disclosure of consultants and lobbyists hired to pursue federal funds. 

   Current restrictions on oil exploration in U.S. waters would be significantly expanded in the fiscal year beginning Oct. 1, and the measure includes precedent-setting language seeking to lift injunctions on timber harvests in the Pacific Northwest. 

   Final passage came last night on a simple voice vote, but only after daylong debate in which both oil and lumber interests suffered setbacks at the hands of environmental forces in the Democrat-controlled chamber. 
By a 72-27 margin, senators rejected an industry-backed amendment seeking to undermine the moratoriums on oil exploration, and timber allies failed on a 52-47 roll call to head off a $65 million cut from funding for new forest roads. 

   The Senate action came as the House Appropriations Committee approved a $27 billion transportation bill for fiscal 1990, and moved to interject itself in a renewed fight over military base closings. 
Congress authorized the Pentagon in April to begin shutting down all or part of 91 military installations next year, but the House committee yesterday amended a pending military construction bill to reopen the issue in as many as a dozen cases. 

   Facilities in San Francisco, Illinois and New Jersey would most likely be affected by the provision, which had the backing of the powerful defense subcommittee chairman, Rep. John Murtha (D., Pa.). 
The amendment withholds any funds from being used to carry out the closings until cost studies by the General Accounting Office are completed, and the money would be released only if GAO finds the expected savings meet criteria set by an independent commission last year. 

   The Interior Department and Forest Service are among the major accounts funded in the Senate-passed bill, which anticipates an additional $460 million in fiscal 1990 for clean coal technology beyond the $10.9 billion counted in the measure itself. 

   Funding for the National Endowment for the Arts was only marginally reduced below the $171 million level approved by the House, but the Senate measure goes further in seeking to penalize those directly involved in awarding NEA money to support two recent exhibits criticized as sacrilegious and sexually explicit. 
The NEA would be prohibited from awarding any direct grants for the next five years to the two sponsors in North Carolina and Philadelphia, and Sen. Jesse Helms (R., N.C.) attached an amendment specifically prohibiting the use of any funds for exhibits of homoerotic art. 

   Though weaker on some points than the corresponding House bill, the Senate restrictions on offshore drilling cover the same 84 million acres of U.S. waters, including Alaska's Bristol Bay and much of the East and West Coasts. 

   Industry allies responded this week by attaching an amendment to prohibit oil tankers from entering these same waters if the moratoriums are still in effect in two years, and the goal was to put pressure on lawmakers to either relax the restrictions or see shipping obstructed in their home state ports. 
The industry also saw the provision as a means to highlight evidence that tankers pose more of an environmental risk than offshore rigs. 
But yesterday's vote to strip the amendment underscored how such policy distinctions are lost amid the emotions stirred by the string of spills since March when the Exxon Valdez ran aground. 

   Chief among the timber provisions in the bill are those seeking to force at least a temporary settlement between environmental and timber interests in Oregon and Washington states, where logging has been halted on tens of thousands of acres of federal land by lawsuits seeking to protect the northern spotted owl. 
The language, which has the backing of Speaker Thomas Foley in the House, would overturn current injunctions if no agreement is reached in the next two months. 
But environmentalists are assured of reduced cutting levels and language to protect old stands of timber favored by the owl. 

   Further refinements were added yesterday to ensure that the courts have a role in overseeing future forest sales. 
But timber interests themselves were on the defensive in the fight over road funds for the Forest Service nationally. 
The $65 million cut is a major blow in a chamber where Western interests have such sway, and the remaining $140 million is only slightly more than what has been approved by the House. 
The $27 billion transportation bill approved by the House committee yesterday would provide $11.87 billion in direct appropriations and an estimated $15.17 billion drawn from trust funds for highway and airport development. 
Funding for the Federal Aviation Administration would grow to $7.25 billion, including a 25% increase for facilities and equipment, and funds available for grants to airports would be increased to $1.5 billion-$150 million more than the Bush administration requested. 

   Amtrak, another budget target for the president, would be preserved at a $614 million funding level, but the federal subsidies for air carriers serving rural areas was reduced by $19.2 million, 60% below current levels. 
In a debate etched with bitterness, rural lawmakers angrily challenged the cut as too deep, but after first prevailing on a show of hands, they lost 26-25 in an effort to restore an estimated $9.3 million to the program. 

   The military construction bill -- which carries the new base-closing language -- allocated $8.7 billion to the Pentagon to construct and maintain facilities. 
The House committee yesterday approved a third spending bill appropriating $1.59 billion for Congress's own budget for the next fiscal year. 
Significant increases are provided for the offices of House leaders, but the more sensitive issue going into an election year was a nearly $81 million rise to cover mail sent out by both houses. 

   Separately, the legislative appropriations subcommittee quietly approved recently a $7 million reprogramming to carry out an expansion of the Capitol at the terrace level of the west front of the building. 
Another $9.8 million was previously reprogrammed last fall for improvements to the terrace area, and the funds will come from a major surplus left in the initial $49 million appropriation to restore the west front. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Law:
@  Judge's Order Setting Bail
@  For Kashoggi Is Blocked </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> FREST </CO>
<IN> AEROSPACE (ARO)
MONETARY NEWS, FOREIGN EXCHANGE, TRADE (MON) </IN>
<GV> JUSTICE DEPARTMENT (JUS) </GV>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   A federal appeals court temporarily blocked a federal judge's order setting bail for Adnan Khashoggi, the Saudi arms merchant. 

   The U.S. Attorney's office here had requested the stay, claiming that the judge's order didn't set conditions that would have ensured that Mr. Khashoggi would appear at trial. 
The government asked that Mr. Khashoggi not be released pending the trial. 

   On Tuesday, U.S. Judge John Keenan said Mr. Khashoggi could be released if he posted a $10 million bond and agreed to wear an electronic monitoring bracelet, among other conditions. 

   After being extradited from Switzerland last week, Mr. Khashoggi pleaded innocent to charges of obstruction of justice and mail fraud. 
He has been accused of helping former Philippine President Ferdinand Marcos and his wife, Imelda, hide assets that the Marcoses allegedly stole from the Philippines. 

   In its motion to the appeals court, the U.S. Attorney's office included a copy of a financial disclosure that it received from Mr. Khashoggi after the judge's order. 
That financial statement, which the government said hasn't been verified, puts Mr. Khashoggi's estimated net worth as of July 20 at $53.9 million. 

   The stay will last until the appeals court decides whether to revoke Judge Keenan's order, as the government has requested. 
The appeals court didn't say when it will decide that question. 

   Mr. Khashoggi's attorney, Robert Morvillo, couldn't be reached.

<HL> Business Brief -- Jiffy Lube International Inc.:
@  Plan Will Restructure Debt;
