<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> JLUB PZL </CO>
<IN> TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Jiffy Lube International Inc. said it agreed to a plan that would restructure $75 million of its debt and give 80%-control of the company to Pennzoil Co. 

   Jiffy Lube, Baltimore, which operates and franchises automotive oil-change stations, said the plan would resolve debt problems by reducing debt, adding $35 million or more to stockholders' equity and providing a base for growth. 

   Under the plan, to be implemented within 60 days, Pennzoil, a Houston oil company, will convert its $15 million subordinated debentures and contribute $20 million in cash in exchange for new Jiffy Lube common, thereby acquiring an 80% interest in Jiffy Lube. 
Terms of Jiffy Lube's senior-debt payments would be significantly revised, allowing an overall reduction in future repayments of debt outstanding. 

   The restructured senior debt includes $35 million of senior unsecured notes at 12% a year, maturing over seven years, and a series of contingent notes with an aggregate face value of $29.4 million payable over seven years.

@  National Education Corp.
@  Rehires Former President </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> NEC WNEWS </CO>
<DATELINE> IRVINE, Calif.  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   National Education Corp. said it named J.W. Cwiertnia, 48 years old, as acting president and acting chief operating officer. 
It is the second top management change in as many weeks. 

   The company's board is expected to name Mr. Cwiertnia to the two posts on a permanent basis when it meets late next month. 
Mr. Cwiertnia was president of National Education, but resigned in March to seek a job as chief operating officer at another company. 
The post of chief operating officer at National Education is a new one. 

   After Mr. Cwiertnia's resignation, H. David Bright, who was chairman and chief executive officer, took on the additional post of acting president until resigning last week from all three jobs. 

   Mr. Bright was succeeded by David C. Jones, a director, who became chairman and acting chief executive. 

   National Education, a vocational and industrial training and educational publishing company, last week reported a second-quarter net loss of $1.5 million and said the results included a "much-higher-than-expected" $2.5 million expense because of an unusual problem with customer credits. 

   In composite trading on the New York Stock Exchange, National Education closed at $15.125 a share, up 12.5 cents.

<HL> Economy:
@  Thrift-Bailout Conferees Adopt Steps
@  To Limit High-Risk S&amp;L Investments
@  ----
@  By Paulette Thomas
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> GOVMT </CO>
<IN> SAVINGS AND LOANS, THRIFTS, CREDIT UNIONS (SAL)
TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM)
BOND MARKET NEWS (BON)
BANKS (BNK) </IN>
<GV> CONGRESS (CNG)
EXECUTIVE (EXE) </GV>
<DATELINE> WASHINGTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   House and Senate negotiators on the huge savings-and-loan bill adopted steps to crack down on risky thrift investments, including a ban on investing in high-yield "junk" bonds. 

   As the House-Senate conference on the $166 billion legislation worked into the night, negotiators agreed to force thrifts to divest themselves of any non-investment grade bonds and direct equity investments within five years. 
S&amp;Ls could sell junk bonds to outside investors or to separately capitalized affiliates, while direct equity investments would be sold to outside investors. 

   These agreements are the latest in a series of provisions that toughen accounting regulations for S&amp;Ls and restrict their operations to more closely resemble those of national banks. 

   Yesterday's approval of the provisions, which were contained in a Senate compromise proposal, also was a victory for Senate conferees, who have conceded to their House counterparts on a long list of provisions. 
"We would like a few shreds of skin on our skeletons when we return to the Senate," said Sen. John Heinz (R., Pa.). 
Conferees said they expect to wrap up their work on the landmark bill this week. 

   The House-passed bill would have banned investment in junk bonds, while the Senate's would have maintained current law allowing junk bonds to constitute 11% of a thrift's portfolio. 

   Thrifts hold billions of dollars of junk bonds -- high-risk investments that as part of S&amp;L portfolios are backed by federal deposit insurance, and ultimately the taxpayer. 
Selling the junk bonds to outsiders or separately capitalized affiliates would insulate the federal insurance fund from the risk. 

   Although a recent General Accounting Office study determined that junk bonds have yet to contribute significantly to thrift failures, many economists predict that an economic downturn could topple thrifts with large holdings of junk bonds. 

   The biggest issue still to be determined is how the huge bailout package will be financed. 
When the conferees take up the issue today, the Senate will continue pushing the Bush administration's plan to raise $50 billion through industry bonds, with only the interest payments of more than $100 billion to be included on the federal budget. 

   The House would raise the cash with Treasury bonds, which would carry a lower interest rate but would require an exemption from the Gramm-Rudman deficit-reduction law. 
Administration officials this week said they would recommend a presidential veto if the conference rejects the Bush funding plan. 

   In deliberations yesterday, the conferees spent nearly an hour determining the future of one person: Danny Wall, the embattled chairman of the Federal Home Loan Bank Board. 
In the end, the House acquiesced to the Senate, preserving Mr. Wall's post as top thrift regulator and maintaining the revamped Bank Board's role as regulator of state-chartered S&amp;Ls. 
"These two issues are fundamental to our view of this bill," said Sen. Donald Riegle (D., Mich.), chairman of the Senate Banking Committee. 

   The House bill would have substantially curtailed the thrift regulator's authority by transferring oversight of state-chartered thrifts to bank regulators. 
The House also would have required another Senate confirmation hearing for Mr. Wall, who has incurred lawmakers' wrath for underestimating the cost of the thrift crisis. 

   The negotiators also tentatively agreed that thrifts will have to maintain capital -- which serves as a cash cushion against losses -- equal to at least 1.5% of assets. 
Mortgage-servicing rights, contracts under which thrifts collect payments and otherwise administer mortgages originated by others, could count toward the 1.5% in proportions to be determined by federal regulators. 

   Under a compromise, the tougher capital standards would take effect four months after passage of the bill. 
Enforcement actions against thrifts that don't meet capital standards, such as limitations on growth, would be effective Jan. 1, 1991. 

   Conferees also adopted language raising to 70% from 60% the level of investments a thrift must maintain in mortgage-related assets in order to qualify for low-interest advances from the home loan bank system and tax breaks. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Enterprise -- Washington Update:
@  Tax Credits Could Cool Interest in Minimum Wage;
@  SBA Chief Studies Priorities; Patent Plan Questioned
@  ----
@  By Jeanne Saddler </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> TAXES LABOR GOVMT </CO>
<IN> INSURANCE (INS)
ENVIRONMENT (ENV) </IN>
<GV> CONGRESS (CNG) </GV>
<TEXT>
   TAX CREDITS ADVANCE, pushing minimum-wage measures to the background. 

   Talk grows in Congress about adopting an earned income tax credit for the working poor. 
After President Bush's veto of legislation to raise the minimum wage to $4.55 an hour from $3.35, "members feel they have to vote for something, and enacting the tax credit gives them a fallback," says Susan Cousins, a lobbyist with the Chamber of Commerce's Small Business Center. 

   Such action also would make compromise minimum-wage legislation less urgent. 
The main Democratic proposal by Rep. Austin Murphy, (D., Pa.), would raise the minimum wage only to $4.25 as Mr. Bush supports, but in two years instead of the three that the president wants. 
An alternative by William Goodling, (R., Pa.), would keep Mr. Bush's three-year wage proposal but cut use of his proposed lower training wage to four months from six months. 

   A slowing economy might further cool interest in minimum-wage action. 

   ---

   SBA'S CHIEF BEGINS to focus on priorities and personnel at her agency. 

   One lobbyist asks about SBA Administrator Susan Engeleiter after her first three months: "What's she doing? 
She doesn't have an agenda." 
She counters that she is busy reviewing all SBA activities so "our programs don't offer an opportunity for another Wedtech" scandal. 
She will announce detailed priorities early next month. 
Meantime, she is expanding the SBA's influence: It will be included in working groups that report to the White House Domestic and Economic Policy Councils on such issues as tort reform and technology. 

   Ms. Engeleiter also has replaced some regional administrators and plans to name an associate deputy to oversee all financial programs. 
The latter prospect, some at the agency say, is a factor in plans by the respected head of the Office of Finance and Investment to retire after 21 years with the SBA. 
But the official, Edwin Holloway, 58, says he had planned to retire all along. 

   "We're still happy" with Ms. Engeleiter "and everybody I know says she's doing fine," says John Satagaj, Small Business Legislative Council president. 

   ---

   ROSTENKOWSKI'S REVENGE? 
The National Federation of Independent Business says House Ways and Means Committee Chairman Dan Rostenkowski is trying to punish the group. 
Its offense: pressing for repeal of the Section 89 employee-benefits tax provision instead of backing his compromise bill. 
"A committee staffer called and asked if we'd 'considered the consequences' of pushing for repeal," says lobbyist John Motley. 
A tax deduction for self-employed business owners then disappeared from the panel's budget bill. 
"We're not out to punish anybody; that's unfair and untrue," says a committee aide. 

   ---

   HEALTH-BENEFITS ALTERNATIVE: Small-business groups are talking about a new bill being put together by Senate Banking Committee Chairman Donald Riegle, (D., Mich.), that is expected to propose expanding health coverage without requiring employers to provide the insurance. 
It would be an alternative to Sen. Edward Kennedy's, (D., Mass.), mandated health-benefits bill, which small business strongly opposes. 

   ---

   A PATENT PROPOSAL could hurt U.S. small businesses, some experts warn. 

   The Patent Office considers a rule to require applicants to disclose information that might result in denial of a patent. 
Small firms applaud the goal, but worry about other proposed requirements: Applicants would have to file the new data within three months of finding it or within three months of the original application. 
Otherwise, there would be fees for late disclosure. 

   Researching and filing new information is a technical process that often takes several months. 
That can be especially difficult and costly for small companies, says Washington patent attorney Barry Bretschneider. 
Another worry: U.S. companies will be at a disadvantage against foreign applicants. 
Because of an international treaty, foreign firms get some patent protection in the U.S. for a year after filing a patent abroad, giving them more time to research and disclose new information, Mr. Bretschneider says. 

   "We're just trying to encourage people to give us information as soon as they get it," says Charles Van Horn, the Patent Office's deputy solicitor. 

   ---

   SMALL TALK: The Service Station Dealers of America estimates that the administration's clean-air proposal could cost its members up to $125,000 each and perhaps lead to "chaos in car care.". . . . 
SBA Chief Engeleiter, the mother of two, decides that the agency's new headquarters will include a day-care center. 
But her children, ages 2 and 6, won't attend.

<HL> U.S. and Japan Make
@  Some Slight Progress
@  In Talks on Air Service </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> JAPAN </CO>
<IN> AIRLINES (AIR) </IN>
<GV> EXECUTIVE (EXE) </GV>
<DATELINE> WASHINGTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The U.S. and Japan provisionally agreed to add new chartered flights to serve their major cities, but remained far apart on the expansion of regularly scheduled air services between the two countries. 

   At the conclusion of three days of closed-door negotiations here, the State Department said another meeting has to be held -- probably in September or October -- to narrow these differences. 

   The U.S. has proposed a large expansion package, including seven new routes combining passenger and cargo services and two new all-cargo routes. 
Japan, claiming serious airport limitations, is offering three new routes, including one to haul cargo only. 
Carriers from both countries would serve any new routes. 

   Charles Angevine, the department's chief aviation negotiator, expressed hope that the two sides will bridge that gap. 
The U.S. has told the Japanese delegation that its position "isn't inflexible," he said. 
A Japanese spokesman said, "We have identified our differences and will proceed from there." 

   Currently, each country has 300 chartered flights annually to the other country. 
In addition to agreeing in principle to more chartered flights, Japan indicated that it could accommodate the U.S. in other areas, including an increase in scheduled services to Guam and Saipan and additional stopovers in Alaska by carriers of both countries, Mr. Angevine said. 

   This week's talks represented Washington's third attempt since last December to expand these trans-Pacific links. 
Several U.S. airlines are trying to break into this lucrative market or to gain additional rights, while an even larger number of U.S. cities are bidding to become new gateways to the Pacific.

<HL> Watkins Sets Plans to Form
@  National Energy Strategy </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> NATURAL GAS, PIPELINES (LNG) </IN>
<GV> ENERGY DEPARTMENT (ERG)
EXECUTIVE (EXE) </GV>
<DATELINE> WASHINGTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Energy Secretary James Watkins announced plans to develop a national energy strategy by December 1990 that is to be derived, in part, from an elaborate process of public hearings. 

   The plans to draft a comprehensive Bush administration energy policy came as the president signed a bill removing the last price controls on natural gas at the wellhead. 

   Mr. Watkins said there have been plenty of studies done in recent years but no "action plan" to put U.S. energy goals into effect. 
He promised to give a first draft to Congress by April, with final recommendations for adoption by Mr. Bush in December 1990. 

   The Bush administration's determination to draw up an energy policy contrasts with the Reagan administration, which was accused of not having one. 

   Mr. Watkins, a vocal advocate of nuclear power, said there is no preconceived focus, although he ruled out such devices as price controls in favor of free-market approaches. 
The plan is to address not only future problems, such as global warming, but current problems, such as rising U.S. oil imports. 

   The first hearings will be held in August and September in Tulsa, Okla.; Boise, Idaho; Seattle; and Louisville, Ky. 
In addition, the Energy Department will use scientists at its national laboratories to help draw up short-term, mid-term and long-term policy plans.

<HL> Law:
@  MasterCard, Visa Sued by 12 States
@  Over Actions in Debit-Card Market
@  ---
@  Suit Says Firms Bought Up
@  Potential Competitors,
@  Froze Out Some Others
@  ----
@  By Amy Dockser
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> FINANCIAL, ACCOUNTING, LEASING (FIN) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Twelve states sued MasterCard International Inc. and Visa International Inc. alleging the companies violated antitrust laws in an effort to control the growing market in "debit cards" used by consumers to make retail purchases. 

   Point-of-sale debit cards can take the place of cash, credit cards, or checks by electronically transferring funds out of a consumer's bank account at the time of purchase. 

   The suit, filed in federal court in New York, involves a highly publicized 1987 joint venture begun by the two companies in 1987 to introduce the first nationwide debit card, called Entree. 

   The suit alleges that, as part of the joint venture, the companies agreed not to introduce their own separate systems to compete with Entree and to limit participation in the network to banks that are members of both credit-card associations. 
Non-banks with credit cards, such as American Express Co. and Sears, Roebuck &amp; Co., are excluded from participating in the venture. 

   New York State Attorney General Robert Abrams said the companies sought a stranglehold on the national debit-card market without actually putting the system in operation. 
The idea, he said, is to protect their own well-established credit cards from competition from other companies that might develop debit-card systems. 

   The suit charges that to prevent the entry of any other competitors, Visa acquired control of Plus Systems Inc., a national network of automated teller machines, and Interlink Network, the nation's largest regional debit-card network. 
Meanwhile, MasterCard acquired Cirrus System Inc., a national automated-teller network. 
Mr. Abrams said these acquired companies had been planning on entering the point-of-sale debit-card market before they were purchased. 

   A spokesman for Visa, based in San Mateo, Calif., said the company hadn't yet seen the suit and wouldn't comment. 
Robert Norton, general counsel for New Yorkbased MasterCard, said the company hadn't violated antitrust laws. 

   The suit claims that by February, more than 177 financial institutions had joined Entree and 1.4 million automated-teller cards had been issued with the Entree seal but that no merchants had been brought into the program. 
Thus, the cards could not be used. 
At the same time, the suit alleges, Visa and Mastercard continued to promote their own credit cards and ATM cards, which don't involve immediate electronic transfer of funds out of the consumer's bank account. 

   A MasterCard spokesman said the company has been a catalyst in the creation of a national network but that it is up to the banks to sign up merchants and market the concept to consumers. 
He said that when merchants perceive consumer interest they will be willing to make the economic investment required to install the machines in their stores. 

   The suit seeks to enjoin the Entree joint venture, compel the divestiture of Cirrus, Plus and Interlink, and assess civil penalties of up to $1 million for each violation. 
In New York alone, Visa and MasterCard each could face up to $10 million in penalties. 

   The two-year investigation that led to the filing of the suits grew out of an inquiry begun in 1987 when Visa President Charles Russell sent member banks a letter that some interpreted as a call to boycott American Express products. 
The letter was sent following the introduction of American Express's Optima card, which is designed to compete directly with Visa and Mastercard. 

   The Justice Department launched its own investigation concerning the letter but decided not to pursue any action, said a spokeswoman for the department. 
She said she couldn't comment on whether the department was investigating the Entree matter. 

   In a statement, Lawrence T. Kurlander, senior vice president of American Express Travel Related Services, Inc., said the company applauds the states' lawsuit. 
MasterCard and Visa "have pooled their extraordinary assets and resources in a manner designed to exclude American Express and others from potential markets, and thereby deprive consumers of the benefit of vigorous competition," Mr. Kurlander said. 

   James Shannon, attorney general of Massachusetts, said that in recent years the states have taken an active role in pursuing antitrust litigation because they have perceived it as not being vigorously pursued on the federal level. 

   Lloyd Constantine, chief of the antitrust bureau in the New York attorney general's office, said that prior to filing the suit, Visa and Mastercard were asked to abandon Entree but refused. 
"They're not doing anything to let anyone use Entree, but it's a significant barrier to anyone entering the market," said Mr. Constantine. 
"It's not dead. 
It's a half-breathing carcass." 

</TEXT>

<HL> Technology:
@  Compaq Computer
@  Posts Increase of 43%
@  In 2nd-Quarter Profit </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> CPQ </CO>
<IN> EARNINGS (ERN)
COMPUTERS AND INFORMATION TECHNOLOGY (CPR) </IN>
<DATELINE> HOUSTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Compaq Computer Corp. posted a second-quarter earnings increase of 43%, despite analysts' projections of a softening personal-computer market. 

   The computer maker said net income rose to $83.9 million, or $1.96 a share, from $58.6 million, or $1.46 a share, a year earlier. 
Sales increased 58% to $722 million from $457 million. 

   In the first half, Compaq earned $167 million, or $3.92 a share, up from $105 million, or $2.67 a share, a year ago. 
Sales rose to $1.4 billion from $896 million. 

   Rod Canion, Compaq's president and chief executive officer, said he started the year believing the personal-computer market would grow 15% to 20% in 1989 and that sees no reason to change his prediction. 
"We're really not seeing the kinds of things people are talking about," he added, referring to some forecasts that market expansion has peaked. 

   Mr. Canion attributed the company's performance to demand for a recently introduced desktop computer based on a faster Intel microprocessor, the 386/33. 
In addition, foreign sales continued to boom, accounting for 44% of revenue and making Compaq the second-largest supplier of personal computers to the European business market. 
Compaq also started selling its computers in Chile, Venezuela and Colombia, new subsidiaries were established in Denmark and Norway, and the company is planning to nearly double its manufacturing capacity in Ireland. 

   Mr. Canion also said Compaq and other personal-computer makers who joined forces to provide an alternative PC design to challenge International Business Machines Corp.'s Micro Channel Architecture hope to introduce their first machines late this year. 
Then it will be up to the marketplace to decide "if either product delivers anything useful," he said. 

   In New York Stock Exchange composite trading, Compaq shares fell $1.25 to $86.875 each.

<TEXT>
   About 150 people turned out for what was billed as the first "open outcry" auction of bad credit card debt. 

   Between 30 and 40 bidders paid an average of 1.45 cents on the dollar for the $35 million in charged-off debt, said Tom Oliver, a vice president at Bank of America.

   The auction was the subject of a recent Wall Street Journal article.

<HL> Law:
@  Princeton/Newport
@  Jurors Set to Begin
@  Deliberations Today </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> STOCK MARKET, OFFERINGS (STK)
SECURITIES INDUSTRY (SCR) </IN>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Jury deliberations are set to begin today in the five-week racketeering and fraud trial of five former officials of Princeton/Newport Limited Partnership and a former trader at Drexel Burnham Lambert Inc. 

   The government has accused the six defendants of conspiring to create illegitimate tax losses through a series of prearranged securities transactions. 
In these deals, Princeton/Newport allegedly sold securities at a loss and then repurchased them by prior agreement at the same or slightly higher prices. 
Such a practice is known as stock parking. 

   The jury must decide not only whether the defendants are guilty of cheating the government out of taxes, but whether they should be convicted of violating the Racketeer Influenced and Corrupt Organizations law, or RICO. 
The law carries heavy sentences and asset forfeitures upon conviction; it also allows for pretrial restraint of defendants' assets. 

   On trial are James Sutton Regan, the managing general partner of Princeton/Newport, and Jack Z. Rabinowitz, Charles M. Zarzecki and Paul A. Berkman, all of whom are general partners of the investment firm. 
Also on trial are Steven B. Smotrich, Princeton/Newport's comptroller, and Bruce Lee Newberg, a former Drexel junk-bond trader. 

   The government relied mostly on dozens of tape recordings of phone conversations between Princeton/Newport officials to present its case. 
Prosecutors also called several witnesses, including Frederick Joseph, Drexel's chief executive officer, and William Ward Hale, a former Princeton/Newport trader. 

   Among those testifying for the defense were Messrs. 
Regan and Smotrich, and Joseph Harch, a Drexel managing director who was called to testify on behalf of Mr. Newberg.

<HL> Business Brief -- Maxicare Health Plans Inc.:
@  HMO Firm Offers Creditors
@  Plan for Its Reorganization </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> HEALTH CARE PROVIDERS, MEDICINE, DENTISTRY (HEA)
BANKRUPTCIES (BCY) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Maxicare Health Plans Inc., which is in Chapter 11 bankruptcy proceedings, said it distributed to creditors a plan for its reorganization. 

   Maxicare said the proposals call for creditors to receive various amounts of cash, debt and equity securities -- suggesting a dilution of current common shareholders' stake. 

   The Los Angeles-based owner and operator of health maintenance organizations declined to disclose details of the proposals. 
Under Chapter 11 of the bankruptcy law, a company continues to operate with protection from creditors' lawsuits while it works out a plan to repay debts. 

   One of the nation's largest health maintenance organizations, Maxicare filed for bankruptcy on March 15. 

   The proposed settlement terms would apply to debts incurred prior to the company's Chapter 11 filing. 
The company didn't say what settlement it plans to offer more recent creditors. 
A spokesman for Maxicare said that, for current obligations, "generally speaking, the company is paying on a current basis."

<DOCNO> WSJ890727-0038 </DOCNO>
<DD> = 890727 </DD>
<AN> 890727-0038. </AN>
<HL> Eastern Withdraws
@  Its Plea to Break
@  Pilots' Contract
@  ---
@  By Bridget O'Brian
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> TEX LABOR </CO>
<IN> AIRLINES (AIR)
BANKRUPTCIES (BCY) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Eastern Airlines unexpectedly withdrew its bankruptcy court request to break its labor contract with its pilots. 
The carrier asserted that its plea was designed to bring the pilots to the bargaining table but that the ploy had backfired. 

   When Eastern sought permission June 21 to abrogate the contract, the move was widely interpreted as its long-awaited first effort toward becoming a low-cost, non-union carrier like its sister company, Continental Airlines. 
Six years ago, Continental, also a unit of Texas Air Corp., used bankruptcy to abrogate its union contracts. 

   But yesterday, Eastern painted a different picture, maintaining that it filed the motion last month only because it "hoped to initiate a series of negotiations which would ultimately result in a prompt settlement" with its 3,500 striking pilots. 
It contended that it has belatedly discovered the filing "may be an impediment to resolving the dispute," because it made pilots reluctant to discuss any contract changes. 

   The pilots' union scoffed at Eastern's version of events, and hailed the withdrawal as "a major victory." 
Eastern dropped the request "only because it knew it would lose," contended a pilot spokesman. 
Several pilots involved in the matter said David R. Shapiro, the court-appointed bankruptcy examiner, had told them he would oppose Eastern's request to break the contract. 
Last night, Mr. Shapiro couldn't be reached for comment. 

   Whatever the case, withdrawal of the request is significant. 
Had Eastern pressed the issue and prevailed, it would have been free to impose unilaterally on its pilots new wages and benefits. 
It has sought more than $65 million in wage cuts and workrule concessions from them, and has argued that such savings are essential to its plan to slash costs and successfully reorganize. 

   Now, under airline labor law, the current contract between Eastern and the Air Line Pilots Association will remain in force even though the pact expired more than a year ago. 
Officially, the pilots are striking in sympathy with Eastern's machinists, not over their own contract. 
So, to resolve its pilot-labor issues, Eastern still faces the prospect of a lengthy negotiation procedure controlled by the National Mediation Board, rather than the potentially faster route through bankruptcy court. 

   Despite more than three weeks of continued negotiation since Eastern asked to end the contract, the sides reached an impasse last week and remain at a stalemate. 

   Cost savings weren't mentioned in Eastern's announcement yesterday. 
Airline officials say, however, that Eastern is achieving some of the savings it sought as it hires new pilots, who are paid at a sharply lower rate than previously hired pilots. 

</TEXT>

@  Plans Stock Offering;
@  Compaq to Cut Stake </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> CNNR CPQ </CO>
<IN> STOCK MARKET, OFFERINGS (STK)
TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM)
EARNINGS (ERN) </IN>
<DATELINE> SAN JOSE, Calif.  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Conner Peripherals Inc. said it plans a public offering of 6.3 million shares. 

   It said it expects to file a registration statement with the Securities and Exchange Commission within the next few days. 
The computer-products company currently has 36.2 million shares outstanding. 
A price range for the offering hasn't been determined, a spokesman said. 

   Conner said four million of the shares to be sold will be new. 
The remaining 2.3 milion shares will be sold by certain shareholders, including Compaq Computer Corp., which will sell one million of its 13.6 million Conner shares. 
Compaq's current holdings represent a 40% Conner stake. 

   Compaq, Houston, said it is selling the shares because it no longer considers having such a large equity stake in Conner vital for either company's development. 
It said it may lower its stake in Conner further in future years. 

   Conner said proceeds will be used for various capital expenditures and for general corporate purposes. 

   In national over-the-counter trading, Conner closed at $10.625, off 37.5 cents. 

   Separately, Conner said second-quarter net income more than doubled, to $9.4 million, or 26 cents a share, on sales of $164 million. 
In the year-ago period, Conner had net of $4.4 million, or 13 cents a share, on sales of $50.1 million. 
For the six months, Conner earned $15.1 million, or 42 cents a share, on sales of $294.8 million. 
A year ago, it earned $7.7 million, or 24 cents a share, on sales of $92.7 million. 

   Conner, which makes disk drives for microcomputers and workstations, attributed its surging results to broad demand for its entire product line and to profit margins that improved because of cost-cutting, among other things.

<HL> Abreast of the Market:
@  Industrial Shares Advance 29.97,
@  Aided by Heavy Program Buying
@  ----
@  By David Wilson
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> STOCK MARKET, OFFERINGS (STK)
STOCK INDEXES (NDX) </IN>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Heavy futures-related program buying caused the stock market to jump sharply. 

   The Dow Jones Industrial Average surged 29.97 to 2613.05, erasing its losses earlier this week and setting another post-crash high. 
The rally also sent Standard &amp; Poor's 500-Stock Index and the New York Stock Exchange Composite beyond their former all-time highs, set in August 1987. 

   Technology stocks remained out of favor as weak second-quarter earnings reports from Cray Research and Amdahl, arriving on the heels of lower expectations for the personal-computer industry's growth, hurt the group. 

   On the other hand, the latest outburst of takeover speculation in media and airline stocks sent those sectors higher, and consumer-oriented issues -- the market's leaders throughout most of the year -- posted gains amid some well-received earnings reports. 

   While interest in those issues helped establish the market's direction after some indecisive early trading, it took buy programs related to stock-index arbitrage to send prices soaring. 

   The program buying was rooted in renewed optimism about the economic outlook, based on further evidence that the Federal Reserve had signaled an easing of its monetary policy and expectations that the report on the economy's second-quarter performance would point toward slower but sustained growth. 

   Gross national product figures for the quarter are expected to show that the economy expanded at a 2% annual rate, according to a consensus forecast of economists polled by Dow Jones Capital Markets Report. 

   Against that backdrop, stock-index futures prices rallied to relatively large premiums to the cash indexes around 3 p.m. EDT and stayed there until minutes before the close. 
Traders capitalized on the advance by selling futures contracts and buying stocks. 

   The S&amp;P 500, whose previous all-time high was 336.77, easily exceeded that mark by rising 4.17 to 338.05. 
The New York Stock Exchange Composite gained 2.04 to 188.14; its best close ever had been 187.99. 
Also, the Dow Jones Equity Market Index jumped 3.60 to 316.97. 

   Despite a slowdown in trading activity at midafternoon as many investors set their sights on the GNP figures, Big Board volume climbed to 188,270,000 shares from 179.3 million Tuesday. 
Advancing issues outnumbered decliners on the Big Board by more than a 2-to-1 margin, with 981 issues rising and 483 falling. 

   The American Stock Exchange Index, which established all-time highs in six consecutive sessions earlier this month, also closed at a new peak. 

   The burst of confidence about the economy signaled a departure from the market's recent nervousness about the prospects for a "soft landing" -- moderate growth, lower rates and easing inflationary pressures. 

   "One day we're looking at recession, and the next day we're looking at inflation," said Joseph A. DeMarco, managing director for equity trading at the Marinvest unit of Marine Midland. 

   That uncertainty has helped preserve the market's focus on consumer stocks, as their earnings tend to hold steady even when economic growth slows, he said. 

   Consumer stocks benefited from reports of improved earnings at PepsiCo, which rose 1 1/2 to 59, and Anheuser-Busch, which added 1 3/8 to 44 3/8. 
PepsiCo's second-quarter results were better than analysts had expected; earnings at Anheuser-Busch met forecasts, and the company raised its dividend by 22%. 

   Elsewhere in the sector, Philip Morris climbed 6 3/8 to 157 7/8, American Brands rose 3 to 77 1/8, Procter &amp; Gamble added 1 3/4 to 119 1/8, Gerber Products jumped 4 3/4 to 89 5/8, Ralston Purina surged 4 to 99 1/2, CPC International climbed 2 to 69 1/8 and Colgate-Palmolive added 2 1/4 to 57 3/4. 

   The buying in the consumer sector pushed drug stocks sharply higher as well. 
Merck rose 1 7/8 to 75 5/8, Warner-Lambert soared 5 3/8 to 107, Squibb advanced 3 3/8 to 87 3/4, Rorer Group rallied 1 5/8 to 39 1/4, Eli Lilly jumped 2 7/8 to 62 5/8 and Pfizer rose 1 3/8 to 61 1/4. 

   Entertainment and media company stocks wrote a rally script of their own. 
Investors with a fondness for movies, magazines and television started spending the $7 billion they will be receiving from Time's purchase this week of 100 million Warner Communications shares at $70 each. 
Investors won't actually get the money until next Monday, but they can buy stocks with it now because the trades won't be settled until a week later. 

   Walt Disney jumped 5 3/8 to 109 3/4 on volume of more than 1.3 million shares. 
MCA, parent of Universal Pictures, rose 3 3/8 to 67. 
Although some traders cited rumors that either might become a target for Paramount Communications, which failed to win Time, analysts said antitrust problems probably would doom such a bid. 
Analysts also cited Disney's expected rapid earnings growth as a reason for investors to plow their Warner proceeds into Disney. 

   Other media stocks that rose included Tribune, which gained 2 1/2 to 60 1/4; McGraw-Hill, up 1 7/8 to 75 1/4; Time Warner, up 1 5/8 to 139 1/2; CBS, up 5 1/8 to 218 3/4; Capital Cities/ABC, up 9 to 514 1/2; Gannett, up 1 1/4 to 47 7/8; Knight Ridder, up 1 1/4 to 55; and Washington Post, up 2 1/4 to 291. 

   The takeover-driven gains among airline issues sent the Dow Jones Transportation Average up 13.59 to 1266.66, marking its third consecutive record close. 
AMR, which owns American Airlines, rose 1 7/8 to 68 5/8; Delta Air Lines gained 1 5/8 to 72 1/4, and USAir Group firmed 5/8 to 50. 

   Rumors of a possible restructuring sent Chrysler soaring 2 1/2 to 26 1/8. 

   Cummins Engine surged 2 1/2 to 60 1/4. 
Industrial Equity (Pacific), controlled by New Zealand investor Sir Ronald Brierley, disclosed that it holds a 9.9% stake in the company and plans to seek U.S. antitrust clearance to boost its holdings to 25%. 

   Knogo jumped 2 to 15 1/8 on the news that a group including Walter &amp; Edwin Schloss Associates, a New York investment partnership, holds a 5.21% stake. 

   Fischbach rose 5/8 to 25 1/8. 
The company accepted a takeover offer of $29.835 a share in cash from a group led by Asher Devere, a British investor. 

   Northrop, the prime contractor for the Stealth bomber, dropped 3/4 to 25. 
The House voted to halt production of the plane for one year in order for the Air Force to develop a cheaper, scaled-down development program. 

   The Amex Index climbed 2.66 to 372.78, topping the previous high of 372.40, set last Wednesday. 
Volume totaled 13,120,000 shares. 

   Sotheby's Holdings rose 1 5/8 to 24 1/4. 
The company reported that world-wide auction sales for the 1988-89 season rose 48% to a record $2.3 billion.

<HL> Oil Firms Post
@  Varied Earnings
@  For 2nd Quarter
@  ---
@  Restructuring Hurt Texaco;
@  Occidental, Shell Oil Saw
@  Increases in Net Income
@  ----
@  By Caleb Solomon
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> TX RD SC OXY </CO>
<IN> PETROLEUM (PET)
EARNINGS (ERN) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Texaco Inc. said second-quarter net income declined, reflecting the last year's restructuring and asset sales, while Occidental Petroleum Corp. posted a small increase and the Shell Oil Co. unit of Royal Dutch/Shell Group had a large jump. 

   Crude-oil prices that were nearly $3 a barrel higher in the quarter compared with a year earlier contributed to operating gains at Texaco and Shell. 
All three reported robust profits from refined products. 
Toward the end of the quarter and in this month, however, the chemical and gasoline markets have weakened considerably and crude prices have declined, which could soften this quarter's results. 

   "We are encouraged by our record first-half performance," said Frank H. Richardson, Shell president, "but at the same time {Shell is} cautious with respect to the second-half outlook, given current trends." 

   TEXACO 

   Net fell 21% to $366 million, or $1.39 a share, from $464 million, or $1.91 a share, in the year-earlier quarter that included a gain of $225 million, or 92 cents a share, from the sale of its West German unit. 
Revenue for Texaco, which has shed many assets in a major restructuring, fell 8.9% to $8.45 billion from $9.28 billion. 

   Texaco, of White Plains, N.Y., said earnings from continuing operations rose more than 34% in the quarter. 
Texaco's operating profit from U.S. oil and natural-gas production more than doubled to $168 million. 
Foreign-production profit declined, largely because of loss of production from the explosion at the Piper Alpha platform in the North Sea last summer. 
Production was also lower because of the sale of Texaco's Canadian and German units. 

   Chemicals operating profit more than doubled to $88 million and refining and marketing profits also rose sharply. 
But such gains may not be repeated for a while. 
"End-of-quarter margins . . . reflected pressure from a softening of wholesale and retail gasoline prices," said James W. Kinnear, president and chief executive officer. 
He said chemicals margins also narrowed as the quarter ended. 

   OCCIDENTAL 

   Net rose 3.9% to $80 million from $77 million as sales rose 6.5% to $5.05 billion from $4.74 billion. 
Per-share net fell to 29 cents from 34 cents, reflecting an increase in the number of shares outstanding. 

   The bulk of Los Angeles-based Occidental's earnings come from its chemicals operations, in which operating profit rose 38% to $306 million. 
The period's results include a full quarter of Cain Chemical Inc.'s operations, acquired in May 1988. 
Armand Hammer, chairman of Occidental, said, "We are enthusiastic about the continued strong results of our chemical division." 

   The company's oil and gas results plunged because of the absence of crudeoil production in the North Sea because of the accident at the Piper platform, where Occidental is the operator. 

   SHELL OIL 

   The Houston unit of Royal Dutch/Shell Group said profit surged 61% to $440 million from $274 million. 
Revenue rose 8.7% to $5.87 billion from $5.4 billion. 
Strong margins accounted for record chemicals earnings of $186 million in the quarter, up from $133 million. 

   Shell's oil and gas production earnings nearly doubled to $168 million. 
The company said domestic-crude prices it received averaged $16.55 a barrel, the highest in nearly two years and up $2.93 from a year earlier. 
Refining and marketing profits were also up strongly.

<HL> LAB NOTES
@  ----
@  By Amal Kumar Naj </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<TEXT>
   Electric Stimulation 

   Might Halt Bone Decay 

   BONE degeneration might be stopped or even reversed by electric stimulation, medical researchers at the University of Pennsylvania hope. 

   Osteoporosis afflicts many older women because of estrogen loss following menopause or a hysterectomy. 
Osteoporosis also can result from immobilization, such as when an arm or leg is in a cast. 
It even has been found in astronauts after prolonged space travel. 
The inactivity deprives bone of the physical stress necessary to stimulate its growth. 

   Carl Brighton, chairman of the orthopedics department, says four studies on lab rats have formed a "solid foundation" for experiments in humans, the first of which is slated for September. 
He said the studies grew out of his earlier work with electric stimulation to heal fractured bones. 

   The researchers induced spinal osteoporosis in rats by castrating them, the equivalent of menopause, and "disuse" osteoporosis in rats by immobilizing the bone between the knee and ankle. 

   Two to 4 1/2 months later, after the immobilized bones began to weaken, a mild electric current was applied to rats continuously for six to eight weeks. 
The weakened bones began to regenerate, reversing the osteoporosis. 

   In another set of experiments, electric stimulation prevented spinal osteoporosis when applied immediately after castration. 

   Dr. Brighton says that while evidence suggests electric stimulation starts to regenerate bone growth, no one has determined how it works at the cell level. 

   Hearing Loss Starts 

   Early in Farm Kids 

   THE HEARING LOSS commonly found among adult farmers may begin in childhood, says a report in the American Journal of Public Health. 
The study suggests that farm children should begin using ear plugs to try to minimize hearing loss. 

   In the study of 872 vocational agriculture students in central Wisconsin, researchers found that between 71% and 74% of the students, mostly boys, who lived and worked on a farm had some loss of hearing in at least one ear. 

   In contrast, only 46% of those who didn't have any exposure to farm machinery suffered some degree of hearing impairment. 

   The hearing loss was most evident at high frequencies, which the researchers from Marshfield Clinic, Marshfield, Wis., attributed to farm machinery noise. 

   The left ear was most often affected among students living and working on the farm. 
This finding confirmed previous studies showing that a tractor driver's left ear is most exposed to engine noise because he observes his work by looking over his right shoulder. 

   The audiologists also measured sound levels of tractors and other farm implements and found that many emitted noise at levels above those set by the Occupational Safety and Health Administration. 
"It is important to note that these standards are primarily meant to apply to adults," the report said, suggesting that the high noise levels may be even more damaging to children. 

   Exotic Herb May Speed 

   Colon Cancer Detection 

   RESEARCHERS are studying a Central American plant that may help them detect colon cancer early. 

   University of Michigan researchers have isolated a new protein from the seeds of amaranthin, a purplish herb, that binds to colon cancer cells and premalignant tissues, which may eventually become malignant. 

   The researchers said the protein may help identify potential problems early and also may eliminate the need for more painful testing for some people who aren't at high risk of developing colon cancer. 

   "Long before a tissue becomes cancerous, it undergoes a number of changes, but no one is quite sure how to identify these tissues as precancerous," explains C. Richard Boland, associate professor of internal medicine. 

   In a study, the researchers took tissues from people with colon cancer, from people who were in the premalignant stage and from some patients with polyposis coli, a genetic disease characterized by widespread polyps in the colon that often turn malignant. 

   They stained tissues with the amaranthin protein and found that it adhered to all tissues with colon cancer, to 82% of premalignant tumors, to 83% of tissue with tumorous polyposis coli and to 60% of the non-tumorous type of polyposis coli. 

   The researchers hope to use the study to refine diagnosis of colon cancer. 
Currently, colon polyps and cancers are detected visually with a flexible scope inserted into the colon. 
But the colonoscopy usually cannot identify premalignant tissue. 
About 120,000 new cases of colon cancer are reported annually in the U.S., and about 60,000 people die from it. 

   The researchers said amaranthin could be in widespread use for detecting colon cancer within two years. 

   Infrared Rays Tell More Than Melon Squeezing 

   AN INFRARED device may tell whether a cantaloupe or watermelon is as sweet as it looks and smells. 

   U.S. Department of Agriculture scientists say the breadbox-size machine has tested 85% accurate in measuring the sugar content and ripeness of melons. 

   The device sends infrared light through the fruit, and a meter measures the amount of light absorbed. 
"The more infrared (light) absorbed, the sweeter the fruit," says Gerald G. Dull, a scientist at USDA's Agricultural Research Service. 

   The researchers say the machine, which is still being tested, could stop growers from mistakenly harvesting unripe melons and might be useful on other fruits and vegetables as well.

<HL> 'Yield-Curve Arbitrage' Rewards the Skillful
@  ----
@  By Craig Torres
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> FINANCIAL, ACCOUNTING, LEASING (FIN)
SECURITIES INDUSTRY (SCR) </IN>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   When interest rates were rising last autumn, many Wall Street firms took a licking on their big bond portfolios. 

   But traders at Bear, Stearns &amp; Co. turned the rising rates into a $20 million pot of gold through a little understood and often lucrative strategy that bond professionals call "yield-curve arbitrage." 

   The "yield curve" is a line that shows how yields differ on debt securities of different maturities -- ranging, for example, from three-month Treasury bills to 30-year Treasury bonds. 
Usually, short-term interest rates are lower than long-term rates, because investors want higher rates the longer they lend their money. 

   Arbitragers try to exploit changes in the yield curve, rather than making huge outright bets on where rates are headed. 
In a period of rising inflation, the yield curve steepens as long-term bond yields rise. 
In periods of falling inflation, the yield curve tends to flatten as longer rates fall. 

   This trading technique is becoming increasingly important for market-making power in the huge U.S. government bond business. 
A dealer who can ease a billion dollars of highly volatile long-term bonds into his own portfolio and hedge it discreetly through arbitrage often captures the business of coveted accounts such as the World Bank. 

   "When I have to move a billion or two, yield curve arbitrage is very often the only way you get market-making ability," says Yves Balcer, senior manager of the World Bank's $10 billion bond portfolio. 

   But not all dealers are good at this demanding trading strategy. 
Bond managers and even competitors consistently pick Bear Stearns and the secretive bond firm Greenwich Capital Markets Inc. as the Street's best arbitrage firms, rather than giant bond traders such as Merrill Lynch &amp; Co. and First Boston Corp. 

   Because they are good at this technique, Bear Searns and Greenwich often provide better execution than larger firms, bond investors said. 
Both firms have been known as expert arbs for years. 
"When we had to move a billion-dollar position it was often easier to go to Bear Stearns or Greenwich," says Donald Allison, who formerly traded for the World Bank. 

   Of course, the prime mover of the yield curve is the Federal Reserve, which guides short-term interest rates by controlling the federal funds rate, which banks charge each other for overnight loans. 

   Throughout last fall, the Fed maintained a tight grip on money. 
From September to December, it pushed the federal funds rate to about 9% from 8.25%. 

   Here's how Bear Stearns traded the yield curve in that rising short-term rate environment. 

   In three days in September, Bear Stearns sold "short" $5 billion in shortterm interest rate futures contracts on the Chicago Mercantile Exchange, $2.5 billion in one-year Treasury bills, and $1 billion in two-year notes. (In a short sale, an investor sells borrowed securities, hoping to profit by buying them back later at a cheaper price.) Keep in mind that a bond's yield moves in the opposite direction of its price. 
So, by selling these securities short, Bear Stearns expected them to drop in price and rise in yield. 

   In the same period, Bear Stearns bought $375 million of 10-year Treasury notes and $260 million 30-year bonds. 
That position offset some of the risk of the firm's huge short position. 

   By the middle of February, Bear Stearns reversed the trade, buying back the securities sold short and selling the notes and bonds it had bought. 
In the meantime, sharply rising short-term rates had changed the shape of the yield curve. 
Bear Stearns earned millions on its short positions as short-term interest rates ratcheted up, yet barely lost money on the longer-term securities it owned. 

   The result? 
Bear Stearns earned about $20 million on the trade. 

   Yield-curve arbitrage first became highly profitable in the early 1970s, when the Vietnam war sent inflation upward and interest rates began to gyrate. 
Then, in 1979, Fed Chairman Paul Volcker switched monetary policy to focus on controlling money growth, not interest rates. 
The result was tremendous swings in rates, heaven for arbitragers. 

   "It was like picking gold bricks up off the street," recalls William Michaelcheck, co-head of fixed income at Bear Stearns. 

   Now, just about all bond dealers realize the importance of arbitrage. 
But trading styles on Wall Street differ. 
Some firms still run outright speculative positions in which they either own or short huge blocks of bonds. 

   Arbitragers such as those at Bear Stearns don't speculate in large outright positions, but try to make money by arbitraging when the firm trades bonds for its own account or for clients. 

   Mr. Michaelcheck of Bear Stearns made arbitrage a priority at the firm when he joined from the World Bank in the early 1970s. 
At Greenwich Capital, co-founders Edwin Knetzger III and William Rainer saw arbitrage as a way to compete against such giant bond houses as Salomon Brothers Inc. and Goldman, Sachs &amp; Co. that had more capital. 
Arbitrage also allows cost-conscious dealers to discard the expensive appendages of outright bets on bonds -- namely, an economics staff to forecast rates and a staff abroad to watch the house account in foreign markets overnight. 

   Wall Street bond traders say the macho trading style of taking big outright risks will prevail at most firms. 
But that can mean trading results that swing regularly between big gains and losses. 
Arbitrage firms, by contrast, are more likely to make money steadily. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Business Brief -- First Capital Holdings Corp.:
@  Net Gain of 76% Reported
@  By Company for 2nd Period </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> FCH </CO>
<IN> EARNINGS (ERN) </IN>
<TEXT>
   First Capital Holdings Corp. reported second-quarter net income jumped 76%, mainly because of increased sales of insurance premiums. 

   The Los Angeles financial-services company said net rose to $17.7 million, or 48 cents a share, from $10.1 million, or 36 cents a share. 
Revenue rose 37% to $241.4 million from $175.9 million. 

   For the six months, net rose 50% to $33.3 million, or 90 cents a share, from $22.2 million, or 81 cents a share. 
Revenue rose 33% to $454.5 million from last year's $342.9 million. 

   In New York Stock Exchange composite trading, First Capital shares closed at $10 each, down 12.5 cents.

<HL> Credit Markets:
@  Bond Prices Mark Time as Further Signs Emerge
@  That the Fed Is Easing Credit Conditions Again
@  ----
@  By Constance Mitchell and Laurence Bauman
@  Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> MO GOVMT FRE+ EUROP JAPAN </CO>
<IN> FINANCIAL, ACCOUNTING, LEASING (FIN)
BOND MARKET NEWS (BON)
REAL ESTATE, REITS, LAND DEVELOPMENT (REL)
CONSTRUCTION, MATERIALS (CON)
BANKS (BNK)
SAVINGS AND LOANS, THRIFTS, CREDIT UNIONS (SAL)
ECONOMIC NEWS (ECO) </IN>
<GV> FEDERAL RESERVE BOARD (FED)
TREASURY DEPARTMENT (TRE) </GV>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Federal Reserve loosened its credit clamp again, and many economists expect further interest-rate declines in coming weeks. 

   Banks are likely to react to the Fed's latest move by matching Chase Manhattan Bank's prime-rate cut earlier this month to 10 1/2% from 11%. 
That will automatically lead to declines in many consumer lending rates tied to the prime, such as home-equity loans, and it will also mean lower rates for many small and medium-sized businesses. 

   But the Fed's latest credit-easing step did little to stir much enthusiasm in the bond markets. 
Treasury bond prices hardly budged all day, finishing close to Tuesday's late levels. 
One reason is that the Fed move had been widely expected. 
Also, the dollar fell in the currency markets, commodity prices rose, and investors are bracing for a huge volume of new Treasury borrowing expected in August. 

   Rates on short-term Treasury bills rose slightly. 
But the yield on the Treasury's new two-year notes, sold yesterday, averaged 7.75%, the lowest in over a year. 

   Corporate bond prices were flat, both on investment-grade issues as well on junk bonds. 
Junk-bond traders said there was little reaction to news that House and Senate negotiators approved steps to crack down on risky investments by thrift institutions. 
That included a ban on investing in junk issues, the high-yielding bonds of companies with low credit ratings or no ratings at all. 

   The Fed sent strong signals of a credit-easing move when it took no action, for the second day in a row, to prevent a decline in the interest rate on federal funds. 
This rate on overnight loans between banks is watched because the Fed often uses it as a signal for credit-policy changes. 
The rate averaged just over 9%, down from its range earlier this month of about 9 1/4%. 

   Paul DeNoon, an analyst at Manufacturers Hanover Securities Corp., said the first signal of a credit-easing move came Tuesday, and yesterday provided "the confirmation." 
If the Fed wasn't easing, "I don't think they would have tolerated" the lower funds rate for two days in a row. 

   Some bond traders initially reacted warily. 
They said it's too soon to be sure if the Fed has eased credit because yesterday was the final day of a two-week period in which banks must settle their reserve positions with the central bank. 
On such days, the funds rate often moves purely for technical reasons. 

   Nonetheless, the Fed's recent actions have convinced many investment managers that the central bank has moved to ease credit. 
Fed officials have indicated they want to see lower short-term interest rates to help bolster the economy. 

   Government reports today and over the next week are likely to reinforce the case for easier credit and pave the way for further moves. 

   Today, the Commerce Department will estimate the economy's second-quarter performance. 
According to the average estimate of 17 economists surveyed by Dow Jones Capital Markets Report, the economy grew at a 2% annual rate, after adjusting for inflation. 
On Aug. 4, the Labor Department will report July employment. 

   Treasury Securities 

   Individual investors bought large amounts of new two-year Treasury notes at yesterday's auction. 

   The average annual yield on the notes was 7.75%. 
That was down from 8.26% at the previous two-year note auction June 27, 1989, and was the lowest since 7.64% on April 27, 1988. 

   The Treasury received $1.49 billion in non-competitive tenders, which come mainly from individual investors. 
That was up sharply from $987 million at the June auction. 

   In the bond markets, the government's 8 7/8% bonds due in 2019 rose 3/32 to 108 18/32, to yield 8.10%. 
The 10-year notes were unchanged at 107 13/32, to yield 8%. 

   Among short-term rates, the latest three-month Treasury bills were quoted late yesterday at a discount rate of 8.02% bid, up slightly from 8% Tuesday. 
The latest six-month bills were quoted at a discount rate of 7.62% bid, up from 7.59%. 

   Here are details of the Treasury's two-year note auction yesterday: 

   Rates are determined by the difference between the purchase price and face value. 
Thus, higher bidding narrows the investor's return while lower bidding widens it. 
@ 2-Year Notes 
@ Applications $28,675,425,000 
@ Accepted bids $9,006,620,000 
@ Accepted at low price 49% 
@ Accepted noncompetitively $1,491,000,000 
@ Average price (Rate) 100.000 (7.75%) 
@ High price (Rate) 100.136 (7.73%) 
@ Low price (Rate) 100.000 (7.75%) 
@ Interest rate 7 3/4% 

   The notes are dated July 31 and mature July 31, 1991. 

   Corporate Issues 

   Junk bond prices were flat as traders shrugged off news that the nation's thrifts would be required to divest their junk bond holdings within five years. 

   "The market reacts only to surprises and this was no surprise," said Alan Schlesinger, head of junk bond trading at Donaldson Lufkin &amp; Jenrette Securities Corp. "Legislation curbing thrifts from investing in the junk market has been anticipated for some time, so there has been no reaction from the market," he said. 

   Still, some traders said that while the legislation had been anticipated, many investors and dealers had been expecting a bill that would allow limited investments in junk bonds. 

   House and Senate conferees decided yesterday to bar thrifts from directly investing in junk bonds and requiring them to divest their holdings no later than Aug. 1, 1994. 
The proposal allows junk bond investment in separately capitalized affiliates of thrift institutions. 

   Traders and analysts say the five-year time frame for thrifts to divest the $15 billion of junk bonds held by them will temper any negative impact. 

   "Five years is a couple of generations in the junk bond world," said one junk bond trader. 
"The market has grown tremendously in the past few years and this isn't going to be a problem." 

   Today, Goldman Sachs &amp; Co. plans to price a $200 million 10-year senior subordinated debenture offering by Hyster-Yale Materials Handling Inc. 
The issue is already oversubscribed, sources said. 

   In the investment-grade market, corporate issues closed unchanged. 

   In other corporate bond market news, Wall Street underwriting firms will see increased competition as three more commercial banks were given the green light to underwrite and trade corporate bonds. 

   The Federal Reserve Board gave final approval to units of Chase Manhattan Corp., Citicorp and Bankers Trust New York Corp. to underwrite and deal in corporate debt. 
They will join a J.P. Morgan &amp; Co. unit in the business. 

   Philip Morris Cos. tapped the new-issue market with a $300 million, five-year note offering priced at a yield spread of 0.95 percentage point above the five-year Treasury note, to yield 8.725%. 
Municipal Issues 

   Municipal bonds ended narrowly mixed in quiet trading. 

   The negotiated pricing sector was topped by a $192 million issue from the Jacksonville Transportation Authority, Fla., through underwriters led by PaineWebber Inc. 
The issue was oversubscribed, according to the lead underwriter. 

   Yields for the senior lien bonds range from 5.80% in 1990 to 7% in 2013. 
The junior lien bonds were priced to yield from 5.80% in 1990 to 6.50% in 1998. 

   In the secondary, or resale market, New Jersey Turnpike Authority's 7.20% bonds were quoted at 98 3/4, off 1/4, to yield 7.29%. 

   Mortgage-Backed Securities 

   Mortgage securities rose 3/32 point, despite a $500 million sale by New West Federal Savings &amp; Loan Association. 

   New West, formed with the troubled assets of California-based American Savings &amp; Loan Association, once the nation's largest thrift, sold $500 million of Ginnie Mae 9% securities through competitive bidding. 

   The sale is believed to the first from New West's mortgage securities portfolio, estimated at $13 billion. 
Officials at Ranieri Wilson &amp; Co., which is managing the New West mortgage-backed liquidation program, weren't available for comment. 

   Elsewhere, Ginnie Mae's 9% issue for August delivery ended at 98 21/32, up 3/32, while its 9 1/2% issue closed at 100 21/32, up 3/32. 
Freddie Mac's 9 1/2% securities closed at 99 21/32, also up 3/32. 

   Meanwhile, homeowners are increasingly opting for fixed-rate mortgages over adjustable-rate loans, which should mean increased supply of fixed-rate mortgage securities in the secondary market. 

   A Federal National Mortgage Association survey found that applications for fixed-rate loans rose 34% in June after a 17% increase in May, while applications for adjustable-rate loans fell 28% in June after a 22% drop in May. 

   The shift to fixed-rate loans is expected to continue because they now are widely available for under 10%, while fully indexed ARM rates are higher, except for first-year teaser rates. 

   Foreign Bonds 

   British government bonds surged as the pound rallied on news of an unexpectedly small U.K. trade deficit. 

   Some actively traded British bonds rose about 3/4 point. 
The 11 3/4% bonds of 2003/2007 rose 23/32 point to 115, to yield 9.72%. 
The 12% issue due 1995 rose half a point to 106 11/32, to yield 10.44%. 

   The U.K. current account deficit for June was #1.49 billion, lower than the #1.6 billion pound deficit many economists had expected, although up from May's deficit of #1.32 billion. 

   Japanese government bond prices ended flat after swinging widely earlier in the day. 
Japan's benchmark No. 111, 4.6% bonds of 1998 finished unchanged at 96.79 to yield 5.125%. 
Volume was brisk. 

   Japan's Finance Ministry set a 4.9% coupon rate for an issue of 600 billion yen of 10-year government bonds. 
Forty percent of the 10-year issue will be auctioned today in Japan, while the rest will be distributed under fixed syndicate allotments at the average auction price. 
Traders expect the issue to yield just over 5%. 

   West German government bonds rose slightly. 
Bonn's 7% issue due February 1999 climbed 0.11 point to 102.41, while its yield fell to 6.64% from 6.66%.

<HL> Who's News:
@  Seidman Picks Wetstein to Be Chairman
@  And Chief Executive Partner Next Year
@  ----
@  By Lee Berton
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> WNEWS </CO>
<IN> FINANCIAL, ACCOUNTING, LEASING (FIN) </IN>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Gary M. Wetstein, 51 years old, was appointed chairman and chief executive partner of BDO Seidman, the ninth biggest U.S. accounting firm, effective Oct. 1, 1990. 

   Mr. Wetstein will succeed John Abernathy, 52, who was appointed chairman in 1983 and whose second term expires next year. 

   To some accounting specialists, Mr. Wetstein is a man in the hot seat. 
The firm's year-to-year-revenue growth for the year ended June 30 slowed to 14% from 15% in fiscal 1988, and 30% in both fiscal 1987 and 1986. 
Some of the dozen or so mergers made over the past few years have been slow to integrate into Seidman. 
And problems in relatively new corporate finance and personal finances units have slowed growth. 
Also, consulting hasn't expanded as quickly as desired. 

   "I feel we now have our problems behind us," said Mr. Wetstein, who has been regional managing partner in Seidman's Midwest region based in Detroit for the past two years. 
"Now we're ready to charge ahead with a dedicated staff. 
And with the current megamergers of Big Eight accounting firms, we plan to benefit by hiring a lot of top-notch people who fall out of these combinations." 

   Mergers recently have been announced between Ernst &amp; Whinney and Arthur Young &amp; Co. and Deloitte, Haskins &amp; Sells and Touche Ross &amp; Co. 

   Accounting specialists say that because Seidman has been undergoing growth and profit-margin problems, it was important to set up lines of succession early for new leadership. 
They also say that second-tier accounting firms are under enormous competitive pressure from major accounting firms that wish to lure away the smaller firms' middle-sized clients. 

   "The second-tier firms must prosper or they will surely die," said George H. Sorter, an accounting and law professor at New York University's Stern School of Business. 
"If they become mindless, smaller versions of Big Eight firms, they'll never make it." 

   A spokesman for the firm said: "It's ridiculous to say we need early leadership since the firm's current chairman was named a year in advance. 
We do it because the partnership agreement specifies that a new chief executive partner be picked a year in advance in order to have a smooth transition period." 

   The firm said Messrs. 
Wetstein and Abernathy will work together during the transition period. 
The firm's partnership agreements also limit terms for chief executive to two, although Mr. Abernathy's predecessor to the job, B.Z. Lee, was permitted to serve three terms by a special vote of the partners. 

   Mr. Abernathy said "that there is an advantage to getting new blood to head the firm." 
Asked whether he would have liked a third term, Mr. Abernathy said: "It's a very interesting job but a third term wasn't available under the partnership agreement." 

   Mr. Wetstein said that the firm's corporate finance group recently named a new top executive and that it had recently aided in two mergers of manufacturing companies in the Midwest. 
"We're definitely back on track and getting new business," he said. 
The personal finance unit has closed one of five offices in Houston and has cut back personnel to 20 from more than 30 a year ago, he noted. 

   "We can't add all the new products we've been offering to clients, such as litigation suport and real estate appraisal, without having some fallout," said Mr. Wetstein. 

   Seidman, with such public clients as Interface Flooring Systems Inc., Leslie Fay Inc. and Earl Scheib Inc. and such closely held clients as Steelcase Inc. and Amway Corp., had annual revenue of $177 million in the year ended June 30, up from $155 million a year earlier. 
In the U.S., the firm has 315 partners for a total of 2,000 people on its professional staff in 43 offices. 
It is the U.S. member firm of BDO Binder, based in Brussels, which is the seventh biggest world-wide accounting organization with 1988 revenue of $793 million.

<HL> Business Brief -- Loral Corp.:
@  Earnings Declined by 12%
@  In Quarter; Payout Lifted </HL>

<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> LOR </CO>
<IN> EARNINGS (ERN) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Loral Corp. posted a 12% profit drop for the first quarter ended June 30 and said it is "cautiously optimistic" that the government will sustain a contract that a competitor protested. 

   The New York defense-electronics concern's board also voted to lift the quarterly dividend 11%, to 20 cents a share from 18 cents, payable Sept. 15 to stock of record Aug. 28. 

   Net income declined to $17.3 million, or 69 cents a share, from $19.6 million, or 78 cents a share, in the year-earlier period. 
Profit from continuing operations rose 36%, to $16.6 million from $12.2 million, because of lower interest cost and improved profit margins. 

   Sales fell 9% to $232.7 million from $256 million. 
But Bernard L. Schwartz, chairman and chief executive officer, told shareholders at the annual meeting here that he expects softer-than-expected bookings to be reversed. 

   Mr. Schwartz also said he expects per-share earnings from continuing operations for the year ending March 31 to rise 40%, to about $3.40 from $2.41 a year earlier.

<HL> World Markets:
@  Tokyo Stock Prices End Lower on Profit-Taking;
@  London Shares Close Down but Above Day's Low
@  ----
@  A Wall Street Journal News Roundup </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> EUROP JAPAN FREST CANDA </CO>
<IN> PRECIOUS METALS, STONES, GOLD, SILVER (PCS)
COMMODITY NEWS, FARM PRODUCTS (CMD)
STOCK MARKET, OFFERINGS (STK) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Tokyo stocks retreated from a morning surge to close lower on profit-taking. 

   London share prices closed easier but above their lows for the day as a few of the leading stocks managed to buck the market's overall negative tone. 

   In Tokyo, brokers said investors pulled back in the afternoon in the belief that the market is due for a correction. 
Upward pressure on prices pushed the Nikkei index to a record Tuesday. 

   "The market has been going up on a lot of hot air so it's deflating," said Pelham Smithers, an analyst at Yamaichi Securities Co. 

   The market has been flush with cash from investment trust funds, which played a role in this week's push on the Nikkei, brokers said. 

   Yesterday, the Nikkei average of 225 issues fell 23.07 points to 34515.83 after reaching a high of 34771.21 points in early trading. 
The index gained 445.57 points Tuesday. 

   Volume on the first section was estimated at one billion shares, in line with 1.03 billion shares Tuesday. 
The market reached one billion shares, a benchmark for a healthy trading day, Tuesday for the first time since June 1. 

   Advancing issues outnumbered decliners 569 to 330, and 187 issues were unchanged. 

   The Tokyo Stock Price Index of all issues listed in the first section, which rose 37.46 points to a record Tuesday, was up 2.06 points at 2586.13. 

   In Tokyo Thursday, the Nikkei index rose 126.27 points to 34642.10. 

   Yesterday was the last trading day for July settlement, so some brokers were anticipating quiet dealings. 
Traders usually hesitate to take new cash positions on the last day of trade for fear of hurting the month's accounts. 

   Brokers anticipate heavy trading this morning, which is technically the first day of trade for the August settlement. 
The market opened higher, with the Nikkei remaining above record levels through much of the session. 
Large institutional investors and individuals took part in the rally, and buying was focused on domestic-demand stocks, brokers said. 

   A broad section of the market traded higher in the rally, with conspicuous gains in construction and housing-related issues. 
But the rally lost steam in the afternoon, with brokers engaging in profit-taking. 

   Steel stocks fell. 
One analyst said Nippon Steel looked as though it could surge beyond 890 yen but hit resistance and was sold off for profits in the afternoon. 
Nippon Steel closed down 2 yen (1 cent) at 865 yen ($6.10) after reaching a high of 880 yen. 
Kawasaki Steel fell 10 yen to 930 yen, off from a high of 955. 
Sumitomo Metal dropped 23 yen to 826, off from a high of 853. 

   Construction stocks were also sold off in the afternoon. 
Taisei ended flat at 1,690 yen, off from a high of 1,710, and Ohbayashi was flat at 1,900 yen, off from a high of 1,930. 

   Banks, which were a large part of the market rally in financial issues early in the week, were easier. 
Sumitomo Bank was down 40 yen at 3,680, Mitsubishi Bank fell 30 yen to 3,360, and Fuji Bank was down 20 yen at 3,610. 

   Bid speculation in the stores sector and the start of trading in the shares of newly merged SmithKline Beecham were rare highlights of a lackluster session. 

   News early in the day of a better than expected British trade deficit in June failed to inspire the market, though analysts said the figures again reduced the likelihood of a further rise in U.K. base lending rates. 

   Trading conditions remained quiet throughout the day, with many participants absent or unwilling to deal because of another one-day transport strike in London. 

   News that representatives of the U.S. House and Senate have agreed to require savings and loans institutions to sell off junk bond holdings over a five-year period came too late to affect the London market, which closed an hour early because of the transport strike. 

   The Financial Times-Stock Exchange 100-share index closed 4.9 points lower at 2264.5, well above the day's low of 2258.3. 

   The Financial Times 30-share index closed off 0.2 points at 1885.8. 
Volume was a modest 315.3 million shares, compared with 461.7 million shares in Tuesday's full trading session. 

   Britain's June trade deficit rose to #1.89 billion ($3.09 billion) from #1.72 billion ($2.82 billion) in May but was below general market expectations of a trade gap of around #2 billion. 
The current account gap widened to #1.49 billion from #1.32 billion but was also below expectations of a #1.6 billion deficit. 

   The major stocks tended to drift in negative territory through the day. 
Among the leading shares, Glaxo Holdings ended 10 pence (16 cents) lower at #13.93 ($22.82), and British Aerospace closed 4 pence lower at 703 pence. 

   The stores sector saw some gains in active trading, boosted by vague rumors of a bid for Dixons Group by an unnamed West German consortium. 
But the rumors failed to provoke much of a rise in Dixons, which closed 2 pence higher at 168 pence. 

   Among the other stores, Kingfisher advanced 4 pence to 342 on trading volume of 3.9 million shares, and Marks &amp; Spencer was down 0.5 pence at 201.5 on volume of 2.6 million shares. 

   In Frankfurt, share prices climbed further, boosted by bullish market sentiment and waning expectations of higher West German interest rates. 
Traders said stocks rallied in the morning after a strong close Tuesday and indications of easing interest rates. 

   Elsewhere, share prices closed higher in Zurich, Brussels, Stockholm and Milan and mixed in Paris and Amsterdam. 
South African gold stocks closed higher. 

   In other Asian and Pacific trading, share prices closed lower in Hong Kong, Singapore and Seoul and higher in Sydney, Manila, Taipei and Wellington.

To make them directly comparable, each index is based on the close of 1969 equaling 100. 
The percentage change is since year-end.
