<HL> Commodities:
@  Precious-Metals Futures Prices Edge Higher
@  In Reaction to Weaker Dollar, Firmer Market Tone
@  ----
@  By John Valentine
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> PRECIOUS METALS, STONES, GOLD, SILVER (PCS)
COMMODITY NEWS, FARM PRODUCTS (CMD)
MINING, METALS (NON-PRECIOUS) (MIN) </IN>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Precious-metals futures prices edged higher, mostly in reaction to a weaker dollar. 

   Gold for August delivery ended with a gain of 90 cents an ounce at $375, while September-delivery silver was up 1.5 cents an ounce at $5.275. 
October platinum advanced $4.60 an ounce to $501.20. 

   Some buying of contracts by traders who had sold short in the belief that prices would fall contributed to the slightly firmer tone, according to Jeffrey Nichols, president of American Precious Metals Advisors Inc., New York. 

   There was some demand for gold from Zurich, Mr. Nichols said, but rather than being a factor in futures prices, the Swiss demand was actually caused by the steadier tone in the futures market, he said. 

   Dealers said there was slightly more activity in the bullion market, according to Mr. Nichols, but he said their comments seemed to be mostly attempts to find a reason for the market's gains. 

   "Actually, all we have is a market that moves a few points higher one day and a few points lower another day," he said. 

   Mr. Nichols said the August gold contract was trading in a range of about $369 to $380. 
"If it rises or falls outside that range, then there will be good reasons why the action has taken place, but not until then," he said. 

   But despite yesterday's rise in futures prices, according to Craig Sloane, an analyst for Smith Barney, Harris Upham &amp; Co., New York, precious metals "haven't moved up as much as the dollar has gone down over the past two days." 

   But he said foreign currencies are displaying a persistent strength against the dollar that may signal a new trend. 

   "If there is a definite breakout by foreign currencies, and they become much stronger than the dollar -- something that can happen by the end of the week -- precious metals could well take their cue and react accordingly," Mr. Sloane said. 
"When precious-metals traders become convinced that a down trend in the dollar has been established, they may react quickly to catch up" by buying metals. 

   Another analyst said that although the weaker dollar didn't bring much buying into precious metals, "it did tend to stifle short selling, so in that sense it was a positive factor." 

   John Norris, director of precious-metals trading at Citibank, New York, said August gold went above $375 an ounce but couldn't stay up there, indicating the market could only hold onto minor advances. 

   "What the market needs is a really sharp fall in the dollar, accompanied by a drop in interest rates," he said. 

   Traders were awaiting today's release of U.S. gross national product data, he said. 
If the figure is low, it could give traders confidence that there will be some easing of interest rates by the Federal Reserve Board, he said. 

   In other commodity markets yesterday: 

   SUGAR: Futures prices extended Tuesday's advances. 
The October contact ended with a gain of 0.13 cent a pound at 14.22 cents. 
It rose to as high as 14.67 cents in early trading after the Soviet Union confirmed it bought a large quantity of sugar, but profit-taking later trimmed the advance. 
The head of Prodintorg, the Soviet buying agency, said Moscow bought 250,000 tons of refined sugar, in addition to the 150,000 tons of raw sugar it bought previously, to meet rising domestic demand. 
In addition, an analyst reported that the Indonesian Sugar Council said Indonesia's sugar imports this year will amount to 621,000 tons, far above the 180,000 tons it imported last year. 
The analyst also said traders were still awaiting results of Syria's tender on Tuesday to buy 24,000 tons of refined sugar for September shipment. 

   COPPER: Futures prices extended Tuesday's easing trend. 
The September contract fell one cent a pound to $1.1155. 
Fred Demler, metals economist for Drexel Burnham Lambert Inc., New York, said the market has been seeing profit-taking ever since prices rallied early this month. 
Mr. Demler also expressed concern over the relatively low level of copper supplies. 
Inventories in U.S. producer hands, as of the end of June, amounted to 24,000 tons, compared with a high point of 70,000 tons in March 1988. 
Globally, supplies held by producers, consumers, merchants and exchanges total only 590,000 tons, or about 3.6 weeks' consumption, Mr. Demler said. 
This is a gain of 20,000 tons since the end of 1988, which contrasts with the usual pattern of bigger increases at this time of year. 
"Since 1973, there has been a gain on the average during July and August, the slow production months of the year, of 60,000 tons," he said. 
If there isn't a pickup in accumulation of supplies this month and next, there could be tightness in the market this fall when demand for copper picks up, he said. 

   GRAINS AND SOYBEANS: Prices closed mixed in narrow trading as the markets "consolidated somewhat from their losses of the previous two days," said Joel Karlin, a futures analyst in Chicago with Research Department Inc. Buoyed by reports of continued export demand, wheat prices remained the firmest among grain futures, said Ted Mao, a futures analyst with Shearson Lehman Hutton Inc., New York. 
September wheat futures gained two cents to close at $3.8825 on the Chicago Board of Trade. 
Scattered showers across the Eastern corn and soybean belt, including a thunderstorm that lingered over the Board of Trade building and Chicago's Loop business district, kept futures prices for these crops from hanging onto gains posted early in the trading session.

<HL> Heard on the Street:
@  Niagara Mohawk Problems With Power Plant
@  Spur Expectations That It Will Omit Payout
@  ----
@  By Bill Paul
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> NMK GOVMT </CO>
<IN> HEARD ON THE STREET (HRD)
STOCK MARKET, OFFERINGS (STK)
NUCLEAR POWER, FUEL (NUK)
DIVIDENDS (DIV)
UTILITIES (UTI) </IN>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Today, Wall Street is expecting Niagara Mohawk Power to commit what, for any utility investor, is the ultimate sin: omitting the dividend. 

   "It'll be close to a miracle" if directors at today's meeting vote to maintain the regular quarterly payout of 30 cents a share, says Edward Tirello of Shearson Lehman Hutton. 

   The problem is rooted in the company's 20-year-old nuclear plant, Nine Mile Point 1. 
Persistent problems with management, training and equipment have kept the plant out of service since December 1987. 
And so Niagara Mohawk, based in Syracuse, N.Y., has had to buy replacement power from other utilities, a cost that keeps eating away at its earnings. 

   Normally a utility can recover such costs from customers. 
But New York state officials decided late last year that Niagara Mohawk has brought this problem on itself through poor management, and thus should suffer the financial consequences. 

   The fallout has been severe. 
First-quarter profit declined 37%, as the company shelled out roughly $400,000 a day to buy replacement power. 
"They're at the breaking point as far as the dividend is concerned," says Daniel Scotto of Donaldson, Lufkin &amp; Jenrette Securities. 

   Niagara Mohawk isn't saying much. 
Asked specifically if the dividend is in trouble, a company spokesman says: "Management hasn't made its recommendation" to directors yet. 

   But while analysts are convinced that Niagara Mohawk is going to bite the bullet on the dividend, they are sharply divided on whether investors should buy or sell the stock. 

   Danielle Seitz of Smith Barney, Harris Upham acknowledges that Niagara Mohawk isn't for the "faint-hearted." 
Yet she calls it an attractive buy at yesterday's closing price of 12 1/8. 
"Sure you may lose money on the dividend {if it's omitted}, but you stand to make a lot when the stock goes up," Ms. Seitz says. 
Nine Mile 1 should be back in operation within six to eight months, she says, and within about 12 months the shares should hit 14 to 15. 
"Niagara Mohawk is one of the most undervalued stocks right now," she says emphatically. 

   But John Kellenyi of Drexel Burnham Lambert says sell. 
He acknowledges that Nine Mile 1 probably will come back on line by year end or so, with dividends resumed shortly after that. 
But he doubts the utility will generate sufficient profit to make payouts at the current annual rate of $1.20 a share. 
Instead he looks for an annual dividend rate of no more than 80 cents to $1 a share in 1990. 
Only if the stock price drops to around 10 would he be a buyer. 

   Barry Abramson of Prudential-Bache Securities rates Niagara Mohawk a "weak hold." 
If you're an investor who looks primarily at asset values, he says, "you've got to like" Niagara Mohawk because it's selling below book value, compared with the electric-utility group's average of 35% above book value. 
But if you're more interested in income, he says, "you want to avoid" the stock. 

   Other recent asset plays in the electric-utility group have paid off handsomely for the more speculative investor. 
General Public Utilities, CMS Energy and Long Island Lighting are just a few of the power companies whose stock prices have bounced back nicely after being pummeled by nuclear-plant woes -- although in Lilco's case especially, there have been plenty of ups and downs along the way. 

   Whether Niagara Mohawk will join that group, some analysts say, depends on whether it can convince the federal Nuclear Regulatory Commission that it is finally coming to grips with its nuclear problems. 
In May, NRC's Region I Administrator William Russell blasted Niagara Mohawk's nuclear management in a written assessment. 
"Russell makes a Marine drill sergeant look like a pansy," observes Mr. Tirello of Shearson Lehman. 

   For investors, the problem is that the longer it takes Niagara Mohawk to convince the NRC that Nine Mile 1 is ready for a restart, the harder it will be for the company to turn around. 
The Niagara Mohawk spokesman says only that Nine Mile 1's restart won't come before the end of the third quarter at the earliest. 

   Analysts suspect that a deal may be in the works between the utility and New York state officials, under which the company could start recovering its replacement-power costs from customers in exchange for either a moratorium on dividend payouts, a change in top management, or both. 
Niagara Mohawk acknowledges that "discussions" are under way with the New York State Public Service Commission, the state attorney general and others. 
But it won't divulge the subject of the talks. 

   ---

   Niagara Mohawk 

   (NYSE; Symbol: NMK) 

   Business: Electric and gas utility Year ended Dec. 31, 1988: 

   Revenue: $2.80 billion 

   Net income: $208.8 million; or $1.21 a share 

   First quarter, March 31, 1989: 

   Per-share earnings: 44 cents vs. 77 cents 

   Average daily trading volume: 

   333,308 shares 

   Common shares outstanding: 135.6 million

<HL> Earnings Rise
@  At 2 GM Units,
@  Fall at Another
@  ----
@  By Joseph B. White
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> GM GMH GME </CO>
<IN> EARNINGS (ERN) </IN>
<DATELINE> DETROIT  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   General Motors Corp.'s two big technology subsidiaries reported improved second-quarter earnings, but the auto maker's finance unit said its net income fell 19.6%, partly because of higher borrowing costs. 

   GM's data processing subsidiary, Electronic Data Systems Corp., said profit rose 9.6%, while GM Hughes Electronics Corp., the unit that includes GM's aerospace and vehicle electronics businesses, improved its earnings 42% from a year earlier. 
The two units have independently traded classes of stock representing dividend interests in their earnings. 

   General Motors Acceptance Corp. earnings are combined with profits from the No. 1 auto maker's other operations. 
GM is expected to report results for its common stock today. 

   GMAC said net income in the latest quarter dropped to $268.6 million from $333.9 million a year ago. 
Largely because of cut-rate vehicle financing and leasing programs undertaken to stimulate GM's car and truck sales, GMAC's financing and leasing business in the U.S. rose 27%. 
But the unit said earnings were hurt because average world-wide borrowing costs were 20% higher in the latest quarter than a year earlier. 

   In the first six months, GMAC's earnings fell 9.2% to $625 million from $688.3 million a year earlier. 

   At EDS, profit rose to $104.9 million, or 87 cents a share, from $95.7 million, or 78 cents a share, a year earlier. 
A 29% jump in business from non-GM accounts helped boost revenue in the quarter 15% to $1.37 billion from $1.19 billion a year ago. 
Non-GM revenue accounted for 44% of EDS's total business, compared with 39% a year earlier. 
EDS officials have said they want non-GM business to equal revenue from the parent by the end of next year. 

   In the first half, EDS earned $204.9 million, or $1.69 a share, up 11% from $184.8 million, or $1.51 a share, a year earlier. 
Revenue rose 15% to $2.66 billion from $2.32 billion. 

   GM Class E shares, which represent a dividend interest in EDS earnings, closed at $52.50, up 12.5 cents, in composite trading on the New York Stock Exchange. 

   At GM Hughes Electronics, the unit that includes Hughes Aircraft Co. and Delco Electronics Corp., profit was $203.1 million, or 60 cents a share, up 42% from year-earlier earnings of $142.9 million, or 45 cents a share, before a one-time gain of $21 million, or five cents a share. 
The gain was from sale of holdings in Nippon Avionics Co. Revenue rose to $2.88 billion from $2.87 billion. 

   In the first half, GM Hughes had net income of $376 million, or $1.11 a share. 
In the year-earlier period, profit before one-time gains was $291.6 million, or 90 cents a share. 
A $68.3 million gain from sale of Nippon Avionics stock and an $18.7 million gain from an accounting change made net income for the year-earlier first half $378.6 million, or $1.13 a share. 
The unit's revenue for the first six months of 1989 was $5.89 billion, up 6% from $5.54 billion in the year-earlier period. 

   GM Class H shares closed at $30, up 25 cents, in Big Board trading.

<HL> International:
@  Two German Banks
@  Make Acquisitions
@  Of Foreign Firms
@  ----
@  By Terence Roth
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> G.BHW G.DBK EUROP U.FRC </CO>
<IN> TENDER OFFERS, MERGERS, ACQUISITIONS (TNM)
BANKS (BNK) </IN>
<DATELINE> FRANKFURT, West Germany  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Two West German banks announced foreign acquisitions, reflecting the German banking industry's drive to broaden its presence and product offerings in Europe in anticipation of an integrated European Community financial market after 1992. 

   Bayerische Hypotheken-&amp; Wechsel Bank AG, West Germany's fifth-largest private-sector bank, said it acquired a 50% stake in Foreign &amp; Colonial Management Ltd., a London investment trust manager, for #17.5 million ($28.8 million). 
And Deutsche Bank AG, West Germany's largest bank, said it purchased Antoni, Hacker &amp; Co. of Vienna, a small, privately held bank, for an undisclosed sum. 

   Foreign &amp; Colonial and Hypo-Bank, as its West German purchaser is known, said in a joint statement that they expect investments in managed funds by private and institutional investors to grow substantially in Europe over the next few years. 
The arrangement significantly expands the investment range of the Munich-based Hypo-Bank, which is better known for its extensive mortgage business. 
Foreign &amp; Colonial had invested funds of #2.8 billion ($4.61 billion) as of June 30th. 

   For Foreign &amp; Colonial, the agreement brings financial clout and a broader distribution range for its investment products, said James Ogilvy, the firm's chairman and chief executive. 
Mr. Ogilvy revealed plans to jointly expand the trust's retail activities into France, Italy and Spain. 

   "Every U.K. investment manager today has two choices: either to find one's niche and become a boutique, or to expand internationally," Mr. Ogilvy said. 
"If you decide on the latter, as we did, you have to stake your claim early and find the right partner." 

   Deutsche Bank has been staking out new territory since 1986 with a string of bank acquisitions in Italy, Spain and the Netherlands. 
Antoni, Hacker will be used as the nucleus for a major expansion of Deutsche Bank's Austrian business, which will include asset management, trading in securities and foreign exchange, securities underwriting and corporate finance. 

   Deutsche Bank said business opportunities with Austrian industry were underscored by Vienna's recent application for admission to the EC.

<HL> Three More Banks
@  Cleared to Deal
@  In Corporate Debt
@  ----
@  By Robert Guenther
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> CCI CMB BT </CO>
<IN> BOND MARKET NEWS (BON)
SECURITIES INDUSTRY (SCR)
BANKS (BNK) </IN>
<GV> FEDERAL RESERVE BOARD (FED) </GV>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Federal Reserve Board gave three more commercial banking companies -- Citicorp, Chase Manhattan Corp. and Bankers Trust New York Corp. -- the green light to underwrite and deal to a limited extent in rated and unrated corporate debt securities in the U.S. 

   Within minutes of the approval, Chase Securities Inc., the new securities affiliate of the big bank, made its first trade of corporate debt. 
Similarly, a Bankers Trust spokesman said its BT Securities unit was prepared to exercise its powers immediately. 

   Regarding Chase's quick use of the new powers, Richard J. Canty, senior vice president for corporate securities at Chase, said, "When you have had a corporate trading desk set up for five months and when there are no impediments, legal or otherwise, you let people do their job." 

   The securities industry, which has fought banks' entry into their domain, had expected the latest round of approvals. 
On June 19, the Fed made J.P. Morgan &amp; Co. the first commercial bank to gain clearance for underwriting and dealing in corporate debt securities since the Glass-Steagall Act divorced banking from securities in 1933. 

   The Securities Industry Association has a lawsuit pending in the federal appeals court for the District of Columbia challenging the Fed's approval as a violation of federal laws separating banking and securities. 
The trade group's request for expedited handling of the case has been denied. 

   The banks' entry into the corporate securities market comes as Wall Street firms are retrenching in the face of sagging trading volumes and slimmer margins. 
Banks have wanted to enter the securities business because many of their better corporate customers forsake traditional bank loans in favor of raising debt through the securities markets. 
One executive at a leading Wall Street firm, informed of the Fed's latest approvals, said, "The banks can have all the low-margin corporate debt offerings that they want." 

   But in a reference to the huge losses suffered following the deregulation of the London securities market earlier this decade, Mr. Canty said, "We don't want to aid and abet Big Bang II. 
A lot has changed during the last two years of what success means in the securities business. 
It isn't being at the top of the league tables anymore. 
We're only in the beginning of a major restructuring of the securities business, and we're going to be focusing on areas where we can add value to transactions for our clients." 

   Under the Fed approval, banks' securities affiliates are limited to having no more than 5% of their revenue come from corporate debt securities and other securities that had been off limits. 
The balance of the securities affiliates' revenue must come from such traditional bank activities as underwriting and dealing in federal and municipal debt. 

   The Fed currently has similar applications pending from a number of foreign banks, including Barclays PLC and Royal Bank of Canada. 

</TEXT>

<DOCNO> WSJ890727-0009 </DOCNO>
<DD> = 890727 </DD>
<AN> 890727-0009. </AN>
<HL> Stocks Soar on Program Trades
@  ---
@  Wednesday's Markets:
@  DJIA Up 29.97
@  In Late Surge
@  ----
@  By Douglas R. Sease
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> STOCK MARKET, OFFERINGS (STK)
STOCK INDEXES (NDX)
MONETARY NEWS, FOREIGN EXCHANGE, TRADE (MON)
FOREIGN-EXCHANGE MARKETS (FRX)
FINANCIAL, ACCOUNTING, LEASING (FIN)
BOND MARKET NEWS (BON) </IN>
<GV> TREASURY DEPARTMENT (TRE) </GV>
<TEXT>
   A sudden burst of program trading that may have been triggered by a news report about Chrysler Corp. sent stock prices soaring. 
Bond prices languished, and the dollar fell. 

   The Dow Jones Industrial Average spurted 29.97 points to close at 2613.05, the sixth time the average has set a post-crash high this month. 
Broader market measures advanced sharply too, with the Standard &amp; Poor's 500-stock index ending at 338.05, its highest close ever. 
Long-term bond prices were about unchanged, and the dollar slumped against the mark and the yen. 

   Several analysts said the most plausible explanation for the explosive rise in stocks in the final half-hour of trading was a report on Dow Jones News Service that Chrysler was planning a restructuring. 
The report was soon denied by the company, but the market had already closed by then. 

   The report apparently triggered a surge in stock index futures, which then touched off program trading as arbitragers sold the futures and bought the underlying stocks. 
Hugh Johnson, chief investment officer at First Albany Corp., said such a transaction could instantly lock in a gain of 15%. 

   Mr. Johnson, however, said he was skeptical of that explanation for the surge, saying the stock index futures hit their peak at 3:37 p.m. but the Chrysler report didn't appear on the news wire until 3:43 p.m. 
He acknowledged, however, that word of the report could have reached some traders a few minutes early. 

   Some analysts suggested that the gains were touched off when the federal funds rate -- the interest rate banks charge one another on overnight loans -- fell to just above 9% from its recent average of just above 9 1/4%. 
When the Federal Reserve for the second day in a row took no action to stop the decline in the rate, its inaction was widely interpreted as a sign that it had eased its credit policy and that interest rates will be declining further. 

   Meanwhile, technology stocks continued to slump, but entertainment issues staged a daylong rally. 
Analysts said many investors were buying in anticipation of the $7 billion they will be receiving for the 100 million shares of Warner Communications Inc. that Time Inc. bought Monday. 

   Bond prices languished as traders awaited today's second-quarter gross national product report. 
Economists' average projection is that second-quarter GNP rose at a seasonally adjusted annual rate of about 2%, compared with 4.4% in the first quarter. 

   In major market activity: 

   Stock prices spurted higher in active trading. 
Volume on the New York Stock Exchange totaled 188.3 million shares. 
Advancing issues on the Big Board were ahead of decliners 981-483. 

   Bond prices were about unchanged. 
The yield on the Treasury's benchmark 30-year bond stood at 8.10%. 

   The dollar slipped against other major currencies. 
In late New York trading, it was quoted at 1.8675 marks and 140.02 yen, compared with 1.8830 marks and 141.45 yen. 

   (See related story: "Tech Companies Take a Beating" -- WSJ July 27, 1989) 

</TEXT>

<HL> Capital Dogfight:
@  Grumman F-14's Fate
@  -- And Firm's Course --
@  Face Day of Decision
@  ---
@  Scuttling Carrier-Based Plane,
@  As Pentagon Is Seeking,
@  Would Cost 3,000 Jobs
@  ---
@  Will Top Gun Be Shot Down?
@  ----
@  By Andy Pasztor and Charles W. Stevens
@  Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> GQ </CO>
<IN> AEROSPACE (ARO) </IN>
<GV> DEFENSE DEPARTMENT (DEF)
CONGRESS (CNG) </GV>
<DATELINE> BETHPAGE, N.Y.  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   When a Grumman-built F-14 Tomcat shot down two Libyan fighters over the Mediterranean early this year, workers at Grumman Corp. headquarters celebrated by wearing special T-shirts emblazoned "U.S.: 2, Libya: 0." 

   Such macho pride has defined the corporate culture of Grumman throughout its 60 years as the premier supplier of naval aircraft. 
Sporting menacing names such as Hellcat and Avenger, sturdy Grumman planes helped turn the tide in the Pacific during World War II, with a young pilot named George Bush playing his part. 
Later -- in Korea, Vietnam, and most recently the Middle East -- Grumman jets roared off carrier decks to battle the hottest Soviet-built warplanes. 

   But now Grumman's planes may have finally met their match, done in by Pentagon budget cutters and deficit-conscious lawmakers. 
The House today is scheduled to take up Defense Secretary Dick Cheney's proposal to kill production of the F-14 as well as Grumman's venerable A-6 bombers, and the vote is expected to be close. 
The Senate Armed Services Committee already has voted to end production, and the full Senate is likely to go along. 

   The Pentagon chief argues that the planes, designed in the 1950s and '60s, are too expensive and that the military can't afford to keep the plants operating at inefficient low rates. 

   The cuts would eliminate thousands of jobs, lop off about a third of the company's $3.65 billion in annual sales, and forever change its character. 
Grumman would survive. 
In fact, current funding provides for production of F-14 and A-6 planes through 1991. 
But in one stroke, Grumman would be slated for elimination as a major aircraft manufacturer. 
Its only remaining aircraft would be non-combat craft such as cargo and radar planes that it builds for the Navy, although it is the lead contractor on developing the high-tech Joint STARS flying command post for the Air Force. 

   Analysts generally expect Grumman to earn about $73 million this year, off from last year's $86.5 million, which included a one-time gain. 
But profit margins are expected to improve in the company's electronics and truck businesses. 
Sales this year are expected to match or exceed last year's $3.65 billion, with 84% representing government business. 

   Loss of the F-14 contract would be unlikely to have much effect on Grumman's stock price, analysts say. 
Gary J. Reich, an aerospace-industry analyst at Shearson Lehman Hutton in New York, notes that Grumman's stock has been stuck between $19 and $24 since the October 1987 crash. 

   Some analysts see Grumman as a takeover target as a result of its troubles. 
Chairman John O'Brien, however, says the company will stay independent, with 40% of the stock in the hands of employees. 

   The test of political wills over Grumman's fate has national as well as corporate implications. 
Grumman has "a proven track record of building carrier-based aircraft," says Robert Costello, who resigned as the Pentagon's top acquisition official several months ago. 
"That's a national asset. 
You can't just throw it away." 
Grumman's elimination would leave McDonnell Douglas Corp. as the only producer of carrier-based fighters. 

   But Grumman might not be the only defense contractor to see its role reduced or eliminated. 
In an era of shrinking military budgets and stepped-up arms-control negotiations with the Russians, defense planners predict an extended period of retrenchment and world-wide consolidation. 

   "The number of U.S. aircraft manufacturers clearly will have to shrink," says consultant Donald Hicks, a former defense undersecretary for research. 
"Firms that didn't look or plan far enough ahead will be changed forever, or perhaps disappear." 

   With the debate on Capitol Hill intensifying in recent days, Grumman's champions are fighting back ferociously. 
Hoping for a $1 billion package to save the F-14, home-state lawmakers are cutting deals to win votes by agreeing to back the pet programs of other congressmen. 

   Grumman's supporters have joined forces with powerful congressional delegations from Texas and Pennsylvania battling to save another weapon scheduled for Mr. Cheney's budget ax: the Marine Corps' V-22, a troop transport that takes off like a helicopter and cruises like a plane. 

   Company lobbyists are bad-mouthing competitors, leaning hard on subcontractors for political support and recruiting retired Navy brass to press their case. 
Nationwide television and newspaper ads trumpet the F-14s prowess, while Grumman's public-relations office has flooded newspapers and television stations with prepared articles and press releases intended to be used as news stories. 
But at best, Grumman can only delay the inevitable. 
The same budget pressures are bound to haunt the company's combat planes year after year. 

   "There's not enough money to go around for everybody's wish list," Vice President Dan Quayle told reporters Monday during a break in his efforts to lobby lawmakers to support the administration's defense cuts. 
Secretary Cheney, he said, has "made a very tough call" in targeting the F-14 and other programs. 
"Now we've got to deliver on it." 

   Although the company is battling to save the F-14 for a few more years, management already has grudgingly concluded that Grumman's long-term growth lies in other areas. 
Once the Navy moves on to the next generation of fighters, Grumman Chairman O'Brien concedes, "I see us having a difficult time staying in the carrier fighter business." 

   The economic impact would be huge -- and the human cost, too. 
Grumman estimates that killing the F-14 would cost 3,000 company jobs and perhaps force 20,000 or so layoffs by subcontractors, with the Long Island economy suffering the most. 
That would come on top of 5,000 jobs the company has eliminated over the past two years. 

   Generations of Long Island families have flocked to work in its assembly plants since Leroy Grumman, a former Navy officer and hotshot test pilot, founded the company in a garage in 1929. 
Grumman can outlive the Cheney cuts because much of its revenue already comes from other sources, including specialty military aircraft, work on satellites and the proposed space station, the assembling of mail trucks and even the manufacture of pontoon boats. 

   In fact, Grumman's booming military electronics business -- the most promising area for sustained growth -- is likely to exceed all of the company's current aircraft operations in the next several years. 

   But however lucrative the new work may be, it lacks the heart-pounding appeal of building supersonic jet fighters like the F-14 Tomcat, glorified by Hollywood in "Top Gun" and other movies. 
"It's hard to get the same product identification if you're making software packages for dataprocessing systems," Kurt Schroeder, the company's chief test pilot, observes. 

   Turning away from combat planes clearly would be traumatic for many of Grumman's roughly 30,000 employees. 
Dan Culleton, a Grumman manager who boasts that his father, sister, brother and an uncle all worked for Grumman, now brings his five-year-old son inside giant Building One to see how F-14 cockpits are built. 
Mr. Culleton worries that "the odds are only 50-50, or even a little less," that his boy will have the chance to continue the family tradition. 
"It's hard for me to conceive of Grumman not building airplanes," he says. 

   The firm's problems really began in the mid-1980s, when it decided against seeking a share of the new generation of fighters envisioned by the Navy in order to concentrate its technical and financial resources on an overriding goal: winning the multibillion-dollar contract to build a replacement for the bulbous-nosed A-6 Intruder, the Navy's only long-range, all-weather bomber. 
But the company isn't among the firms picked for that contract, leaving it without a new line of warplanes when production of its battle-tested but aging models is phased out. 

   The next blow came last December, when Pentagon officials took aim at the A-6 years before Grumman had expected. 
Then, just as the company revved up efforts to protect the A-6, Mr. Cheney came into office and immediately launched a surprise attack on the F-14, the fastest of all U.S. Navy fighters. 

   Suddenly on the defensive, Grumman's lobbyists swung into high gear. 
Peter Oram, head of Grumman's aircraft operations, hurriedly arranged a trip to visit the air commanders of the Pacific and Atlantic fleets, playing off their fears and recruiting them in a campaign to lobby with top brass against the cutbacks. 
Since then, the Navy's leadership has formally appealed Mr. Cheney's proposed cutbacks a number of times -- without any success. 

   Grumman also sought help from prominent former Navy and Marine pilots who over the years have entrusted their lives to Grumman equipment. 
Among the most emotional supporters are Democratic Sen. John Glenn of Ohio, a decorated Marine Corps pilot who flew Grumman planes in Korea before his renowned space exploits, and Adm. Thomas Moorer, former chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff, who helped develop the F-14. 

   In weekly meetings organized by Grumman officials on Capitol Hill, New York's congressional delegation maps out strategy. 
Their efforts have been aided by Mr. O'Brien, Grumman's combative chairman, who has flown to Washington to meet with at least 15 additional lawmakers since the spring. 

   A former flight-test analyst who relishes belittling archrival McDonnell Douglas Corp.'s F-18 carrier-based fighters, Mr. O'Brien says bluntly: "I don't see F-18s shooting down any Libyans." 
Mr. Cheney's proposed budget nevertheless earmarks nearly $2 billion a year to continue hefty production of the shorter-range and slower -- but much less expensive -- McDonnell Douglas jets. 

   Grumman's supporters contend that relying on McDonnell Douglas isn't enough. 
Dismantling the F-14 assembly line will hobble the nation's carrier fleet with an acute shortage of front-line fighters in the 1990s, they insist. 
The Pentagon counters that it can quickly develop the F-14's replacement -- a super-sophisticated aircraft called the Advanced Tactical Fighter, or ATF -- and buy it in large enough numbers over the next several years to keep carrier decks filled. 

   But ATF design teams already have been slowed down by two previous budget cuts. 
And many critics, including Pentagon insiders, doubt that the ambitious schedule will be met. 

   Canceling F-14 production without having replacements ready is like jumping out of a tall building "because someone promised to bring a net before you hit the ground," says Sen. Alfonse D'Amato. 
The New York Republican is expected to lead the fight for Grumman during the appropriations process. 

   Traditional coalition politics may yet save the F-14. 
In addition to casting their lot with backers of the jeopardized V-22 troop transport, Grumman's supporters have rounded up support from Virginia lawmakers, who defend Navy planes because the carriers that haul them are built, and many are based, in that state. 
The coalition recently won an important test vote when the House Armed Services Committee retained money for the F-14 and V-22 programs alike -- at the expense of the B-2 Stealth bomber and other high-tech Pentagon projects. 
Grumman happily counts on unwavering support from one committee member, Democratic Rep. George Hochbrueckner, a former Grumman engineer whose Long Island district includes thousands of worried company employees. 
"The issue is larger than the fate of one company," he asserts. 
"We're fighting to prevent a potential disaster for all of naval aviation." 

   Such superheated rhetoric doesn't sit well with many other members of the committee, including its ranking Republican, Rep. William Dickinson of Alabama. 
He dismisses Grumman's plight as a "very parochial issue," noting that New York lawmakers rarely advocate higher defense spending. 
"We hear from them only when it affects their district," he says. 

   Meanwhile, out in the fleet, sailors have more concerns of their own. 
Once-proud Grumman planes increasingly are being relegated to secondary roles after they pass their prime. 
Older models are being converted to tankers used for in-air refuelings -- to keep newer combat aircraft buzzing above the carriers.

<HL> Law:
@  Student Indicted
@  On Charge Linked
@  To Computer Virus
@  ----
@  By John R. Wilke
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> COMPUTERS AND INFORMATION TECHNOLOGY (CPR)
SOFTWARE (SOF)
TELEPHONE SYSTEMS (TLS) </IN>
<GV> JUSTICE DEPARTMENT (JUS) </GV>
<TEXT>
   A federal grand jury in Syracuse, N.Y., indicted a Cornell University student on a felony computer-crime charge for the release of the software bug that crippled thousands of computers last year. 

   Robert T. Morris, 24 years old, of Arnold, Md., is the first person to be prosecuted under the 1986 Computer Fraud and Abuse Act, the Justice Department said. 
Mr. Morris, who is on leave from Cornell, faces as much as five years in prison and a $250,000 fine, and could be required to pay restitution for the incident. 

   The software virus choked thousands of computers at universities and military bases that use the Arpanet computer data network. 
In the indictment, Mr. Morris is charged with illegally accessing computers at a National Aeronautics and Space Administration computer center; the University of California at Berkeley; Wright Patterson Air Force Base in Dayton, Ohio; the Massachusetts Institute of Technology; and other computer sites that aren't specified. 

   Mr. Morris's attorney, Thomas Guidoboni, said his client would not comment on the indictment. 
But he said that "after many months of uncertainty, Mr. Morris accepts this event as a step toward the final resolution of this matter." 
He said Mr. Morris will plead not guilty and contest the charge. 
"He looks forward to his eventual vindication and his return to a normal life," Mr. Guidoboni added. 

   Mr. Morris told friends that he created the virus program but that he didn't intend for it to spread as quickly and destructively as it did. 
The virus raced across the network Nov. 2, replicating itself so rapidly that it forced computers to shut down. 

   Paul Graham, a graduate student in computer science at Harvard University and a friend of Mr. Morris's, said that Mr. Morris is being unfairly used as an example by federal prosecutors eager for their first prosecution under the computer-crime law. 
"He's an honest person with a genuine interest in computer security, and it was an accident the program spread to all those other computers." 

   But computer-industry executives see it differently. 
Maryanne Karinch, a spokesperson for the Computer and Business Equipment Manufacturers Association, said that "hacking with criminal intent is not a sophomoric prank but a serious violation of the law." 
She said violators "should be punished the same way someone who robs a convenience store should be punished. 
Computer users have a right to security and privacy, and that is what these statutes are designed to protect."

<HL> Technology:
@  Amdahl Net Fell 38%
@  During Second Period
@  Despite Revenue Rise </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> AMH </CO>
<IN> EARNINGS (ERN)
COMPUTERS AND INFORMATION TECHNOLOGY (CPR) </IN>
<DATELINE> SUNNYVALE, Calif.  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Amdahl Corp. said second-quarter net income fell 38% to $32.8 million, or 30 cents a share, despite increased revenue. 

   The results were slightly worse than the computer vendor had forecast late last month, when it surprised Wall Street by saying that shrinking profit margins would lead to a 25% to 35% earnings decline. 
At the time, Amdahl said it expected revenue to rise about 20%, but its reported revenue of $470.6 million is only an 11% increase. 
In the year-earlier quarter, Amdahl earned $52.8 million, or 48 cents a share, on revenue of $423.8 million. 
The year-earlier results have been restated to reflect a 2-for-1 stock split. 

   A spokesman called the results "not terribly out of line with our projections" and said the discrepancies between the forecast and the actual figures reflect "problems inherent in making those kinds of calculations." 
He added that the company "didn't do any worse than we figured we'd do." 

   Amdahl has been hurt by aggressive price-cutting by International Business Machines Corp. Amdahl sells computers and memory-storage devices that mimic those sold by IBM, so it is under pressure to match IBM's prices. 

   A spokesman said profit margins also suffered because sales of its 5990 mainframe computer are making up a larger portion of total sales; the 5990, introduced in May 1988, costs more to make than other Amdahl products. 
He said Amdahl also was hurt by the strength of the dollar against foreign currencies. 

   Amdahl said it expects results to pick up later this year as deliveries of new storage products increase. 

   For the six months, Amdahl earned $78.4 million, or 72 cents a share, down 18% from $95.4 million, or 88 cents a share. 
Revenue rose 19% to $941 million from $791.4 million. 

   In American Stock Exchange trading yesterday, Amdahl closed at $15, down 62.5 cents.

<HL> Child Care Gets Big Share
@  Of Poor Women's Income </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<DATELINE> WASHINGTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Poor women spend a large share of their monthly income on child care, according to the Commerce Department. 

   The Census Bureau reports that working women with monthly incomes less than $1,250 paid an average of $35 weekly for child care, or 18% of their monthly income. 
Women with monthly incomes more than $3,750 paid an average of $58 weekly, or 4% of their monthly income. 

   "There is quite a wide equitable disparity," said Martin O'Connell, chief of fertility statistics at the bureau. 
"Poor women don't have much money coming in, but more going out {for child care}." 

   Of the 5.8 million working women responding to the survey conducted in 1986, 353,000 were considered poor. 

   The survey estimates that in 1986, $14 billion was spent on child care for children 14 years old and younger. 

   Most working mothers utilize child-care services outside the home. 
Of the nine million preschool children in the survey, four out of 10 were cared for in someone else's home, and 21% attended formal child-care facilities. 

</TEXT>

<HL> International:
@  Lord Alexander
@  To Find Outsiders
@  For NatWest Posts
@  ----
@  By Joann S. Lublin
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> NW EUROP </CO>
<IN> BANKS (BNK) </IN>
<DATELINE> LONDON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Lord Alexander, the new chairman of National Westminster Bank PLC, vowed to hire more outsiders for top jobs and review its investment-banking role after the stock scandal that toppled his predecessor. 

   A sharply critical government investigation of County NatWest, the giant bank's investment-banking unit, triggered the exodus of Lord Boardman, NatWest's chairman, plus two deputy chief executives and a third senior director, although Lord Boardman wasn't cited in the government report. 
The investigation found evidence of misconduct and failure to comply with British company law in County NatWest's handling of a #837 million ($1.35 billion) rights issue for Blue Arrow PLC in 1987. 

   This week's management purge, unprecedented in recent U.K. banking history, has seriously shaken London's financial community and damaged both NatWest's blue-chip reputation and its investment-banking ambitions. 

   Lord Alexander, 52 years old, who takes the NatWest helm Oct. 1, called the Blue Arrow affair "the saddest episode in the history of the bank." 
He said "every activity will be under review" as a result. 
"I do not envisage entrenchment," he told a small group of reporters here yesterday. 
"But an organization that has had an episode such as this should properly look at all aspects of its activities." 

   Lord Alexander may find it difficult, however, to fill the gaps in top management and restore NatWest's tarnished image. 
Though he is Britain's best-known lawyer, he never has managed a business or been a banker. 
His inexperience doesn't bode well "for restoring confidence in NatWest rather quickly," one London banking analyst said. 

   Lord Alexander, currently chairman of Britain's Takeover Panel, a regulatory agency, said his banking inexperience "is one of the reasons I was selected" as NatWest's new chairman. 
Lord Boardman, 70 years old, previously had planned to retire from the #178,320-a-year chairmanship at the end of the year. 
While praising NatWest's existing senior management, Lord Alexander indicated he intends to bring in fresh blood -- including other non-bankers. 
He said he would seek "different kinds of talent that might give rise to a different sort of balance" against the bank's insiders. 
That could spark corporate culture shock. 
Like most major British banks, NatWest traditionally promotes from within, which breeds both fierce loyalty and insularity. 

   Lord Alexander declined to identify which top posts he might fill with outsiders, but said replacing the three departing senior directors won't wait "until I formally become chairman." 
Some analysts think the new chairman also should streamline the bank's top-heavy, 31-member board. 
Changing the board structure "must all be part of the consideration," Lord Alexander agreed. 

   In his planned wide-ranging review, he said he will make sure NatWest remains involved only in activities where it can "offer valuable service." 
But he insisted the bank won't abandon investment banking. 

   A more likely development, some analysts said, would be County NatWest's retreat from securities dealing, while remaining in the corporate-finance field. 
But it has fallen far behind competitors in corporate finance, largely since the investigation of the Blue Arrow affair began last December. 
During the first half of this year, County NatWest handled five deals with a total value of #265 million -- ranking 18th among financial advisers in British public takeovers, reports Acquisitions Monthly, a British publication. 
By contrast, it held fifth place for 1988, advising on 24 transactions with a total value of about #7.3 billion. 

   The Blue Arrow affair "undoubtedly has cast a shadow" over County NatWest, said Lord Alexander, who appeared frazzled after a week of crisis. 
But after the parent bank's management shuffle, County NatWest's relatively new leadership "can seek to restore its reputation and business," Lord Alexander said. 

   Analysts aren't so sure. 
The scandal "has damaged their long-term reputation," said Robert Grant, a banking analyst at Nomura Research Institute. 

</TEXT>

<AN> 890727-0002. </AN>
<HL> REVIEW &amp; OUTLOOK (Editorial):
@  Poverty Explained </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> ECONOMIC NEWS (ECO) </IN>
<GV> EXECUTIVE (EXE)
CONGRESS (CNG) </GV>
<TEXT>
   In Washington, the poverty debate now centers on one crucial issue: How to blame the other guy. 
The Democratically controlled Ways and Means Committee recently produced a study, smilingly called "nonpartisan," that implicitly blamed the Reagan administration for recent increases in the poverty rate. 
To do so, it had to count 1979 and 1980 as Reagan administration years. 
Then it could show that poverty indeed increased between 1979 and 1987. 
The New York Times ran a front-page opinion piece based on this report and headlined, "Forces in Society, and Reaganism, Helped Dig Deeper Hole for Poor." 

   The Republican Study Committee screamed bloody murder. 
It used the Ways and Means data to show that between 1982 and 1987, years when Reagan budgets were actually in place, real income for those in the poorest fifth of the nation actually increased 4.1%. 
It was in the Carter years, it says, that the poor got poorer. 

   The fuss is all good politics. 
But it has nothing to do with poverty. 
Poverty trends are complex and deep; they simply can't be pegged to inauguration schedules. 

   Here is what really happened. 
Through the postwar era, the number of people living in poverty declined until about 1973. 
Then, remarkably, the poverty rate leveled off and began to increase. 
Since then, it has been fluctuating around a higher plateau. 
The years between 1979 and 1983 were brutal on the poor. 
Since 1984, the situation has been somewhat better. 

   The crucial question really is what happened around 1973 to cause the long decline in poverty to reverse itself. 
That is what honest poverty experts generally debate, some arguing that Great Society programs caused the change, some that the cultural revolution of the 1960s was responsible, others saying there had been a more fundamental change in the U.S. economy. 
Meanwhile it has become nothing short of hilarious to watch the political operatives in newspapers, magazines and on Capitol Hill pretend that troubling poverty trends started with Ronald Reagan. 

   To play their political games, the partisans rely on statistics and categories that are quaintly archaic. 
Back in the '60s and '70s, it was believed that poverty was a problem of scarcity. 
If government could divert resources to the poor, then they would be pushed out of poverty. 
Economists dominated the poverty debate, talking about income quintiles, and amassing data from little green books. 

   With the recognition of the underclass around 1982, it became obvious that behavior, not merely income level, had a lot to do with who was poor. 
Sociologists began to dominate discussion, and a welfare consensus emerged around the need for developing good work habits, sexual self-restraint and individual accountability. 
Even the liberal Ford Foundation has acknowledged that family breakdown is a major cause of social problems. 

   Savvy experts stopped relying on income quintiles because they tell you nothing about how poor people behave. 
When the Ways and Means Committee comes out and focuses on poverty rates and income distribution, it is a bit like watching a 20-year-old rerun of Dick Clark's "American Bandstand." 
It's hard to believe people really talked that way. 

   Here's a hoary old chestnut that stands as the centerpiece of the Ways and Means report: "The rich are getting richer and the poor are getting poorer." 
This formulation has always appealed to envy, but not much to common sense. 
In the first place, it's never been clear what the first observation has to do with the second. 
It is incoherent to pretend that the poor are getting poorer because the rich are getting richer. 
The economy is not finite. 
In fact, members of the working poor usually get richer only when the entire economy, including the rich, does better. 
To introduce the rich into talk of how the poor are doing is merely a lame effort to transform the problem into a pseudo-Marxian class struggle. 

   The more fundamental problem with the saying is that it pretends there is such a thing as "the poor." 
The obsolete economic perspective on poverty picks an income level and then lumps everybody under it as "the poor." 
In reality, 41% of this group own their own homes; many, however, are drug addicts living in the street. 
Some 11% have completed college, but many have virtually no marketable skills. 
Almost 40% of poor families have both parents in the home; the other 60% are female headed. 
The differences are profound, and they have to do with situation, not income. 

   It's time to stop defining poor people by how much they make, and to start defining them by behavior -- by education, by job history, by family status. 
In the 1980s, we have arrived at a more realistic perception of poverty, but statistical measures haven't caught up. 
Until they do, we will be inundated by silly, partisan reports and nonsensical news articles. 

</TEXT>
</DOC>
<DOC>
<DOCNO> WSJ890727-0001 </DOCNO>
<DD> = 890727 </DD>
<AN> 890727-0001. </AN>
<HL> What's News --
@  World-Wide </HL>
<DD> 07/27/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<TEXT>
   THE HOUSE VOTED to suspend production of the radar-evading Stealth bomber. 

   By a 257-160 vote, the chamber moved to bar output of the plane until the White House agrees to a revision of the $70 billion program, including slashing its request for 132 of the bombers and demonstrating the aircraft's technical capabilities before production can resume. 
The House also voted to temporarily block Pentagon moves to put MX nuclear missiles on railroad cars, removing $502 million for rail gear and extra MX missiles. 

   The Stealth hurdles that would be imposed by the House are significantly tougher than B-2 spending restrictions expected to be adopted by the Senate. 

   ---

   ETHNIC UNREST INTENSIFIED in Soviet Georgia and other republics. 

   Two bombs exploded and thousands of people demonstrated in Georgia to press demands for the independence of the Black Sea region, where fighting has been reported between Abkhazians and Georgians. 
There weren't any injuries reported. 
In Latvia, about 100,000 people marched through Riga to demand inclusion in an economic autonomy plan for the Baltic republic. 
And strikes inspired by ethnic legislation were reported at factories in Estonia. 

   A nationwide strike by coal miners, which had threatened Gorbachev's economic restructuring, drew to a close as the ethnic conflicts increased. 

   ---

   Exxon's president agreed to abide by a Coast Guard determination next spring about whether further cleanup is needed in Alaska's waters because of the March 24 Exxon Valdez oil spill. 
The company, which has said cleanup operations would cease by Sept. 15, had called for only a survey of the beaches next year. 

   ---

   Solidarity lawmakers voted to back Walesa and reject Jaruzelski's call for the union-led opposition to join Poland's Communists in a governing coalition. 
The Solidarity caucus also approved a resolution saying it wouldn't be "useful" for its members to accept government posts even as individuals. 

   ---

   The PLO has offered to soften its objections to Arab elections in the Israeli-occupied lands, saying it would accept the balloting if Egypt and the U.S. sent observers and Israel agreed to swap land for peace, Israeli newspapers reported. 
The U.S. called the plan, disclosed in a memo, unacceptable. 

   ---

   Cambodia's warring factions have agreed to meet today to try to resolve differences over how they should be represented at Sunday's 20-nation conference in Paris on the Cambodian conflict, Prince Sihanouk said. 
Talks between Phnom Penh's government and the rebels had collapsed Tuesday. 

   ---

   The Senate approved a nearly $11 billion natural resources bill incorporating timber and oil provisions, and requiring the disclosure of those hired to pursue federal funds. 
The bill also seeks to penalize those involved in awarding arts money to support recent exhibits faulted as sacrilegious and sexually explicit. 

   ---

   Current and former IRS employees told a House subcommittee that they were harassed by superiors in the agency after reporting violations by fellow employees. 
The testimony came on the second day of hearings into ethical conduct and standards at the agency and follow a yearlong investigation by the panel. 

   ---

   A Cornell University student was indicted by a federal grand jury in Syracuse, N.Y., on a felony charge stemming from the release last year of the software virus that crippled thousands of computers. 
The graduate student, who is on leave, faces as long as five years in prison and a $250,000 fine. 

   ---

   A study found that affluent women are about 75% more likely to give birth by Caesarean section than are poor women. 
The study, conducted by researchers at the University of California at Berkeley, provides evidence that financial factors contribute to what some believe is excessive use of the procedure. 

   ---

   The FDA authorized hospital-supervised use of an experimental drug shown to prevent a breathing disorder that is the leading cause of death and disability among premature infants. 
The drug, called exosurf pediatric, is made by Burroughs Wellcome Co. 

   ---

   Police in eastern China have arrested more than 3,000 people, including a Beijing student leader, in a three-day search for counterrevolutionaries, an official newspaper reported. 
China also has sentenced four people to death for their roles in last month's pro-democracy protests. 
At least 12 people already have been executed. 

   ---

   Japan's ruling party set a mid-August deadline for swearing in a successor to Prime Minister Uno, who announced his intention to resign following the Liberal Democrats defeat in Sunday's parliamentary elections. 
Political sources said a successor could be named as early as next week, even though a frontrunner hasn't yet emerged. 

   ---

   Five ships delivered food to Lebanon's besieged Christian enclave, drawing only light fire from Syrian gunners and their Moslem allies. 
The ships' flags or owners weren't disclosed. 
Despite reports of an undeclared truce in the latest round of the country's 14-year-old civil war, sectarian skirmishes erupted across Beirut.

<HL> International:
@  Iranian Radicals Support Rafsanjani,
@  But Watch for Threat to Revolution
@  ----
@  By Geraldine Brooks
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> MDEST </CO>
<DATELINE> TEHRAN, Iran  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Hamideh Marafat spent 1979 in what she still refers to as "the nest" -- as in "nest of spies." 
She entered the U.S. Embassy at the request of fellow students who had stormed the premises a day earlier. 
She has never quite gotten over her first glimpse of the huge American kitchens used to prepare the diplomats' state banquets. 

   Ms. Marafat was one of the "Muslim Students Following the Imam's Line" and, 10 years later, she still is. 
Like many of the Islamic revolution's hard core, she is watching closely for signs of deviation from that line following the death of Ayatollah Ruhollah Khomeini. 
On Friday, she will vote for Hashemi Rafsanjani for president of the Islamic Republic. 
But she isn't without reservations. 

   "Many people are asking how, if the liberals are for Rafsanjani, we can be for him too," says the 29-year-old, who wears a severe black chador over a cowl-like hood, a long tunic and pants. 
Last week, she and her friends took that question to a weekly discussion group led by a young mullah who ardently supports Khomeini. 
"He said that this label of 'liberal' has often been used as a trick, by those who want to divide us," she says. 
"We recognize that Rafsanjani is something different, but we don't see him moving the revolution away from the path the imam has set." 

   For "hezbollahis", or religious revolutionaries like Ms. Marafat, there isn't any question of what will happen if he does. 
She would agree with Ali Akbar, a rug merchant in Tehran's bazaar, who answers the question by harking back to the early days of Islam, when someone asked if Omar -- the second calif to lead Islam after Mohammed -- would follow the prophet's path. 
A man brandished his weapon and said, "This sword will make sure he does." 

   The merchant, Ms. Marafat and many others like them see themselves as continuing the role of this early Islamic warrior, and don't doubt they have the necessary strength. 

   Just how many hezbollahis there are in Iran is uncertain. 
The millions who jammed the streets for the ayatollah's funeral have melted back into ordinary life: The crowd at the graveside for the traditional 40-day mourning ceremony was far smaller, probably numbering in the thousands. 
Gatherings at Friday prayers have returned to around the same size as before the ayatollah's death. 
But there also may be many, like Ms. Marafat, who no longer feel they have to express their zeal on the streets, yet would be prepared to do anything if they saw a threat to their revolution. 

   Ms. Marafat has never questioned the justice of what she did at the U.S. Embassy. 
During much of the 444-day crisis, her job was translating all the mail that went in and out of the building -- the hostages' messages to their families, the letters of Americans writing to the hostages to express their grief and support. 
Many of the letters were abusive notes to Maryam and Habib, the two spokesmen who took turns appearing on the U.S. television networks to read out the students' demands. 
But the letters she remembers best are the numerous notes written by American school children, designed to cheer up the captives. 

   "Sometimes I felt pity for them when I read those children's letters," she recalls. 
"But I knew they were spies who had tried to ruin this country." 

   Working now on the finishing touches to her master's degree in English, Ms. Marafat is a soft-spoken revolutionary who defied her mother to take part in anti-Shah demonstrations, and who had to convince both parents that her work at the embassy wouldn't interfere too much with her studies. 
She has kept track of about 10 of the students who led the embassy seizure. 
Four of them, she says, died at the front during the war with Iraq. 
One has become a provincial leader of the Revolutionary Guards, one has joined the Majlis, or parliament, and the rest, like herself, have quietly pursued academic careers. 

   Unmarried, she lives with her parents in a comfortable house with cool stone floors and Persian carpets. 
Indoors, away from the gaze of men, she discards severe Islamic dress for a smart silk blouse and tan skirt. 
Uncovered, her wavy brown hair bobs stylishly, just above her shoulders. 
Her ambition is to travel to Britain next year to begin work on a doctorate, which she hopes will lead to a teaching post at an Iranian university. 
Then, she says, "I will have to be involved in politics, because it is important for teachers to take positions." 

   For now, though, her daily routine is distinctly non-political. 
It takes her from the classroom, where she teaches English ("Since the revolution, everybody studies well, because there's nothing else to do," she says), to the language laboratory, to standing in line for the family groceries. 

   She is well aware that the Iranian economy is a mess, and she welcomes Mr. Rafsanjani's promises of reform. 
But she is yet to be convinced that restoring relations with the U.S. is justifiable, even in Iran's economic or strategic interest. 

   Her views on the U.S. show the height of the ideological hurdles Mr. Rafsanjani will have to vault if he chooses to move in this delicate area. 
Ms. Marafat is firmly convinced that the U.S. deliberately shot down the Iran Air passenger jet last year and that Iran's war with Iraq was masterminded by the U.S. to deflect aggression from Israel. 
Indeed, U.S. support for Israel is, to her mind, the Great Satan's greatest sin. 
When a Westerner wonders why the Iranian press has termed "heroic" the act of a Palestinian who drove an Israeli bus of a cliff, killing 14 people, Ms. Marafat is astonished by the question. 
"If they are Israelis, they are the enemy," she says. 
"When Israelis attack Palestinian camps, do they consider individuals? 
When Americans bombed Hiroshima, did they distinguish between combatants and civilians?" 

   Her dream, she says, is to visit the battlefront of a great Islamic campaign to crush the Zionists. 
"Israel, as a state, shouldn't exist," she declares.

<HL> West German
@  System Similar
@  To Nasdaq Set
@  ----
@  By Terence Roth
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> EUROP IBM </CO>
<IN> BANKS (BNK)
SECURITIES INDUSTRY (SCR)
STOCK MARKET, OFFERINGS (STK)
BOND MARKET NEWS (BON)
COMPUTERS AND INFORMATION TECHNOLOGY (CPR) </IN>
<DATELINE> FRANKFURT, West Germany  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   West German banks are planning the launch of a new computer-based system for off-floor securities transactions, a bid to catch up with more progressive financial centers elsewhere before the integration of European financial markets after 1992. 

   The banks, which supervise and effectively control West Germany's stock exchanges, have scheduled the Inter-bank Information System, or IBIS, to start operating Dec. 1. 
They hope it will expand and increase the transparency of securities trading outside the official stock exchange hours, 11:30 a.m. to 1:30 p.m., one of the shortest trading spans in the world. 

   In its first stage, the system will link subscribing banks and brokers to a central data bank supplying price updates on 30 blue-chip stocks and certain domestic bond issues, based on telephone transactions. 
IBIS rates will be updated by subscribers as trades are made, similar to the Stock Exchange Automated Quotations system in London and the National Association of Securities Dealers Automated Quotations system for trading stocks in the U.S. 

   In a later stage, the IBIS organizers envision a fully computerized market in which deals also are transacted over the system itself at the push of a button. 

   The German Securities Data Center, which is controlled by West German banks, has contracted IBM Deutschland G.m.b.H., a unit of International Business Machines Corp. of the U.S., to design and install the system. 

   Ulrich Gottschalk, a director of the center, said subscriptions to the system will be limited to banks and brokers based in West Germany, including West German subsidiaries of foreign entities. 
Eventually, the network could be extended to foreign trading centers, he said. 
IBIS is to operate every business day from 9 a.m. to 4 p.m., the operating hours set for the planned German Futures Exchange to begin operations later this year, reflecting another West German catch-up measure to avoid being eclipsed by other European financial centers. 
The new exchange will offer futures contracts on West German equities and fixed-income securities, as well as a futures index. 
Mr. Gottschalk said IBIS will be shut down during the two-hour period when stock exchanges are open for business. 

   "The purpose of IBIS was to augment regular trading hours with a parallel system, not replace it altogether," Mr. Gottschalk said. 
"A second motive was to supply accurate, after-hours prices as a reference for trading on the new futures exchange." 

   The decision to adopt the system was made by the so-called Council of Experts, a panel of West German bankers regularly advising the supervisory boards of West German stock exchanges, themselves made up of bankers. 
Deutsche Bank AG and Dresdner Bank AG, West Germany's top two banks, were the principal forces promoting the IBIS project. 

</TEXT>

<HL> International:
@  NatWest Chairman, 3 Senior Directors
@  Resign Under Cloud of Blue Arrow Issue
@  ----
@  By Joann S. Lublin and Craig Forman
@  Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> NW WNEWS BAW EUROP </CO>
<IN> BANKS (BNK)
STOCK MARKET, OFFERINGS (STK)
SECURITIES INDUSTRY (SCR) </IN>
<DATELINE> LONDON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   National Westminster Bank PLC's chairman and three senior directors resigned in one of Britain's biggest banking management purges, prompted by harsh government criticism of the bank's role in a 1987 Blue Arrow PLC rights offering. 

   The exodus of Chairman Lord Boardman, two deputy chief executives, Charles Green and Terence Green, and a general manager, John Plastow, reflect NatWest's desperate attempt to cope with the government investigation of the bank's underwriting of the Blue Arrow offering. 
But the affair is far from over. 

   NatWest and individuals named in a government report released last week still could face criminal charges stemming from their actions during the underwriting flop. 
The Bank of England has yet to decide whether any individuals are unfit to remain in British banking. 
And British regulators are considering whether to restrict individuals' or NatWest's activities in the securities markets. 

   Lord Boardman's sudden change of heart about resigning highlights the seriousness of the affair. 
Only last Friday, the NatWest chairman -- who wasn't cited in the government report -- said he wasn't contemplating resignation because "the ritual falling-on-the-sword thing" didn't do much good. 
But yesterday, he acknowledged that the Blue Arrow affair has been "very expensive" for NatWest and that "morale has suffered." 
The biggest casualties from the affair are NatWest's blue-chip reputation and its already-stumbling investment-banking aspirations. 
A NatWest spokesman declined to comment on speculation about the future of the bank's investment-banking operations. 

   In the short term, the departures aren't likely to alter the bank's day-to-day operations substantially. 
Thomas Frost, the bank's strategy-setting chief executive, keeps his post; he wasn't criticized in the report. 
And younger managers stand ready to assume greater responsibilities. 
The departing directors all were nearing retirement age. 
Lord Boardman, 70 years old, had planned to retire at year end and be succeeded by Lord Alexander. 
He now will leave Sept. 30. 
NatWest didn't immediately name succesors for the departing senior directors, whose resignations take effect immediately. 

   In an unusual press conference yesterday, Lord Boardman said he objected to certain findings in the government's investigation. 
But he added that he felt he couldn't stay after the three directors resigned. 
"Very serious errors were made in one part of the group," he said. 
"Ultimate responsibility is mine." 

   The resignations cap a string of departures following last week's publication of the highly critical government inquiry into NatWest's handling of the #837 million ($1.36 billion) Blue Arrow rights issue. 
The report found evidence of misconduct and failure to comply with British company law by not disclosing for three months a holding of more than 9.5% in Blue Arrow. 
It cited more than half a dozen current and former NatWest executives, including the three senior directors, "for conduct falling below that to be expected." 

   The Bank of England yesterday said NatWest Deputy Chairman Sir Philip Wilkinson resigned as a member of the Bank of England's board of banking supervision, though he remains at NatWest. 
And Elizabeth Brimelow, a former compliance officer with the bank's County NatWest investment-banking unit, stepped down as compliance director at Charterhouse PLC, though she remains employed by the financial-services group. 
Last week Ms. Brimelow, who was criticized in the government report, resigned as a director of the Securities Association, a securities-industry watchdog group. 

   On Monday, NatWest confirmed that investment banker David Reed quit County NatWest and Nicholas Wells, a former NatWest corporate financier, stepped down from a post at a Barclays PLC unit. 
Both were criticized in the government report. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Carl N. Karcher,
@  6 Relatives Agree
@  To Settle SEC Case
@  ----
@  By Frederick Rose
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> CARL </CO>
<IN> STOCK MARKET, OFFERINGS (STK)
SECURITIES INDUSTRY (SCR) </IN>
<GV> SECURITIES AND EXCHANGE COMMISSION (SEC) </GV>
<DATELINE> LOS ANGELES  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Carl N. Karcher, chairman and chief executive officer of Carl Karcher Enterprises Inc., and six members of his family agreed to pay $664,000 to settle insider-trading charges. 

   The preliminary pact with the Securities and Exchange Commission is subject to court approval. 

   The SEC alleged that members of Mr. Karcher's family used advance knowledge of a plunge in profit to sell stock in the Anaheim, Calif., fast-food chain in 1984. 
Mr. Karcher himself isn't alleged to have sold any stock at that time. 

   Under the proposed settlement, Mr. Karcher will pay a fine of $332,000 to the SEC. 
His relatives, whom he allegedly tipped off, will pay a further $332,000, representing losses the SEC says they avoided by selling stock on inside knowledge. 

   According to the proposed consent decree, neither Mr. Karcher nor his relatives will admit or deny the charges. 
Mr. Karcher, who faced fines of as much as $996,000, has declined to make any comment on the agreement, a secretary said. 

   Yesterday, Carl Karcher Enterprises stock slid $1.25 a share, to close at $15.75 in national over-the-counter trading. 

   The current agreement follows an aborted settlement attempt last May that foundered when lawyers for Mr. Karcher and his family sought assurances that no criminal charges would be lodged against them. 
The proposed settlement of SEC civil allegations doesn't contain any such agreement, said John C. Koutsos, SEC counsel. 

   Apparently also in process is a separate agreement with Alvin A. DeShano, the director of accounting at Carl Karcher Enterprises, who in June was acquitted of criminal fraud and insider trading. 
The anticipated pact would settle the SEC's civil allegations against Mr. DeShano. 
The initial case filed by the SEC in April 1988 alleged that 16 people were involved in insider activities. 
Since that filing, six family members agreed to pay back more than $187,500. 
In September 1988, one of Mr Karcher's sons was found to have breached insider-trading laws and was required to repay $10,500. 
Charges against Mr. Karcher's wife, Margaret, were dropped earlier this year.

<HL> Technology:
@  Data General Loss
@  In Quarter Bodes
@  Ill for Industry
@  ----
@  By John R. Wilke
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> DGN </CO>
<IN> EARNINGS (ERN)
COMPUTERS AND INFORMATION TECHNOLOGY (CPR) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Data General Corp. reported an unexpected loss of $23.1 million on flagging sales for its fiscal third quarter, fueling fresh concern for the slowing pace of minicomputer sales. 

   Data General's results set the stage for steep losses forecast by Wang Laboratories Inc. and for weakening results from Prime Computer Inc. and Digital Equipment Corp., expected to report this week. 
Unisys Corp., another major computer maker, reported a 67% drop in net income Monday. 

   "Data General and Unisys have already entered the recession," said Jay Stevens, an analyst at Dean Witter Reynolds Inc. 
"The big question mark is U.S. sales and orders for Digital," he said. 

   Data General's loss for the quarter ended June 24 compared with net income of $592,000, or two cents a share, a year ago. 
Revenue fell 7.4% to $306.1 million from $330.7 million. 
Weakening domestic demand was a major factor, it said. 

   "In the short term, revenues and earnings remain under pressure from soft U.S. demand and from the negative impact of translation of international currencies into a stronger U.S. dollar," said Ronald L. Skates, executive vice president and chief operating officer. 

   The company had an operating loss of $28.7 million, before a $6.3 million pretax gain from the sale of a plant in California, compared with operating profit of $3 million a year earlier. 

   Data General closed at $17.75 a share, down 50 cents, in active New York Stock Exchange composite trading. 

   The Westboro, Mass., company is in the midst of a restructuring of its product line, embracing industry-standard Unix software and reduced instruction set computer, or RISC, technology. 
But it must also serve existing buyers of its older computers, which provide the bulk of sales. 
"The decline of Data General's existing proprietary products continue to overshadow the transition to the new Unix-based line," said Barry F. Willman, an analyst with Sanford C. Bernstein &amp; Co.
