<DOCNO> WSJ890726-0156 </DOCNO>
<DD> = 890726 </DD>
<AN> 890726-0156. </AN>
<HL> At the Best Parties,
@  Guests Steal Maids
@  Instead of Silverware
@  ---
@  In Lovely Beverly Hills, Calif.,
@  Maidnapping Has Become
@  A True 'Crime' of Choice
@  ----
@  By Kathleen A. Hughes
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> LABOR </CO>
<DATELINE> BEVERLY HILLS, Calif.  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Sure, residents here seem to have it easy. 
But they have their problems, too. 
Big problems. 

   There's a servants shortage. 

   The crisis is so severe that so-called friends have been stealing one another's housekeepers. 
Guests are taking servants aside at dinner parties and offering them $500 a week and up to jump ship. 
Agencies are placing servants for big fees, then calling them on the sly with higher offers. 

   In the Platinum Triangle, the ritzy area that includes Beverly Hills, Bel-Air and Holmby Hills, this breach of etiquette is called "maidnapping" -- and it's ruining friendships. 

   Consider the saga of Barbara Mann, co-owner of the Sandra Taylor employment agency. 
She had a wonderful maid named Angela Gomez. 
"She was outstanding. 
I loved the way she was with my children," Mrs. Mann says. 
"I loved her refinement, her class." 

   But when Mrs. Mann returned from a trip East to visit her family, Ms. Gomez was gone. 
No note. 
An empty closet. 
Then, a few weeks later, she saw her close friend, the wife of a well-known director, drive past her house with Ms. Gomez in the car. 
It was the same friend who had taken her to the airport. 
When she called to confront her friend, Ms. Gomez answered the phone. 

   "What are you doing there?" inquired Mrs. Mann. 

   "She no home," responded Ms. Gomez, somewhat enigmatically. 
When Mrs. Mann finally reached her friend, she says the woman claimed that "Angela came to me. 
She wanted to leave." 

   "I call it maidnapping. 
What do you call it?" 
Mrs. Mann angrily asked her friend. 
"I don't want to talk about it," the friend replied, according to Mrs. Mann. 

   Evidently the friend still doesn't want to talk about it. 
When called for comment, the alleged maidnapper's husband answered the phone, confirmed the couple once had a maid named Angela, but then slammed down the phone. 
Mrs. Mann and her friend no longer speak to each other. 

   The servant shortage has been magnified recently by immigration laws that granted amnesty to many illegal aliens. 
Many live-in housekeepers, now becoming legal residents, are suddenly free to find less onerous work outside the mansions. 
Ask Lucy Mires, a Peruvian housekeeper, who just threw down the mop at one palatial estate. 
"This lady likes working me like a slave," Ms. Mires said, shortly before quitting. 
"When I am busy with something, she wants more and more and more. 
Every day I work from eight in the morning until 11 at night. 
It's too much] I'm sorry] This is America]" 

   "It's gotten out of hand, really," says June Scott, a local real estate agent. 
"The help has become very discriminating as to who they want to work for." 

   As market forces change, servants are naturally "trading places." 
Linda Shanedling, the owner of Home Maid Connection Agency, says she recently received a disturbing call from a Polish houseman she had sent to a family here two years ago. 
"Some friends of the family approached him on the side and hired him away," says Ms. Shanedling. 
Actually, she notes, they had their secretary call and offer him $550 a week, up from $400. 
He accepted. 

   All this servant shuffling is changing the social life in the Platinum Triangle. 
Not so long ago, the wealthy liked to show off their household help and well-run homes. 
No more. 
"You never let your maid answer the door," says Herbert Fink, the owner of Theodore, a local clothing shop. 
"It's easier to get a new friend than it is to get a good housekeeper." 

   Dinner parties are fraught with anxiety. 
James C. Katz, the producer of "Scenes From the Class Struggle in Beverly Hills," a current film about upwardly mobile Beverly Hills servants, says he was at a party for the movie a few months ago when he noticed the hostess peering nervously over his shoulder. 
When he turned, he saw a guest chatting with one of the servants. 
"Just leave it to Phyllis to steal her," snapped the hostess. 
"She has done it before." 

   Obviously, bragging about a great housekeeper is naive. 
Lois Marx, a daughter-in-law of the late Groucho Marx, hired Ubol, a part-time housekeeper from Thailand and helped her to become a citizen. 
"She was a marvelous cook," says Mrs. Marx, who can't recall Ubol's lengthy last name. 
So when a movie producer called and asked about the woman, Mrs. Marx delivered a glowing report. 

   No more Thai cuisine. 
Frances Bergen, the mother of actress Candice Bergen and a friend of the producer, eventually "lured her away," according to Mrs. Marx. 
"She wasn't a close friend but she knew my husband's father was Groucho Marx. 
It was snaky," says Mrs. Marx. 
"The way she did that, she probably felt guilty." 

   Not exactly. 
"I didn't steal her from Mrs. Marx," retorts Mrs. Bergen. 
"The lady in question needed a place where she could live in." 
Indeed, she claims, it was Ubol who suggested the switch. 
Mrs. Bergen says she called Mrs. Marx to discuss the matter first and thought she understood Ubol's need to live in. 
"Mrs. 
Marx hated to lose her. 
I would be sorry to lose her, too," she adds. 
"She is just a jewel." 

   Sometimes the alleged snake is an employment agency. 
Many residents say agencies supply housekeepers, then call when the family is out with higher offers. (The going price ranges from about $200 to $500 a week.) Agencies deny this. 
But Pamela Beck, the author of "Rich Men, Single Women," says she played back her answering machine one evening and found this accidentally recorded conversation with her nanny: "Are you happy? 
Because we can get you another job." 
It sounded like the voice of an employee from the very agency that had supplied the nanny for a $250 fee. 

   "I was just enraged," says Mrs. Beck, who stood listening to the conversation with her husband. 
"The agent was a snake," she says, though she declines to name the agent or the agency. 
The nanny turned the offer down, but Mrs. Beck says, "I could feel her thinking about it." 

   Agencies themselves are desperate. 
Debbie Campos, the owner of Meet My Maids, says, "I'm going nuts. 
Life was a breeze a couple of years ago. 
Now if a client wants a live-out maid I have a million good ones. 
But if they want a live-in maid it's a struggle." 
To recruit maids, she says she now visits churches with immigrant congregations and chats with pastors about her business. 
She even sent her business card to the Guatemalan consulate. 

   Those housekeepers still willing to suffer life with the families of Beverly Hills often have their employers jumping through hoops. 
Many families now pay their housekeepers' school tuition, as well as health and car insurance. 
Servants quarters are being upgraded with big-screen televisions. 
To humor one of his housekeepers, Mr. Fink, the clothier, knocked down a wall to enlarge her bedroom, improved the closets and added a sitting room. 

   "Ten thousand dollars later she was gone," Mr. Fink says with a sigh. 

   Cathy Orell, the owner of Baby Buddies Agency Inc., says a distraught client complained that her new housekeeper refused to make the children's bunk beds. 
"She didn't want to climb on the top bunk," says Ms. Orell. 
"She felt she was too much of a lady to climb." 

   Other housekeepers are adopting the manners of their employers. 
Sandra Moss came home one day and discovered her houseman heading out to her tennis court for a game with his son. 
"I told him he can't play tennis during his workday. 
He told me his exercise is more important," says Ms. Moss. 
"I said I understood that." 
Then, she fired him. 

   Another rude awakening occurred when Jim Roth, a marketing consultant, offered his $24,000-a-year houseman the use of his station wagon. 
"I prefer my BMW," the houseman sniffed. 

   If all of this sounds like a TV sitcom, stay tuned. 
The Manns, the couple who lost Angela Gomez to a friend, say they are negotiating with Warner Bros., a unit of Warner Communications Inc., to create a new series called "Get Help," based on the saga of the maids of Beverly Hills. 
"It's going to show that the true brokers of the rich and famous are domestics," says Robert Mann. 
"Without them, the affluent can't seem to survive." 

</TEXT>
</DOC>

<HL> Law:
@  Judges, Immune No More,
@  Seek Malpractice Insurance
@  ----
@  By Ann Hagedorn
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> LAW AND LEGAL AFFAIRS (LAW)
INSURANCE (INS) </IN>
<TEXT>
   First came doctors, then lawyers, engineers and accountants, and now judges. 
Nearly 3,300 judges nationwide have decided that they, too, need malpractice insurance. 

   "Judges are sued far more often than the public would believe," says Kent Batty, court administrator for the state courts in Wayne County, Mich. 
"And for the past few years, there's been a mounting concern over liability." 

   Judge Phillip J. Roth of the Oregon Supreme Court says, "Judges realize now that one moment they can be on the bench and the next, they're in the pit being defended." 
Judge Roth, chairman of the Judicial Immunity Committee of the American Bar Association, says at least 800 suits are filed against judges each year. 

   On the average, between 250 and 300 insurance claims are filed by judges each year, says Frank Bolen, a partner at Herbert L. Jamison &amp; Co., the West Orange, N.J., insurance broker that handles judicial liability policies for National Union Fire Insurance Co., the nation's largest supplier. 
About 3,000 state judges and 300 federal judges have policies now, he says. (There are many more state judges than federal judges in the country.) 

   Until the mid-1980s, not a judge in the land would have considered buying malpractice insurance. 
Under common law, English judges were guaranteed absolute immunity from litigation related to their judicial duties -- and the immunity extended to the New World. 

   "The traditional rule recognizes that judges operate in a glass bowl and protects them by making the glass unbreakable," wrote Judge Roth in a recent article, "The Erosion of Judicial Immunity." 
Absolute immunity was considered necessary to insulate judges from vexatious litigants. 
For years, suits against judges were "extremely rare," the judge says. 

   In 1978, the U.S. Supreme Court upheld judicial immunity in a case in which a judge had granted a mother's petition for her daughter's sterilization. 
After marrying, the daughter sued the judge, claiming a denial of due process. 
In essence, the court ruled that judicial independence was more important than the civil rights of the plaintiff. 
The case, as it turns out, was the high-water mark for judicial immunity. 
In May 1984, the nation's highest court seemed to alter course. 

   That year, the court ruled in favor of two plaintiffs who claimed that a state judge in Virginia had violated their constitutional rights by incarcerating them while they awaited trial for nonjailable offenses. 
The court upheld an injunction against the judge's action and instructed the judge to pay the plaintiffs' attorney fees under a federal statute. 

   That decision, says Judge Roth, "opened the door to litigation. 
It hit judges right where they sit." 

   The Virginia judge ended up paying more than $30,000 in legal fees to the plaintiff. 
The judge was not insured, although she could have been: In 1981, Complete Equity Markets Inc. in Wheeling, Ill., had begun offering policies to judges. 

   Then in 1984, National Union joined the market. 
Its policy for state judges -- the biggest clients -- costs $800 a year for a $1 million policy with no deductible. 
"The judges are covered for acts, errors or omissions in the performance of professional duties," Mr. Bolen says. 

   The policy covers the client's legal expenses in all cases unless the judge pleads guilty to a criminal act or it is proven that he or she intentionally committed a crime. 
It also covers damages and the payment of plaintiffs' attorneys fees in negligence cases related to the judge's duties. 
Federal judges have similar coverage under a different program. 

   In 1984, state judges filed 46 claims with Mr. Bolen's firm, he says. 
In 1985, the number soared to 185 claims. 
Now it has leveled off at 250 a year. 
Mr. Bolen says that a large part of the claims are frivolous "harassment suits," although some do go to trial. 
Currently, he says, a case is pending in which the judge's legal fees have already passed $100,000 and could go to $300,000. 

   Though most judges take out individual policies, some state judges have group coverage paid for by the state. 
The states include Ohio, Vermont, South Dakota and Rhode Island, according to Judge Roth. 
Though Michigan has no group coverage, Mr. Batty, the state court administrator, says all 64 judges in Wayne County have been covered for four years by individual policies bought by the state. 

   The number of federal judges showing interest in the program is growing. 
"The cost of this insurance is relatively cheap for what you get," says U.S. District Judge Frank A. Kaufman of Baltimore. 

   Mr. Bolen stresses that his firm doesn't believe in advertising its judicial malpractice coverage. 
"If everyone looks at judges and knows they're covered," he says, "there's a likelihood of more suits." 

</TEXT>
</DOC>

<AN> 890726-0154. </AN>
<HL> Fox's Gamble on Release of 'The Abyss'
@  ---
@  Film Isn't Easy
@  To Market, Has
@  No Major Stars
@  ----
@  By Richard Turner
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> NWS </CO>
<IN> RECREATION, ENTERTAINMENT, TOYS, MOVIES, PHOTOGRAPHY, SPORTS (REC)
MARKETING, ADVERTISING (MKT) </IN>
<DATELINE> HOLLYWOOD  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   What's an "abyss"? 

   According to research done for Fox Inc.'s film division, many Americans don't know the meaning of the word. 
This caused Tom Sherak to worry, but then he made the best of it. 

   "Remember a picture called 'Apocalypse Now'?" 
Mr. Sherak, Fox's marketing and distribution president, asks hopefully. 
"Tough title, tough title." 
But the name stuck in people's minds. 
"You can use that to your advantage," he says. 

   Mr. Sherak needs all the advantages he can get, because he faces Hollywood's toughest challenge during this now-ebbing summer season. 
His task is to ensure that by Aug. 9 everyone in America is aware that "The Abyss" is a motion picture and is clamoring to see it. 
Fox, a unit of Rupert Murdoch's News Corp., has its reputation -- and upwards of $60 million -- riding on it. 

   The odds are fierce: Conventional film-industry wisdom says that Aug. 9 is too late in the summer to open a blockbuster. 
Even to meet its three-times-delayed opening, special-effects houses and post-production facilities all over town are working feverishly (and on expensive overtime) to finish the picture. 
When it hits the screens, it may give new meaning to the term "wet print." 

   In this extravagant year of record-breaking box-office grosses, Fox so far has missed the party. 
It ranks seventh among the eight major distributors in market share to date; and since Memorial Day, it has watched several studios feast on audiences with a bunch of sequels ("Indiana Jones," "Ghostbusters," "Star Trek," "Lethal Weapon," "Karate Kid") or at least well-known icons like "Batman." 
Walt Disney Co. lacked this edge but compensated by convincing people it had clear alternatives: "Honey, I Shrunk the Kids" for safe family viewing; "Dead Poets Society" for adults seeking high-toned fare. 
Fox has released only "Weekend at Bernie's." 

   "The Abyss" has nothing obviously promotable going for it. 
The film hasn't any well-known stars to provide box-office draw. 
Fox believes the public is as dumbfounded about the actors as it is about the title. 
Who are Ed Harris and Mary Elizabeth Mastrantonio, the film's leads? 

   Moreover, most of the picture takes place underwater, a locale considered problematic for two reasons. 
First, it's hard for actors to do much acting zipped up in deep-sea diving gear; second, the most recent underwater movies, "Deep Star Six," "Leviathan," and a low-budget flick called "Lords of the Deep," all gurgled and died in theaters. 

   The film is also long -- two hours and 18 minutes -- and therefore theaters can't schedule shows in neat 2-4-6-8-10 blocks. 

   The final hurdle from a marketing standpoint is that "The Abyss" has no easy-to-explain concept. 
Fox's big hit of last year, "Big," could be fed to audiences in one bite-sized line: "Tom Hanks becomes a 12-year-old." 
In "The Abyss," the plot twists and spectacles that take place at the end of the film can't be given away by the marketers. 

   "It's a love story, very much set in an atmosphere of adventure and suspense," producer Gale Ann Hurd says, trying gamely to sum things up. 

   If there's a star, it's unquestionably director James Cameron, who won admirers for "The Terminator" and made the cover of Time for "Aliens." 
But even these credits present risks. 
While Fox pushes them on TV spots aimed at young male audiences, it omits them when trying to attract women, for fear the female audience will think the film is a science-fiction or horror film. 
In female-oriented commercials, the relationship is stressed: "For him, it was a matter of honor. 
For her, it was a matter of trust . . . " 

   Plagued by production and editing delays, "The Abyss" was originally set for a July 4 weekend release, then pushed back to later in July, then set to "platform" with openings in a few major markets Aug. 2 before "going wide" Aug. 9. 
Now, the luxury of platforming lost, it's simply opening on 1,500 screens across the country Aug. 9. 

   Some might call this fortunate. 
By waiting until after "Batman" and the other summer hits have cooled down, "The Abyss" eluded early summer's heavy traffic. 
Still, Fox is left with precious few weeks to recoup its investment before kids start going back to school. 
"The fountain just shuts off at Labor Day," says the head of one large theater chain. 

   "That's silly," says Fox Inc. Chairman Barry Diller. 
"The movie business has proven again and again that all conventional wisdom is worthless." 
He cites "An Officer and a Gentleman," which opened in August 1982 -- and ran through Christmas. 

   Another supposed truism is that no movie recovers from a poor opening. 
For a big action/adventure film, it's considered mandatory to play to overflow crowds from the start, so Fox must generate a sense of excitement during the next two weeks. 
The studio is playing host to about 150 reporters at a junket and screening this Friday. 
It ran a "teaser" ad in the New York Times last Sunday for a special Radio City Music Hall showing Aug. 8, trying to create an "event." 
The next day, Fox issued a press release saying the show had sold out in 24 hours. 

   But there already have been false starts. 
A "teaser" trailer that ran last spring was yanked because "it wasn't powerful enough," says Mr. Sherak. 
Then Fox bought two-page ads July 9 in New York and Los Angeles newspapers showing silhouettes of two lovers and the slogan "There's everything you've ever known about adventure. 
And then there's . . . 
The Abyss." 
The ad studiously avoided any mention of water. 
The campaign was yanked. 
Mr. Sherak simply calls it "phase one, to alert the media we're out there" and says new print ads haven't been completed yet. 

   "The Abyss" not only has to open big but also has to have another not-incidental ingredient -- be a good movie. 
"Cameron's very talented," says a rival studio chief, "but this picture's got to be a nine or a 10, not a seven or an eight, or it's in trouble." 

   Consider Fox's own "Star Wars," which opened in 1977 with then-unknown actors, a title the studio felt was too militaristic, little advance publicity and an ad campaign many felt was unfocused. 
"Star Wars" went on to rupture box-office records, of course, buoyed by what research showed was dizzying word of mouth. 
In short, it became an "event." 

   But "Star Wars" also opened big. 
In the 36 theaters where it had its premiere that first weekend, all-time records were set at 34 of them, recalls Alan Ladd Jr., who ran Fox back then. 

   It helped that "Star Wars" producer George Lucas had worked the science-fiction conventions, whose attendees, after seeing presentations about the film, were the first in line opening night. 
Fox, too, has been attending gatherings of hard-core science-fiction fans and has been sending actors and representatives to diving shows, where the underwater technology used in the film will be best appreciated. 

   Much of "The Abyss," was filmed underwater in an abandoned nuclear plant's containment vessel and required new lighting techniques and specially developed technology -- all of which led to endless problems. 
The tank leaked. 
When it did fill up, the water often became murky, leading to prolonged delays. 

   All that made the budget overflow. 
"There's no question that we honestly misjudged the complexity of the production," says Craig Baumgarten, who supervised the beginning of filming for Fox before becoming an independent producer. 

   How much the picture went over budget is hard to determine. 
Mr. Diller says that "The Abyss" was budgeted at $35 million and that it actually cost in the low $40 million range to produce the negative. 
Given the source, that's a conservative estimate that may not include such items as studio overhead and capitalized interest. 
Costs associated with releasing the picture, which include a lot of television advertising, could be at least another $20 million. 

   As of yesterday, Mr. Sherak had spent only about 25% of his television budget, much of it on Fox's owned and affiliated stations. 
The target has been males aged 15-21, and "awareness" among this group has crept up to 56%, from about 27% prior to TV ad-buying, Mr. Sherak says. 
"Want-to-see" is 36%, which Mr. Sherak says is encouraging. 

   Fox will begin targeting female and older viewers during the next two weeks, when reviews begin to come out. 
The one reviewer who was allowed to see it early, Rolling Stone critic Peter Travers, raved so strongly that Fox has gotten permission to use quotations from it. 

   "It's moving nicely," says Mr. Sherak, who admits the next two weeks will be difficult. 
He concludes: "You play with the cards you're dealt." 

   ---

<HL> Swedish Producer Prices Fall </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> EUROP </CO>
<IN> MONETARY NEWS, FOREIGN EXCHANGE, TRADE (MON) </IN>
<DATELINE> STOCKHOLM  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Sweden's producer prices fell 0.2% in June from May but were up 8.9% from a year earlier, Statistics Sweden said. 

   The month-to-month drop followed a 0.3% rise in May and a 1% increase in April. 
The decrease was due in part to a 3.2% drop in prices of refined petroleum products. 

   Sweden's import prices fell 0.6% in June from May but were up 6.8% from a year earlier. 
Its export prices fell 0.4% in the latest month because of lower prices for basic metals and engineering products but were up 8.3% from June 1988.

<HL> Consolidated TVX
@  And Placer Dome Plan
@  To Expand Chile Mine </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> PDG T.CVX </CO>
<IN> COMMODITY NEWS, FARM PRODUCTS (CMD)
PRECIOUS METALS, STONES, GOLD, SILVER (PCS) </IN>
<DATELINE> VANCOUVER, British Columbia  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Placer Dome Inc. and Consolidated TVX Mining Corp. said they will develop the second stage of their jointly owned La Coipa gold and silver mine in Chile for about $276 million. 

   The companies said the expenditure includes about $248 million for a new 16,500-ton-a-day processing plant and $28 million to cover preproduction costs and working capital. 
The plant is expected to be completed by the first half of 1991, raising La Coipa's projected annual output to 200,000 ounces of gold and 16 million ounces of silver. 

   Vancouver-based Placer Dome and Toronto-based Consolidated TVX said they recently completed development of the first phase at La Coipa, allowing initial annual output of about 52,000 ounces of gold and seven million ounces of silver. 
An 1,100-ton-a-day plant, built during the first phase, will be closed once the larger processing plant starts operating. 

   La Coipa is expected to produce about 2.1 million ounces of gold and 142.9 million ounces of silver over a 13-year mine life, at a cash production cost of $178 an ounce of gold or its equivalent. 

   The companies said their development plans at La Coipa are subject to regulatory approval. 
Financing is being arranged through a consortium of banks.

<DATELINE> MANILA  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Philippines' oil imports, including costs of insurance and freight, rose 20% to $591.4 milllion in the first five months of the year from $494 million a year earlier, the Energy Affairs Office's demand management division said. 

   The division attributed the increase to higher industrial demand as well as the increase in oil prices. 
It said oil consumption rose 15% in the five months to 33.6 million barrels from 29.3 million. 
An analyst in the government division said the average f.o.b. price of a barrel of imported oil was up 61 cents in the 1989 period to $15.81.

<CO> LABOR PCO </CO>
<IN> MINING, METALS (NON-PRECIOUS) (MIN) </IN>
<DATELINE> CLEVELAND, Va.  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The vice president of the United Mine Workers union was jailed for blocking traffic to a Pittston Co. coal preparation plant. 

   Cecil E. Roberts and 18 other union members were arrested for sitting on the road outside the plant in Russell County, Va. 
Mr. Roberts was taken to Blackford Correctional Center, a Virginia state prison, according to a state police spokesman. 

   UMW members have repeatedly blocked access to Pittston's mines and plants in the union's continuing strike against the Greenwich, Conn., company, which has refused to sign a national labor agreement with the UMW. 

   Meanwhile, the UMW's international executive board will meet today in Duffield, Va., to discuss the union's strategy against Pittston. 
Some miners have called for a national coal strike to support the Pittston miners.

<CO> EME </CO>
<IN> EARNINGS (ERN) </IN>
<DATELINE> NORTH BERGEN, N.J.  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Emerson Radio Corp. reported that net income for the fiscal first quarter more than doubled and said that second-quarter results also should improve. 

   The consumer-electronics and personal-computer marketer is "cautiously optimistic about the future," said President Stephen L. Lane. 
He added, "We feel good about the year in general, but based on the history of the industry -- the oversupplies that developed -- we decided it's prudent to be cautious." 

   Net for the quarter ended June 30 jumped to $3.5 million, or 10 cents a share, from $1.3 million, or four cents a share, a year earlier. 
The year-ago figure reflects a $199,000 special credit tied to use of tax-loss carry-forwards. 
Sales advanced 35% to $221.2 million from $163.4 million. 

   In New York Stock Exchange composite trading, Emerson closed at $5.50 a share, up 37.5 cents.

<HL> Letters to the Editor:
@  Attending to Nuances
@  In Israeli Politics </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> MDEST </CO>
<TEXT>
   Surprisingly, American journalists and policy makers often ignore many of the critical nuances that frequently shape and define the Israeli political process. 
Your July 10 report "Israel's Labor Party Tries to Find a Role" is an example. 

   You offer the expressions of former Israeli Knesset member Abba Eban as the primary basis of the story. 
You neglect, however, to explain that Mr. Eban and his views were rejected by the Labor Party last year when the former foreign minister was passed over for a position in the current Parliament. 

   Labor joined the current coalition with the Likud Party because the alternative was for the party to accept the reality of its decline into a marginal influence within the Israeli political spectrum. 
Both at the polls and in a variety of recent opinion studies, the Israeli electorate clearly has shown its disdain for Labor's policies and the party's socialist leadership. 

   As well, Labor gambled that the powerful finance portfolio would allow the party to win back its former friends and influence through a series of self-serving, politically motivated economic actions. 
Contrary to the early hopes of Finance Minister Shimon Peres, these efforts have served only to intensify the decline of Labor's standing among Israeli voters. 

   If we truly desire to understand the actions of our close Middle Eastern friend, it would be wise to pay less attention to the voices of Israel's naked kings, and give due scrutiny to the nation's current mood and leadership. 

   Seth D. Eisenberg 

   Brooksville, Ohio 

</TEXT>
</DOC>
<DOC>
<DOCNO> WSJ890726-0144 </DOCNO>
<DD> = 890726 </DD>
<AN> 890726-0144. </AN>
<HL> Japan's Leading Indicator </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> JAPAN </CO>
<DATELINE> TOKYO  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>

<HL> British Business Optimism
@  Declines, a Survey Shows
@  ----
@  Special to The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> EUROP </CO>
<DATELINE> LONDON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Confederation of British Industry said its latest quarterly survey of industrial trends found a "sharp fall" in business optimism. 

   Citing slower growth of orders and output, the confederation said investment "is expected to increase much more slowly over the next 12 months." 

   The organization's report warned that the decline in business optimism could damage Britain's ability to compete with other European countries, and said it is looking to the government to curb inflationary pressures over the long term. 

   The report noted that the decline in confidence is the steepest since October 1982, with growth in demand moderating for the fifth consecutive survey. 

   David Wigglesworth, chairman of the confederation's economic situation committee, said, "The fall in business confidence reflects the more difficult economic conditions being experienced by companies." 
He said that in the past four surveys, expectations of new business haven't been realized. 

   "Manufacturers are therefore being much more cautious about future investment plans, and this could be bad news for Britain's ability to compete in the single European market after 1992," he said. 

</TEXT>

<HL> PSE Expects to Post
@  34% Decline in Profit
@  For the Second Quarter </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> POW </CO>
<IN> DOW JONES INTERVIEW (CEO) </IN>
<DATELINE> HOUSTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   PSE Inc., an energy engineering concern, expects to report second-quarter earnings of $1.7 million to $2 million, or 18 cents to 22 cents a share, Albert J. Smith, chairman and chief executive officer, said. 

   The results would represent a decline of at least 34% from a year earlier, when net income was $3 million, or 33 cents a share. 

   PSE estimated first-half net at $2.3 million to $2.6 million, or 19 cents to 23 cents a share, compared with $1.4 million, or 15 cents a share, a year earlier, an increase of at least 64%. 

   Mr. Smith, in an interview, attributed the improved six-month results to closing debt financing on the company's cogeneration power plant in the San Joaquin Valley of California. 
The $58 million joint venture with Dominion Energy Inc., Richmond, Va., will produce electricity and steam, from one fuel source, for a glass manufacturing plant under a 15-year contract. 

   Two years ago, the small engineering firm began a marketing advance into California and the Northeast from its Texas base. 
By 1991, with its new California energy projects, PSE expects to be generating 550 megawatts of electricity from natural-gas fired plants, compared with 470 megawatts of electricity now being produced in Texas. 

   With public utility commissions warring with investor-owned utilities over placing pricey nuclear plants into the consumer rate base, many utilities simply will not build any more power plants, Mr. Smith said. 
"It has left a void and the independent power industry has moved in to fill it," he said. 

</TEXT>

<HL> Letters to the Editor:
@  Starry-Eyed Ads </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> MARKETING, ADVERTISING (MKT) </IN>
<TEXT>
   When you say "consumers are having more trouble believing what stars say in TV ads" ("Marketing &amp; Media" July 5), you miss the point of celebrities in advertising. 

   That consumers say they do not find celebrities credible is not relevant when brands such as Cover Girl makeup, Nike Air-Jordan sneakers and Miller Lite beer have seen tremendous sales growth directly attributable to celebrity endorsements. 

   The issue of credibility is relevant when a spokesperson says, "More people prefer Pepsi over Coke," but when Whitney Houston drinks Diet Coke in a commercial, what difference does credibility make? 
The consumer's attention is caught, a brand image is reinforced and products are sold. 
Where is the message to question? 
I am surprised that anyone can take seriously a consumer-opinion poll about advertising when many consumers still believe that advertising has no effect on their buying habits. 

   Samuel S. Kim 

   Edgewater, N.J.

<HL> Letters to the Editor:
@  Shaking Up the East's
@  Quake Consciousness </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<TEXT>
   Your July 11 article on earthquake potential in the Eastern U.S. brings to mind another quake-related hazard in the East -- a danger not faced by Californians. 

   The same geology that makes California quake-prone also helps contain damage from earthquakes to a reasonably small geographical area. 
Rock strata under California have been so fractured and jumbled that they form a "shock absorber" that dissipates quake vibrations. 
This effect is like hitting a feather pillow, and is why the 1906 San Francisco quake had no great impact outside the Bay area. 

   In the East and Midwest, however, the situation is different -- and potentially more dangerous. 
East of the Rockies, one can trace largely unbroken rock formations for hundreds of miles in a given direction. 
When the next major earthquake strikes the East or Midwest, these rocks could act as long-distance conduits for quake vibrations and carry damage far beyond the epicenter of the earthquake. 
This effect is more like striking a gong than a feather pillow, and could transmit the shock from a "superquake" in the Mississippi Valley (site of the most powerful earthquakes in U.S. history: the New Madrid, Mo., quakes of 1811-12) all the way to the Atlantic shore. 

   Incidentally, I have spent much time in California and have experienced only one earthquake that was strong enough to send me running for safety. 
That one was in Vermont. 

   David Ritchie 

   Baltimore 

   ---

   (Mr. 
Ritchie had a book about earthquakes published by Crown last year.) 

</TEXT>

<HL> Letters to the Editor:
@  Some Businesses Put Prisons to Work </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<TEXT>
   I read with great interest your May 22 article describing problems many small businesses are experiencing as a result of chronic labor shortages. 
You vividly portray the stress many firms are experiencing and you correctly report the demographic and social trends that portend even greater difficulties in the years ahead. 

   A growing number of businesses, however, have discovered an innovative solution. 
They are going to prison to address labor-shortage problems similar to those cited in the Journal. 
A recent survey commissioned by the National Institute of Justice identified more than 70 companies that are employing prison inmates in 16 states to perform a variety of manufacturing, service and light assembly operations. 
In Arizona and California, inmates work as travel-reservations clerks for Best Western and TWA respectively; in Nebraska and Washington, prisoners sew casual wear for garment manufacturers; in Nevada they make water-bed mattresses; and in New Hampshire, inmates assemble electronic components, which are used in various high-tech devices. 

   These joint ventures suggest that there are opportunities in prison for many kinds of companies. 
Prisoners are readily available day and night, and are eager to perform the entry-level jobs that an increasing number of companies find difficult to fill. 
At the same time, we all benefit when idle inmates become working taxpayers with the means to compensate the victims of their crimes, contribute to the support of their own families, and offset the cost of their incarceration. 

   Prisons may be the alternative source of labor that a growing number of companies are looking for. 

   James K. Stewart 

   Director, National Institute of Justice 

   Department of Justice 

   Washington 

</TEXT>

<DOCNO> WSJ890726-0124 </DOCNO>
<DD> = 890726 </DD>
<AN> 890726-0124. </AN>
<HL> Asia:
@  LDP's Loss May Be Japan's Gain
@  ----
@  By Steven Platzer </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> JAPAN </CO>
<DATELINE> TOKYO  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The stunning defeat of the Liberal Democratic Party in Sunday's balloting suggests momentous changes have begun in Japanese society. 

   Of the 126 seats at stake, the LDP, which has ruled Japan for the past 34 years, won only 36. 
The Japan Socialist Party won 46, Komeito 10, Japan Communist Party five, Democratic Socialist Party three, and a cluster of smaller parties 26. 
That leaves the LDP with a total of 109 seats, far short of a majority in the 252member Upper House. 

   The outcome makes it most unlikely that the LDP can maintain its single-party rule for much longer -- particularly not in the form that its leaders have insisted is necessary for Japan's political and economic stability. 
Japanese voters are so disenchanted with the corruption spawned by 34 years of uninterrupted LDP rule that they now are willing to experiment with a more pluralistic form of democratic government. 
That judgment will transform the fabric of Japanese political life. 

   But no one should be deceived about the kind of changes that these forces will -- and will not -- make possible. 
Politically Japan must change, but ideologically it may not. 

   A large percentage of the votes cast for the Japan Socialist Party were actually a protest against the scandal-tainted LDP. 
There is little reason to believe Japan's electorate would support the JSP if it tried to move the country far to the left. 

   Thus, even if the LDP were to lose its control over the more-powerful Lower House -- a general election must be called by next summer -- the JSP probably would not tamper with the economic system that has made Japan a capitalist superpower. 
These constraints aside, Japan's political landscape will be quite different. 

   The LDP will be weakened if it continues to formulate national policies in secret, or if it tries to impose new laws in the same manner employed earlier this year to pass an extremely unpopular 3% consumption tax. 
The members of the Upper House will find themselves obliged, finally, to discuss their nation's problems with the representatives of opposing parties in an open and rational manner. 

   As a consequence, the Upper House gradually will move away from the party-oriented form of politics now practiced, and begin to focus on issues. 
And since no single party will be able to impose its will, they all will have to learn how to defend and promote their policies. 
The highly divisive customs of insult and denunciation typical of post-war parliamentary debate will go out of fashion. 

   There are four popular explanations as to why this situation came about: the fallout from the Recruit influence-peddling scandal; the hated 3% consumption tax; farmers' anxiety over liberalization of Japan's markets to agricultural products; and the furor about Prime Minister Sosuke Uno's private habits. 
But other factors also have been at work. 

   Japanese women, long taught to regard political matters as taboo subjects that are the privileged domain of men, have been deeply angered. 
Many of them have started to vote for female candidates who promise to assert what is now being referred to as "women's power." 
The recent comments of one LDP minister about the unsuitability of women in the world of politics -- remarks directed against JSP Chairwoman Takako Doi -- probably persuaded a good number to vote against the LDP. 
The JSP has capitalized upon this trend by fielding a record number of successful women candidates, but it remains to be seen whether the JSP will be able to accommodate the interests of both women and labor, its traditional base. 

   Less obviously, but no less significantly, LDP leaders inadvertently have disproved their long-held claim that Japan's economic prosperity was a direct result of the political stability LDP rule guaranteed. 
Since the Japanese economy is still going strong, in spite of the political instability the LDP has wrought, voters no longer believe that the economy will fall apart without the LDP's stewardship. 
This was confirmed when the Tokyo Stock Market rose 193.9 points Monday, to finish at 34,093 -- even as rumors were spreading about Mr. Uno's impending resignation. 

   Japan's Diet now must decide what to do about the consumption tax, widely viewed as the main cause of the ruling party's defeat. 
LDP leaders probably will move to reform this tax in the Lower House since continued support for it in its current form would mean political suicide. 
But they most likely will do so in the same strong-armed manner originally used to pass it in the first place, without much debate. 
When the new bill they ratify reaches the Upper House, however, it will be discussed and probably rejected. 
If the LDP stays true to form, it will ignore the Upper House and enact the legislation in the Lower House, where it still has an overwhelming majority. 

   But if this scenario plays out, LDP leaders inevitably will contribute to the Japanese voters' desire for a less party-oriented form of politics. 
This will in turn deepen spreading dissatisfaction with the party's long-running attempt to enforce a form of parliamentary government in which the only issues discussed are the ones on the LDP's agenda. 
And within this emerging environment, a more pluralistic form of issue-oriented politics will be regarded as providing the best way to further the nation's strength and recover its damaged politics. 
If the political transformation Japanese voters launched on Sunday proves successful, it probably will work itself out along these lines. 
If it does, the extreme form of social conformity long generated by a lack of pluralism may well begin to break down. 
The end result would be the development of a more-open form of political life. 

   How will this affect Japan's relations with the rest of the world? 

   The Liberal Democrats long have argued that foreign companies have not been successful in penetrating Japan's markets because the Japanese don't want many foreign products. 
Of course, what they don't say is that the Japanese people have not been able to purchase freely foreign-made items, or to do so at prices they would find attractive. 

   But if Japan's political system is opened up, there is good reason to believe that the interests of Japanese consumers finally will be recognized. 
That naturally would ease many of the tensions between Japan and its leading trading partners. 

   Moreover, the fears that Japan's East Asian neighbors have expressed about its continuing military buildup will be allayed if a socialist-centered alliance succeeds in slowing the pace of military expenditures. 
Given the recent lessening of East-West tensions, the Socialists may very well be able to realize this longstanding part of their political platform. 

   Sunday's election, then, did not just mean fewer seats for the LDP; it has important implications for the future of Japan and the world. 

   ---

   Mr. Platzer is a visiting fellow at Tokyo University. 

</TEXT>

<HL> USX Profit Rose 17% in 2nd Quarter
@  Due to Oil Sector; National Steel Gains
@  ----
@  By Clare Ansberry
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> X NII J.NKK </CO>
<IN> MINING, METALS (NON-PRECIOUS) (MIN)
EARNINGS (ERN) </IN>
<DATELINE> PITTSBURGH  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   USX Corp. posted a 17% gain in second-quarter net income as higher oil prices and stronger demand for petroleum products offset flat sales and declining operating profit in its steel operations. 

   The company earned $250 million, or 91 cents a share, including gains of $72 million, or about 28 cents a share, from the sale of assets. 
In the year-ago period, USX earned $213 million, or 74 cents a share. 
Sales rose 16% to $4.96 billion from $4.29 billion a year ago. 

   Separately, profit at National Steel Corp., the nation's sixth-largest steelmaker, jumped to $32.4 million from $19.6 million a year earlier, as steel shipments benefited from strike hedge buying and fewer maintenance shutdowns. 
Sales increased to $674 million from $659.3 million for the company, a joint venture of National Intergroup Inc. and NKK Corp of Japan. 

   Both companies are expected to see a second-half softening in the steel sector, reflecting the sluggish automotive and construction markets. 
For its part, National Steel already has noted that it lost 330,000 tons in third-quarter shipments after its steelworkers initially vetoed a labor contract. 
Since then, its steelworkers have approved the pact and National is working to recapture some of those orders, although at lower profit margins. 

   At USX, profit per ton of steel shipped was $53, compared with last year's $70-a-ton level. 

   Peter Marcus, an analyst with PaineWebber Inc., noted the difficult comparison with the year-ago second quarter when the industry benefited from peak prices and unusually strong demand. 
Since that time, demand and pricing have fallen off, while inflation has hit raw material and labor costs. 

   The steel segment reported operating earnings of $171 million, down 23% from the year-earlier $223 million. 
Sales were flat at $1.6 billion. 

   Still, USX managed to improve its profit per ton from the first quarter's $33 because of increased shipments linked to strike hedge-buying and increased exports. 
Mr. Marcus noted that second-quarter profit per ton was higher than the $45 he had projected and said the improvement was especially impressive given the continued weakness in steel's oil-country tubular-goods business. 

   "USX is running a very tight ship with aggressive pricing," he said. 
Moreover, he said USX's shipments remained relatively strong, as a result of increased exports. 

   However, he said he's not changing his outlook, which shows operating profits per ton of steel shipped dropping 50% for major mills by year's end because of declining volume and a continued squeeze on costs and pricing. 

   Meanwhile, USX's oil operations buoyed overall performance, with strong demand for refined petroleum products and higher average crude prices. 
Operating earnings at the company's Marathon Oil Co. unit more than doubled to $257 million from $119 million last year. 
Even in the disappointing Texas Oil &amp; Gas Corp. unit, losses narrowed by $3 million, to $13 million. 

   In composite trading on the New York Stock Exchange, USX closed yesterday at $34.375 a share, down 37.5 cents. 

   National Steel managed to avoid the trend of steelmakers this year by topping both last year's second quarter and this year's first quarter results. 
Profit per ton of steel shipped totaled $32 in the second quarter, up from the $22 the year earlier when the company was beset with unscheduled shutdowns from equipment breakdowns. 
Moreover, the second quarter profit per ton of steel shipped was significantly better than the $8 a share in the first quarter of 1989, when the company had several scheduled maintenance shutdowns. 

   In the first six months, USX earned $546 million, or $2 a share, compared with $370 million, or $1.26 per share, a year earlier. 

   National Steel reported earnings in the first half of $42.2 million on sales of $1.33 billion, compared with earnings of $33.2 million on sales of $1.28 billion in the year-ago period.

<HL> Chicago Exchange Doubles
@  Soybean Contract Margin </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> I.FAF </CO>
<IN> SECURITIES INDUSTRY (SCR)
COMMODITY NEWS, FARM PRODUCTS (CMD) </IN>
<DATELINE> CHICAGO  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   The Chicago Board of Trade doubled the amount of margin money traders have to put up to buy or sell its August soybean futures contract. 

   The August soybean contract has been more volatile than normal during the past few weeks, mainly reflecting uncertainty surrounding the number of contracts held by the Italian conglomerate Ferruzzi Finanziaria S.p.A., traders said. 
Ferruzzi's huge holdings of soybeans and July soybean futures contracts triggered a liquidation order from the exchange on July 11 that sent soybean futures prices gyrating wildly. 
The July contract expired last week. 

   Ferruzzi officials won't specify how many August soybean futures contracts they hold. 

   The exchange didn't say why it was raising the margin requirements, although price volatility is most often cited by market participants as a reason for such a move. 
They noted that the margin increase won't have much of an impact on large, wealthy traders, such as Ferruzzi, but smaller speculators may have to trim their positions to stay in the market. 

   The Board of Trade raised its initial, or speculative, margin requirements to $3,000 a contract from $1,500, and boosted hedging, or maintenance, margins to $2,500 from $1,250.

@  Rumors He May Quit Post </HL>

<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<DATELINE> LOS ANGELES  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Embattled Los Angeles Mayor Tom Bradley dismissed speculation that he might resign, despite recent revelations that indicate he ordered the deposit of $2 million in city funds in a bank that paid him as a consultant. 

   "I'll be around here four years from now as mayor of this city," Mr. Bradley said in response to reporters' questions at the close of a City Hall news conference on another subject. 
The remark represented a departure for the mayor, who has refused to comment on city, state and federal investigations into his financial dealings. 

   In testimony before the City Council earlier this week, a city auditor disclosed that officials in the city treasurer's office had doctored a document that indicated the mayor had requested the funds be transferred to Far East National Bank, a Chinatown bank that had paid him $18,000 as a consultant. 
A reference on the document saying "per the mayor" was covered with correction fluid, and the name of City Treasurer Leonard Rittenberg was written in its place. 
Mr. Bradley said he knew "nothing" about the doctored documents. 

   The mayor has acknowledged that he discussed the city's ties to Far East with Mr. Rittenberg, but has denied that he directed him to deposit the funds there.

<HL> Boeing Net Rose 20% in 2nd Quarter,
@  But Firm Cites Continuing Challenges
@  ----
@  By Rick Wartzman
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> BA </CO>
<IN> EARNINGS (ERN)
AEROSPACE (ARO) </IN>
<TEXT>
   Boeing Co.'s second-quarter net income jumped 20% on stronger revenue, but the world's leading aircraft maker stressed that it continues "to face significant challenges and risks" in both its commercial and military lines. 

   The Seattle concern said, for instance, that its defense and space business wouldn't be profitable this year. 
Even the most optimistic analysts have expected the company to barely break even in this arena, though Boeing hinted earlier that it might be able to begin turning things around by the end of 1989. 

   Boeing also said it is worried that suppliers won't be able to keep pace with its brisk jetliner delivery schedule. 
Some 60% of 747 deliveries in 1989 are now slated for the fourth quarter. 

   Boeing profit rose to $195 million, or 85 cents a share, from $160 million, or 70 cents a share, a year earlier. 
Sales soared 7.3% to $5.1 billion from $4.7 billion. 

   Analysts cautioned that Boeing has a penchant for understating good news and playing up bad. 
"They're terminally dour," said David Smith, who researches the company for Raymond James &amp; Associates Inc., a St. Petersburg, Fla., brokerage firm. 
"You never want to let the airlines know you're doing well, or they'll come and pinch you for another half-percent." 
Labor negotiations, he noted, also begin soon. 

   Yet the company's concerns, highlighted by Chairman Frank Shrontz in a statement, are real enough. 
Several key Pentagon programs that Boeing is involved with, including the B-2 Stealth bomber, the Space Station and the V-22 tilt-rotor transport, are now under fire as Congress grapples with the federal deficit. 

   And analyst John Simon said that on a visit to Boeing a week ago, he found 27 747 jets "sitting around waiting for parts." 

   Boeing's results, in many respects, are a perfect encapsulation of the state of the aerospace industry: things booming on the commercial side, to the point of straining manufacturing capability, while money made on the military side continues to dwindle. 

   A number of defense contractors, including Lockheed Corp., General Dynamics Corp., McDonnell Douglas Corp. and Northrop Corp., have been squeezed in the first half by overruns on fixed-price research and development contracts. 
Just this week, moreover, Honeywell Inc. announced it will reduce its dependence on weapons sales as part of a restructuring. 

   And Boeing, which is bidding for work on the Advanced Technology Fighter and has numerous other Pentagon pacts, is no exception in feeling the strain. 
Said Mr. Simon: "Boeing's been one of the leading defense contractors for years, and even they can't make money at it." 

   Still, Mr. Smith noted, underneath all the gloom rests the fact that the company's earnings are headed higher still -- a situation he believes bodes well for the company's performance on the New York Stock Exchange. 

   In Big Board trading yesterday, Boeing's shares fell to $52.125, down 37.5 cents. 
But Mr. Smith, one of the most bullish Boeing analysts around, has tracked a close correlation between the company's earnings performance and it share price. 
"There's a lot more room for this stock to grow," asserted Mr. Smith, who predicts the company's earnings won't peak until 1994. 

   In the first half, net was $356 million, or $1.55 a share, on sales of $9.1 billion. 
That compares with net of $296 million, or $1.29 a share, on sales of $8.4 billion a year ago. 
The company reiterated its projection that 1989 sales will be about $22 billion.

@  Virginia Hotel to College </HL>

<CO> NSC </CO>
<IN> REAL ESTATE, REITS, LAND DEVELOPMENT (REL) </IN>
<DATELINE> ROANOKE, Va.  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Norfolk Southern Corp. said it plans to donate its century-old Hotel Roanoke to Virginia Polytechnic Institute and State University for use as an academic and convention center. 

   The 385-room hotel will be closed Nov. 30. 
Downtown development plans call for spending about $30 million to upgrade the hotel. 

   At the same time, Norfolk Southern said it plans to build an office building in Roanoke to house about half of the 3,000 or so employees in the area. 
Price of the new building hasn't been established. 
It is expected to be completed in about two years. 

   A Virginia Polytechnic spokesman said the college hopes to find a private partner to renovate the property and jointly manage it. 
The property would become a conference center as well as housing certain graduate and continuing education programs and a hotel/restaurant management program. 

   Virginia Polytechnic is located in nearby Blacksburg, Va. 
Norfolk Southern, a transportation and energy company, has its headquarters in Norfolk, Va.

<HL> Politics &amp; Policy:
@  Investigators Say
@  The IRS Ignored
@  Wrongdoing Data
@  ----
@  By Hilary Stout
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<GV> CONGRESS (CNG)
INTERNAL REVENUE SERVICE (IRS) </GV>
<DATELINE> WASHINGTON  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Congressional investigators said the Internal Revenue Service ignored several reports of wrongdoing among high-ranking employees during the past few years in an effort to protect its public image. 

   The allegation follows a yearlong investigation for the House Commerce, Consumer and Monetary Affairs Subcommittee, focusing on eight cases involving 25 senior officials in 10 offices across the country. 

   The subcommittee investigators, Leonard Bernard and Richard Stana, told the panel at a hearing yesterday that the cases range from personal trips on the agency's tab to the release of confidential tax information from an IRS official to a businessman who gave the official theater tickets and other gratuities. 
In another case, the agency's top criminal investigator in Los Angeles helped develop a tax case against the owners of Jordache Enterprises, a jeans manufacturer, based on information from the founders of Guess Inc., who were involved in bitter civil litigation with the Jordache owners. 
The official then said he was leaving the IRS to work for Guess, although ultimately he didn't take the position. 

   Only one individual in the eight cases was punished within the agency and no one has been prosecuted, the investigators said. 

   The IRS says that many of those involved have retired from the agency and thus can't be penalized administratively, but that most of the cases have been referred to the Justice Department for possible prosecution. 

   But subcommittee Chairman Doug Barnard (D., Ga.) said, "Our investigation indicates that there are serious employee integrity problems among senior managers at the IRS; inadequate internal investigations and punishment of senior-level misconduct; a pervasive fear at all levels of the IRS over retaliation for the reporting of such misconduct; and a driving concern that publicly exposing wrongdoing by senior managers will tarnish the agency's public image and make its tax enforcement responsibilities more difficult." 

   The subcommittee is taking a hard look at how the IRS polices itself and is preparing recommendations to make it more accountable to outsiders. 

   "Wrongdoing by IRS senior managers is too often ignored entirely or ineptly investigated, resulting in a clearance for the wrongdoers," the investigators said in a report submitted to the subcommittee. 

   One of the cases at the heart of the committee's inquiry involves Ronald Saranow, the former head of the Los Angeles office's criminal investigation division. 

   In March 1985, the investigators say, Paul, George, Armand and Maurice Marciano, founders of Guess, went to Mr. Saranow with evidence of tax fraud against Joe, Ralph and Avi Nakash, owners of Jordache. 
At the time, the Marcianos were deep in litigation trying to regain the 50% of Guess they had sold to the Nakashes a few years earlier. 
By the end of 1985, the investigators told the committee, Mr. Saranow was negotiating for a job at Guess. 
In 1986, he asked for an unpaid leave from the agency to go to work for Guess. 

   The congressional investigators, who disparaged the evidence the Marcianos provided, charged that the agency's internal investigation of Mr. Saranow's alleged conflict of interest was sloppy, ignoring some important witnesses. 
The investigation, they said, was headed by an employee who had a "close personal relationship" with Mr. Saranow. 

   Mr. Saranow, who couldn't be reached for comment yesterday, left the agency before the IRS finished its investigation. 
He never went to work for Guess. 

</TEXT>

   Morton Downey Jr., stung by the cancellation of his talk show because of a lack of advertising, announced a variety of plans.

<HL> Short Interest
@  Increases 0.9%
@  In OTC Stocks
@  ---
@  Rise for the Overall Market
@  Contrasts With Declines
@  Reported by Exchanges
@  ----
@  By Sonja Steptoe
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<IN> STOCK MARKET, OFFERINGS (STK)
SECURITIES INDUSTRY (SCR) </IN>
<DATELINE> NEW YORK  </DATELINE>
<TEXT>
   Short interest in Nasdaq over-the-counter stocks grew only 0.9% as of mid-July, primarily because investors aren't betting on further sizable declines among big OTC technology stocks. 

   The overall increase on the OTC market, however, contrasted with the recent declines in short interest on both the New York and American stock exchanges. 
On the Big Board, short interest dropped 8.35%; it slipped 0.89% on the Amex. 

   Although many analysts and investors are unsure about the outlook for stocks in computer-related industries, and prices of many of those issues have declined in recent weeks, short-selling of OTC technology stocks wasn't nearly as heavy as it was last year when similar conditions prevailed. 

   Michael Murphy, who edits newsletters on short-selling and on technology issues in San Francisco, said the reason is that short-sellers don't view the group as overpriced. 
"The valuations just aren't excessive, compared to the rest of the market," Mr. Murphy said. 
"So, even though the outlook may be shaky, {with} the stocks already down, the opportunity to make money on the short side is reduced." 

   Among the largest OTC issues, many of which are stocks of high-technology companies, short interest fell 0.7%. 
In the same period in 1988, it rose 5.9%. 
Stocks with big short-interest declines as of mid-July included technology issues Apple Computer, Intel, Sun Microsystems, McCaw Cellular Communications and Novell. 

   Typically, short-sellers bet that a stock's price will fall. 
They sell borrowed stock that eventually must be replaced, aiming to profit by replacing it with stock purchased later at a lower price. 
Short interest is the number of shares borrowed and sold but not yet replaced. 

   Nasdaq stocks that showed a surge in short interest included Daisy Systems, an engineering systems issue. 
Short interest in it rose 32.5%, or 449,201 shares, to 1.8 million shares. 
Digital Microwave, a microwave radio and fiber-optics concern, saw its short interest rise 267,229 shares, or 19%, to 1.7 million shares. 

   Short interest in First Executive, an insurer that is a large buyer of junk bonds, rose 35%, or 324,566 shares, to 1.3 million. 
Mr. Murphy said many short-sellers are flocking to issues of companies with ties to the junk bond market, including First Executive. 
Many analysts have said that if the economy goes into a recession, there could be a wave of defaults among these high-yield, high-risk debt issues. 

   Although heavy short-selling is generally regarded as a bearish sign for stocks, it can be interpreted more positively on the grounds that shorted shares must eventually be replaced through buying. 
In addition, changes in short interest in some stocks may be caused by arbitrage. 
For example, an investor may seek to profit during some merger situations by buying stock in one company involved and shorting the stock of the other. 

   The National Association of Securities Dealers, which operates the Nasdaq computer system on which 5,200 OTC issues trade, compiles short interest data in two categories: the approximately two-thirds and generally biggest of the Nasdaq stocks that trade on the National Market System and the one-third that aren't a part of the system. 

   The rise in OTC short interest in July resulted from a surge in short-selling of the smaller stocks. 
In non-NMS stocks, short interest in 1,335 of those issues totaled 47.2 million shares, compared with 44 million shares in 1,268 securities in June. 
This represents 1.31 days' average trading volume, compared with 1.11 days a month earlier. 

   Short interest in 2,406 National Market System securities totaled 173.2 million shares as of mid-July, down from 174.5 million shares in 2,390 issues in June. 
July short interest in these issues represents 1.94 days' average trading volume on the system for the reporting period, compared with 1.69 days the previous month. 

   The adjacent tables show the issues in which a short interest position of at least 50,000 shares existed as of July 14 or in which there was a short position change of at least 25,000 shares since June 15. 

</TEXT>

<HL> PEOPLE PATTERNS
@  ----
@  Compiled by the Staff of American Demographics Magazine </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<TEXT>
   Travel Industry 

   Gets More Businesslike 

   BUSINESS TRAVEL is really taking off. 
Twenty-four percent of trips taken last year in the U.S. were for business reasons, up from 19% in 1983, according to the U.S. Travel Data Center. 

   The share of pleasure trips remained stable, at 69%, while the share of trips for such things as weddings and family emergencies dropped to 7% from 12%. 

   The demographic characteristics of business travelers are somewhat different from those of pleasure travelers. 
Business travelers are more likely to be men, 61% compared with 50% of vacation travelers. 

   Because business travelers are concentrated in the 25-to-44 age group, they are slightly more likely than pleasure travelers to be married and have children. 

   The biggest differences between business and vacation travel show up in the trips themselves. 
About 43% of business trips involve air travel vs. just 19% of pleasure trips. 

   In addition, the average business trip is longer in distance than the average pleasure trip -- 1,370 miles vs. 950 miles. 
At the same time, business trips are shorter in duration, averaging 4.4 nights, compared with 5.2 nights for pleasure trips. 

   Two-thirds of business trips involve hotel stays, compared with only 39% of pleasure trips. 
And business travelers are twice as likely as vacationers to use a travel agent's services, 20% compared with 11%. 

   Business travelers are human, too. 
Nearly half combined business and vacation on at least one trip last year. 

   Couples Without Kids 

   Most Apt to Own Homes 

   IF YOU THOUGHT the typical homeowner was married with children, think again. 

   Census Bureau survey data show that 32% of U.S. homeowners are married couples with related children under 18. 
But 37% are married couples without any kids living with them. 

   The disparity is partly because there are more couples without children than with children, 26.3 million vs. 25.5 million. 
But it's also due to the fact that couples without kids have a higher homeownership rate, 82% compared with 74% for those with children. 

   Many of these couples without children are older empty-nesters, rather than young couples starting out. 

   Overall, 64% of American households are homeowners, according to a 1988 Census Bureau survey. 
Married couples, regardless of whether they have children, lead the pack, with an ownership rate of 78%. 
Men who head families on their own follow, with 58% owning homes. 

   All other types of households have homeownership rates of less than 50%. 
Only 45% of women who head families by themselves own a home. 
This is even lower than the 47% ownership rate for people who live alone, many of whom are older widows. 

   People who live in other nonfamily households, such as unmarried couples and college roommates, are least likely to own homes, with a rate of 31%. 

   Family Types Vary 

   In Education, Income 

   NOT ALL MARRIED couples with children are the so-called traditional family of yesteryear. 

   The prevalence of divorce and remarriage in contemporary America has given rise to a growing number of reconstituted, or blended, families, and there are major differences among them. 

   Jeanne Moorman of the National Institute on Drug Abuse and Donald Hernandez of the Census Bureau found that couples who have only biological children are the largest group of two-parent families -- 79%. 
These parents tend to have more education and higher incomes, and most have been married only once. 

   Adoptive parents are similar to biological parents in education and income, but are a much smaller group -- just 2% of couples with kids. 
They also are a little older than biological parents and have been married longer. 

   The 2% of couples with both biological and adopted children are slightly less educated than purely biological or adoptive parents, and on average, have more kids. 

   As for couples with stepchildren, they tend to be younger and to have lower educational and income levels. 
Couples with both biological and stepchildren are the youngest parents of all. 
They also are the least educated and have the smallest incomes. 

   Odds and Ends 

   ONE OUT OF FIVE American wives is better educated than her husband, according to a 1988 Census Bureau survey. 
In 26% of married couples, the husband is better educated. 
In 54%, both spouses have the same level of education. . . . 
Teen-age acne notwithstanding, the average age of patients who visit dermatologists is 41, according to a 1985 survey from the National Center for Health Statistics. 
And perhaps in anticipation of midlife crises, the average age of psychiatric patients is 39. 

</TEXT>
</DOC>
<DOC>
<DOCNO> WSJ890726-0113 </DOCNO>
<DD> = 890726 </DD>

<HL> Who's News:

<TEXT>
   Donald E. Martella, formerly vice president, operations, was named president and chief executive officer of this maker of parallel processing subsystems. 
He succeeds Jack Harper, a company founder who was named chairman. 
Mr. Harper succeeds as chairman Jesse Aweida, who resigned because of a potential conflict with other business interests. 
The company said Mr. Aweida, founder of Storage Technology Corp. and a technology investor, will remain an adviser to and major shareholder of the company.

<HL> IBM Cancels
@  New-Product
@  Announcement
@  ---
@  Embarrassing Move Comes
@  As Rivals Make Inroads
@  In Disk-Drive Business
@  ----
@  By Paul B. Carroll
@  Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal </HL>
<DD> 07/26/89 </DD>
<SO> WALL STREET JOURNAL (J) </SO>
<CO> IBM EK </CO>
<IN> COMPUTERS AND INFORMATION TECHNOLOGY (CPR) </IN>
<TEXT>
   International Business Machines Corp. canceled a major disk-drive product announcement scheduled for yesterday, an embarrassing move that dims IBM's short-term outlook. 

   The computer giant contacted customers in the past few days after running into last-minute technical problems that could delay the products until at least early next year, consultants said. 
That will hurt, because growth in IBM's huge disk-drive business has slowed this year as such competitors as Amdahl Corp., Hitachi Ltd. and Storage Technology Corp. have caught up with IBM's technology and waged a price war. 
The new high-end products would have put IBM safely back out in front. 

   Market researchers had predicted IBM would generate some $5 billion of revenue from high-end disk drives this year. 
Thomas Donovan, an analyst at IDC Financial Services, said $1.5 billion to $1.8 billion of the $5 billion had been expected to come from the new products. 
He said that IBM has been telling customers that it may still be able to ship the disk drives in volume this year, but that he thinks they may not be available until 1990. 

   "Any time a major company pulls an announcement at the last minute, after they've invited analysts (to briefings) and lined up hotel rooms, you know there's a major problem," he said. 

   An IBM spokeswoman, while confirming that the announcement had been pulled, wouldn't explain what caused the problems or say when the products would be available. 

   Steve Cohen, a securities analyst at SoundView Financial Group Inc., said IBM is putting together an interim marketing plan, "and you don't put one together if you're just going to have a 30-day slip." 
He said that even if customers continue to buy from IBM, the profit margins on the existing products are much lower than they would have been on the new ones. 

   It isn't surprising that IBM would have trouble with the technology. 
High-end disk drives, which are hooked up to mainframes, use phenomenally complex technology. 
Moreover, the new products are expected to store three times as much as current drives while operating at much faster speeds. 

   What's surprising is that IBM didn't catch the problem earlier in its testing program. 
IBM knows how treacherous disk-drive technology can be. 
In the early 1980s, the company had disastrous delays after announcing some disk drives. 
More recently, IBM went through a long delay before shipping the current generation of controllers used with the disk drives. 

   This year, IBM has also gone through some well-publicized mainframe manufacturing problems, as well as problems in making personal computers and low-end disk drives. 
Together with the latest problem, those incidents have some analysts wondering whether IBM didn't lose too much expertise through an early retirement program and whether it hasn't dispersed decision-making a bit too much. 

   Separately, IBM announced an unusual agreement under which it will build and operate a data center for Eastman Kodak Co. 
The IBM operation will take over the work done by four Kodak centers, and 300 Kodak workers will become IBM employees. 
Kodak will continue to develop its own software applications and plot its own strategy for information services, but said it hopes to cut costs as much as 50% by turning the operation over to IBM. 

   David E. McDowell, an IBM vice president, said such an arrangement doesn't necessarily make sense for all companies. 
But he said that "if we've accomplished anything, I hope, it's that we've demonstrated how far we'll go" in putting together a solution for a customer. 

   IBM also said it has four-megabit memory chips -- the latest generation of DRAMs, or dynamic random access memory chips -- available on memory add-in boards for PS/2 personal computers. 
IBM has been the leader in this technology, even though Japanese companies dominate the market, and the announcement is a technological milestone. 
At the same time, IBM's list prices -- at around $900 a megabyte of memory -- were not aggressive, so the announcement is likely to have little effect in the market soon. 

   In New York Stock Exchange composite trading yesterday, IBM shares fell 75 cents to close at $112.75. 

</TEXT>
